# [CHAPTER 1What’s Wrong with Monoliths?](contents.xhtml#ch01a)

Awareness is the great agent for change.

— Eckhart Tolle

Monoliths are the great boogeymen of modern-day software engineering. Developers will gather around the (virtual) campfire, trading horror stories about that terrible Windows Desktop app that had a week of downtime because the JVM crashed every 24 hours or the 1990s banking application that required a team of 40 people to release. We’ve all had a coworker or friend commiserate while they are stuck in a big Monolith, wishing there was a way out.

Seeking better solutions, people embraced microservices.

Result: An 8-9 figure software development project, 100s of people, half the features scrapped, and now they have TWO terrible platforms to maintain, not just one. The cure can be worse than the disease.

Let us be the ones to tell you that there is hope. Real hope. Not one found in pursuing the latest and greatest software development trends, but real tactics, real progress that can be made to improve the software architecture and lives of developers, teams, and businesses worldwide.

But it all starts here. Understanding Monoliths is the key to being able to truly address some of their inherent flaws. After all, not everything is entirely bad or good. By understanding the problems and benefits of Monolithic architecture, you will avoid common pitfalls and provide real solutions for business and engineering.

[Structure](contents.xhtml#sc2_1a)

In this chapter, we will cover the following topics:

  * What are monoliths?

    * Big codebase

    * Few deployment units

    * Other monolith smells

      * Centralized

      * Old

    * Sidebar: Does size matter?

  * Issues with monoliths

  * Patterns in software engineering

  * Living with Monoliths: anti-patterns, side effects, and amplifiers

    * Monolith anti-patterns

      * High coupling

      * Wrong abstractions

      * Lack of isolation

    * Monolith side effects

      * Difficult deployments

      * Insufficient testing

      * Lack of ownership

      * Slow adoption of new technologies

      * Slow development cycle

    * Sidebar: Automation and anti-patterns

    * Amplifiers

      * Broken windows/copy and paste

      * Microservice envy

      * Lack of Talent Density

      * Fear of change

  * Are all monoliths bad?

    * Monoliths are a form of software architecture

  * Monolith benefits

    * Refactoring and change impact

    * Simplify some migrations

    * A good starting point

    * Simplified infrastructure

  * Types of monoliths

    * Classical, distributed, and modular monoliths

    * Modular monoliths: the good kind of monolith

    * Modular monoliths: Istio

    * Modular monoliths: mobile SuperApps

  * Can bad monoliths be avoided?

  * Things to remember




[What are monoliths?](contents.xhtml#sc2_2a)

First, we need to understand monoliths deeply to fight them. Otherwise, what are we fighting against? How do we know when we win? How can you define success? Monoliths are a very common theme in the software industry. Everybody has their definition of what a monolith is and many preconceptions. However, we need to start with a common understanding. Therefore, we need to define what a monolith is. So, what is a monolith? What comes to your mind when you think of a monolith?

To answer this question, refer to the following Figure 1.1, which consists of monoliths:

Figure 1.1: Monoliths  
Source: [https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_works_similar_to_the_2020_Utah_monolith](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_works_similar_to_the_2020_Utah_monolith)

What do all these images have in common? Based on the preceding figure, we see big blocks that are hard to move and not easy to understand. We get the same traits when it comes to code.

Monoliths are a style of software architecture where a large amount of code is deployed using a small number of deployment units like exes, JARs, WARs, etc. In short, a big codebase with a few deployed artifacts. It is the natural state of most application development when it just gets started, having all the code in the same code repository and deployment unit simplifies your life in the early days where finding product-market fit trumps premature optimization.

[Big codebase](contents.xhtml#sc3_3a)

Monoliths typically have a lot of code. It could be in one source code repository or multiple repos for some types of monoliths which we will discuss later. But, there is ALWAYS a lot of code involved. Of course, what is big is relative. Big codebases are not a problem per se; it is more like a smell, but it happens often enough that it is a key characteristic of a monolith.

Sometimes monoliths are big in other ways. Monolithic applications can be quite complex with tons of features and often need big teams. Actively managing a monolith takes a lot of resources (and brave hearts!). Monoliths may or may not have many users or need lots of computing to accomplish their goals, but they have big, complex codebases.

[Few deployment units](contents.xhtml#sc3_4a)

When we build and release software solutions, we often have a system to do so. We use code versioning tools like git, for instance. We have issue trackers to track bugs, we capture requirements and needs in JIRA tickets, and we have ways to organize people to transform needs into solutions, often via Agile and Lean methods like Scrum, XP, Kanban, and many others. Teams use these and many other systems to divide their work into small, understandable chunks.

However, by its nature, a monolith shapes all of those diverse processes because of one simple fact: monoliths tend to have few deployment units1, to the point of being just a single massive binary. Think about a massive .exe for a Windows desktop application or a JAR or WAR in Java.2

Having one or a few big artifacts similarly drives a huge process to get that deployment unit ready and tested. This reality applies regardless of whether the team is shipping a change in a single line of code or a massive feature; it still triggers the same army of developers and testers because it’s all in one (or very few) packaged artifacts containing the entire application.

Here, you can see a great example of how software architecture shapes organizations. Structure and design influence the way in which a thing can be accomplished and who needs to do it. As an architect, you may think your role is limited to software, code, and how it is structured and deployed; in truth, your systems shape organizations and lie at the heart of an organization’s ability to scale. Organizations will take the safe path, and if your architecture means the safe path to releasing code requires all teams to work night and day to release, debug, and then patch your monolith in that order… your organization will do that. This is not fun at all. Architecture is the great enabler or disabler, and a simple thing like “my app is deployed with 15 teams updating a single WAR in production” can lead to dysfunction and misery… misery only you can fix.3

[Other monolith smells](contents.xhtml#sc3_5a)

Monoliths are typically big and produce few deployment units, but there are other frequently found characteristics seen in monoliths. They are not a guarantee of a monolith, but they are so often found in monoliths that they are closely associated.

If you come across these, chances are you are dealing with a monolith. We call these sorts of items “smells”. The term code smell4 was coined by Kent Beck in the late 90s; a code smell is something that might lead to a deeper problem. In the context of monoliths, smells are more tricky, they may indicate a problem or a symptom, not a root cause, and the problem might be something else.

[Centralized](contents.xhtml#sc4_6a)

Monoliths favor centralization, with all features, configurations and tests all in one place. A simple way to think of it is that all our code will be running on one machine, like a desktop application. All our code is deployed together and just uses one machine to deliver all capabilities your business needs. All features, capabilities, and GUIs will share the same resources: CPU, memory, disk, and network.

The opposite of centralization is distribution, where everything is split apart. Imagine a microservices architecture where your software is running on multiple machines, accessing resources like databases or configurations that also reside on other devices.

Is centralization inherently bad? If it were, we would have an easy solution: divide the codebase. Let us introduce microservices and slice and dice our monolith into many pieces. The monolith would be gone, and our problem would be solved. Using the Microservices style of architecture (MSA), we move away from centralization and embrace distribution. We moved the needle from one extreme to another. But did this approach actually fix all of our problems? The following Figure 1.2 depicts the centralization versus distribution spectrum:

Figure 1.2: Centralization vs. Distribution Spectrum

Microservices are distributed but may share elements that, under the hood, can make them still centralized. Internal shared libraries, hidden contracts, shared databases. If you look beyond the surface, that is, beyond the deployed artifacts, but instead look at all of the components of your system; will you find that they are all properly isolated and truly distributed? Or is it a deeply centralized system just masquerading as a distributed system? We will discuss this later in our book.

The centralization vs distribution spectrum does not mean all or nothing. However, with microservices, you tend more toward distribution than centralization. Monoliths tend more towards centralization. Some monoliths, let’s say the “classical” monoliths, are almost entirely centralized. But more modern monoliths have distributed elements too. Truthfully, monoliths can fall at various points on this spectrum.

But for now, let’s all agree that monoliths tend towards centralization.

[Old](contents.xhtml#sc4_7a)

Along with being big, monoliths are often old. After all, it can take a long time to accumulate the LOC and the features set. Of course, there is a range here: it could take only 2 years for a massive team to accumulate millions of lines of code or 20 years for an old beast to accumulate features over time.

Old software is not necessarily legacy software. Much of the software that drives internet-scale companies is several decades old. Nevertheless, it is stable, battle-tested, and “just works”: think of Linux, relational databases like Postgres, Java, and many more open-source solutions. But, old software can often make it difficult to find developers with the expertise in such languages or frameworks involved.

So while monoliths are often old, they are not always old.

[Sidebar: Does size matter?](contents.xhtml#sc3_8a)

Microservices are the architectural style frequently depicted in opposition to monoliths, as we did in Figure 1.2. Microservices are independently deployed services which are micro in terms of responsibility and granularity, directly opposing large solutions with many responsibilities (like monoliths).

You might have heard the following statement in the real world, “Microservices are better than monoliths! One is small, and the other is big, and big is the problem! Case Closed!”

The answer is much more complicated than that. Whether small is truly good really depends on the project’s needs.

In the real world, complexity always needs to live somewhere. In order to have value, a service needs to provide functionality accessible through abstractions; and depending on the nature of those abstractions, it is not always possible to keep things small, and add the necessary value with a few lines of code. In short, having an extensive code base inside a single service does not necessarily mean it is awful.

While having a “big” service could be a smell, when doing any kind of evaluation of a proposed design or existing architecture, you need context from other aspects of the solution. And for that, you must pose questions such as the following:

  * How painful is it for the engineers to maintain the solution?

  * How frequently do the team members create bugs when making simple code changes?

  * Are there unit, integration, or end-to-end tests? Do they have consistent pass rates? Are they automated?

  * Is the design/architecture robust and well-suited to the domain, adding new features easily without significant changes or requiring multiple teams and cross team coordination?

  * Can you easily understand the code and figure out what is going on?

  * Can you debug the solution easily? Can you isolate problems quickly and test parts of the application independently?

  * Is the release process automated? How much manual work is needed to build, test and deploy the solution?




One could argue that we are generally talking about technical debt, which is true! Size is often associated with trouble because once you have more people, coordination is more difficult, keeping a high bar of technical quality is generally more difficult, and under pressure to release features, teams compromise. Size is a smell in that fashion: more people, more changes, more features, more technical debt accumulates. It is not just size, though - quality tends to decay when many people touch the same code base, combined with low or zero code ownership.

Monoliths are big in terms of their size, and as size is correlated with these issues, it is easy to get confused and say, “Monoliths are a problem because they are big.” It is more that big projects tend towards having more technical debt, whether they are monoliths or not.

Going to the other extreme (refer to Figure 1.3), having a distributed system, might make it seem that the problem would magically disappear. But, the inherent complexity of your solution has not changed. The complexity has been shifted around; it could be to a different service layer, to your UI, in the creation of an aggregation layer, to the database, to the client; but it has to go somewhere.

Figure 1.3: Size spectrum

We would argue there is no given right or wrong answer on solution size, but you have to be aware of the risks. Being too big, you risk having too much responsibility or features, or coupling items that should not be coupled; being too small, you could make the wrong tradeoff in organizing your complexity5, creating coordination issues across teams, technical debt, and causing the organization to move slower.

In short, judging the impact of size on a software architecture is a complex decision. It is not as simple as “Monoliths are Big, Big is Bad”. As you explore throughout this chapter, you will see some of the tradeoffs in practice, and we will explore the art of rightsizing services in [Chapter 6](ch06.xhtml), Principles of Proper Services.

[Issues with monoliths](contents.xhtml#sc2_9a)

Now that we have defined a monolith, what are the consequences of having a monolith? How do they affect systems, designs, and organizations?

Software development is tricky, and today it is never more true that every company is a software company. So, no matter your company’s industry and reality, your business leaders have expectations of the software you develop and/or manage. And in turn, when those expectations are not born out, we see the same negative perceptions time and time again, whether you are dealing with legacy monoliths or not and whether they are true or not.

So, let us start with what businesses want from technology (Table 1.1). And what does it look like when those are not met?

Expectation |  The Perception  
---|---  
Better user experience |  Complex and difficult for the user.  
Innovative customer solutions |  Old technologies, lack of differentiation, barely working solutions, stability issues, too many bugs.  
Short time to market (aka lead time) |  Slow, never on time, expensive, bad surprises, hard to rely on. It feels like the tech organization doesn’t want to improve. Should we outsource tech, or build a shadow IT6 org instead?  
Reasonably predictable maintenance and operating costs |  High maintenance cost. Huge, hidden, unpredictable, and growing operational costs.  
  
Table 1.1: Business Expectations vs Perceptions

Similarly, technology organizations (Table 1.2) worldwide have expectations; engineers have things they want to accomplish for the business or their career, and have perceptions about the code and possible technological solutions… even if they might be the source of the problem.

Expectation |  The Perception  
---|---  
Technology freedom |  Lack of trust. No flexibility, difficult to introduce new ideas or freely change the code. It feels like the business doesn’t want to improve.  
Exciting and challenging problems to solve |  Boring tasks. Exhausted fighting technical debt which makes it harder to deliver business value.  
Work in systems that are stable and sane |  Drowning in technical debt, a huge amount of bad practices, anti-patterns, a lack of testing, lack of talent density, stifled by a huge bureaucracy. All of this is a big morale killer. Should I look for a new job?  
  
Table 1.2: Technology Expectations vs Perceptions

When those negative perceptions rule the interactions between technology and the business, it’s not good for anyone and creates an ‘us vs. them’ mentality. Whether you have monoliths or not, this reality can set in regardless of what software or industry you work in, or even the truth: perception can drive reality, and a fixed mindset can set in, limiting growth and change.

We describe this here because it’s key to understanding how monoliths can damage organizations because monoliths lead to anti-patterns and side effects and are subject to amplifying effects that make these problems even worse:

  * Testing takes too long, often not fully automated and lacking ideal coverage.

  * Takes too long to implement changes, more than it should.

  * Long time spent debugging, troubleshooting, and struggling with technical debt rather than delivering value.

  * Very difficult and painful to perform migrations in libraries, frameworks, and tools.

  * Fear of changing the code due to bad past attempts.

  * Burnout and low developer morale.

  * A fertile environment for Anti-Patterns and technical debt that are not fixed but increase over time.




We will now discuss each of these elements in greater detail. But the message again is: the right architecture is key. It can inherently drive the right culture, enable business and technological innovation, and ensure you have motivated, capable developers.

[Patterns in software engineering](contents.xhtml#sc2_10a)

Before we cover Anti-Patterns, let’s briefly describe Design Patterns. Design Patterns in Object Oriented Programming (OOP) have existed since the 90s, classically embodied by the book: Design Patterns - Elements of Reusable Object-Oriented Software.7

Design Patterns are a set of commonly found patterns in software architecture, describing frequently found structures and designs, and providing a set of shared words and concepts to facilitate communication. Patterns8 are divided into 3 groups: Creational, Structure, and Behavior, and became very popular in languages like Java, C++, and Smalltalk. The original GOF book has 23 classical patterns, but hundreds of patterns exist in software engineering; see Figure 1.4 for an overview.

Figure 1.4: Vince Hudson - Design Patterns periodic table  
Source: <http://www.vincehuston.org/dp/>

The definition of a Pattern is very interesting: each possesses a context, describing the specific intent, purpose, and motivation behind the pattern. Each has specific structures manifested by arranging object-oriented code with practical code examples.

Patterns are useful because they speed up our discussions and reduce our cognitive load when applied correctly: after all, code is read many more times than it is written. However, we create technical debt and complexity when we apply Design Patterns without any need, or incorrectly apply a pattern.

[What is an anti-pattern?](contents.xhtml#sc3_11a)

In 1995, Andrew Koenig coined the term “Anti-Pattern”9, in contrast to Design Patterns:

An antipattern is just like a pattern, except that instead of a solution, it gives something that looks superficially like a solution but isn’t one.

Like design patterns, anti-patterns are commonly encountered ways of approaching a problem that clearly make the problem worse. Anti-patterns lead to technical debt and further unnecessary complexity when tackling problems. The benefit of clearly defining these anti-patterns is that we can also define a better pattern that can be applied and, therefore, a better solution.

[Living with Monoliths: Anti-patterns, side effects, and amplifiers](contents.xhtml#sc2_12a)

Living with monoliths means a world of challenges, where anti-patterns rule the codebase, side effects make the effect of change hard to predict, and amplifiers reinforce and further spread the negative effects. 

[Monolith anti-patterns](contents.xhtml#sc3_13a)

As a consequence of being monoliths - being big, generally old, having few deployment units, and tending towards centralization - there are some anti-patterns frequently found in monoliths.

Sociological and industrial forces also make common anti-patterns happen, such as Digital Transformation, the push to public cloud, bimodal IT, Agile Transformations, and many other movements. We are not saying these movements are necessarily bad. Sometimes companies use these movements as push factors to move the business along, get investment, and justify larger change. Whatever the motivation, they create high-profile investments and big, expensive migrations that demand quick results. When you need to make significant technology changes and make your organization move fast, plus your talent density is not ideal - you have a formula for trouble. At the same time, what value are you delivering to the business? So, for the business, you just spend a lot of capital with no visible return.

So, while these anti-patterns are not specific to monoliths, they’re found so frequently because of how monoliths work that they are closely associated with them. In short: the traits of monoliths frequently give rise to these anti-patterns.

[High coupling](contents.xhtml#sc4_14a)

Coupling could be seen as how much information and software dependencies know about each other. Coupling is not bad – it is necessary for software. Components need to be connected, your UI needs to call your backend, which needs to call your database… coupling is inevitable.

But you need proper boundaries. Your UI shouldn’t know about your database schema, and your backend should not be needed to deliver properly formed CSS. When you fail to limit those boundaries - to have clearly defined, slow-moving contracts guiding communication and action - changing components can have terrible, unexpected effects.

When components are unnecessarily coupled to one another, we call that phenomenon “high coupling”, and Monoliths are a perfect playground to create a nightmare of high coupling.

Monoliths create many temptations and lures that can lead a team towards high coupling – see Figure 1.5 for an overview. After all, the code is in one place: teams have access to read inner methods, public classes. With only one quick change, that feature is live, and you’re the business’s hero! But, has the change been evaluated for correctness? For longevity? For whether those methods should have been shared in the first place? Monoliths tear down the barriers and easily enable such terrible architectural decisions, creating swamps of high coupling that give engineers nightmares.

Figure 1.5: High coupling in monoliths

[Wrong abstractions](contents.xhtml#sc4_15a)

Thinking back to Design Patterns, there are a whole host of good ways to solve specific problems. Ways in which you’ve matched the context of the problem to the solution. Anti-patterns inherently describe ways in which we think we’ve matched the right pattern to solve a problem, but we’ve made it worse. In short, our “Wrong Abstractions” anti-pattern actually refers to the general case where we’ve applied a design pattern incorrectly.

Let’s take a simple example.

An engineer sees code in one part of a monolith and realizes he needs to use that code in an entirely different section. No problem, he thinks; code reuse is a great thing, so let me make a reusable method. (Probably in a util class, full of about 1000 other such methods.) Both instances point to this shared method: great, problem solved.

Except, that didn’t turn out to be so clear-cut. Over time, another engineer is faced with a feature request that affects this code, and suddenly they’re faced with a problem: the first implementation needs one version of method 1, and the second now has different requirements. So they choose to go forward and modify the shared method, thinking again that code reuse is the highest good, which is the best choice.

Now, fast forward by several years - remember, monoliths are old! - and many other instances of the same problem faced by different engineers. Suddenly, you have a util class and a method that is thousands of lines long, unnecessarily complex, and terrifying to change; because an engineer decided that code reuse was the most important thing.

We might be being unfair to that engineer - after all, it’s almost inevitable that some of our right decisions may become wrong over time. But it’s a clear example where attempting to force-fit an abstraction - a new shared method - incorrectly and failing to recognize that the two cases you’re trying to abstract are not the same.

Monoliths risk having wrong abstractions with greater frequency because they lack a firm boundary – see Figure 1.6 for an overview. With the trivial ability to reuse code, like with our high coupling example, it’s easy to look at an implementation and “force fit,” an abstraction that does not apply. Wrong abstraction can be manifested in different forms, like missing or leaky abstractions where the boundaries are not respected, like in Figure 1.6. Service classes are responsible for holding business rules. Data Access Objects (DAO) should handle persistence and data access. No rules are being enforced here, which is bad, and boundaries are not being respected. Calling the DAO directly is the wrong abstraction because it skips the business rules and introduces high coupling.

Figure 1.6: The Wrong Abstraction

Microservices can suffer from the same problems through the concept of internally shared libraries, which we’ll cover in greater detail in [Chapter 4](ch04.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Internal Shared Libraries.

[Lack of isolation](contents.xhtml#sc4_16a)

Isolation is key to being able to move quickly. If you want multiple teams developing in parallel, if you want safety when deploying hundreds of changes at the same time, you need isolation. Isolation is key to safety, speed, and everything the business and technology want. While high coupling is one form in which monoliths can encourage a lack of isolation, there are many more; you’ll hear about this in the next upcoming chapter, [Chapter 2](ch02.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Lack of Isolation.

[Monolith side effects](contents.xhtml#sc3_17a)

Once you have the 3 key antipatterns in place - high coupling, wrong abstractions, and a lack of isolation - the consequences they drive are equally as destructive.

[Difficult deployments](contents.xhtml#sc4_18a)

Some problems are not anti-patterns created by bad culture or prior technical choices but are inherent to the Monolith Architecture model. Difficult deployments are one of them, along with general stability issues and frequent restarts.

Back to first principles: barring languages or frameworks that permit hot or partial deployments, typically, you need to deploy a monolith as one complete piece. (Figure 1.7) You cannot safely deploy elements or update pieces of the monolith independently, a consequence of centralization and a result of having few deployment units. All teams are gated on the deployment event, whether in non-production or production, waiting to confirm that their changes are present and work as expected.

Having to deploy a software element as a complete element is not inherently a bad thing. You want to avoid downtime and the resulting lack of application availability.

Downtime is sometimes avoidable for classical monoliths, especially backend services. To support high availability, typically, there are multiple instances of a service spread across physical infrastructure, such as in Amazon Web Services (AWS) use of Availability Zones (AZ), by deploying one instance per zone. This permits DevOps techniques like rolling deployments, where you would first deploy on node 2A, then 2B, and then 2C, or Blue/Green, where the software lives in a dark pool before being promoted to being a live instance. Both can allow you to mitigate deployment downtime.

Figure 1.7: Monolith Drawbacks: Deployment + Restart

The real issue that makes deployments difficult and inevitably leads to downtime is related to a bigger problem: the lack of isolation anti-pattern we discussed earlier. (Figure 1.8) With monoliths and their high coupling, hidden ties make any change risky, inherent to the lack of failure isolation. Cascading failure is very dangerous. For example, your reporting module or analytics reports could tear down your online system, creating constant downtime. Your malfunctioning profile module could return broken data, corrupting your online reports. Often the safest route is to have full application downtime to avoid side effects.

Figure 1.8: Monolith Drawbacks: Availability / Failure Isolation Issues

Solving a backend monolith may require the application of microservices. Inherently, deploying individual components can mean easier deployments as long as they are properly designed, as we will cover in [Chapter 6](ch06.xhtml), Principles of Proper Services.

Remember that monoliths are not just limited to backend services or full-stack applications. Customer-facing websites and mobile-first applications have similar drawbacks: one big deployment, with the risk of a substantial bug or downtime. Thankfully, similar patterns to Microservices exist for frontend code, like Microfrontends.

While beyond the scope of this book to cover in detail, one example of an effective micro frontend technology stack is Webpack’s Module Federation ( <https://webpack.js.org/concepts/module-federation/> ), and mobile-first applications can leverage code split solutions like Repack ( <https://github.com/callstack/repack> ). 

While difficult deployments are one key side effect, they are a part of a broader theme: the lack of stability often seen in monoliths. The inherent lack of stability created by difficult deployments is one of the key factors that can make a business finally throw up its hands and support change.

[Insufficient testing](contents.xhtml#sc4_19a)

Software can go wrong in many ways, and given the high coupling, wrong abstractions, and lack of isolation, testing a monolith is often a nightmare. Even simple changes can need a massive number of scenarios that need to be covered to truly ensure there are no hidden side effects from any change. The resulting test suites are often slow to execute and painful to debug.

Given a monolith’s age and inherent complexity, the testing codebase itself can become as big and complex as the monolith itself and just as badly understood. As such, most teams find it difficult to fully test a monolith and miss essential failure scenarios that only emerge at the worst times.

Effective software testing is hard. Testing a monolith makes a hard task even harder, given our additional constraints, and the result is usually not up to the challenge.

Smaller, isolated components mean easier, compartmentalized tests and faster testing cycles. We’ll cover the essential part testing plays in exiting a monolith in a later chapter, [Chapter 7](ch07.xhtml), Proper Service Testing.

[Lack of ownership](contents.xhtml#sc4_20a)

Ownership of code is about knowing which individual or team is principally responsible for a set of codes. In well-constructed software projects, ownership ensures code is reviewed, well-tested, stays stable, and kept up to date with security concerns.

Due to their lack of isolation, the lack of clear boundaries often found in monoliths follows on to the organization itself. It can be unclear where any team’s ownership boundaries begin and end. Looking at the commit history of a monolith, especially a frequently accessed or used class, can be an absolute nightmare. These essential elements of the monolith become “shared code”, used by everyone but owned by no one in particular, ultimately suffering from the tragedy of the commons.

Not all monoliths are created equal, and some teams can overcome this burden through discipline and a tight process. But the monolith itself inherently makes overcoming these burdens more challenging than otherwise.

We’ll stress the importance of ownership in [Chapter 5](ch05.xhtml), Assessments.

[Slow adoption of new technologies](contents.xhtml#sc4_21a)

When you have all your solutions in a single deployment unit, updating one technical component can mean changing many other components. Doing so safely is one of the key challenges of managing any monolith.

Some of these updates happen as you introduce new features or new bugs. But, even simple library updates can lead to an entanglement that is hard to unravel. This is known as Binary Coupling, where components in your system are effectively coupled with binary10 dependencies. Let’s illustrate this with a simple example.

Take the sample monolith application depicted in Figure 1.9. Your reports module uses Jasper Reports, and you use services that provide data via Hibernate 4, now past EOL. Everything is working in the reports module.

Figure 1.9. Monolith Drawbacks: Binary Coupling - Limits Technology Adoption

Now, the sales team wants to migrate to Hibernate 5. This could be for many reasons: the team encounters a critical security bug fixed in Hibernate 5, a key feature they want to use in the new version or an upgrade of Spring Boot forces an upgrade of Hibernate. Or maybe they are just interested in new technologies!

Regardless of the reason, now you have a problem. The monolith deployment unit already includes Hibernate 4. You cannot have Hibernate 4 and Hibernate 5 under the same classpath/project. The sales team is blocked from upgrading Hibernate on their own. Either all the modules, reports, profiles, and sales, or none, are upgraded. The teams are coupled, not directly by their code, but by the binaries they share.

The all-or-nothing factor of these upgrades is particularly bad, making it difficult for teams to act proactively and do organic migrations on their own schedule. This creates more coordination points for management, and everything gets more complicated when multiple migrations happen simultaneously.

By contrast, when a service A calls another service B, service A is not highly coupled with the operating system, internal libraries, and database of service B. Services provide natural decoupling and separation of concerns. However, when an internal library C calls another library D, they are coupled and restricted in many ways. They are forced to use the same language and share all the dependency graphs, which impose further coupling and limitations.

Considering the sales team, the result of the scenario frustrates the technical team to the point where some people just give up. Because it is too hard to make improvements, which has other bad effects on developer morale and promotes bad culture and anti-patterns. The reports team is happy with Hibernate 4 and doesn’t want to get distracted from their sprint goals by doing migration which gives nothing to them. To be clear, Hibernate here is not the issue. The same problem happens with other libraries. The more you use a library, the more popular and the more chances you have to be hit.

As you can see, binary coupling is a big problem for monoliths where they are entangled, and they limit technology adoption and even change. Binary coupling creates all-or-nothing scenarios where incremental change is more difficult and sometimes impossible.

Services are not immune from binary coupling; we will cover this in detail in [Chapter 4](ch04.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Internal Shared Libraries.

Binary Coupling is one clear, easy-to-understand way in which a team’s change can affect all the others sharing the monolith or deployment unit. The general point to take home is that ANY change made by a team affects any other team, which is inherent to the centralization found in monolithic architectures. Whether it is renaming a table, refactoring a shared class, or simply adding a library that brings in MBs of additional dependencies, there is little safety due to no boundaries. Commonly performed tasks by engineers come with risks.

This is why we bring all of this back to the theme of introducing new technologies. If common tasks come with risks, how about aggressive refactoring or modernization? If forced to be done under the monolith, fear takes root, and changes are either delayed or don’t happen at all.

Technology adoption can still be performed with a classical monolith to some degree. For instance, here are some ideas of how a team can limit exposure when building a new feature:

  * Develop the new feature: … entirely outside of the monolith. Perhaps use a different tool for the job, for example, reports could be handled by a Big Data stack outside of the monolith.

  * Build the feature in the monolith but partially rely on external services: For instance, you could use a Serverless function rather than a dedicated service, like AWS Lambda.

  * Don’t build this: Use a 3rd party, full-stack service, that is, if you need to make payments, maybe use Stripe.

  * Reduce your exposure to binary coupling: By considering:

    * Using thinner libraries, i.e. MyBatis, JDBC Template. We will revisit this theme in [Chapter 4](ch04.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Internal Shared Libraries.

    * Can you copy the code instead of loading a whole new library?

    * Can you develop the capability yourself?

    * Do you need that library at all? Could you come up with a different solution?




Within reason and being careful, you definitely could explore these options. You need to be careful because, depending on the choice, these options can worsen the problem. For instance, imagine you develop a new feature outside of the monolith. How isolated would this feature be? Does this feature depend on some other features of the monolith? What about database access? Technology adoption can still be pursued in classical monoliths, but this is a double-edged sword. New technology has benefits, but embracing the new without fully assessing your current environment is also a big source of mistakes and pain. We will cover assessments further in [Chapter 5](ch05.xhtml), Assessments.

[Slow development cycle](contents.xhtml#sc4_22a)

Monoliths often have longer build times than other software architectures. (Figure 1.10) With more code and more tests to run, the result is slow. Tests can always be optimized, deleted, and run in parallel, but the more code you have, the slower it will be. Cycle time is the time a developer takes from writing a line of code to executing compilation and initial unit tests. Dealing with a high cycle time is one of the biggest pain points for developers working on monoliths.

While cycle time and build/deployment times are problems for developers working on monolith applications, the business is concerned with the overall release or lead time, the time taken to go from idea to working code in production. Overall, cycle time affects lead time, and we have already seen how embracing new technology brings additional risks for monoliths. Patches, hot deployments, or cherry-picking a change from a single branch to be deployed to production are all possible for monoliths.

Regardless, the deployment itself will be all or nothing: it is all deployed simultaneously, and all need to be tested simultaneously, as we discussed for Difficult Deployments. Of course, not all monoliths will suffer from this problem equally, but slow builds are likely inevitable, and there is no escape from the blast radius risk of any deployment. All of this takes away from lead time.

Figure 1.10: Monolith drawbacks: Slow development cycle - Engineers breaking each other

[Sidebar: Automation and anti-patterns](contents.xhtml#sc3_23a)

Automation is at the heart of the DevOps movement. Automation has several benefits, such as:

  * Improving lead time and cycle time.

  * Consistency via automation leads to greater predictability: fewer bugs and errors as compared with any manual process.

  * Automation allows your technology organization to scale more easily, allowing people to focus on the real business problems facing your company rather than rote work.




In our experience, some monoliths have automation, but more frequently, classical monoliths do not have good automation. As you consider transitioning to a distributed architecture like Microservices or Microfrontends, automation becomes increasingly important to manage the increased number of deployment units. Cloud providers like AWS make automation much easier for teams to accomplish. When you have scale, automation is non-negotiable.

What should be automated? Everything. Here are some examples:

  * Code deployments (frontend and backend)

  * Database schema changes

  * Data migrations

  * Infrastructure changes

  * Dependencies updates

  * All forms of testing (Smoke Testing, Unit, Integration, E2E, Chaos, Mutation, Stress/Load, A/B Testing, Testing in production)




So, let’s agree: we want automation. But thinking back to our goals, the business wants us to scale to ship new features and functions quickly. One way to scale is to allow multiple teams to work safely in parallel. (Figure 1.11) Does automation automatically lead to the ability to deploy and operate all of your components independently? Does automation mean your teams can work in parallel or without coordination?

Figure 1.11: Monolith drawbacks: Automation - Do you have deployment independence?

Speaking of a classical monolith, the answer to the question of independent deployment is likely: no. You need coordination which slows things down, creates bottlenecks, and holds us back. Automation might reduce the level of difficult deployments you encounter, but it can’t solve this problem, which is inherent to centralization.

Let’s say you have 100 microservices. Does that automatically mean you can deploy them all independently without coordination? Well, that depends on the degree of hidden coupling, measured by the quality of their contracts, whether they share databases or libraries. In extreme cases, you can’t deploy them independently without severe coordination. We’ll revisit these hidden ties later in this chapter when we speak about distributed monoliths and talk about how to avoid common pitfalls in service development in [Chapter 6](ch06.xhtml), Principles of Proper Services.

So: automation is good, and organizations want to embrace DevOps, CI/CD, which makes sense. But remember that the real business bottlenecks are not always fixed by better pipelines or more modern tooling.

[Amplifiers](contents.xhtml#sc3_24a)

With these antipatterns and side effects affecting your monolith, bad problems can frequently lead to worse ones, with compounding effects over time. We call these amplifiers and touch on a few of them here.

[Broken windows/copy and paste](contents.xhtml#sc4_25a)

In 1982, social scientists James Q Wilson and George L Kelling introduced the Broken Windows theory, which states that visible signs of crime, anti-social behavior, and civil disorder encourage further criminal behavior. (Figure 1.12) In other words, when you have broken windows, you tend to get more broken windows over time.

Figure 1.12: Broken windows of the Stehli Silk Mill in Manheim Township, Lancaster County, Pennsylvania  
Source: [https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Stelhi_Silk_Mill_Lanco_broken_windows.JPG](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Stelhi_Silk_Mill_Lanco_broken_windows.JPG)

Although the Broken Windows theory is controversial in some circles11 It makes a lot of sense in the context of software engineering of legacy systems. Monolith code tends to decay over time, with technical debt growing due to the anti-patterns and side effects we’ve documented here. Having zero technical debt is impossible even in the best software architecture, but having it at a managed threshold is possible even in the context of a monolith. Decay can be mitigated through discipline, process, and maturity - cultural behaviors which differ from company to company and how your team approaches problems.

For instance, unseasoned developers might copy and paste code incorrectly to get things done faster. We would argue that there is nothing wrong with copying and pasting per se: engineers often copy and paste when we use tools like StackOverflow, Reddit, CoPilot, and ChatGPT. Copying and pasting run into issues because of a code supporting a specific use case might be correct, but for another use case, it might be incorrect. Engineers need to understand concepts deeply when they work, and generally, experienced engineers are more concerned about thinking and understanding the problem space and the trade-offs of a solution or architecture rather than how quickly they can accomplish a task. You need to watch for these behaviors and have time to fix things; otherwise, technical debt is created.

Where copying and pasting intersects with the Broken Windows theory is that once you have several poorly written or understood pieces of code, there is a good chance that bad code or bad behavior will be replicated because of engineers copying code.

Suddenly, a piece of code that you know is an anti-pattern becomes the standard of the project because the team permitted the bad code to exist in the first place. You would be surprised how many people think their code base is “good” when it is full of anti-patterns, technical debt, and super bad technical choices… and because unseasoned engineers copy and assume: it’s there, it must be good, right, it spreads faster. One broken window, and suddenly you have broken windows everywhere.

Of course, that’s why code and design reviews are important to reduce complexity.

Because monoliths are big, old, and can have significant issues due to high coupling, worse abstractions, and lack of isolation, they can be filled with anti-patterns and bad code examples, which engineers keep replicating. Plus, they can all be reached from the span of your single IDE, as all the bad code is in one place and equally accessible. While all software projects are filled with technical debt, monoliths are uniquely placed to let that technical debt spread more quickly.

As for why the broken windows behavior happens because of their natural incentives: finish new features quickly to move on to the next problem, and copying and pasting is the way to go faster. What is needed is other incentives to offset these natural incentives, so it is critical to take certain steps:

  * Measure how long it took for the engineer to read the code.

  * Measure how many times the dead code has been migrated.

  * Measure how many hours the team has spent chasing ghosts and dead code when troubleshooting.

  * Create OKRs around reducing code complexity.

  * Create OKRs to ensure test pass rates are 100%, at all times.

  * Budget 20+% slack time in sprints to mitigate tech debt.

  * Reward engineers for deleting code.




It is easy to just blame monoliths and their style of architecture, instead of focusing on the real causes of anti-patterns and bad code, resulting in teams having the wrong mindset going into a Microservices transition. Nothing can go wrong, right?

[Microservice envy](contents.xhtml#sc4_26a)

Another source of risk and possible complexity is that engineers love shiny things: new ideas, frameworks, and approaches. The issue with shiny things is that we also approach them with the mindset of learning their drawbacks, limitations, failure modes, and pain points. Many teams pursue microservices without understanding the real issues they are solving with their monolith and the inherent drawbacks of the Microservice Architecture Style. ThoughtWorks calls this anti-pattern “Microservices Envy” ([https://www.thoughtworks.com/en-us/radar/techniques/microservice-envy](https://www.thoughtworks.com/en-us/radar/techniques/microservice-envy)).

Microservices have a lot of benefits, but they are not a silver bullet, and certainly not for all companies. Beginning in the mid-2010s, many organizations worldwide have tried to embrace a microservices architecture, with some companies like Amazon, Netflix, SoundCloud, and Spotify succeeding greatly. However, many other companies tried and failed.

Every failure is a bit unique, but there are common patterns that we can see from our experience: from a lack of proper understanding of monoliths and microservices to a lack of proper training for engineers, a lack of high talent density, and many other reasons. The net effect of that is that many companies still have their monolith and now also have a slate of microservices. And in many cases, the result is a worse problem than they started: a distributed monolith. As described in this chapter, distribution is not a magic solution for too much centralization. Why is adding more moving parts a guaranteed solution if companies cannot get the software right while centralized? Very likely, what happens is you just have distributed problems now.

We will cover this in more detail in [Chapter 3](ch03.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Distributed Monoliths.

[Lack of Talent Density](contents.xhtml#sc4_27a)

Netflix is well known for the unique culture deck they published in their early transition to online services, describing their goals for the company, called Freedom and Responsibility ([https://www.slideshare.net/reed2001/culture-1798664](https://www.slideshare.net/reed2001/culture-1798664)).

In the deck, Netflix introduces the concept of Talent Density (Figure 1.13). Companies start off with a high Talent Density as they brave new frontiers and are successful. As companies get bigger, two things happen: the complexity of the business increases, and so does hiring to keep up with it, which naturally reduces the Talent Density of the organization. This new talent is unable to keep up with the complexity, and the result is chaos. As a result, to tame the chaos, the company introduces a process, which inherently reduces the freedom of movement for any individual. This lack of freedom reduces the impact of the high-performing talent, who get fed up and leave, further reducing Talent Density, exacerbating the problem, and leading to bureaucracy.

Figure 1.13: Netflix Freedom and Responsibility Deck - Talent Density  
Source: [https://www.slideshare.net/reed2001/culture-1798664](https://www.slideshare.net/reed2001/culture-1798664) (slide 56)

Monoliths perfectly represent the outcome of this process. As monoliths encourage organizations to be slow to adopt new technologies and inherently have slow development processes, the natural bureaucracy drives the high-performing talent away. This makes it harder to make the necessary changes to evolve and go forward, exacerbating the problem.

Talent always matters. There is, and there will always be, a fight for talent. Unfortunately, we cannot get all the talent we need or want. Even with all the talent in the world, mistakes happen, and we can still produce technical debt. But without talent, you can’t exit or move forward at the rate you need.

The inertia of a monolith is real, where your organization is aware of the problem, sociological and architectural, but cannot find the will or talent to express where the future needs to go. So, while you can understand the nature of your monolith, you still need the talent to overcome it; it is not enough to acknowledge the problem; you need the organization, the overall strategy, and the funding to hire the talent and restore sufficient talent density to move forward.

It would take another book to fully cover all the topics around building effective organizations and driving the right agreements across all company levels to tackle a monolith transition. For now, remember that talent density is important, and lack of talent can drive compounding effects in your monolith.

[Fear of change](contents.xhtml#sc4_28a)

Fear of change is the pinnacle of issues with the monolith. Engineers become afraid of refactoring the code to improve the structure. Engineers are often aware of the anti-patterns like high coupling, wrong abstractions, lack of isolation, lack of testing, lack of ownership, and technical debt on the monolith. Some engineers try to fix the problems or introduce new ideas and concepts via refactoring; however, they fail and get burned by breaking other engineers’ code or even critical bugs in prod. Management tries to control the monolith by freezing, asking engineers not to touch the code or do “as little as possible,” making what is already bad, even worse. (Figure 1.14)

Figure 1.14: Anti-Patterns and Amplifiers reinforce each other

At the same time, engineers are not afraid to add new features but are scared of refactoring and improving existing code; it’s an interesting social phenomenon. Adding new features does not mean just coding in new files; you will eventually need to deal with existing code structures which will need to change and be adapted. Engineers coding new features could copy and paste code full of anti-patterns, amplified by Broken Windows, which we explained earlier in this chapter. It is ironic - they would be much safer if they refactored the code over time. Their fear of change leads to more inherent risks coding new features, leading to more fear and less change.

[Are all monoliths bad?](contents.xhtml#sc2_29a)

Monoliths have a lot of what we’ll call “negative karma”: baked in anti-patterns, difficult side effects, all compounded by amplifiers. As monoliths decay over time, the path to a bad outcome seems inevitable. So: given everything you have just read, you might think there is a simple answer to the question: are all monoliths bad?

But the answer is: No! Monoliths are not always bad.

In fact, sometimes, the right solution is to design something centralized from the outset. To reduce the number of deployment units, regardless of the inevitable size of the codebase, because of the nature of the problem you are solving.

Software architecture and software design are essentially about trade-offs. When modeling something and deciding which capabilities should go on each service, we often face a big dilemma: together, or apart? Centralized, or distributed? See Figure 1.15.

Figure 1.15: The big design dilemma

Simply put, the answer as to whether the monolith will be bad depends on the software architecture, and the architecture depends on the requirements. If the right design is to have things together, the monolith will be good; if not, it is on the path to being bad. You will pay the penalty when you split things that should not be split, or combine things that should be combined.

[Monoliths are a form of software architecture](contents.xhtml#sc3_30a)

This book is about software architecture. We have spent this entire book talking inherently about software architecture, so let’s take a moment to define it.

Software architecture is all about structures: all the classes, modules, libraries, and elements of your system and how they are connected and interrelated. The discipline of software architecture is all about decisions: the difficult tradeoffs required when designing those structures to solve multiple problems at once. Software Architecture is required throughout a project to produce the best software possible, given the requirements and constraints.

You likely associate software architecture with non-functional requirements: scalability, stability, resiliency, performance, security, and the like. This is not wrong; but software architecture is more. Yes, software architecture is a key and critical element responsible for scale: scaling users, scaling hardware, scaling requests per second but software architecture is also about scaling your people: development teams and engineering practices all inherently respond to design structures, and the decisions you make as a software architect will be responsible for how an organization can scale engineers and teams.

Software architecture does not have many absolutely correct or absolutely incorrect decisions. Instead, it is all about what makes the most sense given your use cases and trade-offs. Sometimes teams get the architecture right from the get-go, but, over time, because of the inevitability of change and many other factors it no longer works for its intended purpose. For example, you started off by designing a boat, but then it turned out you needed a spacecraft.

As such, software architecture should be something living and organic, not discrete; it is not something you do once, and it’s over forever, but rather a practice that runs across the life of any software system.

[Monolith benefits](contents.xhtml#sc2_31a)

Monolithic software architecture is a very old yet common style of software architecture. For example, the first monolith was the mainframe! Like any architectural style, there are trade-offs: pros and cons, both good and bad design elements.

In short: monoliths have benefits. Let’s review them.

[Refactoring and change impact](contents.xhtml#sc3_32a)

Perhaps the biggest advantage of the monolith architecture is how easy it is to refactor code, when all your code is in one place. Most modern IDEs and code editors like Eclipse, IntelliJ IDEA, Sublime Text and VSCode have amazing capabilities for refactoring, and monoliths let you apply them all, easily.

Imagine you want to know if a class is used, how widely, and whether it is directly coupled to your code or found deep in the dependency graph. When you have all the code in one place, you can simply delete the class and let the compiler show you the impact.

By contrast, with microservices, your code is distributed in different codebases. Coupling is expressed in ways that it is harder for an IDE to inherently understand. The advanced refactoring capabilities of IDEs are lost, and figuring out the impact of changes is more challenging.

[Simplify some migrations](contents.xhtml#sc3_33a)

To some degree, monoliths allow easier migrations by having all the code base in one place; it’s fewer independent repositories to change, and one single PR can contain all changes. When you have a microservices architecture with multiple code repositories, you need to fire several PRs and do much more coordination.

Of course migrations suffer with classical monoliths, due to factors like Binary Coupling, which generally slow the adoption of new technologies. However, we do have to recognize that it’s easier to drop in one PR to update one library, rather than commit, review, and merge hundreds of PRs with microservices.

[A good starting point](contents.xhtml#sc3_34a)

Having everything in one place, one code base, and few deployment units, means less organizational overhead. Monoliths make it easier to reason about the code base. To some degree, with fewer moving pieces it requires less coordination, and ownership can be a bit clearer. When you begin your journey, whether enterprise or startup, it can be faster and often better to start with less structure and let the patterns emerge as you go.

[Simplified infrastructure](contents.xhtml#sc3_35a)

The more machines you need to run your software, the more deployment units, the more coordination you need. Again: going from centralization to distribution does not necessarily fix all your problems; distributed systems can have much higher overhead. When the nature of the software permits, a monolithic solution will be cheaper to operate than distributed software. However, at scale, having a mix of distributed and centralized solutions is often not possible.

[Types of monoliths](contents.xhtml#sc2_36a)

Let us have a deeper look into types of monoliths. We will classify monoliths in three types: classical monoliths, distributed monoliths and modular monoliths.

[Classical, distributed, and modular monoliths](contents.xhtml#sc3_37a)

Figure 1.16: Types of monolith architecture,

Now that we understand the traits of monoliths, their drawbacks and even potential benefits, let’s talk about the 3 kinds of monoliths we have seen in the wild, two negative, and one positive. (Figure 1.16)

  * Classical/Legacy monolith: Big codebase, few deployment units, fully centralized and usually quite old. Think of a Windows Desktop Application, old-style MVC application, or an older mainframe-originated application like a banking core from the 1980s or 19990s. Whenever we refer to a monolith, by default, we are referring to this style.

  * Distributed monolith: A Microservices architecture or SOA, but where design failures have led to high coupling between components, a lack of isolation, and in practice the whole system must be deployed and operated as one. Distributed Monolith combines all issues from classical monoliths and Microservices; we would argue the worst of all. We have touched on this bad outcome through this chapter, and will focus on it explicitly in [Chapter 4](ch04.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Internal Shared Libraries.

  * Modular monolith: A monolith that was explicitly designed to split and isolate functionality across discrete boundaries, and where system evolution and change can be safely modeled and executed due to the application of correct abstractions and modularization.




For many companies, you can consider there to be a trajectory across all 3 monoliths:

  * The company starts start with a monolithic architecture, finds success, but growth without technical evolution traps them into a cycle, inevitably leading them to Classical Monolith. 

  * Undergoing microservice envy, the company overcomes its fear of change and decides to embrace distribution and rapidly introduce microservices. But, due to the lack of talent density or other factors, the team applies wrong abstractions, and ends up producing a set of microservices that suffer from an anti-pattern, such as sharing a database, or widespread internal library coupling. The resulting system is just as difficult to evolve due to hidden coupling.

  * Armed with the lessons learned from the failed migration, the company embraces the right balance between centralization and distribution with a mixture of microservices and classically centralized components that express correct abstractions through a proper modular design.




In short, not all monoliths are bad. While it is easy to blame monolithic architectures, not all monoliths are created equally. The real issue has multiple causes - whether it is a software architecture that has not evolved with the business, or technical debt accumulated from a lack of proper engineering principles and practices.

While it is easy to understand why teams are naturally adverse to the idea of monoliths, it is essential to understand the role of centralization in software architectures, how monoliths go wrong and what they get right.

Else, your organization’s attempts to exit the monolith will make the problem worse.

Let’s look in depth at good monolithic architectures: Modular monoliths.

[Modular monoliths: The good kind of monolith](contents.xhtml#sc3_38a)

Modular programming is not a new concept in software engineering: it is the notion of splitting up the functionality of software into separate units called modules, where each module contains everything needed to execute a discrete aspect of the needed functionality. Modular Programming was first defined by Larry Constantine in 1968, and modules released with the ALGOL programming language via ALGOL 68-R in 1970.12

A Modular Monolith is a simple concept: apply modular programming to deal with the root of many problems in Classical Monoliths, the lack of isolation. You can take the best characteristics of monolith development - few deployment units, simpler organizing principles and less overhead - and through the use of modularization with clear contracts and discrete boundaries, you can reduce the risk of anti-patterns affecting overall productivity.

A modular monolith alleviates the pains which microservices inflict mostly around complexity, such as:

  * Debugging and observability: It is easier to see components connect through the software architecture, and trace individual requests through the application flow.

  * Reuse and refactoring: It is easy to see the impact of a single line refactor on your codebase via the benefits of modern IDEs.

  * Pushing the complexity someplace else: Because Microservices externalize everything, when new requirements emerge, the heavy emphasis on distribution can sometimes force awkward outcomes such as splitting the complexity of a process incorrectly across multiple services, or introducing new services just to solve simple orchestration concerns.

  * Dependency management: While still protected through isolation, the staggering array of individual libraries across 100s of services can make overall dependency management, patches, security fixes, and coordinated upgrades complex.




Figure 1.17: Solutions spectrum

Modular monoliths aren’t solely from older companies trying to overcome the limitations of aged software; modern companies are embracing modular monoliths by design. Many companies have failed to embrace microservices, suffering from the inherent flaws in badly designed microservice architectures, and are less scared about applying a monolithic design in the right situations. Here are some examples of companies embracing Modular Monoliths:

  * Istio Service Mesh, which we will review as an example of a good modular monolith design shortly in this chapter.

  * Shopify started with a Ruby on Rails monolith, and has embraced Modular Monoliths as the next step in its architecture journey ([https://shopify.engineering/deconstructing-monolith-designing-software-maximizes-developer-productivity](https://shopify.engineering/deconstructing-monolith-designing-software-maximizes-developer-productivity)).

  * Segment transitioned to microservices, but with overhead costs and bottlenecks rising, chose to revert to a Monolithic architecture with the introduction of Centrifuge ([https://www.infoq.com/news/2020/04/microservices-back-again/](https://www.infoq.com/news/2020/04/microservices-back-again/); [https://segment.com/blog/introducing-centrifuge/](https://segment.com/blog/introducing-centrifuge/)).

  * The Inertia Framework describes itself as “building JavaScript Apps the Monolith Way” ([https://inertiajs.com/](https://inertiajs.com/)).

  * The Spring Framework formally supports Modular Monoliths through its experimental Spring Modulith support ([https://spring.io/blog/2022/10/21/introducing-spring-modulith](https://spring.io/blog/2022/10/21/introducing-spring-modulith)).




[Modular monoliths: Istio](contents.xhtml#sc3_39a)

Istio is a Service Mesh framework, designed to reduce the overhead encountered by distributed microservices architectures by handling common tasks like load balancing, routing, observability, and security.

Figure 1.18: Istio Original Architecture with Microservices version 1.1  
Source: [https://istio.io/v1.1/docs/concepts/what-is-istio/](https://istio.io/v1.1/docs/concepts/what-is-istio/)

Istio began as a microservices architecture with 4 components/services: Mixer, Pilot, Galley, and Citadel (see Figure 1.18). Overtime, it evolved to add the Policy and Injector microservices, leading to a total of 6. Suffering issues, Istio evolved from a microservices architecture to a single binary called istiod, constructed as a modular monolith.

Figure 1.19: IstioD Architecture - Monolith Architecture  
Source: [https://istio.io/latest/docs/ops/deployment/architecture/](https://istio.io/latest/docs/ops/deployment/architecture/)

Christian Posta is an active committer to the Istio project, and summarizes the transition in his blog post: “Istio as an Example of When Not to Do Microservices”13. Christian cites an excellent Design Document14 from Louis Ryan, which outlines the case to transition Istio to a monolith, all centering around complexity:

  * A simpler installation and upgrade process.

  * Reduced configuration complexity.

  * Easier to debug Istio setup and operation.

  * Increased efficiency and reduced overhead, such as via sharing caches.

  * Eliminating needless coupling by reducing the need for Istio agents to access privileged configuration.




For Christian and other members of the Istio community, the overall complexity of the microservices approach just made Istio unnecessarily painful to use and operate. Some other key points to highlight from Christian’s excellent post:

  * Microservices are not the only approach. Simply put, there is no one right answer in software engineering, and Microservices are not the “utopian application architecture”.

  * Staying away from microservices is the best place to start. As we have covered in our Monolith Benefits, when early stage companies are starting out, a centralized architecture can be the right way to keep your focus on product market fit, and not in fixing technology.

  * Honesty about when it is not working. Even if the project started with the right intentions, recognize that it is ok to make mistakes and change architectures as part of a purposeful evolution.




Having a monolith does not automatically mean you have a spaghetti codebase with little to no isolation or modularization. And in some cases, having few deployment units is a distinct advantage and can be an excellent technical solution.

[Modular monoliths: Mobile SuperApps](contents.xhtml#sc3_40a)

Yes, mobile applications can be excellently constructed modular monoliths. Whether written in Kotlin for Android, Swift for IOS, or hybrid frameworks like React Native or Flutter, you certainly can meet the standard of a big codebase, and unless you want your consumers to have to download multiple mobile applications, you will have few deployment units.

For mobile development the pattern is currently described as a SuperApp15. SuperApp designs bundle all an organization’s mobile application functionality into one application. SuperApps make sense for a variety of reasons:

  * Younger generations expect mobile-first, deeply integrated and engaging experiences.

  * Consumers get consolidated services across the enterprise, all in one place.

  * Easier to create and apply a consistent user experience.

  * Opportunity to cross-sell products in a richer application.




Gartner expects that by 2027, more than 50% of the global population will be active users of multiple SuperApps daily. Uber used to have 2 apps - Uber for ridesharing and UberEats for food delivery. Now both solutions are accessible from a single application architecture.

Inevitably, these architectures force a monolith architecture onto these companies. At a modern pace of development found in big tech, a modular monolith is the only practical solution to enable SuperApp teams to safely work and release in parallel.

Figure 1.20: Uber SuperApp  
Source: [https://www.forbes.com/sites/bizcarson/2019/09/26/uber-overhauls-app-for-eats-rides/?sh=2133640045c8](https://www.forbes.com/sites/bizcarson/2019/09/26/uber-overhauls-app-for-eats-rides/?sh=2133640045c8)

[Can bad monoliths be avoided?](contents.xhtml#sc2_41a)

As we’ve said earlier in this chapter, software architecture does not have many absolutely correct or absolutely incorrect decisions. Consider again the trajectory between Centralization and Distribution, in Figure 1.2; there is no one, right choice for a project. In fact, the right choices tend to change over time given the inherent complexity, business demands, social environment and hardware constraints; what was once right, becomes wrong later.

Monoliths are a great example of where we can start with a very positive and effective architecture: they reduce complexity and deliver more with less. One common monolith origin story is at startup or early stage companies; they are well advised to keep their technical architecture simple/straightforward while they pursue product market fit, and focus on refactoring for scale and resiliency after they find success. It is easy to waste time on premature optimization of technology that doesn’t meaningfully deliver for the business.

On the opposite end of the spectrum, established companies can arrive at the same result by pursuing Big Designs Up Front (BDUF). Piling up requirement after requirement, ending up with too much complexity housed in a single system, or even worse, a distributed monolith.

Regardless of whether the monolith started off with good intentions or discovered unwanted complexity, monoliths eventually become the pain point that prevents organizations from effectively scaling. This inflection point is where monoliths stop having positive characteristics, and turn into a nightmare (Figure 1.21). It could take 10 years, or 1 year. It is the moment when the anti-patterns have taken root, the side effects begin to overwhelm any benefits, and it is all compounded by the amplifiers. It is when the growing awareness of the debilitating state of the monolith becomes clear to both the tech and business teams.

Figure 1.21: Value turns into Debt, and we go downhill.

The key to avoiding this inflection point is to recognize the patterns and acting. Unfortunately, this is more complex than it appears. For instance, when we talk about the high coupling anti-pattern, the tricky piece is that it is not always easy to spot until after the fact - it takes time, commitment to understanding business requirements, performing timely design reviews - in short, it takes a disciplined development practice across both the business and engineering. Unnecessary complexity needs to be managed and fought at all times.

In short, if you want to avoid your own monolith inflection point, you need good practices and to avoid bad ones. While it is easier said than done, help your business stakeholders avoid these behaviors that promote technical debt and poor outcomes:

  * Unrealistic deadlines: It’s easy to fall into a design or software pitfall if you’re running faster than your ability to both build new features and avoid technical debt. Remember that the best solutions result from collaboration across product, design, engineering and the business - and teams that are frantically trying to hit a date will skip that step first.

  * Insisting on only new features: Every business has to accept that time must be spent on maintenance to avoid additional decay. Demanding a relentless series of new features is a surefire way to create an overburdened team without proper time to unwind tech debt and introduce good practices.

  * Lack of structure: Racing from feature to feature without prototyping, user testing, or allowing ideas or designs to reach maturity is a perfect way to stretch a team and produce technical debt, especially when compounded by unrealistic deadlines.

  * Directing the solution instead of defining the problem: “All we have to do is change this one screen and we’re all done! This must be easy!” Trust your engineers and architects to know their systems, benefits and consequences: tell them what, don’t tell them how. Collaborate, don’t dictate; and take their advice, don’t overrule it.

  * Cutting corners on cost: When it comes to engineering, you often get what you pay for. Skilled engineers cost money, and cheap contractors don’t but you still pay more in the end as the Lack of Talent Density leads to even worse outcomes. The right way to operate is with talented, properly trained engineers which are given proper direction, regardless of whether they are in your company or outside. Companies skip this at their own peril.




The net result is an engineering team pressured into following bad practices. Great engineering teams apply discipline and rigor to ensure good practices; the poor ones let bad practices flourish and compound. Common bad practices include:

  * Lack of proper code review: LGTM on every pull request, no comments, no refactoring: there is no faster recipe to hit a Broken Windows scenario in record time.

  * Shallow retrospectives: 15 minutes doesn’t cut it. You need to celebrate past successes and call out common failures, otherwise, you repeat the mistakes of the past and don’t build on your strengths. Teams need to examine and revisit their beliefs, and you can’t get there without going deep, and that takes time.

  * No slack time: All teams need unstructured time to attend to maintenance and clean up their solutions. Otherwise, small bits of technical debt snowball into massive snarls quickly.

  * Failing to mentor and grow the team: Software engineering is an endlessly evolving discipline. Getting good at it requires conscious work by team members on their individual development path. Software engineering is a sport, not a war; you win by strategy and individual execution, not throwing bodies at the problem. Mentorship amplifies the learning ability of your team members, improves their practice, and helps them grow to reach their individual potential - for your benefit, and theirs.

  * Missing technical leadership: Someone has to stand up and say “no” to poor decisions, regardless of the pressure, and support all the good practices previously mentioned in this section.




How we produce software matters. Monoliths aren’t created in a vacuum: you hit that inflection point through poor engineering practices, often influenced by a business chasing impractical results. The inflection point is not inevitable - address these practices and the terrible monolith in your future can still be avoided.

[Things to remember](contents.xhtml#sc2_42a)

Congratulations on reaching the end of [Chapter 1](ch01.xhtml), What’s Wrong with Monoliths! We have taught you the key traits and characteristics of monoliths, why monoliths are often associated with trouble and how teams end up creating them. We also learned that not all monoliths are equal, not all are necessarily bad, and in some scenarios monoliths might be the right architecture to solve a problem.

From here, we will go deep into the most important anti-patterns which affect monoliths and software architectures overall: Lack of Isolation, Distributed Monoliths, and Shared Libraries. Only by understanding the anti-pattern can we properly design the solution to the problem. Let’s keep going!

To summarize your learnings:

  * Monoliths are a style of software architecture where a large amount of code is deployed using a small number of deployment units. Monoliths have other code smells, like tending towards centralization, or being old.

  * Monoliths are an architectural style with significant trade-offs.

  * Monoliths are not bad because they are big.
