  * Monoliths are bad because their architectural style encourages anti-patterns and creates side effects that create technical debt. The result is negative outcomes for both the business and technology, such as high maintenance costs and frequent downtime.

  * Monoliths decay over time.

  * Monoliths inherently promote antipatterns:

    * High Coupling, where software components are unnecessarily coupled to one another.

    * Wrong Abstractions, where you use the wrong pattern to solve a problem.

    * Lack of Isolation, which a lack of boundaries limits software independence and evolution

  * Because of these anti-patterns, Monoliths experience side effects:

    * Difficult Deployments, making software releases painful and risk downtime. Classical Monoliths lack the ability for teams to perform independent deployments.

    * Insufficient Testing, where the inherent complexity of the monolith makes it very difficult to evaluate all possible outcomes

    * Lack of Ownership, where no boundaries leads to little direct team ownership of components

    * Slow Adoption of New Technologies, as a result of failing to make changes due to phenomena such as Binary Coupling

    * Slow Development Cycle, the final result of all of the above.

  * Monolith pain points are amplified over time by these changes:

    * Broken Windows, where technical debt spreads throughout the codebase.

    * Microservice Envy, where teams that hate the Monolith rush to a poorly designed microservice solution, making the problem worse

    * Lack of Talent Density, as a result of your monolith’s bureaucracy driving talent away and increasing turnover. Engineers often are not motivated by working with monoliths.

    * Fear of Change, which prevents you from making the right changes to improve the monolith’s design and address its technical debt.

  * Not all monoliths are bad. Sometimes, they represent the right architectural approach where centralization is the correct answer.

  * Properly implemented, Monoliths have these benefits:

    * Refactoring the Monolith is easier, as with the code in one place it is easier to see the impact of certain changes.

    * Simplify some migrations, as you can make one change and update everything

    * With less organizational overhead and a simplified infrastructure, you can move quickly with monoliths, making them a good place for any early stage startup.

  * There are three kinds of monoliths that share these elements, two bad, and one good.

    * Classical/Legacy Monolith, like a Windows desktop application or older Java MVC app.

    * Distributed Monolith, where a group of microservices appear to be independent, but are actually subject to high coupling and have to be treated as one unit.

    * Modular Monolith, where you have properly designed the boundaries between components, and choose to deploy them as a few deployment units.

  * Istio and Mobile SuperApps are two great examples of Modular Monoliths. Modular monoliths are under active development by many companies, such as Shopify and Segment.

  * Cultural or business pressure can create bad monoliths by demanding unrealistic deadlines, pushing just for features, not collaborating, not letting engineers drive technical solutions, and an overall lack of discipline and structure.

  * Bad Monoliths can be avoided, but chiefly by following good engineering practices, such as proper code review, good retrospectives, encouraging slack time to retire technical debt, encouraging mentorship and strong technical leadership.




* * *

5 A Philosophy of Software Design, 2nd Edition by John K. Ousterhout defines complexity as degree of Obscurity [https://www.amazon.com/Philosophy-Software-Design-2nd-ebook/dp/B09B8LFKQL](https://www.amazon.com/Philosophy-Software-Design-2nd-ebook/dp/B09B8LFKQL)

# [CHAPTER 2Anti-Patterns: Lack of Isolation](contents.xhtml#ch02a)

Your personal boundaries protect the inner core of your identity and your right to choices.

— Gerard Manley Hopkins

Software engineering is hard. Building systems that last with quality is even more challenging; for many, it is just impossible. How often do you wish to press the Reset button and start over? Do you think just starting over would fundamentally fix all your problems? Are you 100% certain you know what the problems are? How can you prevent them from happening again?

In this chapter, we will unlock one of the most important secrets for successful software architectures, perhaps one of the master keys of the universe: Isolation. Isolation is liberating, its freedom is magical; it is like having your cake and eating it too. Isolation is the way to go when designing software architectures. Isolation will allow you to make mistakes and fix your mistakes without re-writing the whole universe.

[Structure](contents.xhtml#sc2_43a)

In this chapter, we will cover the following topics:

  * What is isolation?

    * Isolation in software

    * Isolation temptations

    * Exposure

      * Existing components, BFF

      * Existing components, change existing endpoint

      * Existing service, add new endpoint

      * New service, share the database

      * Sidebar: what is a Backend for Frontend (BFF)?

      * New service, share the data

      * What is event sourcing?

      * Event sourcing in software

      * Sidebar: CQRS and its relationship with ES

      * New service, share via API

    * Centralization

      * Benefits of centralizing engineering teams

      * Issues with over-centralization

      * Delivery bottleneck and innovation bottleneck

      * Centralization versus standardization for observability and deployments

      * Bad shared libraries

  * Breaking Isolation

    * Sharing databases

      * When is it ok to share databases?

      * Big Data sharing application databases

      * Hidden contracts and preserving isolation

      * Legacy system integrations

    * Shared libraries

      * Binary coupling

    * Lack of stable contracts

    * Pushing complexity upwards

      * Granularity and distribution: side effects

  * Let us isolate everything

    * Databases

    * Shared libraries

    * Stable contracts

    * Sidebar: deep modules and A Philosophy of Software Design

    * Operating Systems and infrastructure

    * Advanced isolation

      * Failure isolation and bulkheading

      * Fallbacks

      * UX isolation: degradation and exploration

  * How to prevent isolation issues

    * Design reviews and tooling

  * Things to remember




[What is isolation?](contents.xhtml#sc2_44a)

Isolation is part of nature; you can see it in the cells of live organisms or watch it change due to substances shifting phases between gas, liquid, and solid. Isolation is a fundamental construct of how the universe works. It is beautiful. Before we try to define it in software terms, let us start with a simple metaphor. Imagine you have two different ice trays. 

Figure 2.1: Ice tray metaphor for Isolation

Monolith ice tray (Left, Figure 2.1): One cannot easily cut out an ice cube; it is all-or-nothing. Either we have a whole bar of ice, or we have water sitting in the tray. Yes, you could try to hack at the ice and get out a chunk, but good luck getting the ice back into the tray. Let us face it, the tray is not helping us; we need to do a lot of work on our side, and nothing is preventing us from messing it up.

Isolated ice tray (Right, Figure 2.1): The tray separates the ice into smaller cubes. Each cube can be in a different state; for instance, some cubes might be filled with water, another cube could be made with orange juice, another cube could have chocolate milk, and some can be completely empty. One benefit is that you can get a small ice cube out of the tray without touching the other cubes. The tray isolates each cube from one another, making things more convenient, safer, and faster. While the ice cubes are not entirely isolated from one another - they share a tray, and sit in the same freezer, thus affected by the same temperature - separation gives us some freedom to work with individual cubes, protects cubes from mixing with one another, and global effects are reduced overall.

[Isolation in software](contents.xhtml#sc3_45a)

Isolation is a way to organize software and hardware technologies, where each component is separated from the other. A component could be anything like an OS Process16, Browser Tab17, Database Transactions18, Port19, Resource20, Container21. Isolation can be physical, local, or virtual. Many other forms of isolation will be covered in this chapter.

Isolation is a core principle in software architecture. It is a key element for success and a highly desirable property. It is so important to the point that we devote an entire chapter so you know what happens when isolation is missing or is not properly applied.

We would not deceive you: achieving the right balance of isolation is not easy. It requires a lot of discipline and careful attention at all times. By the time you leave this chapter, you will know what isolation is, the temptations on the road to achieving balanced isolation in your architecture, what goes wrong when you break isolation - and how to fix it.

When software is appropriately isolated, it is GOLD. You have a lot of benefits. When something is not properly isolated, you are in trouble. Isolation is a way to reduce the blast radius and constrain the potential impact of change. Isolation helps you avoid global impact, global state, and global side effects. After all, do you want to change 1 line of code and impact all classes in the codebase? Isolation is a way to make the code more independent, precise, reliable, scalable, and safe to work with. Isolation is a highly desirable property, and isolation is always a good idea. 

Figure 2.2: Isolation spectrum

Isolation is a spectrum (Figure 2.2); it is not all or nothing. However, this property is so crucial that you want to pursue isolation at all times. Applying a proper level of isolation results in many benefits, such as safety, contained blast radius, information hiding, encapsulation, clear boundaries, security, and much more.

[Isolation temptations](contents.xhtml#sc2_46a)

The upcoming concepts are like a double-edged sword; it is a risk. The outcome might be good, or misapplied, the outcome might be bad. Anything can happen. So you will be tempted to move quickly but you need to be paying attention at all times. This is like a warning signal: Attention, be careful: danger ahead. (Figure 2.3) There are many temptations on your path to achieving proper isolation. Temptations do not necessarily break isolation, but they can lead to breaking isolation if you are not careful enough. Some technical decisions are better or worse than others. 

Figure 2.3: Isolation temptations - Be careful  
Source: [https://dmv-permit-test.com/road-signs/slippery-road-sign](https://dmv-permit-test.com/road-signs/slippery-road-sign)

[Exposure](contents.xhtml#sc3_47a)

For software to be valuable, we need to be able to interact with it. There are several ways we can interact with software. Often that interaction can be human to machine or machine to machine. Human-to-machine interactions are facilitated through user interfaces, often customer facing: desktop based, mobile based, web based, text or conversational based, a terminal/CLI or a chatbot, audio-based like Alexa, AR, VR or extended reality (XR). Machines talk to machine often using a form of API that can be text-based, such as with JSON or XML, or binary-based using binary serialization formats like Google Protocol Buffers, Google FlatBuffers, WebAssembly, Thrift, or many other binary formats. When a machine is talking to a machine, the API needs to be exposed in order for us to be able to call it. (Figure 2.4)

Figure 2.4: Public exposure

To help illustrate points throughout this book, we will start using a sample, customer-facing web application that allows employees at the Acme Widget company sell their widgets. The app is written in Java and JavaScript and has 4 key functions:

  * Profiles: Tracks basic customer info, like name, address or phone number; also known as a user profile.

  * Sales: Manages the sales process, including shopping carts, widgets, amounts and total cost.

  * Payments: Safely collects cash from customers and directs it to the company.

  * Reports: Delivers PDF usage and sales reports on individual customers, or across an entire day.




Of course, in the real world, Acme Widget’s application would have many, many more functions, handling authentication, widget definitions, widget inventories, and other requirements. But for now, these will do.

For our example, we have a typical web application stack. To see it in action, take the simple requirement to display a customer’s first name when they log in.

  * A React-based web UI triggers a call to the BFF - a backend for frontend layer, see our side note - to request the user profile for the logged in user.

  * The JavaScript-based BFF layer authenticates the user’s session, validates the input for safety, and calls the profile service via REST, launching an HTTP GET to /user/25.

  * The Java-based profile service checks that the request is valid and the user does in fact have the right to see the data, and uses its implementation of a database driver to create a request to the data store, a MySQL database.

  * The MySQL database evaluates the SQL and returns the user’s name.

  * The profile service formats a return response, in properly formatted JSON.

  * The BFF removes any non-essential information from that JSON, and returns it to the React application.

  * The React application renders the user’s name.




Let us say that the profile service is written in Java, and has an internal capability to provide a PDF report on the user’s yearly purchases, and we have a new requirement to return it to our customer. No entity outside the profile service can invoke the capability, even with the code present, because it is not exposed. It was only possible for the BFF to fetch the user profile from the Profile service because the service exposed an API; a REST API returning JSON. So, a new React UI or BFF component cannot access this capability from the backend.

Something in the architecture needs to change to satisfy the requirement. There are options; however, not all options will lead to good outcomes in the sense of architectural design and technical debt.

Naturally, this scenario leads us to the beginning of the isolation discussion. Each option has trade-offs that need to be made, trade-offs that impact the isolation of the individual components in our architecture.

  * Existing components:

    * BFF: Re-implement the functionality at the BFF layer, and call the same database called by the profile service.

    * Change existing endpoint: Deliver both functionalities from the existing endpoint (/user/25), returning more data in its JSON payload.

    * Add new endpoint: Expose another endpoint on the profile service: for example. /user/report/25

  * New service:

    * Share the database: Move this functionality to a different service (the report service) and expose it there, but use the same database aka, sharing the database.

    * Share the data: Share the data, but do not share the database. Make a new service, but get data indirectly from the Profile Service via Event Sourcing.

    * Share via API: Make a new service, but get data from the Profile Service by calling its API.




[Existing components, BFF](contents.xhtml#sc4_48a)

Reimplementing the reports service functionality in the BFF has much the same issues as in any of the new service options. In some senses it is slightly better; there is less chance you will have a badly shared library between implementations because the code is in another language, Java vs JavaScript. But we have other issues like unnecessary code duplication, bugs between different implementations, and you still need to coordinate database or other changes across multiple teams. Really, you have only pushed the complexity around the stack, not removed it.

[Existing components, change existing endpoint](contents.xhtml#sc4_49a)

Adding more data to the existing profile service endpoint seems attractive. Since we have two different MIME types, JSON data for the user profile vs a binary PDF report, we now need to mix plain text and base64 encoded data in a JSON object. Every time you call the endpoint for the user’s name - a small piece of data - you get a PDF with all of their sales for the year. What if, in the future, you want to request specific time periods to cover in the PDF report? The outcome here is obscure code and an unnatural API - just more technical debt.

So why even consider this option? For this use case it may not be the best option, but many times, adding another operation to existing code is easier and could be correct. You need to consider all dimensions like:

  * Is there the right place for this feature/capability?

  * How much does this change compromise the conceptual integrity of the service?

  * Do other services and UIs need to call this?

  * Would all the functionality share the same access patterns?




Adding a new endpoint is not a free lunch. You could be increasing complexity, creating more coupling, or breaking isolation. Any move must be considered carefully and a tradeoff analysis should be performed.

[Existing service, add new endpoint](contents.xhtml#sc4_50a)

Adding a new endpoint to the profile service is the simplest solution, and does not create database isolation issues or hidden contracts. The separate endpoint will have no problem returning a binary PDF and not bloat the initial profile service call. However, this option can also lead to problems; if the feature is in the wrong service, we can have coupling, unnecessary complexity, and technical debt.

Before you dismiss this option, it may be a better starting point than you might think. You can always decouple the service and move the code to another place.

Whenever you need to expose operations, features, or business capabilities, run this trade-off analysis. Think about what makes more sense in the short and long term. While this may seem like a lot of introspective, decisions are often better framed when you have the vision of the whole, not only what your team needs to do.

The main risk here is that we compromise the contract and the overall cohesion and purpose of the service; which might be wrong, but given all evils, this might be the least of all.

[New service, share the database](contents.xhtml#sc4_51a)

We can create a dedicated service as we have a separate UI in React and a separate BFF in NodeJS. But that service needs to access the profile data; in this option, we share the same database.

Sharing the database creates obvious isolation issues between the Profile service and Reports service. (Figure 2.5) What happens if the other team changes the user profile functionality and renames the table from user to profile? Well, your user report is now broken. The same can happen from the report side. So, both services can break each other if they introduce database changes. What happens if the Profile service team wants to use Cassandra, and the report team does not want to move to Cassandra? We have coupled these two services; they are no longer fully independent. 

Figure 2.5: New service, share the database (Bad idea)

You might be wondering - But only the database layer is coupled! The rest of the code is isolated and has no issues! Well, yes and no; it depends on our implementation. Imagine you have POJOs to represent the user in a profile object. Are you duplicating that code in both services, or are you creating a common library with these POJOs, and then both services use this library? Either way is a bad idea since you are sharing the database. Now both services are coupled, and you have coupling not only on the database layer but also the core domain of each service. Sharing a database invites these sorts of temptations that lead to breaking isolation. We will cover this anti-pattern in more detail in [Chapter 4](ch04.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Internal Shared Libraries.

[Sidebar: What is a Backend for Frontend?](contents.xhtml#sc4_52a)

SoundCloud pioneered the term Backend for Frontend (BFF)22 back in 2013. SoundCloud defines BFF as “an architectural pattern that involves creating multiple, dedicated API gateways for each device or interface type, with the goal of optimizing each API for its particular use case.”

BFFs live between the frontend and backend (Figure 2.6), being the backend for the frontend, as the name says. BFFs are often written in JavaScript or TypeScript running on NodeJS, but they can be written in any language. BFFs shine where you have some code that is UI-specific and not generic enough to live on the service. The code in a BFF typically must be reused, has performance requirements, or handles security or another sensitive function, so it should not be implemented by the front end. A BFF is often an aggregator of API calls for a single UI or group of UIs. 

Figure 2.6: BFF by SoundCloud  
Source: [https://developers.soundcloud.com/blog/service-architecture-1](https://developers.soundcloud.com/blog/service-architecture-1)

[New service, share the data](contents.xhtml#sc4_53a)

Create a new service, but decouple the profile service from the reports service by creating a messaging queue; the profile service writes data changes to user profiles to this queue as well as its database. The report service can read the queue and write the data it needs to its local database, reading it back as requests come in. This is an implementation of the event sourcing pattern.

[What is event sourcing?](contents.xhtml#sc4_54a)

Event sourcing (ES)23 is a way to capture changes in forms of events instead of final values. Perhaps the most classic and old example would be for a financial ledger where you don not store the final values but each transaction movements.

[Event sourcing in software](contents.xhtml#sc4_55a)

Event sourcing is not about technology, but it is really about data modeling. You can do event sourcing in a message queue, a database, or even appending to a file: what matters is recording an immutable event log, not just adjusting the final values themselves. Of course, some technologies make it easier than others, such as purpose-built software like Kafka. 

Figure 2.7: ES example with financial ledger

Here in Figure 2.7 for simplicity, we illustrate an example with a single ledger entry. In reality, accounting uses a double ledger. Imagine, one table for Diego and another for Sam for each transfer; you can cross check deductions. This is the basis of modern accounting, as created by Luca Pacioli in 149424.

Event sourcing in software means instead of just storing data in the database we also publish events to a message queue, like Kafka or RabbitMQ. (Figure 2.8) The modeling of the events is different from the modeling of the tables on the relational database. 

Figure 2.8: Event sourcing approach - Event sourcing with Kafka

Compared with previous options, you decouple the profile and reporting service as they do not share the same database. Isolation is improved, once there are database changes, you have some layer of indirection delivered by the message queue. This layer of indirection will still break if the data published to the message queue changes, but each database can evolve independently. 

The reports service can read/write directly to the messaging queue, or alternatively we can create an independent reader/writer process in a technology like Spark Streaming. The messaging queue itself can be in any number of technologies: Kafka, RabbitMQ, or JMS/ActiveMQ.

The data published to the queue can be expressed in an intermediary format like JSON, making the solution less coupled to the specific database technology. Therefore better; not perfect, but better. Of course, the issue is that the system is more complex; there are more points of failure and more software and infrastructure to maintain. However, this option is valid.

Ask yourself: how many contracts does the profile service have now? Did you say 1? In fact, it has 2; the public endpoint in REST+JSON (/user/{ID}), and the data it publishes to the messaging queue.

The data itself is the contract; even though it does not come with a swagger definition or get published via an API gateway, the data is just as critical. Often, these unrecognized contracts can be missed or ignored, and we call them hidden contracts. We will cover contracts and hidden contracts in more detail in [Chapter 6](ch06.xhtml), Principles of Proper Services.

Returning to ES, it represents a great architectural pattern but it is not a free lunch. ES introduces other challenges, as it is not a database at the end of the day. Depending on the technology, you will have more or fewer challenges.

  * Ordering: Some workloads require events to be strictly ordered when processed, such as bank account transactions.

  * Consistency: ES systems often provide delivery consistency guarantees, like exactly once, at least once, at most once. They are not traditional relational databases, and can lack constructs for data consistency within a payload, such as allowing two users to have the same email by using relational database constraints like primary key.




ES also introduces eventual consistency, which is typical in big systems at scale. But you need to acknowledge that in your user experience and sometimes dealing with the business.

[Sidebar: CQRS and its relationship with ES](contents.xhtml#sc4_56a)

Created by Greg Young, Command Query Responsibility Segregation (CQRS) is a software architecture where commands and queries have different models and different data stores. Event driven architectures such as event sourcing are typically used to keep the two data stores in sync. The data structure of events by nature are less constrained than a set of SQL commands for a specific database technology. ES is often delivered using solutions like Apache Kafka, RabbitMQ, Apache ActiveMQ, AWS Kinesis, and others.

Because CQRS separates commands and queries, different structures, classes and potentially code bases are necessary. This pattern allows better scalability of the system because different models can run on different hardware, for instance, scaling reads and writes in a different way. CQRS can be combined with caching technologies, queue systems, and persistence storage. It is also possible to use the same technology in the sense of storage, but different nodes or servers in order to avoid writes and reads being affected by each other.

[New service, share via API](contents.xhtml#sc4_57a)

Lastly, we can create a new service for reports, but have it called the profile service by its exposed API. This approach is classic Service Oriented Architecture (SOA). Coupling is limited to the published API, not indirectly via data structures or directly via sharing the same databases.

Sharing the API may seem like the obvious choice, but every decision has tradeoffs. As we articulated earlier in the chapter, having a new service means overhead and maintenance burden, creates the opportunity for aged libraries to cause security vulnerabilities, and team members who have mastered the codebase can leave your company and expose you to team erosion and lack of ownership.

However, from the perspective of Isolation, we have a great tradeoff in this example. Exposure is necessary to satisfy the requirement, but the break of isolation is bound to a concept most engineers understand: a published API using a well-known approach.

Regardless, complexity has increased; only time will tell whether the tradeoff is worth it.

[Centralization](contents.xhtml#sc3_58a)

We covered centralization in [Chapter 1](ch01.xhtml), What’s Wrong with Monoliths?, where we focused on how centralization plays a key role in Monoliths and their pitfalls. Centralized solutions intrinsically have components that are not isolated from one another. Only one solution is needed to deploy or release software, or monitor operational uptime, or access databases, message queues caching systems or NoSQL data stores. Centralized solutions can allow teams to move fast, but they are not isolated; if one team breaks database access, the entire monolith’s database access is likely broken.

Centralization inherently encourages solutions that are not isolated. That does not mean that centralized solutions are inherently wrong; if you correctly group things that should be grouped, you will gain advantages in speed and time to market. But centralized architectures can encourage grouping things that are not supposed to be grouped, thus breaking isolation in improper ways.

Let us look at centralization and isolation with a different lens: what happens when you centralize engineering teams. How does it impact the software they create?

[Benefits of centralizing engineering teams](contents.xhtml#sc4_59a)

Centralizing engineering teams has some advantages. These teams often can be called DevOps teams, Platform teams, Core Teams, Architects, and many other names. Often present in engineering organizations, these teams have:

  * Charter

  * Budget

  * People

  * Expertise

  * Goals




Figure 2.9: Centralization - Platform/DevOps teams

Service teams benefit by having centralized teams to help with deploys, tooling, databases, performance, security, and many cross-cutting concerns, allowing them to focus and deliver more value to the business with fewer distractions. (Figure 2.9)

When we use the monolith style of architecture, such teams may not be necessary because once someone does it, it is done for everybody. Even with monoliths, you might need the expertise and have reasons to do so. But when we embrace distribution and have lots of teams at scale, we really need these teams. A Microservices Style of Architecture (MSA) forces our hand here. That is not necessarily a bad thing. However, there are scenarios where the roles these teams play are taken to the extreme and we end up with more problems.

[Issues with over-centralization](contents.xhtml#sc4_60a)

Centralization can be a double-edged sword because if not applied with careful management, we can end up with two big problems:

  1. Delivery and innovation bottleneck

  2. Bad shared libraries




[Delivery bottleneck and innovation bottleneck](contents.xhtml#sc4_61a)

DevOps is a movement, where development and operations teams break silos, co-create, and work together to write better, maintain and operate software. By definition having just one team doing DevOps is wrong; imagine a company having a single test team, Agile team or (worst of all) innovation team. Concepts like Tests, Agile and Innovation are cross cutting and should be in the DNA of the company. All teams should be practicing these disciplines. By having a single team, you are saying this is a centralized problem, and nobody else’s.

A good example of a bottleneck is where the DevOps team becomes the only team allowed to use cloud services/AWS, and all services teams depend on the DevOps team to make any changes. (Figure 2.10) This is against the original definition of the DevOps movement, full stop - a DevOps team should be enabling engineering teams with good tools and patterns, not blocking their ability to change infrastructure. The DevOps team should be teaching, mentoring and guiding other teams, it should not be a ticket central that reduces options and slows things down.

Another anti-pattern is when the DevOps teams create their own customer wrappers or layers. This is a typical yet bad pattern, because AWS already has APIs for everything, and developers know how to use APIs! Requiring DevOps to bless AWS or put a wrapper in front of everything is not necessary and creates bottlenecks. In the days of data centers and physical hardware, we understood how it made sense to centralize access and have a specialized team. However, on the cloud, we need to have a different mindset; we cannot operate using the same physical data center principles. Delivery Bottleneck problem is exacerbated as often engineering teams are orders of magnitude larger than DevOps teams - we have seen 50:1 and even 100:1 developer to DevOps engineer ratios.

Figure 2.10: DevOps bottleneck for innovation

How can we fix these bottlenecks:

  * Inner Sourcing: Use the GitHub inner sourcing collaboration model and allow engineers to submit PRs to DevOps git repositories. Let them self-service and build their own automation.

  * Allow direct access to the cloud: Do not create wrappers on top of AWS services and use them directly, or via well-known projects like terraform so developers can rely on the internet, documentation, and Stack Overflow.

  * Document and train: If you must build infrastructure solutions in-house, have outstanding documentation, resources and videos, and train people to use your solutions.

  * Embed DevOps engineers: Have embedded DevOps engineers in Scrum teams, or ideally cross-train developers to have DevOps skills themselves.

  * Allow developers to do infrastructure as code: Leverage CDKs like Pulumi, Terraform CDK, or AWS CloudFormation CDK, so engineers can use their native languages rather than Ops-specific tools or dialects.




[Centralization versus standardization for observability and deployments](contents.xhtml#sc4_62a)

A good form of centralization for DevOps teams to pursue is less about shared code, and more about standardization. You do not need to standardize everything, but when it comes to observability, or deployments, you do not want everyone using their own snowflake tool.

While MSAs are great in that they allow for a diversity of languages and infrastructure details, microservices should share common approaches to publishing metrics, logging, alerting, and deployments. Log using different libraries, but publish those log events in a common format; emit metrics using standard semantics, or expose health checks at a common URL. DevOps teams can support a common syntax to build and run your software in containers without being overly limiting, or let teams define CI/CD pipelines in YAML that support arbitrary actions while enforcing safety. All of these standardizations assist with deploying and debugging software in production without limiting innovation.

We would argue that none of these approaches are centralized, per se, but rather standardized. DevOps teams can then focus on delivering tools that support and deliver to the standard, without constraining engineering innovation; Engineering teams can ship new features, but safely, and without disregarding fundamentals.

[Bad shared libraries](contents.xhtml#sc4_63a)

We will cover this topic in-depth in [Chapter 4](ch04.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Internal Shared Libraries, and repeatedly through this book. Centralized teams can have a disproportionately high impact with terribly designed libraries. When you have a shared service, you need to be concerned with availability; you can somewhat relax availability concerns around libraries. But when you deliver global libraries to the whole company, you have different concerns but with equal impact: bloated solutions that lead to binary coupling, forced waterfall migrations by product teams, people avoiding the library and thus duplicating code, duplications, and an array of other problems.

Bad libraries by definition are not isolated: a platform team shipping bad, unnecessary shared libraries to the entire company couples them all together.

[Breaking Isolation](contents.xhtml#sc2_64a)

We dare say Isolation is an essential principle, if not the number one. We do not get everything right in software from the get-go; you might be learning about the business or some new technology, and making mistakes is OK. We need to leverage isolation as much as possible because this is how we will survive those mistakes, recover and improve things even when we make bad decisions.

Let us codify the ways teams typically break isolation.

[Sharing databases](contents.xhtml#sc3_65a)

Lack of Isolation can and often is manifested in several different forms; the most common is lack of isolation by sharing databases.

Do not share databases! Sharing databases across services is evil, equivalent to original sin. Databases need to be isolated. If you can take one thing away from this book, do not share databases.

By databases, we mean any way an application encodes its data, metadata, and underlying schema in a specific technology/infrastructure. For most applications, data is the slowest moving element of its infrastructure and the most dangerous to change; how many horror stories have you heard about a junior engineer deleting a database in production and causing a multi-day outage?

Once a piece of technology is coupled with a database, it changes, slowly, if at all. Over time, team erosion creates an environment where no-one remembers why that index or column is there or what feature it supports.

Compound those issues with multiple independent teams and codebases sharing the same database. By sharing the database, these two teams are now coupled; the data model of the database is a hidden contract. And now, your already slow process is burdened with the need to coordinate changes across multiple teams. (Figure 2.11)

Figure 2.11: Lack of Isolation: Anti-Patterns #1 Shared Databases

What are the motivations that lead teams to share databases?

  * Unrealistic business expectations: Release pressure ends up forcing bad compromises and poor design.

  * Wrong solution: Sometimes, people want to fix the monolith and slice and dice it, but the DB is not part of the plan. There are no well designed, proper service patterns in place, and bad patterns are replicated repeatedly, such as ignoring the database and focusing on the code.

  * Lack of design reviews: Perhaps code reviews in the form of Pull Requests (PRs) are common and standard for your team, but Design Reviews are not in place, or happen at the beginning of a task/ticket but not throughout the project.

  * Lack of proper technical retrospectives: Beyond 15-minute meetings: no introspection, time to reflect on design failures or improve technical debt.

  * Lack of slack time: Few incentives for engineers to improve the engineering process, the product, the architecture, fix technical debt and do work beyond new features.




To be precise, it is possible to share the database server but avoid sharing the schema and the data. (Figure 2.12) Pretty much all data stores have ways to provide that to you. Sometimes in lower/non-production environments, you likely want to share the same database server with a group of applications to reduce costs. It is even possible to share the same database server and still have isolation via schemas in production; however, we would not advise using this in production because of availability and resiliency reasons. You reduce isolation by increasing the blast radius of a database failure. 

Figure 2.12: Shared database server - Isolated schemas/data

In our example, the payments service is a critical service; if the payments service is down, the company cannot collect money and experiences a big business disruption. Now, the reports service is not so critical, but they share the same database hardware, but at least the data and schemas are isolated from one another. However, the risk here is that the physical hardware is not isolated. The report service queries consume shared hardware resources, and if their queries start using 100% of database server CPU, now, the sales service is impacted. (Figure 2.13) This is why we recommend avoiding this pattern in production. 

Figure 2.13: Shared database server - Availability/resiliency impact

As you can see, Isolation does not only apply to database data/schemas but servers as well. The lack of isolation creates much pain and trouble.

So, do not share databases! You are making an already bad problem worse.

[When is it ok to share databases?](contents.xhtml#sc4_66a)

In the context of Online Transaction Processing (OLTP)25 services, sharing databases is an anti-pattern. Still, there are other contexts where sharing can happen and be accepted as long as it is limited to specific use cases and there is awareness and some degree of control over the sharing.

[Big Data sharing application databases](contents.xhtml#sc4_67a)

One of the benefits of monoliths is that we typically have the whole solution using the same database. When you have all the data in one place, answering any question with a SQL query is possible.

When we embrace distribution and have services (SOA) and microservices (MSA), we have more relational databases and different kinds of persistence technologies like Document DBs, Graph DBs, Columnar DB, K/V Stores, and many others in the realm of NoSQL and NewSQL. Now, no single SQL query can answer all your questions. That is where Big Data comes to the rescue by creating an environment where all data sources can be ingested, enriched, and accessed simultaneously. In a limited sense, Big Data can seem like a Big Join machine. 

Figure 2.14: Big Data use case

Big Data is about accessing and augmenting vast volumes of data. We have already established that online services should hide their data stores and expose public interfaces. However, in the Big Data world it is the opposite: you are meant to see and join everything. Big Data stacks need to access data from some or all data stores in the company as well as 3rd party data sources. Often, online data is supplied to the Big Data environment via reading the data from a database replica using Change Data Capture (CDC) techniques or by a mediator like Apache Kafka or AWS Kinesis. (Figure 2.14)

In Big data, this is not a problem; it is desired. We need to be able to share the data.

Figure 2.15: Big data ingestion options

In Figure 2.15, we show three different options to consider when ingesting data:

  * Direct database replication: The Big Data team reads directly from the application databases, and may or may not transform into an interim format. The result is direct coupling from the Big Data environment to the online environment, and the data is likely not suited to analysis and will require expensive transformations.

  * Big data owned ingestion: Databases publish events via CDC to Kafka. Using the enterprise information model, the Big Data teams figure out the CDC format and event structure to transform data and ingest into the Big Data environment. The application and Big Data teams both need to fully understand the application database model, and coordinate changes to avoid breaking.

  * Services publish events: The application team transforms and publishes data into Kafka using the enterprise information model. Only the application team needs to understand the format of its internal databases.




[Hidden contracts and preserving isolation](contents.xhtml#sc4_68a)

In Big Data, we have seen teams act as if accessing the isolated databases and data models of online services is a good and necessary thing, a way to access critical data. This may also seem self-evident - how can the Big Data environment access all the data if they cannot access the databases? - but that does not mean application teams have to give away key protections for their online systems, or push unnecessary work to the Big Data environment.

It all rests on a key discipline of the best Big Data teams: a master information model, which governs the data used by their environments and processes. The master information model stands independent from the online services, modeled on the lifecycle of the enterprise; and becomes the foundations of all future transformations to support analytics and other use cases.

Application teams can transform their data to the master information model, ideally publishing events using a mediator like Kafka and a format like JSON or a binary format like Avro, following the event sourcing pattern. Instead of leaving it to the Big Data teams to figure out the puzzle of how each microservice is put together, the best Application Teams take on the act of publishing their data to the master information model with the same discipline as supporting any data contract, maintaining it and avoiding backwards compatibility, rather than throwing their DB schemas at the data team and wishing them the best of luck. This approach ultimately promotes proper isolation by ensuring application teams can avoid sharing database internals from the service.

[Legacy system integrations](contents.xhtml#sc4_69a)

Another use case where sharing databases is around legacy systems, and where the most practical way to make two systems interact is to share data via a shared database. (Figure 2.16) This integration pattern is fragile, and will create a lot of headaches. Still, there are good reasons to use it (with caution):

  * One or both of the systems use legacy technologies that your team does not know.

  * The systems will be decommissioned eventually in the short or medium term.

  * You do not fully control the systems; for example, a 3rd-party integration with an external company or vendor and you cannot use an API or another sane technique.




Figure 2.16: Sharing-databases - Integration or Band-aid

As you can see, there are some limited use cases where sharing databases might be necessary and okay to facilitate machine-to-machine communication. However, these situations should hopefully be rare and under strict and tight control. Otherwise, you risk falling into a much bigger and darker trap: the Distributed Monolith, which will be covered deeply in [Chapter 3](ch03.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Distributed Monoliths.

[Shared libraries](contents.xhtml#sc3_70a)

You may think of software libraries as benign objects - they only do good. No matter your architecture pattern - Monolith, Modular Monolith, Microservices, Services - you likely use libraries from 3rd party providers, open sources, or internal providers.

Libraries help you replace code you would have written, save time, and get you to market faster. This is partially true; but in return for these benefits, you are directly using externally authored code, which has both direct and indirect points of coupling. Coupling is sometimes necessary but is inherently dangerous, and when mishandled, absolutely breaks the isolation of your code.

Libraries are not bad things. But it is how the industry deals with internally shared libraries that is a big problem, bringing big trouble to services two ways: through binary coupling and having unstable contracts. Unstable contracts can affect both libraries and services, which we will cover in the next section, so let us keep going.

[Binary coupling](contents.xhtml#sc4_71a)

We covered this in [Chapter 1](ch01.xhtml), What’s Wrong with Monoliths? Binary coupling is the phenomenon where your software, your software’s dependencies, and the dependencies of those dependencies, can be entangled in such a way that attempting to upgrade a component or dependency creates an inherent conflict. You cannot upgrade to Hibernate 5 because half of your software is coupled with Hibernate 4, for example.

Internally shared libraries make this problem even worse.

Figure 2.17: Lack of isolation: Anti-Patterns #2 Shared Libraries

Your company could create a simple logging library; you could send your logs to AWS S3 because, later on, you can do queries using AWS Athena to enable various use cases like Observability, Fraud Detection, Auditing, and much more. The Payment Service team was the first to send logs to S3 for Observability reasons; they thought – let us build a library that others can use as well. Nothing wrong with that, right?

So, the Payment team builds a simple wrapper around S3, using Jackson, which is a popular library in Java to serialize/deserialize objects into JSON or XML, and uses the aws-java-sdk-s3 client to send the JSON files to S3. However, this team prefers to avoid coding getters/setters in Java, so they also decided to use Lombok to remove Java’s boilerplate verbosity. (Figure 2.17)

Now the report service and the profile service teams also need to send logs to S3 but for other reasons, for anti-fraud use cases. They see the logging library the payment team wrote and think, well, we can use it, it fits our use case, and we do not need to code anything extra. Repeat this process over and over, and without realizing it, your company has hundreds of services using this shared library.

Take the internal logging library itself in isolation. We might think that it is all good and there is no problem. What happens if one or many other services also want to use Jackson, but they have different versions for some reason? Unless Jackson implements forward and backward compatibility and/or you try to standardize everybody on the same version of Jackson, you are already in trouble. And that is just for Jackson, never mind all the other libraries in the aws-java-sdk-s3 client, or even all the shared libraries in your company. 

The result is a lack of isolation, because now all the services have Binary Coupling to the internal logging libraries, and all dependent libraries it might have across the whole dependency graph; for instance, Jackson itself is also used by the AWS SDK. There are other consequences here; what if other teams do not want to use Lombok? Well, whether they like it or not, this decision is forced upon them. (Figure 2.18)

We also end up with a bad scenario for the service teams. Because now they might be forced to deal with migrations - upgrades, security patches, breaking changes which will force refactoring - they do not want, do not care, and do not have time to do, but they need to do anyway. Hold on a second!

Now step back and think about what is going on here. Most of the solutions we have presented to you so far have big consequences. Microservices and Services styles of architecture are supposed to protect us from these problems and provide isolation: we should have independence as every service has its own servers, deployment pipelines, and even different teams. Several benefits from Services just got lost because of the lack of isolation in the shared libraries. However, it is not what we are getting here. 

Figure 2.18: Lack of Isolation: Anti-Patterns #2 Shared Libraries - Issues

Shared libraries can often be a great source of technical complexity and technical debt. Libraries need to be managed carefully, but often, that is not what happens since libraries are typically hidden and unmanaged: they often do not have a charter or product owner, lack a proper lifecycle, and many other problems. While we have discussed the problem of Lack of Isolation in Shared Libraries, in [Chapter 4](ch04.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Internal Shared Libraries. We will go much deeper into this topic and present other solutions.

[Lack of stable contracts](contents.xhtml#sc3_72a)

Contracts are the interfaces published by services or libraries to advertise their capabilities. Contracts create expectations from consumers that the contract provider needs to oblige. Creating and fulfilling great contracts are at the core of making usable software solutions, and not breaking consumer expectations is at the core of making stable software solutions.

Bad Contracts can be manifested in multiple ways. Let us take a look at the following Java code:

  1. import java.math.BigDecimal;
  2. import java.util.Calendar;
  3.   4. public class TaxProcessor {
  5.   6. public BigDecimal computeSalesTax(Long salesID,BigDecimal value,String state){
  7. // use salesID to fetch specific sale from the Database...
  8. // filter by state and consider different rates per state...
  9. // let's say by default it's 30%
  10. return BigDecimal.valueOf(value.longValue() * 1.3);
  11. }
  12.   13. public String getDayOfTheWeek(int year,int month,int day){
  14. Calendar cal = getCalendar();
  15. cal.set(year, month - 1, day, 0, 0);
  16. return switch(cal.get(Calendar.DAY_OF_WEEK)){
  17. case 1 -> "Sunday";
  18. case 2 -> "Monday";
  19. case 3 -> "Tuesday";
  20. case 4 -> "Wednesday";
  21. case 5 -> "Thursday";
  22. case 6 -> "Friday";
  23. case 7 -> "Saturday";
  24. default -> throw new RuntimeException("IDK this day of the week!");
  25. };
  26. }
  27.   28. private Calendar getCalendar(){
  29. Calendar cal = Calendar.getInstance();
  30. return cal;
  31. }
  32.   33. private void internalMethodHardToTest(){
  34. System.out.println("I do nothing!");
  35. }
  36.   37. private void anotherMethodHardToTest(){
  38. System.out.println("I do nothing too!");
  39. }
  40.   41. }



Code 2.1: Sample Contract in Java 17

Our Java class represents the most common form of contract, a public method in a public class. We need to unpack many things here; let us get started:

  * The method computeSalesTax is part of the contract because it is public.

  * The method getDayOfTheWeek is also part of the contract because it is public. However, this method should not be public; what does getDayOfTheWeek have to do with tax processing? Maybe it should be in some util class, regardless it does not matter because it is public; it is part of the contract.

  * The method getCalendar is not part of the contract, even being in a public class with public methods; this one is private, so not part of the contract.

  * The methods internalMethodHardToTest and anotherMethodHardToTest also are not part of the contract because they are not public. These methods are hard to test because they receive no parameter and return void; they should be removed, but in regards to the contract discussion - they are not part of the contract.




So, what is a contract? Anything public, composed of the following parts:

  * A method Name.

  * A method signature (parameters, input, and output).

  * The format of the data. e.g. on getDayOfTheWeek is a string, but the format is written in English – i.e. Monday. If this String changes to MONDAY, this might break the consumers.

  * Here, we have the code in Java, which would be consumed via JAR in a method-class fashion; it could be an internal module or a library. If instead we exposed this via REST + JSON, those elements would be part of the contract too.




In other words, data. Contracts are about data.

What are the problems with the current contract definition we see above?

  * The contract is not explicit: The contract is mixed with the implementation and thus does not appear to be properly managed, owned and maintained.

  * The contract is not decoupled: Again, the contract is mixed with the implementation. As the implementation changes, the contract can easily break. Ideally, the contract should not break with any implementation changes.




We can make this contract better by separating the contract for the implementation and moving the public parts of the contract to an Interface. Let us take a look into this another Java code snippet.

  1. import java.math.BigDecimal;
  2.   3. public interface TaxProcessorV2 {
  4. BigDecimal computeSalesTax(Long salesID, BigDecimal value, String state);
  5. }



Code 2.2: Improving Contracts - Separating the Implementation from the Contract in Java 17

Now it is much better. The contract is explicit instead of implicit and is much clearer as a result. We can change the implementation of this method computeSalesTax freely, as long as we do not break consumers’ expectations of behavior and data types, both input and output. However, there are still issues to consider:

Interoperability: Consider exposing this last contract over the network, via REST + JSON or binary like gRPC; details do not matter. Now we have another issue: BigDecimal is a Java Type. How about your consumer services or UIs not written in Java? When we consider exposing the contract over the network, relying on primitive types might be a better idea from an interoperability point of view.

High cohesion and loose coupling: What happens if the Tax keeps changing? What if your company is global – every country has different ways of calculating taxes, can this interface keep up? Our contract focuses on sales tax, and as you can see in the definition is coupled specifically with a salesID. The contract is not exclusively focused on calculating taxes alone; it must understand the sales lifecycle as well. Is that well isolated?

The contract is not resilient to change: It is not clear that this contract is stable; not only may the requirements change, but it is likely misdesigned. In the sense of granularity, it would have been better to take on a bigger view of the tax lifecycle, rather than be narrowly focused on sales taxes only. Because of the high risk of the business granularity being incorrect, breaking changes seem inevitable.

Let’ us take a look at another contract example in Java.

  1. public interface ProfileService {
  2. UserOutput retrieveByID(UserInput userInput);
  3. }
  4. 


Code 2.3: ProfileService Contract – The Operations

So far, we have a clean contract. In the sense of being explicit, the contract also appears to have clearly defined Input and Output objects, which has some key benefits. As we saw on the previous code, we want to make the contract decoupled from the implementation. Let’s review the Input object definition:

  1. import java.util.Objects;
  2.   3. public class UserInput {
  4.   5. private Long id;
  6.   7. public UserInput(){}
  8.   9. public UserInput(Long id) {
  10. this.id = id;
  11. }
  12.   13. public Long getId() {
  14. return id;
  15. }
  16. public void setId(Long id) {
  17. this.id = id;
  18. }
  19.   20. @Override
  21. public Boolean equals(Object o) {
  22. if (this == o) return true;
  23. if (o == null || getClass() != o.getClass()) return false;
  24. UserInput user = (UserInput) o;
  25. return Objects.equals(id, user.id);
  26. }
  27.   28. @Override
  29. public int hashCode() {
  30. return Objects.hash(id);
  31. }
  32.   33. @Override
  34. public String toString() {
  35. return “UserInput{“ +
  36. “id=” + id +
  37. ‘}’;
  38. }
  39.   40. }



Code 2.4: ProfileService contract – The Input object

And the Profile service’s output definition:

1\. import java.util.Objects;

2\.

6\.  private String name;

15\.  }

16\.

19\.  }

21\.  this.id = id;

22\.  }

23\. 

24\.  public String getName() {

25\.  return name;

26\.  }

28\.  this.name = name;

29\.  }

32\.  return email;

33\.  }

34\.  public void setEmail(String email) {

35\.  this.email = email;

36\.  }

37\.

39\.  public boolean equals(Object o) {

40\.  if (this == o) return true;

41\.  if (o == null || getClass() != o.getClass()) return false;

42\.  UserOutput user = (UserOutput) o;

43\.  return Objects.equals(id, user.id);

44\.  }

45\. 

46\.  @Override

47\.  public int hashCode() {

48\.  return Objects.hash(id);

49\.  }

55\.  ", name='" + name + '\'' +

58\.  }

59\.
