61\. 

Code 2.5: ProfileService contract - The output object

Looking at the contract, it is very likely we would have a table called Users in the implementation, as well as a user POJO in our core domain. So why not return that same class in our contract and re-use the code? Well, if we do, we would be coupling the internal implementation with the explicit contract. We do not want to do this if we want to have stable contracts. (Figure 2.19)

Figure 2.19: Lack of Isolation: Lack of Stable Contracts

When you have shared libraries, we need to be 10x more careful than services. We have already reviewed the risks associated with Binary Coupling, and you also need to be careful about Enums, shared POJOs, and other subtle forms of hidden contracts. Stable contracts can and should be applied in the macro and the micro for services and libraries. 

Figure 2.20: Contracts summary

In summary, contracts are essential and key to proper services, as a lack of stable contracts breaks isolation. (Figure 2.20) We should not care as much for private or internal operations; public operations matter most. Avoid having implicit and hidden contracts. Contracts are not only defined by inputs and outputs but also protocols and formats; and remember, at the end of the day, it is all about data.

[Pushing complexity upwards](contents.xhtml#sc3_73a)

So far, we have seen all the issues and side effects that lack of isolation creates. Isolation is required on all fronts, from stable contacts to shared libraries, databases, processes and server instances.

The underlying issues that break isolation in those cases are largely technology driven; however, a lack of understanding of the business domain can cause the same effects.

Microservices enforce micro granularity; sometimes you cannot match the feature or code to the business domain of the service, or you just do not want to fit it there because it makes the service too big; you want to be micro. So, what do you do?

One antipattern: you ignore it, treat it as the consumer’s problem, not yours, and let them push the complexity up the stack. While sometimes this is the right answer, oftentimes it is not, and the side effects of these hacked designs break isolation.

[Granularity and distribution: Side effects](contents.xhtml#sc4_74a)

When we have a monolith style of architecture, we have all business domains in our monolith. Having all business domains in the same backend has advantages because we have all the complexity in one place; therefore, the clients/consumers, browsers, and external APIs tend to be simpler. Less aggregation is likely necessary on the consumer’s side because the backend is deep, with more code, more modules, and more business domains, as compared with shallow, single-purpose microservices in distributed architectures.

Monoliths tend towards centralization instead of distribution; and as we saw in [Chapter 1](ch01.xhtml), What’s Wrong with Monoliths?, there are plenty of problems in regard to classic monoliths. By comparison, Microservice architectures push for more distribution instead of centralization. (Figure 2.21)

Figure 2.21: Monolith vs microservices (centralization vs distribution)

As services get more focused, they tend to be smaller, which creates a problem when you have a requirement that covers a broad array of your application’s functionality. Some component likely needs to call multiple services, and this inherent complexity found in distributed architectures is often pushed upwards to the caller. Calling multiple services, or aggregating their output, is not wrong per se, but can cause issues.

Let us examine some options to solve the problem:

  * Move to a Service: We could build the aggregation or feature within one of the services we have (Profile/Sales).

  * Aggregation Service: We can create a dedicated orchestration/aggregation service in order to gather the data for this feature.

  * BFF: We could push the aggregation to the upper layers in our stack like BFF or even UI.

  * GraphQL: Similarly to the aggregation service we can use GraphQL to build an aggregation solution.

  * API Gateway: We could have an API Gateway as Gateway for the domain.

  * Re-org the services by domain: We could regroup the services and group them by business domain rather than function/capability.




Let us analyze each option in the sense of architectural trade-offs:

  * Move to a service: You could consolidate all orchestration or aggregation into one of the services we have (Profile, Sales). Perhaps it does not matter if the feature lives only in one domain or the other. Alternatively, and more likely, you could be coupling two domains that should not be decoupled, and the service now lacks cohesion; not so ideal.

  * Aggregation service: We can create an orchestration/aggregation service in order to handle the aggregation/feature. We are not coupling sales and profiles, and since we have a new service, we can easily reuse this across different consumers and future needs. but it could be wasted effort if only the UI cares about this aggregation. It would have been better to implement the BFF so the frontend engineers can do it faster and with fewer dependencies.

  * BFF: As we mentioned before, the most common way to push complexity upwards is by moving business logic and aggregation to the BFF. In fact, it is great because JavaScript/TypeScript engineers like to keep things idiomatic and often are not big fans of Java, so they can quickly implement what they need without requiring a bunch of management sprint coordination and back and forth with backend engineers.

    * The main issue with this approach is that if other services or APIs also need this aggregation, you are in a difficult position. Also, you need to code everything from scratch at all times, compared with GraphQL, where it is possible to reuse the same APIs and change different queries. Aggregation penalties can often be compensated with caching systems.

  * GraphQL: We can use GraphQL as an aggregation solution. GraphQL is a popular choice and often a good idea because it allows the frontend (website and/or mobile) to cherry-pick what data points they need, do one call, and reduce latency. This solution is future-proof since you can add domains and data as you go. GraphQL requests are typically synchronous; for web applications, relying solely on GraphQL means you could lose your flexibility in applying async patterns, limiting your ability to make requests ahead of the user or in the background.

  * API Gateway: We could have an API Gateway as Gateway for the domain. API Gateways provides a layer of indirection between consumers and the services. Aggregations, orchestrations, transformations, data enrichment, validations, multiplexing/demultiplexing, could all happen in the API Gateway layer. API Gateways are an older pattern that predates GraphQL, more oriented to the backend. They are similar solutions, but API Gateways might require more work for your infrastructure teams.

  * Re-org the service by domain: We could regroup the services and group them by business domain rather than function/capability. Often engineers break their services by layers or capabilities, not by business domain. If you are into Domain Driven Design (DDD), this is an elegant solution that reduces the frequency or effect of complexity being pushed upwards; it cannot mitigate it 100%. The issue with this approach might be that you are not doing this since you might have a big refactoring/migration project to deliver this change.




As you can see, many of the common practices to handle MSA complexity involve pushing the complexity upwards. Teams can approach this ad-hoc with an Aggregation Service or a BFF style approach; if you commonly find yourself in this situation, consider a turnkey approach like a common GraphQL solution or API gateway. However, none of these answers may be truly right for your architecture, and could have side effects that break isolation. In that case, perform a Design Review, evaluate your business domains and consider Re-organizing your services by domain.

[Let us isolate everything](contents.xhtml#sc2_75a)

Isolation is a key principle for proper software architecture. The bottom line is: let us isolate everything.

[Databases](contents.xhtml#sc3_76a)

Databases are one of the most important elements that need to be isolated. An application should avoid sharing databases. When an application needs to share, it should put a service contract in the front of the database, decoupling the underlying persistence choices. There is a cascading effect when we share databases which could be as basic as just data coupling or more pervasive as sharing persistence objects and libraries. Classical monolithic and distributed monoliths style of architecture share databases, and if you take only one thing away from chapter: Do not share databases.

Services should have purpose-built databases, and each service should have its own database. When sharing needs to happen consider API calls or advanced patterns like Event Sourcing.

[Shared libraries](contents.xhtml#sc3_77a)

Shared libraries also need to be isolated as well, but need different approaches from databases or services. For instance, due to binary coupling, having fewer libraries or making sure they are lean in dependencies is a better choice. We will cover this in depth in [Chapter 4](ch04.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Internal Shared Libraries. For now, remember that Isolation also needs to be applied to libraries, and internally shared libraries can be a great source of breaking isolation. Watch out for your libraries.

[Stable contracts](contents.xhtml#sc3_78a)

Contracts should be explicit, and not mixed with your implementation. When you couple your public contract with your internal classes, you are giving away the benefits of an MSA or SOA because any internal change could introduce coupling with your public contract, damaging contract stability and ultimately breaking isolation. Stable contracts have public, explicit contracts that are resilient to change and rarely break consumers.

Breaking changes on public contracts can cause a cascading effect. For instance, if the Profile Service APIs are used across 100+ services internally and 50+ external partners via customer-facing APIs, breaking changes means every single consumer must change their code. Breaking changes introduces slowness to the overall development cycle, damages business delivery, and slows lead time.

If we have to pick between fixing a poor implementation with a great contract, vs a great implementation but a poor contract, we would pick the great contract every time. Great contracts let you refactor code, redesign implementations, and change pretty much everything under the hood with minimal or no disruption to consumers - and that is pretty powerful.

[Sidebar: Deep modules and A Philosophy of Software Design](contents.xhtml#sc3_79a)

In his seminal work A Philosophy of Software Design26, John Ousterhout establishes the definition of complexity in software systems, how to identify complexity and the symptoms of complexity.

In [Chapter 4](ch04.xhtml): Anti-Patterns: Internal Shared Libraries, John argues that modules should be Deep. Deep modules tend to have more code, and therefore contain complexity, whereas module interfaces should be simple to use and understand. We could not agree with him more. If we take John’s principles and apply them to software architecture and service design (Figure 2.22), we believe they have perfect alignment with the concepts discussed in our book, where:

  * Interfaces == Stable Service Contracts

  * Modules == Service Implementations




Figure 2.22: Applying philosophy of software design to services (MSA & SOA)

[Operating Systems and infrastructure](contents.xhtml#sc3_80a)

Software infrastructure is one of the most important things you can isolate.

Running workloads on virtual hardware is one of the most common tasks for companies working in the cloud, such as deploying on EC2 instances in AWS. EC2 instances run an Operating System (OS), and all OSs have bugs that need patching.

The leaner your OS - the fewer packages it has - the fewer items to be maintained, patched and migrated. For instance, not installing python unnecessarily if your software does not need it, or skipping compilers like gcc or clang. After all, many OS libraries are not lean libraries, suffering from bloated dependencies where one small install can lead to conflicts, managing multiple conflicting packages. While package maintainers do their best, they too can break contracts accidentally or otherwise; and the more contracts you are exposed to, the higher the risk that an unstable contract breaks your environment. 

Figure 2.23: OS Isolation - Heartbleed patching example

Anyone who has lived through the Heartbleed27 bug, a critical TLS bug in OpenSSL, will remember the urgency to upgrade the openssl 1.0.1 installs on all their Linux distros with urgency (Figure 2.23). Many teams found themselves debugging a broken environment due to a mismanaged OpenSSL configuration or needing to recompile multiple dependencies.

Running a leaner OS, ideally via a containerized workload, makes the patching process more streamlined and smooth, reducing the impact of OS changes and increasing isolation for any deployed solution.

[Advanced isolation](contents.xhtml#sc3_81a)

Many techniques can be applied to improve the Isolation of your software architectures, and the topic could easily be its own book. Let’s leave you with 3 advanced concepts in isolation: bulkheading, fallbacks, and UX degradation.

[Failure isolation and bulkheading](contents.xhtml#sc4_82a)

Naval engineering has a concept called Bulkheading, a fascinating engineering concept that has existed since the 5th century. The idea is simple: to have upright walls within a ship’s hull, partitioning the ship and compartmentalizing it so that if one area becomes flooded, it is isolated from the others. (Figure 2.24) Bulkheading prevents the whole ship from sinking if only one small portion of the ship is breached. 

Figure 2.24: Bulkhead from Wikipedia  
Source: [https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bulkhead_(partition)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bulkhead_\(partition\))

In the field of software engineering, similarly, software failures tend to cascade: failure is not contained, cascading to the next element on the stack, eventually causing the whole stack to fail or, in an extreme case, go down entirely. Here, we see the impacts of a lack of isolation again, now in the form of failure isolation.

The Bulkhead Pattern28 is a design pattern that makes the application tolerant to failure. Netfilix was a pioneer of these concepts through its Hystrix library29. Using Hystrix, outbound service invocations can be grouped and bound to thread pools or semaphores. If one group begins to fail, it can only fully consume one thread pool or fail to release its semaphore, thus not affecting other service invocation groups. 

Figure 2.25: Lack of failure isolation - Classical modular monoliths

One of the big drawbacks of monoliths is their lack of availability and fault tolerance. The whole application can be affected if one module goes down. (Figure 2.25) MSA fixes these problems because when you have services, one service being down does not necessarily mean the others will go down; though MSAs can still cascade failures, as we illustrated.

JVM crashes might not be the only catastrophic reason that brings your whole application down. Since we are in a monolithic style of architecture, any exhaustion of shared resources could cause the same issue, such as:

  * Exhaustion of Memory

  * Exhaustion of CPU

  * Exhaustion of Disk/IO

  * Exhaustion of Network bandwidth

  * Exhaustion of Centralized or Shared Databases

  * Exhaustion of Centralized or Shared Services or any Single Point of Failure (SPOF)




Even if you do not have downtime, resource strain can still end up affecting your user experience. Someone can be requesting expensive and heavyweight Reports and now people can barely submit sales requests. Lack of failure isolation can go beyond availability impacts but also introduce performance issues. Performance issues can be mitigated by performing proper capacity planning, stress tests, and overprovisioning hardware; but availability and resiliency cannot always be so easily addressed. 

Figure 2.26: Isolation in microservices architecture

Figure 2.26 illustrates how a microservices style of architecture can provide better Isolation, since it is very likely Reporting capabilities will not impact Sales and Profile operations. MSA enables this because we have isolation for the:

  * Codebase, both contracts and implementation.

  * Infrastructure, with each service running on independent EC2 servers.

  * Databases, with dedicated clusters for each service.




Drawbacks of the Microservices architecture style for this case include:

  * Cost, more resources, hence even more cost.

  * Complexity, harder to debug and maintain.




In reality, this problem is likely more complex than it looks. In our example, it is easier to visualize Reports working in isolation from Sales. However, take the Payments service; if the Payments service is down, this could easily impact Sales even if they are decoupled and fully isolated, as they are coupled via the overall consumer business process. Testing is the key to evaluate possible cascading failure scenarios, such as:

  * Chaos engineering: Preemptively break your applications and infrastructure and see if they can recover from failure.

  * Stress/load testing: Under pressure, see where your application breaks and discover bottlenecks. Immensely useful for capacity planning.




Chaos Engineering, Testing in Production, and other aspects of Testing will be covered in depth in [Chapter 7](ch07.xhtml), Proper Service Testing. As you can see, these are all forms of isolation, and here isolation becomes super important for availability and resiliency.

[Fallbacks](contents.xhtml#sc4_83a)

Beyond pure scaling and resiliency fixes, engineering fallbacks is an effective way to mitigate the impact of failures. For instance, if the payments service is down, could we queue a message in a Kafka topic, buffering, resuming payment processing once payment returns? If the failure is in a 3rd party payments service, could we introduce a backup provider if our primary is down? Not all scenarios permit a fallback or partial success, but where possible they are powerful tools.

Fallbacks can be implemented in a wide variety of ways:

  * Retry: If a consumer call receives a failure, can we just try again?

  * Failover: If a service dependency is down, could we try an instance in another Availability Zone (AZ) instance in AWS?

  * Caches: Can we fetch less current data from a cache? This technique applies to all forms of caching: static, dynamic, internal, external, local or remote. It is possible to start with a remote cache and fall back to a local one.

  * Buffers: Can we store the request in Kafka or a persistent queue to reprocess later?




However, there are gotchas for each of these options.

Retries need to be used with caution; otherwise, they can create additional availability problems and create cascading failures. If a group of services is down, you can have lots of retries that can make the service go down and then get up again; this is called the thundering herd problem. Safe retry algorithms typically introduce jitter, a random time between multiple attempts; and exponential backoff, where we exponentially increase the wait time between retry attempts. Mark Brooker shares AWS’ experiences with retries, jitters, and exponential backoff here: [https://aws.amazon.com/builders-library/timeouts-retries-and-backoff-with-jitter/](https://aws.amazon.com/builders-library/timeouts-retries-and-backoff-with-jitter/). One interesting fact is that retries, jitter and exponential backoffs are a built-in feature of AWS SDKs, but you need to configure it.

For failover, the failure could be beyond any AZ and include an entire AWS region; the failover fallback might require you to retry in a completely different region. Region failover is more complex and costs more; your business might not need it - it might be okay just to accept the downtime impact.

Caches can have bugs, need to be tested very well, and inherently become part of the reliability path. Like any software component, caches can also fail and create their own problems. Whether you have a code or cache fallback, you must test it, which might not be practical or easy.

Buffers can encounter exhaustion issues; what happens if the queue is never cleaned up? You might create a Denial of Service (DoS) problem. Safe use of buffers requires Time To Live (TTL) and additional compensating mechanisms, such as ensuring observability (monitoring and alerting), and perhaps humans need to review the queue from time to time.

We believe fallbacks are great; however, fallbacks can be abused and used wrongly. Here is a nice contrasting article from Amazon Web Services; their Builder’s Library has a great series of articles sharing the lessons learned from building AWS. Jacob Gabrielson explains the issues and problems with fallbacks in one of these articles: [https://aws.amazon.com/builders-library/avoiding-fallback-in-distributed-systems/](https://aws.amazon.com/builders-library/avoiding-fallback-in-distributed-systems/).

[UX isolation: Degradation and exploration](contents.xhtml#sc4_84a)

Another available fallback option is to degrade your user experience, avoiding full application downtime. User Experience (UX) isolation is the ultimate form of isolation: isolation now goes beyond software, databases, and infrastructure to the UX design of the application itself. Microservices do not fix all our resiliency problems. MSAs are great in the sense of failure isolation, but we need to design our application UX to safely accommodate partial failures and keep on serving customers.

When you see the Twitter Fail Whale (Figure 2.27), it means that Twitter is completely down. There is no partial degradation of service. If your business is down, it has a direct impact on customers and can mean revenue loss. And if that downtime is frequent, you could have a serious brand issue, and customers might lose trust in your digital product. 

Figure 2.27: Twitter’s famous Fail Whale  
Source: [https://techcrunch.com/2010/06/18/twitter-fail-whale-3/](https://techcrunch.com/2010/06/18/twitter-fail-whale-3/)

We need to design for resiliency and availability. Back to our example, consider if the Reports Service is down, or its downstream dependencies are down, what would be the impact? Would that be a partial failure, or would that mean the whole business is down? If it is a partial failure, there are tactics we can apply on the business, service, and infrastructure side. For instance, could the business agree to degrade the user experience?

Netflix is famous for preserving core streaming application availability through customer experience degradation30. For instance, Netflix provides machine-learning recommendations for the next movie to watch. If that service is down, can the application provide a list of the top 10 global movies, or just a list of all movies? In any application, some services will be more critical than others; for instance, if Netflix’s core streaming delivery is not working, Netflix’s business is effectively down. But not all software failures in a complex enterprise carry the same business impact.

[How to prevent isolation issues](contents.xhtml#sc2_85a)

The best way to create and preserve isolation is to have good principles in software engineering and design, and to ensure your developers are aware of these principles and know how to apply them to their day-to-day projects. Training is important for sharing knowledge.

[Design reviews and tooling](contents.xhtml#sc3_86a)

In software architecture design review sessions, developers and architects present their engineering design proposals for peer review and feedback prior to starting engineering. Design Reviews should cover new features, fixing meaningful bugs or projects to retire technical debt, and present a perfect opportunity to review key software design elements:

  * Architectural integrity: Also known as Conceptual Integrity, anywhere you look in your code, you can tell that the design is part of the same overall design and consistent the entire time.

  * Design consistency: Whether the individual component designs are consistent and correct.

  * Clear: Given the inherent complexity, the design is well documented and easy to understand.

  * Lean: The design fixes its problem and no further, and does not mix domains unnecessarily.

  * Apply core principles: Such as Isolation, High Cohesion, Loose Coupling, and applying Correct Abstractions.

  * Avoid anti-patterns: Such as Lack of Isolation, High Coupling, or Wrong Abstractions.

  * Avoid breaking isolation: As discussed throughout this chapter, avoid sharing databases, coupling to terrible libraries, and apply stable contracts.




Code reviews can be useful too, but as we see it, code reviews often focus on the code deltas and small changes, and tend to miss these big architecture questions. Applying design rigor during the code review lifecycle could also be an option.

Your design review practice should be continuous. Software architects and engineers should be looking into the software architecture of services and code from time to time, and evaluating things to ensure all is as it should be. As we mentioned before, if you have a stable contract and proper isolation, even if you have other issues, bad decisions, mistakes, and plenty of technical debt, you will have a clear path to fix things.

At scale, you can never have enough software architects. Design reviews are a good idea, but you might need to invest in automation and build tools that can identify design gaps for you, such as:

  * Does my service access more than 1 database?

  * How often does my contract project change over 3 months?

  * What do my dependencies look like? For maven: are there any shared libs in my pom.xml?




[Things to remember](contents.xhtml#sc2_87a)

Congratulations, we have reached the end of [Chapter 2](ch02.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Lack of Isolation! This chapter teaches us how Isolation is key in software architectures, and how a lack of isolation creates many significant problems. To summarize your learnings:

  * Proper Isolation is a key to effective software architectures.

  * In the process of creating real software, you will encounter ways you can break isolation. Avoid the temptations to break proper isolation.

  * Exposure is a necessary element of all modern software architectures. Correctly exposing elements of your architecture requires a correct and complex tradeoff.

  * CQRS and event sourcing can be powerful techniques to manage distributed systems, but also incur complexity.

  * Centralization can be powerful when done correctly with properly sized services and modular monoliths, but can also cause you to break isolation by grouping the wrong things together.

  * Centralized teams such as DevOps, Platform teams can become a bottleneck for innovation and delivery, and create vehicles to break isolation, such as shipping badly designed internal libraries or services. They can be a powerful benefit, too; manage them carefully.

  * Sharing databases needs to be avoided as much as possible to preserve isolation. Ideally, every service should have its own isolated data store.

  * Shared libraries might have isolation issues: watch out for 3rd party libraries and Internal Shared Libraries.

  * Internal Shared Libraries can be a huge, hidden drain on your organization through binary coupling, waterfall migrations and lead to the Distributed Monolith anti-pattern.

  * Contracts are public operations with well-defined input and output objects with clear behavior expectations. Contracts include both calling protocol and data formats. In the end, contracts are Data.

  * Stable Contracts are an essential element to well isolated software. A Stable Contract supported by good testing can allow you to easily update your implementations without impacting consumers. Keys to Stable Contracts include:

  * Explicit contracts

  * An interface clearly decoupled from implementation

  * Resilient to change, though ensuring a deep understanding of the business domain and requirements.

  * Complexity needs to live somewhere. Bigger services might not be bad; be careful not to push complexity upwards and finding a mess in your BFFs and UIs.

  * You should aspire to isolate all the elements of your software architecture: Databases, contracts, libraries, servers, operating systems, and infrastructures.

  * The Bulkhead Pattern is a great way to isolate the underlying resources used by your services and limit cascading failures.

  * Fallbacks can effectively survive failures in a degraded mode, including retries, failovers, buffers, and caches.

  * Designing your UX to adapt to degradation is a great way of protecting your consumer experience.

  * Active design reviews and effective tooling are two great ways to prevent isolation issues in your organization.
