# [CHAPTER 3Anti-Patterns: Distributed Monoliths](contents.xhtml#ch03a)

There are no Big problems, there are just a lot of little problems.

— Henry Ford

“Finally, the business approved our refactoring plan. That massive hunk of C# is going into the dustbin! Acme Widget Company is going to Microservices! Our project has a great new name (“Project New Horizons”) and an aggressive, 12-month roadmap to overhaul and builds an entirely new application stack. The sky’s the limit!”

“Well, we had to make a few compromises, but 24 months later, here we are. The first services are in production! They are functioning well, and everything is great. We have a high backlog of new features, but that shouldn’t be a problem on our amazing, distributed system.”

“What do you mean, the Reports team broke the payments service with their schema change? Didn’t we use Liquibase?”, “Are you telling me that we have to upgrade 50 services because the Sales team changed the way sales tax is calculated in Brazil?”, “Look, this bug is critical… Why must we wait for the monthly release train to go out?”

Does any of this sound familiar? If so, then welcome to the distributed monolith.

[Structure](contents.xhtml#sc2_88a)

In this chapter, we will cover the following topics:

  * What is a distributed monolith?

    * Traits of distributed monoliths

  * How are distributed monoliths created?

    * Sharing databases across services

    * Shared persistence libraries

    * Shared contracts

  * Issues with distributed monoliths

    * Binary coupling

    * Temporal coupling

      * Push the complexity upwards

      * Embrace async with an Event-Driven Architecture

    * Vulnerabilities and security fixes

    * Testing

    * Management problems

    * Reasoning problems

  * Amplifiers

    * Team erosion

      * Software ownership

    * Anti-science mentality

    * Features and bugs

      * Sidebar: landmines

    * Bad environments

  * Types of distributed monoliths

    * Backend

    * BFF and frontend

    * Serverless

      * Complexity in serverless architectures

  * Backfire: worse again!

    * Architects as gatekeepers do not scale

    * New capability/feature

    * New table

    * EOL technology

  * Stop the bleeding

    * Consider going back to the monolith

  * Things to remember




[What is a distributed monolith?](contents.xhtml#sc2_89a)

Classical monoliths are hard to maintain and lack flexibility. The Distributed Monolith anti-pattern has all of these problems, plus all the problems that microservices have. The act of creating a distributed system means that your maintenance and flexibility problems are spread in hundreds of codebases and services, rather than just one; and now teams can easily be in denial, praising their new Microservices architecture, while the rate of change is worse than ever.

The essence of the distributed monolith anti-pattern is lack of isolation: where the services or components of a distributed system are deeply entangled and highly coupled, whether explicitly known or hidden. The degree of this coupling informs how deeply compromised you are by the anti-pattern. Think about it like a spectrum (Figure 3.1), with a distributed monolith on one end vs a properly engineered SOA or MSA, depending on the degree of that unnecessary coupling.

Figure 3.1: Distributed Monolith vs Proper Services (SOA, MSA) spectrum

Answering these 3 key questions can help diagnose where your team sits on that spectrum:

  * Can you deploy independently, or do you need all teams (aka, a release train)?

  * Can you safely upgrade any library or framework without breaking the world?

  * Can you change your data stores without consulting other teams?




If the answers to these questions are all, “no”, then you are likely in an advanced stage of your distributed monolith. For the readers of this book, we hope the truth is a more nuanced, “sometimes”. Whether you are suffering from the distributed monolith anti-pattern is not a yes or no question, it is more a set of degrees, a range on the spectrum in Figure 3.1. Where you sit describes the level of difficulty in applying a fix, but don’t lose hope, and don’t let the problem get worse.

[Traits of distributed monoliths](contents.xhtml#sc3_90a)

Figure 3.2: Traits of Distributed monoliths

Distributed Monoliths may appear distributed on the surface, but in practice have the worst traits of centralized architectures. (Figure 3.2) The three key traits of Distributed Monoliths are:

  * Lack of isolation: As reviewed in [Chapter 2](ch02.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Lack of Isolation, isolation is the key to software architectures; Distributed Monoliths break isolation, typically on the shared database, internal shared libraries, and stable contracts.

  * Lack of independence: While their services or microservices might appear to be independently deployable units, in practice, the high coupling means no component can move alone and requires teams to sync development activity frequently.

  * Distributed complexity: As distributed monoliths tend towards distribution they are much more operationally and organizationally complex, making it harder to reason about certain problems and incurring much higher overhead to solve them.




[How are distributed monoliths created?](contents.xhtml#sc2_91a)

Companies end up with Distributed Monoliths when they try to adopt the Microservices style of architecture but fail for one or multiple of the following reasons:

  * Narrowly focusing only on the code and not properly splitting the database.

  * Running out of money and cutting corners.

  * A failure to know or understand proper software architecture principles like isolation, stable contracts, and lean libraries.

  * Lack of architectural review or guidance (do’s and don’ts).

  * Cargo Cult software engineering; not all companies are MAANGs (Meta, Apple, Amazon, Netflix, and Google), and might not need to go all in on microservices.




Embracing the public cloud is good, Microservices architecture is great, and retiring old technology for new ones is great. All good intentions can lead to a Distributed Monolith (Figure 3.3). 

Figure 3.3: How we end up with Distributed Monoliths

The bigger your company is, the harder it gets to have symmetrical consistency across all teams. It’s almost a fact that things will go sideways; it’s common to have companies always re-writing systems and decommissioning systems from time to time.

Using a Microservices style of architecture can be great. Still, they must be used cautiously because it’s very easy to end up with a distributed monolith. As you see in Figure 3.3, we can end up with distributed monoliths in four possible ways. When you slide and dice your monolith, that task needs to be done with extreme caution and care; otherwise, you have a high chance of making things worse. Fixing Distributed monoliths is more complex and costs more money.

Now that we know what a Distributed monolith is, let’s understand how they are truly created.

[Sharing databases across services](contents.xhtml#sc3_92a)

Figure 3.4: Distributed Monolith #1 - Sharing Tables

In Figure 3.4, we can see 3 microservices. Each might have:

  * Separate code base on git

  * Separate CI/CD deployment pipelines (Jenkins)

  * Dedicated engineering teams

  * Dedicated charter, product owners, and roadmaps

  * Dedicated AWS EC2 servers




On the surface, these services look great. However, all this separation is not enough, and there is one big problem: the shared database. Having shared databases illustrates how a lack of isolation can create lots and lots of problems - such as:

  * Availability: If the shared DB is down, all three services will suffer.

  * Resource contention: The Report service can launch a massive query, draining DB resources and affecting all other services.

  * Schema evolution: The Sales service can introduce breaking changes to the schema/tables, disrupting all services. Any data changes need to be synchronized and require extra care.

  * Development coordination: All three services need to be in sync throughout development, high-band communication between the teams to avoid damaging each other.

  * Deployment independence: With any breaking change, you cannot deploy these services in isolation. You must bundle them together. Without breaking changes, you can deploy independently, but do you want to risk it? 

  * Breaking isolation: As we explained in [Chapter 2](ch02.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Lack of Isolation, we also break isolation by not isolating the database.




As you can see, the Microservices style of architecture should allow independence, but if you end up with a distributed monolith, you don’t get independence. You get synchronization.

Sharing databases is the most common way to break isolation and end up with a Distributed Monolith. Unfortunately, it is not the only way; there are other ways to fall into this tricky trap.

[Shared persistence libraries](contents.xhtml#sc3_93a)

Figure 3.5: Distributed monolith #2 - Sharing persistence libraries

Our second example is similar to example #1 with one key difference: the services are not accessing the shared DB directly anymore. (Figure 3.5) Instead, they all use the same shared persistence library with shared POJOs. At first glance, this looks like a good idea since we are not duplicating persistence code anymore; who doesn’t like reuse? If there are persistence bugs, we can address them all in one place. However, in the long run, shared persistence libraries can easily turn into a nightmare, summarized as:

  * Opinionated choices: The team that owns the library may have one preference, but what if the Reports service does not want to use Hibernate directly and prefers Spring Data or simply JDBC/SQL?

  * All or nothing migrations: Teams must coordinate, leading to long waterfall migrations and/or branch explosion. What happens when the Profile Service wants to migrate from Hibernate 4 to 5, but the Reports service does not have the bandwidth or wants to upgrade. A lot of headaches.

  * Sync smell: All three services need to use the same library version and ensure they are in close communication at all times, or they can break each other. Such tight synchronization is painful, and requires cross-team automation to be effective.

  * Fragility: Having shared libraries does not prevent availability issues; if the DB is down, all services are affected.

  * Breaking Isolation: The contract of the shared library will likely be DAO classes, Repository interfaces or other objects tightly coupled with the database and/or persistence frameworks. So we will not have stable contracts; another anti-pattern that breaks isolation.




Internal shared persistence libraries do not fix distributed monolith problems; they make them worse, or even cause the problem in the first place. Let’s take on our final example.

[Shared contracts](contents.xhtml#sc3_94a)

Figure 3.6: Distributed Monolith #3 - Sharing contracts

Some teams decide that sharing code is great, so why not share contract definitions too? (Figure 3.6) After all, contracts could refer to shared concepts; you don’t want one service calling one field firstName, with another team using fname instead. If the contract is coded in Java, then any contract Java JAR would have a dependency on a Base Contract library. Teams gain a centralized place to store common POJOs, and reduced code duplication through reuse.

Base Contract libraries can go bad easily. Imagine if the Base Contract library, which all contracts depend on, gains its own dependencies like Spring, Hibernate, Log4J, and others… now we have similar negative side effects to our previous example:

  * Opinionated choices: Just like in example 2, what if the Reports team does not want to use Spring and prefers Guice or Jakarta EE (the former Java EE) instead?

  * All-or-nothing migrations: Just like in example 2, what if the Base Contract team wants to upgrade to a new version of Hibernate Validators, but the Reports service cannot or will not? We need to migrate all of them at the same time.

  * Sync smells: Spring core is good, being backward compatible, but most libraries are not - Are there breaking changes in all contracts. Therefore service implementations will need to be using the same version of the libs.

  * Breaking Isolation: As we explained in [Chapter 2](ch02.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Lack of Isolation, we also break isolation by not isolating the contracts.




Contract code should be as naked as possible, ideally with zero frameworks and zero dependencies. Try to avoid exotic, language-specific data types, 3rd party open-source frameworks and libraries are fine in a service implementation, but they are a big NO in the service contract.

[Issues with distributed monoliths](contents.xhtml#sc2_95a)

The Distributed Monolith anti-pattern has many drawbacks and no benefits, hence it is an anti-pattern. Let’s understand a bit more what these issues are in detail.

[Binary coupling](contents.xhtml#sc3_96a)

We have discussed Binary Coupling throughout [Chapter 1](ch01.xhtml), What’s Wrong with Monoliths and Chapter 2, Anti-Patterns: Lack of Isolation. Binary coupling in distributed monoliths affects your ability to reason about your systems. Every microservice has its project and its own classpath, and can be used by different internal and external libraries with a wide variety of versions. Discovering who uses what library/version throughout the entire dependency graph can be a real pain point, and every forced library upgrade can lead to a wide variety of surprises at build or runtime. Such unpredictability makes planning difficult.

The more binary coupling in your service, the less flexibility you have to move forward and evolve. If your service has stable contracts, you have a safe way to refactor and fix binary coupling issues. With stable contracts, you can change many things without impacting other teams or the business, such as:

  * Freely change the underlying business logic.

  * Overhaul your entire persistence technology stack and data model.

  * Replace all the services, languages, and libraries you use.

  * Run technical experiments more freely, with easier rollback strategies.




[Temporal coupling](contents.xhtml#sc3_97a)

Temporal coupling31 describes when there are dependencies between one or more systems that involve time, often observed in synchronous programming. Temporal coupling is like any other form of coupling; in some scenarios, it’s difficult to get rid of it but important that you do because it affects availability.

Here is one example of temporal coupling between well-defined services.

Figure 3.7: Distributed Monoliths - Temporal Coupling

Imagine we have a simple travel booking app allowing you to find and purchase the perfect vacation or honeymoon. We have a mobile app written in React Native that hits a public API gateway in AWS, which in turn calls an Experience BFF layer written in NodeJs. The BFF layer passes requests on to Java services that have their own isolated databases. (Figure 3.7)

The overall app workflow is the following:

  1. BFF calls the Hotel Booking Service to book a hotel for the user.

  2. After booking the hotel, the Hotel Booking Service now calls the Plane Booking Service to purchase the plane tickets.

  3. After receiving the plane tickets, the Plane Booking Service calls the Rental Car Service for the additional car rental.




You can see many good things in this architecture, for instance:

  * All 3 services have isolated databases.

  * Dedicated contracts for each service (Stable Contracts)

  * Each service has its own codebase.




You might think we don’t have a distributed monolith here, but unfortunately, we do because the services have high temporal coupling. Ask yourself:

  * What happens if any service is down?

  * Do you see any fallback in place?

  * What if the user doesn’t want to Rent a car? Or only want a hotel room?




Do you really have three independent systems? Yes, in the sense of contracts, databases, and code bases, but in terms of maintenance, testing, and reliability, this is a problem.

So how can we improve this architecture?

[Push the complexity upwards](contents.xhtml#sc4_98a)

We can fix some problems (and create new ones) by pushing the orchestration to an upper layer, which could be either another service or simply the BFF - like in the example in Figure 3.8.

Figure 3.8: Distributed Monoliths - Pushing Complexity Up

The benefit of this solution is that now, the BFF can orchestrate the calls:

  * The BFF can cherry-pick which service it will call and even do it in any order.

  * The BFF can skip servers and even safely retry using exponential backoff32 and jitter33.




Since software architecture is about tradeoffs, this is not a free lunch, and we also have problems with this approach - here are the drawbacks:

  * We still have issues when the BFF is down.

  * Let’s say the Hotel Booking service calls start failing? How long will we retry? What if it takes 2h to be back online?

  * Most of the complexity now lives in the BFF layer.




Some of these problems can be remediated with better infrastructure and fallback techniques, for instance:

  * Make sure you have properly implemented failover on the client side; consider implementing a client-side load balancer.

  * Improve availability by having multiple instances of all services and BFFs; at least 3, one per Availability Zone (AZ).

  * You can use more than one region, allowing failover to a different region.




The main problem with these fixes is the cost will be higher. For some businesses at scale, that’s fine, but that might not be your company’s reality. Overall, this approach works well with the public cloud since we can use Auto Scaling Groups to provision more resources and handle more requests on demand instead of overprovisioning capacity.

[Embrace async with an Event-Driven Architecture](contents.xhtml#sc4_99a)

Now consider introducing an Event Bus, in the form of a classical messaging technology like JMS or RabbitMQ, or a more modern form of distributed log like Apache Kafka. Consider the solution in Figure 3.9.

Figure 3.9: Async Event-Driven Approach.

This solution might appear similar, but it’s greatly improved through the shift from synchronous calls to asynchronous calls. We do not have temporal coupling, and the system arrives at a final, fully booked state as each service processes its requests, also known as Event-driven architecture.

All services are producers and consumers of events from the Bus.

Let’s understand how this async, event-driven approach works, step by step:

  * Step zero: The Experience BFF emits three events: bookHotelEvent, bookPlaneEvent, and bookRentalCarEvent, sending them to the message queue.

  * Steps 1..3 Services: Each service is constantly looking for its corresponding booking event on the message queue. As each message arrives on the message queue, the service picks up the message, and executes its booking action.




The system works very differently than in the previous two examples. Services can pick up messages at any time and in any order. Before, the system was guaranteed to execute in a fixed, sequential order (Hotel, Plane, Rental Car). Now, the order is not guaranteed; the Rental Car Booking service could book first, renting the car before the plane tickets are purchased. If the Plane Booking service is down, the Rental Car Booking service is completely isolated and will still execute successfully.

The main benefits of this kind of architecture are

  * Anti-Fragility: Now, it does not matter if all the services are down. When they come up, messages will be processed and bookings completed.

  * Scalability: Scales well because we can add more instances of the message consumers, e.g. the services, and speed up processing.

  * We fixed temporal coupling: By moving away from synchronous HTTP calls and relying on asynchronous processing using a message bus, the system is no longer temporally coupled to each other.




So this approach has some drawbacks as well. First of all, the message bus cannot be down or lose messages, so it becomes critical in the reliability path. Secondly, the message bus is not really a database; some might argue that Kafka is a database, but it is not. You won’t get consistency for free. You will need to watch for the following:

  * Events arriving late or never processed: What if any service is down for multiple hours, and message processing is very delayed? What if a bug causes messages to expire? What if your system experiences resource exhaustion and messages overwhelm your queues?

  * Duplication: Not all messaging systems guarantee that messages will be processed only once; or an event may appear multiple times.

  * Ordering: If you do not have strict ordering, how does your system behave when messages are consumed in different orders? Can you tell what is the first event or the last?

  * UI/UX: In asynchronous systems, your UI no longer gets a simple call back when all transactions are processed. How will the UI and user experience behave? Will it update when messages are processed? How will the UI react if messages are never processed - do you need to tell your customers to call you if bookings don’t complete in 24h?




With enough care, it’s possible to overcome all these issues. For instance, ordering can be achieved in some systems, like Kafka by using the same topic and consumer group. Duplication can be handled by writing idempotent systems, able to handle duplicate message processing safely that are ready to handle duplication safely.

While async systems are more complex, the best solution to solve temporal coupling issues is to embrace async solutions. There is no free lunch.

[Vulnerabilities and security fixes](contents.xhtml#sc3_100a)

All companies are tech companies, and all tech companies must have an effective Information Security (InfoSec) strategy and process to stay safe and in business. Any decently sized or highly regulated company has dedicated InfoSec teams.

In some senses, InfoSec can be a good source and motivation to push for modernization:

  * Ensuring internal and external libraries are up to date at all times.

  * Protecting critical data (such as PII) at all times: at rest, at transit.

  * Demanding great observability to detect threats (logs, alerts, and metrics).




The side effect of it is: constant migrations. Migrations done right can be good. But the reality is that business teams will always push back on any work that interferes with their need to deliver more direct value. Security migrations deliver value: the brand damage a security breach can create is a huge risk, and could destroy your customer’s confidence in your product.

Due to the high coupling in Distributed Monoliths, migrations are always painful. Painful migrations means that complying with your InfoSec team’s needs is harder for everyone. Applying proper security principles in properly designed and architected services is much easier, as you can easily refactor their implementations without affecting consumers.

[Testing](contents.xhtml#sc3_101a)

Testing is a critical function in any software. Without testing, how can we know things are working as they should? Testing gets tricky and expensive in Distributed Monoliths because you cannot just test the services in isolation. High coupling means you cannot assume they will work together because they work in isolation. The result is that distributed monolith teams tend to test all services together. One side effect is that it can create a sense that end-to-end and integration tests are more important than unit tests and need to happen in higher volume.

Mike Chon, in his book Succeeding with Agile, introduced the concept of the Test Pyramid, a metaphor for thinking about testing in different layers, where each layer takes a steadily increasing amount of time and money. (Figure 3.10)

Referring to the Backend, we should always have more unit tests than E2E and integration tests. Unit tests are cheaper to create and maintain and can run much faster. By comparison, E2E and Integration tests are more complex, and require data and preparation. Because of that, they can be flaky, full of false positives and random unexpected failures.

In a distributed monolith, unit tests cannot give you all the confidence you need. Regardless, the solution is not to go all in on E2E and integration tests but to focus on and introduce other forms of testing such as Mutation, Property/Fuzzy, Configuration, Stress, Chaos, and Testing in production.

Figure 3.10: Test pyramid

Distributed Monoliths also create testing data problems; often you will need to insert data in several tables you don’t own in order to get the dependencies your specific test needs.

[Management problems](contents.xhtml#sc3_102a)

Like all Monoliths, Distributed Monoliths create lots of management headaches. Let’s start with the common perceptions:

  * Why do things take so long to be done?

  * Why do we have the same issues over and over again?

  * Why are developers so unhappy, and it is so hard to retain talent?

  * Why do we have so much drama around engineering features? Why do things need to be so complex?

  * Why do we keep investing money, time, and effort in software but feel like we are not getting much in return?




Distributed Monoliths do not allow teams to move faster; instead, teams need to do more coordination and end up moving slower. All software has “unknown unknowns”, unanticipated issues we discover down the road. But because of high coupling, distributed monoliths always encounter more.

Beyond these perceptions, the reality is likely the same: teams experience real problems in their day-to-day work which are amplified by the distributed monolith, just as with classical monoliths, but worse:

  * More bugs.

  * More coordination between teams.

  * More bad surprises and unexpected setbacks down the road.

  * More estimate buffering, with teams giving even more conservative estimates.

  * More compromises to technical design and feature set.

  * Less business value delivered overall.




We discussed the role of having good engineering practices to help deal with monoliths at the end of [Chapter 1](ch01.xhtml): What’s Wrong with Monoliths?: proper code review, full retrospectives, funding slack time, technical mentorship, and strong leadership. Slack time is one of the key items teams need more than ever with distributed monoliths, as they grant the free capacity to make improvements, refactoring, more testing, re-designs, and ensuring the same incident does not repeat. But, because the business is already under strain due to missed dates and less business value than expected, they do not invest consistently.

[Reasoning problems](contents.xhtml#sc3_103a)

Software requires reasoning. If you can’t make sense of software, you fall back into a trial-and-error approach; you become inefficient, and lack precision, and this hurts your lead time. Software should be approached scientifically. Reasoning allows us to establish cause and effect, and to be able to predict failure and impact.

Distributed Monoliths make reasoning very difficult, due to factors like:

  * Many moving parts: You have tens to hundreds of services that on the surface look independent, but in reality are one application. You now need to look through hundreds of separate code bases, and guess which one has the problem.

  * Lack of Isolation: Distributed Monoliths don’t have enough isolation in their databases, internally shared libraries, and contracts, resulting in side effects: bugs, instability, hiccups, and a bad time for engineers. The lack of isolation makes it difficult to pinpoint the root cause of any issue.

  * Who broke it? Or what broke it?: Many teams can change the shared database and internal shared libraries at any time, breaking each other’s code all day long. It could be your team’s code that introduced the bug or any other team. The blast radius of most changes becomes huge.

  * Lack of testing diversity: With flaky tests and not having important coverage in the right places, testing theories before they hit production and/or affect customers is very difficult.




To make sense of things, you need to have some degree of control. It’s hard to control hundreds of people, so at scale, the Distributed Monolith becomes very, very obscure, complex and full of anti-patterns just like the classical monolith but much worse because it is distributed.

The most significant effect created by the lack of proper reasoning is sluggish productivity and difficulty troubleshooting. You will spend weeks to months fighting problems. It’s bad for the business and also bad for engineering because it creates the perception that engineering is slow and doesn’t deliver. Little does the business know of the PAIN of dealing with distributed monoliths.

[Amplifiers](contents.xhtml#sc2_104a)

Distributed monoliths are also surrounded by social phenomena that amplify all the problems in distributed monoliths that are bad, causing them to become even worse and decay over time.

[Team erosion](contents.xhtml#sc3_105a)

Team erosion is an interesting effect that happens with software. Diego Pacheco, the author of this book, blogged about the team erosion effect in 2021 ([http://diego-pacheco.blogspot.com/2021/01/team-erosion.html](http://diego-pacheco.blogspot.com/2021/01/team-erosion.html)). Over time, engineers come and go from companies, teams are created and disassembled, and software is created and decommissioned. It’s normal to have pieces of software that are orphaned and do not have an owner or even a maintainer. Over time software teams suffer erosion for many reasons:

  * Business evolution: The world changes. Customers change. The world has multiple forces that drive the business to change direction. Like new generations, new technologies, legislation change, and many more.

  * Acquisitions: Your company might be acquiring and selling other companies from time to time, creating tensions and flipping priorities and ownership.

  * Startup pivots: If you are in a startup - keep pivoting ideas to find Product Market Fit (PMF).

  * Macro environment: Due to the macro environment: economy, war, pandemics, consumer reality, and politics, layoffs might happen and let the software be orphaned without the original creators.

  * Talent tensions: People might want different challenges. Engineers want to work in different fields or on different problems, leaving your company.

  * Hot tech markets: Silicon Valley and some European countries like UK, Germany, Netherlands might have better opportunities and make people and teams move to other companies.

  * Software lifecycle: Software gets old and becomes End of Life (EOL); the vendor that maintains it no longer exists or does not want to invest in it further. Expertise might be lost.

  * Re-orgs and leadership change: A change of leadership might move the company to go in a different direction.




There are plenty of reasons why team erosion might happen, and classical monoliths can suffer from team erosion too. However, Microservices and distributed monoliths suffer the most from team erosion. The more software you have, the more chances you will have ownership issues.

In any organization, team erosion has real and lasting effects on your ability to move quickly:

  * As you don’t have owners, if there are bugs or migrations - who will do it?

  * When you need to perform security patches and migrations - who will test it?

  * How can you properly estimate the impact of changes on something that does not have formal owners?

  * How do you prevent some piece of software that does not have an owner from getting worse in the sense of technical debt?

  * In the case of design changes, who says the design is right or wrong? Who keeps the integrity of the design and codebase?

  * In regards to on call and production support - who will do it?

  * You are a new employee or contractor and need to touch this piece of software - who do you ask for help? Who can teach you how it works?




Figure 3.11: Team erosion and shared libraries

The closer teams work to the business, the smaller the chance to have some team without a charter, but it is not impossible. As you get away from the UI, it gets harder for the business to understand and therefore prioritize. Of course, erosion happens even in business teams, but it tends to happen more with technical or platform services, which are services deep down the stack and more technical in nature, hidden dependencies that are harder to explain to the business.

The biggest problem with team erosion often is around shared libraries. Often companies don’t have a strict policy with libraries, it’s the opposite. Even companies with “owners” in all shared libraries do not necessarily imply they really know the library.

Team erosion creates tension between the existing business teams and core/platform teams. (Figure 3.11) It’s not uncommon that teams might push back about owning something they did not create and that might have technical debt. You don’t need perfect ownership, but it is better to have more things owned than none at all.

Who deals with the lack of ownership? How to handle team erosion?

[Software ownership](contents.xhtml#sc4_106a)

Everything should have owners. Ideally, the whole team created a service or library and owns that artifact. Unfortunately, this is not always possible due to team erosion and sometimes we must make difficult calls. Here are some criteria that can help you decide what team should own what service/library:

  * Domain affinity: Is this on the same business domain? Who or what team is closer to this domain?

  * Commit frequency: Who or what team has been doing most of the Pull Requests (PRs) over the last 12 months?

  * Available capacity: What team has more capacity to handle this work from now on?

  * Sympathy and care: What team most cares or might be willing to maintain the service/library in question? Ask your people; you might get volunteers.

  * Technology affinity: What team has a stack that is closer or the same as this service/library has?




Ownership is something that needs to be reviewed from time to time. It’s important to capture ownership information somewhere like a wiki page, or some metadata in a file in your git repository like CODEOWNERS in GitHub

([https://docs.github.com/en/repositories/managing-your-repositorys-settings-and-features/customizing-your-repository/about-code-owners](https://docs.github.com/en/repositories/managing-your-repositorys-settings-and-features/customizing-your-repository/about-code-owners)).

Team Erosion can also be a smell that you have too many services and/or shared libraries. If you can’t keep up with the number of software resources, maybe you can consider simplifying your libraries and even merging some of the services to simplify ownership and support.

[Anti-science mentality](contents.xhtml#sc3_107a)

Science is critical for troubleshooting complex problems. Management often has a lot of pressure to release and does not want to waste time, so all sorts of short cuts and workarounds come to play. Combined with bad environments this short term thinking makes problems get worse and worse over time. If you don’t fix problems at the source, you are just fooling yourself.

Logic, reason, and applying the scientific method are critical for troubleshooting complex problems. Clear reasoning is the only way to cut to the heart of a problem, and the faster that happens, the better the outcome for your customers. This approach applies to software engineering, but as we’ve illustrated, it’s even more necessary when dealing with Distributed Monoliths: their complexity makes it especially hard to reason about the consequences of your actions or safely test theories.

Unfortunately, as we have also just covered, management pressure ramps up in these environments: move quickly, just put a bandaid on it, just fix the symptom, without fixing the root cause. Lots of shortcuts and workarounds come into play. You can’t ignore science: if you don’t reason and fix problems at the source, you are just fooling yourself.

By lack of applied scientific reasoning, the problems of the distributed monolith get worse, because:

  * Tech debt flourishes: If you don’t push to properly fix problems and invest in solid refactoring and continuous improvement, just like with classical monoliths, anti-patterns will spread through the code via the Broken windows theory. When anti-patterns live throughout your code base, fear of change becomes the norm.

  * Leftovers: If the business doesn’t give teams the space to reason, understand, and fix the root cause, preferring shortcuts, migrations will start but often be “paused” or “stopped” at the first workaround. This makes distributed monoliths fertile land for landmines and even slower troubleshooting; the prolific workarounds create more complexity and debt.

  * Rework: Back to the reasoning problem: if you don’t understand what code is used by your app, you will migrate dead code, you will fix bugs in dead code, and you will be worried about a code path or workflow that might never run. You will do more work than you need to be safe.

  * Bad decisions: Management often will push for delivery and quick results, while not wanting to invest in refactoring, improvements, and modernization. At the same time, it will pay the bill for all waste of time, slow development, bugs, and incidents.




Over time, Distributed Monoliths become too big, complex, and insane that fixing them is very challenging. Addressing them properly needs scientific rigor, but proper reasoning can take time and can get expensive. Without funding, people give up, and reject the need for the scientific mentality, preferring to ignore their problems. At some point technical debt becomes your boss because it dictates everything you do. The bill is too high, there is too much software, so you keep making it worse.

The problems created by an anti-science mentality only worsen when you add new features and fix bugs.

[Features and bugs](contents.xhtml#sc3_108a)

No matter what software architectural style you have- a classical monolith, Microservices, or even an anti-pattern like the distributed monolith, you will need to deal with new features and fix bugs. If handled properly, you can stop the bleeding and avoid introducing more technical debt; or you can make things worse. The reality is that often this is not a binary outcome for your whole architecture, and is rather decided team by team, service by service, and shared library by shared library.

Distributed monoliths make dealing with new features and bugs extra challenging through their issues by making it harder to reason about features and bugs properly. Let’s illustrate this with an example: let’s say you have two different services using the same table in a shared database (Figure 3.12). The Sales Service is facing slowness; now, the problem could be with the service itself, but also with the other Profile Service since they share the same database.

Figure 3.12: Bugs in Distributed Monoliths

Because we don’t have a properly isolated service, we cannot rule out the idea that the Profile service could create a problem in the Sales service. This is a clear negative side effect of the lack of isolation that Distributed Monoliths have. The Profile service could be impacting the Sales service in a variety of ways:

  * The Profile service uses more CPU than expected, and there is a high load average on the database CPU.

  * The Profile service starts inserting a huge volume of unnecessary data due to a bug.

  * The Profile team accidentally dropped a table index during a recent database change.

  * The Profile team launched a stress/load test exercise without communicating with the Sales team.

  * The Profile team launched a new A/B test which led to an increase in service requests, consuming more shared resources.

  * There is a “Black Swan” event going on like Black Friday or Cyber Monday, and the entire application is swamped with new sales.




This small service-to-service coupling, created by sharing a database table, made troubleshooting and debugging difficult as now you have a distributed problem. Now, imagine this at scale, easily with hundreds of services having unique combinatorial side effects. Perhaps the Profile team had performance problems in the past, and the Sales team immediately points to them and jumps to a false conclusion. Suddenly you have tension between teams. Now you have both difficulty reasoning AND people problems.

Fixing a bug in this scenario can lead to more complexity and technical debt. Imagine that the slowness in Sales was actually some poor mapping in Hibernate/JPA entities, and similarly, the Profile team investigates and reports the same slowness. The Profile and Sales services share a database, but not a codebase. It is not much of a leap for both teams to decide to create a shared library, move the common code there, and fix it in one place. Review our commentary on Shared Persistence Libraries earlier in this chapter; good intentions are exactly how the problem starts.

Figure 3.13: Features in Distributed Monoliths

Features have the same effect: imagine that the Profile team decides to introduce an audit logging feature using JPA, saving logs to a new table in the shared database. The Sales team likes this feature and wants to use it. (Figure 3.13)

Now, as this audit logging feature performs persistence, it uses frameworks and libraries with their own dependencies, and we have binary coupling. As an owner of the library, the Profile team decided to replace JPA with Spring Data. We could now have tension between teams:

  * The Sales team uses an old version of Spring data, while the Profile team wants to introduce a newer one. Who will downgrade or upgrade? Who will refactor? The owning team likely makes the call, which can create resentment.

  * Management might need to step in and decide what will happen.

  * Time is spent reading code, analyzing, debating, meetings, and further back and forth.




The temptations in the distributed monolith led the teams to introduce a bloated, internally shared library. The approach is not lean, as it uses heavy frameworks and libraries, and the result is more coordination, more difficulty reasoning, less flexibility, team tension, and distraction from the delivery of real business value. For what? A couple of Java objects and a generic DAO to do auditing - is that worth it? The answer is no.

The root cause is that in a distributed monolith, teams often just consider the benefits, such as:

  * Someone else built this, so less work for my team. Let’s reuse it.

  * More free time for our team, since it is already finished.

  * My team can focus on something else, or deliver more business value.

  * The library owners will fix any bugs we find - less work for us to maintain.

  * The library owners will add features for us if we need something.




That might all be true. What teams often do not think about is the drawbacks, and they should be; everything has drawbacks, and in this case:

  * Binary coupling; are there 3rd party dependencies that could create issues during migrations?

  * What if the library owner does not want the changes we need, does not prioritize our features, or never has any capacity for bug fixing?

  * What if they always think the bugs are on our side and ask us to investigate?

  * How much management coordination will this require?

  * Is this library truly stable and well written?

  * How much do we really need this feature? Could we do something simple?

  * The library owners decide what happens to the library, so how much flexibility are we losing? What are the compromises?

  * How much will we depend on the library owned by another team? What’s the benefit? Is it worth it?




Teams have good intentions, but distributed monoliths eat good intentions and create coupling and negative effects. Putting hundreds of services and libraries in this picture becomes chaos. The scenarios we’ve illustrated here are common, and happen every day in our industry; they happen because people take reuse for granted and don’t examine the potential drawbacks. We will discuss the drawbacks of reuse in detail in [Chapter 4](ch04.xhtml), Anti-Pattern: Internal Shared Libraries; for now, remember that blind reuse is never more risky than in a distributed, highly coupled system like a distributed monolith.

[Sidebar: Landmines](contents.xhtml#sc4_109a)

Figure 3.14: Landmines - a repetitive form of the technical debt problem that keeps happening.

A landmine is a trap, it can be an issue or a bug that will make an engineer or group of engineers spend days and hours debugging and trying to figure out the problem. (Figure 3.14) What makes a landmine worse is that often someone already knew about the problem and the solution, but didn’t document it; this turns into a form of technical debt. Landmines are often clustered together in a distributed monolith, and they often happen again and again, whether the monolith is managed by the original team or managed by a new team of engineers. Good engineers build a map of their known landmines in their heads over time; great engineers share this map with others.

How can we better deal with landmines?

The best thing is to share knowledge: on an internal wiki page, or some internal technical presentation. Even better, write a test case that exposes the issue, and starts failing if someone triggers the landmine. 

As we have explained, testing in distributed monoliths is hard because the system is unnecessarily coupled. When you have a shared library, you may be able to test the library in a centralized, isolated fashion. In other scenarios, creating a proper, isolated test case can be expensive, but even so, we would argue it should be pursued to help fully resolve a landmine or at least post obvious warning signs.

As you have the same bugs constantly occurring many times over and over across many different teams, it can be tempting to create more shared internal libraries to solve the problem once and for all. Libraries might seem like a natural solution here, but we would argue that you should approach them with a lot of caution. Landmines are a smell that might mean you have too much software and need some consolidation: having fewer services, fewer libraries, and fewer pieces of software to maintain.

[Bad environments](contents.xhtml#sc3_110a)

Having bad software infrastructure is an amplifying effect on distributed monoliths.

Can you run your distributed monolith on your machine? No; maybe it is too large, or perhaps because of the database and lack of database automation. So, you have to rely on an environment shared by many teams, rather than an isolated one. If you are running on the cloud, you might be able to create isolated environments if you solve the database automation problem; but if you are on premises, with limited resources and hundreds of developers, you are in trouble.

Environments need to be isolated too. When you have more developers than machines, things tend to get wild. Sure, there is the argument that today, you should not run all the infrastructure on your machine because it is a poor substitute for your real cloud environment. Sure, we can test in non-production, but back to our key point: you have a bad environment. It doesn’t work for your organization. The best solution is likely to test in production, a great approach that we will cover in [Chapter 7](ch07.xhtml), Proper Service Testing.

So what makes the environment bad? Is it just because it is shared? No; typically the servers are not the worst sin, but the database is. If a team deletes data, that affects everyone using the database; if they alter a table, it might impact others doing tests and exploration. And if one engineer manages to tear down the database, it’s down for everybody.

Having bad environments is dangerous, because it will create other very bad and aggravating effects such as:

  * Bad duplication: To be more independent, escape migrations, and improve resiliency, people will start duplicating business and persistence code to isolate their specific service in the bad environment. Unfortunately, this backfires pretty badly.

  * No continuous integration: Software should be integrated all the time. Due to the instability that bad environments create with distributed monoliths, people will do all they can to be isolated and stable and commit code early and often. This results in branch explosion, and teams holding back everything in feature branches. And you only get feedback getting close to prod. This is bad because it delays feedback cycles, and makes the whole engineering process expensive and inefficient.

  * Waste of time: Because the environment is unpredictable and unreliable, you waste time chasing ghosts thinking they are real bugs. In reality, this is the result of a lack of synchronization in internal shared libraries, database objects and undocumented landmines that you only solve after days or weeks. This hurts lead time and engineering morale.




[Types of distributed monoliths](contents.xhtml#sc2_111a)

So far in this chapter, we have focused on how distributed monoliths affect backend services. Unfortunately, there is not only one kind of distributed monolith anti-pattern. Distributed monoliths can be created within many layers and components.

[Backend](contents.xhtml#sc3_112a)

A tangle of 100s of microservices, all sharing libraries, and databases, and plagued with unstable contracts. By this point in the book, we hope the pattern is recognizable to you.

[BFF and frontend](contents.xhtml#sc3_113a)

The BFF Pattern is subject to the distributed monolith, which can happen due to internal shared libraries in JavaScript/TypeScript, or because teams often push complexity upwards, through temporal coupling with services.

Figure 3.15: BFF Classical Monolith

Let’s take the example in Figure 3.15; as you can see, our BFF is big and acts as a Single Point of Failure (SPOF). While the availability issue can be fixed by having 3 instances across 3 different AZs, the single BFF naturally is a bottleneck for the multiple engineering teams supporting this monolithic BFF. It might not be fun for the teams. So the natural thinking is to split this into multiple BFFs, maybe 1 or more per domain, right?

Figure 3.16: BFF Distributed Monolith

With multiple apps, so long as we solved the availability issue, we have removed the SPOF. Developers are happier now, need to do fewer merges, and have more flexibility… except for that little box called “Internal Shared Libs” inside each BFF. (Figure 3.16)

Like Maven’s use of a pom.xml, NodeJS has dependencies that can be specified in a package.json file. Imagine this; we have some NodeJS packages that have Express Framework as a dependency. Even with 3 different code bases for the BFFs and having decent availability, we have binary coupling. We can’t upgrade things in isolation anymore. Delivering a new version of an internal shared library is easy, but migrating all your 100+ BFFs might not be. Congratulations: you now have a distributed monolith on the BFF layer.

Unfortunately, the same anti-pattern can also be applied to the frontend, even if your team is using the Micro-frontends style of architecture. The frontend uses React from Facebook, with multiple micro frontends and separate code bases. What do you do with React? Let’s say you use the same version of React in all UIs/pages/components. Now, if one page wants to change the version, you need to change all pages. Now let’s go to the opposite trade-off: you allow different versions of React for each page. What happens with the internal shared libraries now? Would you have multiple versions of the library for each react version? Plus, each page is loading its own react version; this is not nice for the user experience and first-page loading times. We have internal shared libraries and the risk of having a distributed monolith.

[Serverless](contents.xhtml#sc3_114a)

Figure 3.17: Monoliths vs Serverless Spectrum

By comparison with Microservices, the Serverless/Functions as a Service (FaaS) style of architecture tends towards having even smaller services and components. (Figure 3.18) Think back to the Centralization vs Distribution concept we introduced in [Chapter 1](ch01.xhtml), What’s Wrong with Monoliths; Serverless is at the most extreme end of Distribution, where single functions can be your deployment unit. (Figure 3.17)

Serverless can be a nice approach, having these characteristics:

  * Abstract language runtime (but you still need to handle libraries)

  * Event driven

  * On-demand

  * Pay-as-you-go

  * Ephemeral




Figure 3.18: Serverless (FaaS)

Yes, most of these issues are common between Microservices and Serverless, but because of the possibility of extreme distribution you need to approach this style of architecture with caution since it’s easier to lose control and create a distributed monolith by falling into the same traps:

  * Lack of database isolation

  * Lack of lean internal shared libraries aka Binary Coupling

  * Lack of stable contracts

  * Temporal coupling




Figure 3.19: Serverless (FaaS) - Distributed Monolith

To illustrate this, in Figure 3.19 we have an example Serverless architecture, where functions are executed using a great, ephemeral, abstract language runtime. But under the hood, we have the sales and report cross-domain functions using the same shared database, and the risk of the distributed monolith. For convenience, all the functions are using the same internal shared libraries. Imagine some libraries have shared business logic and shared persistence code via Hibernate and Spring Boot; this is binary coupling.

[Complexity in serverless architectures](contents.xhtml#sc4_115a)

Figure 3.20: Complexity Tradeoffs with Serverless

Using Serverless, your application’s need for orchestration doesn’t go anywhere. As we explained in [Chapter 2](ch02.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Lack of Isolation, complexity needs to live somewhere, and often you end up pushing everything outside of the service, either upwards or downwards. Imagine you have a standard service stack, and want to convert it into Serverless functions (Figure 3.20). The service was responsible for ensuring 3 methods executed in sequence. You can push the complexity upwards, and have the BFF layer orchestrate calling the 3 individual functions; or downwards, having the 3 functions execute asynchronously, coordinated by a message bus, shared state in a database (please don’t!), or leveraging a shared function as a coordinator. 

In these scenarios, the key question becomes: who is orchestrating the execution of these functions? We have to push the complexity somewhere, and it is likely to yet another function, or a completely different layer. Serverless’ extreme push to distribution is about moving teams from a service unit to a function unit, but not stopping there. AWS Step Functions offers you a comprehensive orchestration layer that has basic constructs like: ifs, for, switches, filters and mappers. What is the result? Now, you are highly coupled with the cloud, and you have thrown away having a proper programming language; at least the BFF layer’s NodeJS was real code. We saw this mistake on the past, it was called BPEL34; today it is called AWS Step Functions35.

When your architecture mandates having a state across multiple functions, Serverless forces you to have an external state; therefore, you can easily use shared databases. With the explosion of individual “services” or functions that need to be chained together, Serverless can easily lead to Temporal Coupling. And of course, Serverless still can suffer from binary coupling because you still use all your internal shared libraries with Serverless. Yes, we are looking at a completely different technology architecture, when comparing Serverless with AWS EC2 or even containers like Docker, yet we see exactly the same problems, the same drawbacks and pain points, and additionally, some unique ones as well.

Is Serverless evil? No. Serverless is great. But Serverless functions are not a silver bullet, they do not fix all your problems, and Serverless is far from being immune to distributed monoliths. Distributed monoliths can happen in any language and on any distributed technology platform. Distributed monoliths are an anti-pattern and a design/architecture problem, not a technology problem. Cherry-pick the use cases where you will use Serverless, and avoid considering it as the go-to solution for all your service architectures.

[Backfire: worse again!](contents.xhtml#sc2_116a)

Nothing is so bad that it cannot get worse. People try to “fix” the distributed monolith using the wrong solutions, and guess what happens? The problem gets bigger. And worse yet, doing nothing also makes the problem bigger!

Engineers and Managers often want to do something and improve. Teams might have some opportunistic opportunities to improve, from time to time, such as:

  * We need to build a new capability/feature.

  * We will need to create a new table.

  * There is a technology which is nearing End of Life (EOL), and we need to introduce a new one.




Within reason, and a lot of analysis and thinking, it’s possible that this opportunity turns out to be good; the teams deliver value to the business and end up with better software. However, Let’s elaborate more on how this can actually backfire and create more problems than solutions.

[Architects as gatekeepers do not scale](contents.xhtml#sc3_117a)

One way to deal with this problem is to have solution/software architects as quality and design gatekeepers. While this solution might work on a small/mid-size company, but in a large organization, it’s not possible to inspect and intercept all problems up front. Gatekeeping everything behind individual or teams of architects does not scale and creates tension with engineers.

Having regular architectural/design reviews is a good and healthy practice. Review sessions might be able to spot design flaws before they become too big to fix.

We would argue the biggest gap is in education since companies assume teams know good design, good practices, and how to design great software architectures. Ensuring your engineers are educated with the right principles is far more important and scales much better since it is a naturally distributed approach.

Having stable contracts, few and lean internal shared libraries, and isolated databases is always a great start and has many benefits, as explained in this chapter.

[New capability/feature](contents.xhtml#sc3_118a)

Let’s move the new functionality to a new service. We get a new code base, a new deployment pipeline, and, more importantly, a fresh start away from the technical debt. The issue is that the capability/feature might need to interact with the rest of the code. While you might be able to isolate the new service’s code, can you isolate the database? Let’s say you need data from an existing table: what do you do? Just access the table from the old database. If the answer is yes, well, now we have the beginnings of a distributed monolith just by doing that (Figure 3.21). 

Figure 3.21: Backfire - New Capability/Feature

Alternatively, imagine that you manage to isolate further the new functionality. You don’t need old tables, and the new capability can be done completely in isolation. So far, so good! But you don’t have time to write everything from scratch, right? You have business value to deliver and OKRs to beat. So you reuse as much code as you can using internally shared libraries. Is this library lean, with few dependencies and no business logic? If not, congratulations, you are again walking the path to a distributed monolith. (Figure 3.22)

Figure 3.22: Backfire - New Capability/Feature - Internal Shared Libraries

Now imagine there is another service depending on your new service. Regardless of the reason, the new feature dies because the business moves away from it; now, you have a “Phantom” service that is not directly needed by the business but is still needed as an indirect dependency by something else. Where is the ownership?

Even with good intentions and real opportunities, we can easily make things worse. And when you have a scale with multiple teams, it’s easy to make things worse simultaneously.

[New table](contents.xhtml#sc3_119a)

Introducing a new table has the same risk, even if we are not talking about a new feature; it could enhance existing functionality. The same opportunity could lead to the same traps as described previously.

[EOL technology](contents.xhtml#sc3_120a)

From time to time, technologies die. It could be because they have no more maintainers, the company might go bankrupt, or it is no longer relevant, and people lose interest. Other times it’s just that there is so much trouble and pain that your company decided to move away from it; or maybe it is just too expensive. Now we have an opportunity for change and to introduce an exciting new technology. How can that backfire? Again, internal shared libraries. 

Figure 3.23: Backfire - EOL Technology

Previously, the classical monolith was using the same persistence framework everywhere, TopLink. Let’s say your team loses interest in the technology and decides to move to Hibernate. However, a shared internal persistence library was created using Hibernate to make the migration easier. Sure, this made the whole migration easier but as we hope you see now in Figure 3.23, you created a Distributed Monolith.

[Stop the bleeding](contents.xhtml#sc2_121a)

This chapter has been full of painful and difficult real world scenarios with distributed monoliths. You might wonder, how can we effectively stop the bleeding? 

Unfortunately, distributed monoliths are a reality, and we must learn how to handle them effectively. In several cases, it’s better to decommission this system. At scale, you cannot just rewrite all your software at once; it’s neither practical nor viable.

[Consider going back to the monolith](contents.xhtml#sc3_122a)

Distributed monoliths are much more difficult to fix than monoliths. With monoliths, at least the code base is in one place, and you can use your IDE to delete code and see the impact in seconds. When you have a distributed monolith, understanding the impact of changes and even applying refactoring is much more complex as you do not have all the code in your IDE, and certainly can’t run hundreds of services.

While we will cover this subject in great depth and color in chapters: [Chapter 5](ch05.xhtml), Assessments, [Chapter 6](ch06.xhtml), Principles of Proper Services and [Chapter 7](ch07.xhtml), Proper Service Testing, but for now, we will leave you with the following advice.

[Team and process advice](contents.xhtml#sc4_123a)

  * Improve your understanding: Understand your software designs, research, debug and test to improve your knowledge.

  * Read the code: Read it 100 times, analyze the dependencies, and think a lot.

  * Write documentation: On wiki pages, or even record videos explaining your findings. Create a FAQ with common problems and solutions.

  * Share: Establish routines inside and outside your team, using practices like weekly lightning talks, lunch and learns, and internal engineering meetings, and establish communities of practice, guilds, and chapters.

  * Socialize: Talk with other teams, and see where and how is the best way to fix your problem. Create a Slack channel to post your findings and documentation. Don’t suffer alone, other teams might tell stories of past landmines and how to approach your problem.

  * Include your contractors: Train them, mentor them, and invest in them because, after all, they are also creating features and fixing bugs.

  * Plan carefully: Before you start, think how much time, energy, and money your team can actually invest. Could you lose traction in a couple of sprints and create leftovers?

  * Quick wins: Consider delivering small improvements before embracing new libraries and services.

  * Mentor, lead and empower: For engineering managers and directors, it is important to empower teams and drive away fear, let engineers hear that they can and should improve code and design.




[Technology and software architecture advice](contents.xhtml#sc4_124a)

  * Always apply scientific thinking: Clear logic and reasoning are the only way to solve complex problems, and distributed monoliths have the most complex, difficult problems to solve.

  * Master your tools: Bugs can happen because people don’t know how to use the tools they have. i.e. you are slow at creating Hibernate/JPA mappings? Get an online course on mapping or get some FTE expert to train your team. Invest in training.

  * Be suspicious: Do we really need this piece of code - is this feature needed? Don’t take things for granted. Could we make it simple? Is there a way to reduce the complexity here?

  * Be cautious: If in the end, you are not sure, keep things as they are.

  * Truly isolated: Consider going back and re-consolidating your technology in a monolith architecture first. Avoid cross-domain dependencies as much as possible.

  * Consider backfires: Always think through the possibility your new architectures and designs will backfire, and remember the anti-patterns in this chapter.

  * Create lean libraries: Avoid internal shared big libraries and create new internal shared libraries with caution.

  * Don’t create more services: Consider adding code to your existing services. Avoid cross-domain dependencies as much as possible.

  * There is no standard: Engineers often call technical debt and anti-patterns “the standard” of the project. Do not assume that what exists is right; challenge existing ideas and solutions.

  * Write more tests: Tests are great to give us confidence and enable refactoring, but they are also great forms of documentation.

  * POCs: Test your theories and experiments with technology through Proof of Concepts (POCs), not in real services.




[Things to remember](contents.xhtml#sc2_125a)

Congratulations; we have reached the end of [Chapter 3](ch03.xhtml): Anti-Patterns: Distributed Monoliths!

Let’s summarize your learnings:

  * The essence of the distributed monolith anti-pattern is lack of isolation.

  * Distributed monoliths are not a technology problem, but a software design and architecture problem, and ultimately an anti-pattern.

  * Distributed Monoliths can happen in any language and any technology.

  * The 3 key traits of distributed monoliths are:

    * Lack of Isolation

    * Lack of Independence

    * Distributed Complexity

  * Some key causes of Distributed Monoliths include:

    * Sharing databases across services. Don’t share databases.

    * Shared persistence libraries

    * Shared contract libraries. Make contracts as naked as possible.

  * It’s easy to end up with a distributed monolith.

  * Sticking to the right principles like stable contracts, isolating databases, and having few, lean internal shared libraries can still allow you to accomplish a lot.

  * Binary Coupling is a significant problem for monoliths.

    * Unnecessary reuse might lead to binary coupling. Don’t take reuse for granted, it can lead to limitations and potential problems such as coupling, could break isolation, might kill the independence property you want in Proper Services and Microservices. You always need to think about the tradeoffs.

    * Choose your libraries carefully. In all scenarios, be careful with internal shared libs.

  * Temporal Coupling can lead to distributed monoliths.

    * To handle temporal coupling, consider pushing the complexity up to an orchestrator or BFF layer and rely on the cloud for elasticity.

    * A better approach to avoid temporal coupling is to switch to async processing with an event-driven architecture using Kafka, for instance.

  * E2E and integration tests cannot be the only way you test distributed monoliths. Introduce other forms of testing that might be cheaper but execute faster and still cover the monolith.

  * Management problems are very common with distributed monoliths, such as needing more coordination, more buffers in estimates, more compromises, more surprises, and less time for business value.

  * Reasoning about the problems with distributed monoliths, and their side effects, is very difficult given their complexity.

  * An anti-science mentality can have real impacts on distributed monoliths. Failing to understand the problem, delivering shortcuts and workarounds under the pressure from the business to deliver will destroy productivity over time. Always try to solve the real problem.

  * Software and team erosion are real phenomena.

    * Explosions in the number of services in distributed monoliths can lead to team erosion.

    * Maintaining software is very difficult with team erosion. Owners are necessary.

    * Shared libraries and services deep down the stack experience team erosion more frequently, but it can happen to both core/platform and business services.

    * Software ownership is one key to coping with team erosion issues.

    * Ownership criteria includes domain affinity, commit frequency, available capacity, sympathy and care, and technology affinity.

  * Features and bugs could be sources of issues between teams and add to your distributed monolith’s problems.

  * Landmines are difficult, persistent, and unfixed problems that can be encountered in distributed monoliths. Identify and share your understanding of your landmines with others.

  * Bad environments exacerbate the problem with distributed monoliths.

  * Types of distributed monoliths go beyond just backend layers, and they can be created in BFFs, Frontends, and Serverless stacks as well.

  * Architects as Gatekeepers might work in small, mid-size companies but that approach does not scale.

  * Backfires happen when you are trying to fix distributed monoliths, even if you have good intentions, be careful. Watch for backfires when creating new capabilities, features, tables, and technology EOL.

  * In order to stop the bleeding:

    * Make sure your team understands, documents, and shares their insights into the distributed monolith.

    * Empower your team to make key fixes and deliver key wins. Plan carefully, and always apply scientific thinking.

    * You can always stay where you are if you are unsure, not creating more services or even selectively creating classical or (ideally) modular monoliths.

    * Always aim to truly isolate technologies.

# [CHAPTER 4Anti-Patterns: Internal Shared Libraries](contents.xhtml#ch04a)

Don’t let your happiness depend on something you may lose.

— C.S Lewis

Libraries are in everything we do in modern software engineering. However, some of the problems we have are completely related to libraries.

This chapter will cover libraries from all sides: good, bad, and ugly. We will explain in depth and color the anti-patterns in libraries and how to avoid these pitfalls in your software. You will learn when to create libraries and when not to create them, as well as alternative approaches such as services and sidecars. Lastly, with a full understanding of the problems, we will leave you with key tips on proper library design, so you can address the negatives by following good principles.

[Structure](contents.xhtml#sc2_126a)

In this chapter, we will cover the following topics:

  * What is a library?

  * Types of libraries

    * Sidebar: libraries versus frameworks

  * Issues with libraries

    * Constant migrations

    * Lack of stable contracts

    * Binary coupling / breaking isolation

    * Incentives for library creation

    * The Framework Anti-Pattern

  * In defense of libraries

    * Performance

    * Reliability path

    * Language idiomatic

    * Code centralization

    * Avoid reinventing the wheel

  * Pitfalls - bad practices to avoid

    * Internal shared libraries

    * Short blanket effect

    * Big frameworks

    * Bloated libraries

    * Highly popular libraries

    * Libraries shipping configurations

    * Team erosion in shared libraries

      * Service drivers

    * Utils

    * Wrappers

      * Extension

      * New abstractions

    * Lack of governance

  * Better options

    * When should we create a library?

    * When should we NOT create a library?

    * Leverage services

      * No binary coupling

      * Much improved flexibility

      * Easier migrations or no migrations at all

      * Critical reliability path

      * In some cases, performance

    * Sidecar pattern

      * Modern sidecars

      * Sidecars and proxies

      * Sidecars and Kubernetes

      * How can you build a Sidecar? Types of Sidecars

      * Sidebar: service meshes

    * Simple alternatives

      * Leverage existing SDKs and libraries

      * Do it yourself

      * Copy and paste the code

      * Contribute to open source

      * Proper library design

    * Duplication vs re-use

      * Bad duplication: business code

      * Bad duplication: compliance

      * Good duplication: migrations

      * Good duplication: configuration and setup code

      * Reuse: the double edged sword

    * Proper dependency management

      * Cherry pick dependencies

      * Avoid company-wide parent POMs

      * Explicitly declare dependencies

    * Lean libraries

  * The spectrum of options

  * Things to remember




[What is a library?](contents.xhtml#sc2_127a)

According to Wikipedia, libraries are a collection of resources used by computer programs, often containing shared code, but they could include documentation, configuration data, images, or any other common asset36. Software libraries are not a new idea; they date back to the very first computers, discussed by Charles Babbage37 in his 1888 paper on the Analytical Engine. Libraries boost productivity and are the most common form of reuse. Code libraries can be linked statically: referenced at compile time, or dynamically: loaded at runtime.

A library can be a binary file that provides an API for consumers - in Java, this is often a JAR. Libraries can have many public APIs and many internal classes, methods, and packages. (Figure 4.1) Proper libraries should have a public explicit contract and a corresponding implementation, just like services. Languages have multiple ways to benefit from libraries. And not all libraries are binaries, in JavaScript or PHP, you directly share code and other assets, but not through a binary file.

Figure 4.1: What is a Library

[Types of libraries](contents.xhtml#sc2_128a)

For our book, let’s group libraries into two classes, depending on how they originate: internal and external (Figure 4.2). Pretty much all companies create internal libraries; some of them do it very well, or at least do it well for a while until their support fades into the sunset. As for external or 3rd party libraries, they come from many origins, like the commercial Intel DPC++ libraries38 optimized for high-performance computing or the many, many types of open-source libraries on the internet. 

Figure 4.2: Types of Libraries

Within companies, internal libraries are not always called libraries; sometimes, they are called by other names such as:

  * Core, like Core Platform

  * Base or Bootstrap

  * Common, like Common Stack or Common Libraries

  * Shared Layer or Shared Libraries

  * The “Framework”

  * The company’s “Standards” for software engineering




Internal libraries are often seen within companies as the bedrock that assures effective, scalable software development: purely good things, as they drive reuse, standardization, and a common pattern across hundreds of teams. All engineers love reuse! Engineers and managers are often terrified of having the same bug in 2 or 3 places, never mind hundreds or thousands of services, with no easy way to fix it. Engineers who want to be principal engineers or architects often see in libraries a path forward to grow in their technical career, to raise their profile and get noticed by the platform teams, so their career can be purely technical rather than slaving away at business requirements.

[Sidebar: Libraries versus frameworks](contents.xhtml#sc3_129a)

The main difference between libraries and frameworks is their relative size and shape. Often frameworks do more and are bigger, meaning more lines of code and more dependencies and more features for engineers. Libraries are often smaller, more direct, easier to use in isolation, and do not enforce a particular way of thinking, like what objects you create and how you create them. As the name implies, frameworks want to “frame” how you “work”; to influence how you think, and push you in a specific direction.

For this book, it does not matter if it is a library or framework; all the problems described with libraries in this chapter and the previous ones, like binary coupling and/or distributed monoliths, apply to both. The issues are the same. For ease of reading, you will just see us referencing everything as “libraries” or, more specifically, “internal shared libraries”, when we are talking about the main class of library problems discussed in this chapter.

[Issues with libraries](contents.xhtml#sc2_130a)

Libraries, and especially internal shared libraries, can be useful and help companies to be productive. However, they are not a free lunch, and come with drawbacks. Knowing the issues is important to avoid them.

Some of the issues presented in this section can apply to both external and internal libraries. Use this section as criteria for OSS library evaluation when your team is deciding which library to add in your projects.

[Constant migrations](contents.xhtml#sc3_131a)

Libraries require constant migrations. There could be a bug and you urgently need the fix, or the library has a new major version release, with new features that will benefit your business or deliver performance gains.

Of course, libraries also have vulnerabilities and need to be patched. Information security is important, and keeping up with CVEs can be very challenging. For instance, in the ACM article: “On the Discoverability of npm Vulnerabilities in Node.js Projects39, the authors analyzed 6,546 Node.js applications and discovered that 99.42% of the applications depend on undisclosed, vulnerable packages. Vulnerabilities are a good reason to keep your libraries up to date at all times.

[Lack of stable contracts](contents.xhtml#sc3_132a)

In Java, all libraries are present in your classpath, and engineers can easily access everything that is in the Java classpath through their IDE. However, engineers can easily make mistakes:

  * Use internal implementations: Directly use internal objects and functions that are not intended by the library’s authors to be shared.

  * Implement interfaces: Implement interfaces and/or extend classes that should not be allowed to be extended.




Java has some solutions we can use to reduce these mistakes. It’s possible to use final and package levels of visibility in classes to protect the internal implementation of libraries.
