Still, often, internal shared libraries are under less scrutiny than external packages and don’t apply these techniques and just declare all classes as public… engineers are free to use all library classes, including enums, annotations, POJOs, and everything. Inherently, libraries are more exposed than services by comparison.

As we will repeatedly point out, libraries often are taken for granted. When creating or using libraries, engineers often do not think about the risks and drawbacks of libraries, for instance:

  * Lack of ownership: What if there are no more maintainers? We covered the consequences of lacking software ownership in [Chapter 3](ch03.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Distributed Monoliths.

  * Change of direction: What if the owner takes a completely different path than the one you depend on right now?

  * Slow responsiveness: The maintainers could be slow, won’t take many PRs, or simply ignore you. Then what? Do you fork the code forever?

  * Bugs: What if the library initially looks great, but in practice, turns out to be full of bugs?

  * Huge dependency graph: What if the library has many dependencies, and only later do you realize it creates a lot of binary coupling?

  * Security vulnerabilities: What if the library is inherently insecure or full of security vulnerabilities?




As discussed in the previous chapters, companies often have product teams and platform engineers. Product teams might create internal libraries, but often they make poor maintainers because it’s not in their charters/OKRs; instead, they have pressure to constantly deliver business value. Platform engineers are the typical owners of internal libraries; because of the previous point, they are also forced to take over maintenance of abandoned libraries from product teams, and end up doing countless migrations over and over for the same libraries. Ownership is one issue, but it is compounded by binary coupling, forcing cascading waterfall migrations on everyone.

[Binary coupling / breaking isolation](contents.xhtml#sc3_133a)

To recap from our earlier chapters, Binary Coupling is where software has multiple, independent dependencies on a library, directly or indirectly. Without realizing it, your team can no longer easily upgrade your software’s library use, or even some libraries themselves, without breaking your entire application. Let’s say one of your dependencies now needs Hibernate 5, but your service overall uses Hibernate 4; you can’t proceed with that upgrade without the entire application going to Hibernate 5.

Binary coupling is one of the greatest, hidden problems of working with libraries, especially on aged projects like Monoliths. Over time, you accumulate all these mismatched elements, old libraries with old dependencies vs new libraries with new dependencies, and needing to deliver a single deployment unit just tangles everything together.

As we covered in [Chapter 1](ch01.xhtml), What’s Wrong with Monoliths? monoliths are plagued with insufficient testing and an overall slow development cycle; and in [Chapter 3](ch03.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Distributed Monoliths, we showed you how Distributed Monoliths can be even worse. Upgrades can be painful. Having internal shared libraries that didn’t pay attention to their dependencies and triggered binary coupling is one of the main reasons.

We argue that failing to isolate software properly is the number one pain point in modern software engineering, especially for monoliths. Binary coupling is one of the main ways libraries can cause software to break isolation, and even worse because it tends to lie dormant and hidden until teams realize they’re stuck in a deep swamp, with a massive, dangerous refactor being the only way out.

[Incentives for library creation](contents.xhtml#sc3_134a)

Companies build libraries for an array of reasons; we are not saying that all these reasons are valid, and we are not saying we agree, but the following is the reality of why it happens:

  * Avoid duplication: Avoid writing the same code in multiple places.

  * Resume driven development: A principal engineer or architect seeks career growth by creating a shared library or framework for the organization.

  * Future abstraction: Aiming for future reuse, which never comes - Anti-Pattern: Re-use by accident instead of by design - or because some developer is working in a different team and wants to save themselves time.

  * Ownership tension: Put the code in one place and hand the ownership off to another team, avoiding the maintenance burden. This phenomenon can happen a lot in open-source contributions.

  * Developer preferences: Some people love annotations or aspects, so they create libraries for those.

  * Performance obsession: We love performance too, but flexibility is always more important!

  * Lack of trust: From past experience; library wrappers can be a way to “shield” yourself from bad libraries imposed upon your team.




In most organizations, engineers have few barriers to creating libraries, and the rules around libraries can be very relaxed. On top of this, people feel pride in creating libraries and seeing other teams use them and can end up believing this is a big part of their job.

In Silicon Valley, and the software industry generally, there is a truism:

  * Junior Engineers create code, so their PRs are a lot of green or full of additions.

  * Senior/Principal Engineers delete code, so their PRs are lots of red or full of deletions.




Following this line of thought, great engineers not only delete code but solve problems by doing less: less code, fewer services, and no unnecessary libraries. Great engineers are great teachers and spread the right culture, which in this case, is being lean. Management often does not measure deleting code, instead they will track the delivery of new features or bug fixes, which most of the time is what happens. Sure, if your library is the path to more features, you are covered, but if not? Great organizations don’t just prioritize expansion and creation but have OKRs that cover deletion: decommissioning systems, and reducing the complexity of the overall org.

[The Framework Anti-Pattern](contents.xhtml#sc3_135a)

Companies create internal frameworks, no matter the industry or language. It may have positive and negative effects, but as per our observation, sometimes it is just done to feel important.

In Java, they are often referred to in the company in a shorthand, like, “The Framework”. Maybe “The Framework” uses Spring and Hibernate, and all your services are forced to use this core library.

The following Figure 4.3 shows the framework Anti-Pattern:

Figure 4.3: The Framework Anti-Pattern

The Framework in Java is a binary group of one or multiple JARs. Let’s say you have by the hundreds or thousands of Services / Microservices using The Framework. One of your teams says we want to use Spring 6.x. But wait - The Framework is using Spring 4.x. Can the team migrate in isolation?

No! The team will need to wait for The Framework to migrate first to get it, or release a new Spring 6.x fork. Not only is this coupling between teams, we have other major side effects:

  * Migration tensions: Product engineers need to deliver value to the business, creating tension which leads to prioritizing features over doing migrations.

  * Lack of people / bandwidth: Platform teams, Architects, and DevOps Engineers are often orders of magnitude smaller than product engineers, so they struggle to handle the massive workload of keeping up with migrations.

  * Priority challenges: Always competing priorities. Migrations might not be prioritized at all.

  * Ownership issues: If you have 10 teams using Spring 3, 10 teams using Spring 4, and 50 teams using Spring 5 - how many forks of The Framework will you maintain? Practically, internal maintainers do not like that and might avoid doing basic maintenance across so many versions.




Multiply this problem by the number of shared internal libraries your company has; you have a recipe for trouble. The Framework introduced the distributed monolith anti-pattern, which not only kills all benefits of microservices but also adds more technical debt and complexity. For what? What are the benefits? The Framework anti-pattern creates distributed monoliths but is also found in classical monoliths too.

The Framework often adds zero to no value. It’s a wall between developers and open-source frameworks. This anti-pattern often happens because managers and architects don’t trust engineers and believe they cannot fix this problem by themselves; therefore, they create libraries. Libraries backfire as a solution because of a lack of talent density and/or trust.

The Framework is an old anti-pattern, founded on the false promise of productivity. It all starts with internal shared libraries and internal frameworks, with the goal of creating a “Standard Approach” for all developers, making it easier and smoother for engineers to change teams. Ultimately, managers believe that having the same libraries and APIs everywhere is a faster way to get things done, as the code ends up similar most of the time, and problems and solutions are common. We don’t need to say often these ideas prove to be absurdly wrong over time, creating many issues like:

  * Productivity issues: Engineers can’t look up solutions on the internet: StackOverflow, Reddit, YouTube.

  * Issues with developer morale: Engineers don’t want to work on a proprietary solution that no one else uses. The Framework takes away visibility of their work, their ability to take pride in their work and credit for their code vs a public open source library on GitHub.

  * Low bar solutions: We have never seen an internal persistence framework better than JDBC or Hibernate; in fact, the opposite.




The funny part is that The Framework is an attempt to do something better but, by comparison with open source solutions, almost always does something worse. We already have enough great open source solutions out there, so No! Don’t reinvent the wheel, make direct use of open source frameworks and libraries like Spring and Hibernate. Spring and Hibernate are great solutions for their purpose, don’t put a wall between your developers and their frameworks.

Now you must be thinking. Got it! All the issues are because companies create their own frameworks; it’s just a cargo cult. Unfortunately not, other pitfalls might be triggered for a variety of reasons. We will cover these other problems with internal shared libraries in this chapter.

[In defense of libraries](contents.xhtml#sc2_136a)

Let’s be completely clear: libraries are incredibly important to modern development. Most software we interact with on a daily basis depends heavily on libraries, and would not function without them. Open source libraries are awesome - use them! Contribute to their GitHub repositories and write your own! Yes, you should be using open-source libraries like any company in the industry. Properly owned and managed libraries deliver us features that we don’t have to write improving our lead time to delivery, as well as help us ship bug fixes and security patches as needed.

[Performance](contents.xhtml#sc3_137a)

Some operations only make sense to execute locally for the sake of performance. Trying to execute some operations remotely would be absurd, you’d end up sending a lot of data back and forth. Libraries can save you tons of round trips. Services are remote by nature, unless you are doing some kubernetes or infrastructure tricks. A library is simply the best way to execute certain tasks where you want code reuse and/or high performance.

[Reliability path](contents.xhtml#sc3_138a)

By comparison with writing a service for everything that you do, libraries can simplify the reliability path of your software. When you have a service, the service needs to be up, it consumes resources, and needs more attention to SLO/SLA. Many more things can go wrong - a database could be down, consumers can exhaust the service’s resources, the service can become slow. Libraries have less of those risks. By embedding libraries they are in essence become part of your service, and can lead to less complexity and more reliability.

Of course, it’s not a free lunch - this is someone else’s software running in your code. The library still needs to be observable and resilient, otherwise, you’ve let a trojan horse into your codebase that could have dire effects.

[Language idiomatic](contents.xhtml#sc3_139a)

It’s simple enough to understand: engineers want to code in their native languages and in the way those languages function. After all, do you want to write Rust like you write Scala? No; you want to write idiomatic Rust, that is, in the way Rust works best. Engineers don’t want to learn new unnecessary frameworks, configuration files; they don’t want to write Terraform config scripts, they want a Java SDK that lets them write native Java, with Java idioms, to get the same thing done. Libraries can deliver that; services cannot. Libraries can proliferate as well just to match the idiom of the language, which can be both a good and bad thing. The Google Cloud SDK for Go40 lets engineers program in a language idiomatic way. Overall, these kinds of libraries are more like a Service Driver/Client, which we will cover later in this chapter.

[Code centralization](contents.xhtml#sc3_140a)

Using a library also guarantees a central point of execution. As we have explained, centralization is a powerful concept; sometimes, it is easier to fix problems in a centralized way rather than repeating yourself in several places. This trait in libraries is often a good thing for addressing bugs or introducing changes. However, it can be achieved using other techniques such as refactoring, Deep Modules, Agents, and other techniques. Libraries are one of the most simple ways to achieve this centralization.

[Avoid reinventing the wheel](contents.xhtml#sc3_141a)

In Dan McFunley’s article on “Choose Boring Technology”,41 Dan asks companies and teams to imagine that they each possess 3 innovation tokens. If you only had 3 tokens, would you invest them in rewriting SSH? Building your own database from scratch? If you are an infrastructure company, sure, but for most companies, the answer is simple: no. Your people, and their brilliant minds, are a limited resource, so spend them where it counts.

Libraries allow you to focus on your core business domain by reusing established solutions. There is no point in prioritizing resources to fix problems that the tech industry at large already fixed. Why create your own message bus? You can use ActiveMQ. Why create your own Java Persistence framework? There is Spring Data. Why invent your own testing framework if there is JUnit? It makes little sense.

Of course, all those ideas started somewhere. If as an individual, you have a great idea for a brand new approach, you should go forward with it, pursue it, and drive what you fall in love with. Just be conscious that you’re making a tradeoff of your time and attention.

To sum up: libraries are amazing and one of the core innovations that drives modern-day software engineering. The problem is not libraries per se but where they are used, how they are built, and who controls, manages, and maintains them (or doesn’t). To better understand this concept, let’s continue to analyze the common pitfalls of libraries, especially internal shared ones.

[Pitfalls: Bad practices to avoid](contents.xhtml#sc2_142a)

Libraries can backfire in many ways. You need to apply internal shared libraries correctly, otherwise, you will create or increase the size of your distributed monolith. Sometimes it is okay to create internal shared libraries, but even so, they need to be created with the right principles.

Here are some pitfalls to avoid with libraries.

[Internal shared libraries](contents.xhtml#sc3_143a)

Just creating an internal shared library is a risky action. To be clear, Open Source is great, and we are not against open-source libraries. But time and time again, we have seen internal shared libraries break isolation or even create a distributed monolith. So you need to consider if you really really need to create one.

Internal Shared Libraries are often big hidden problems. Every company has them, and most of the time, engineers are unhappy with them. Creating libraries is very easy; creating proper internal shared libraries is way harder than people think. Properly maintaining a library is even harder than that! Internal shared libraries can be created with good intentions in mind, but the results might not be.

Creating internal shared libraries correctly requires caution and proper reasoning. You have all the open-source libraries in the world at your disposal; why do you need to create more than five libraries for instance? Five is an arbitrary number; but hear us out. Ask yourself 3 times in the mirror: “Do I really need to build this internal shared library”. Take the same approach when a team wants to create 10 services at once.

[Short blanket effect](contents.xhtml#sc3_144a)

In Brazil, we have a metaphor called the “Cobertor Curto”, or the Short Blanket effect. (Figure 4.4) Which means when you try to fix one problem, you create another, you fix the new problem, and you get the previous problem back. 

Figure 4.4: “The Short Blanket” Effect  
Source: <https://www.freepik.com/icon/blanket_6629167> (blanket image)

In Java applications, we have names for such traps: JAR hell, classpath hell, or dependency hell. Your application has a broken dependency graph, libraries depending on dependencies with an internal conflict; you fix one library by declaring a fixed dependency, and it breaks something deeply in another library. This is the Short Blanket effect, but seen in software and caused by library dependencies. It is another way of describing binary coupling, and the most extreme Short Blanket effects are seen in distributed monoliths. The Short Blanket effect makes working with libraries really challenging.

[Big frameworks](contents.xhtml#sc3_145a)

Internal shared libraries should avoid big frameworks. When internal shared libraries depend on big frameworks, many consequences are imposed on library consumers, such as:

  * Opinionated choices: What if the library maintainer used Hibernate or Spring, and your team does not want to use it?

  * Big dependency graph: Big Frameworks tend to have a lot of 3rd party dependencies like Apache Commons, Guava, Jackson, and many other common open-source libraries and all their dependency graphs.

  * Bundle size: Size has the biggest effect on the front end; the bundle size impacts the first time/general load times of the application, directly impacting the user experience.

  * All or nothing migrations: You often need to migrate all the consumers or none. Services cannot migrate until the library owner themselves upgrades the library; they are gated.

  * Cold bootstrap: A real impact on Serverless environments, having more dependencies means taking more time to have the application up and running - a slow cold bootstrap can be a pain.




No, big frameworks are not necessarily bad. You can and should use big frameworks in your service; you can find documentation, official guides, and ad-hoc support via StackOverflow and other sites, plus it’s much easier to find talent. However, big frameworks being used in internal shared libraries is a completely different story, given the high risk of binary coupling.

[Bloated libraries](contents.xhtml#sc3_146a)

Libraries can be bloated in multiple ways. One way is feature bloat; imagine a library that does data persistence, configuration in JSON, thread pooling, and business domain-specific validations. Easily too much mixed functionality for one library!

The second form of bloat is in dependencies; libraries should have very few dependencies. Any library with many dependencies and a big dependency graph might be considered bloated. Often, libraries that are bloated in terms of dependencies are bloated in terms of features, as they have to address features in different domains. Beyond that, bloated libraries often have lots of unnecessary dependencies as well.

A comprehensive study of Java library dependencies, “Bloated Dependencies in The Maven Ecosystem” by Cesar Soto-Valero, Nicolas Harrand, Martin Monperrus and Benoit Baudy in 202042 concluded after analyzing 9,639 maven artifacts that 75.1% of dependencies are bloated.

[Highly popular libraries](contents.xhtml#sc3_147a)

Highly popular libraries are often victims of their success. It’s great to have battle-tested libraries that everybody uses at scale and are always maintained with useful features. However, highly popular libraries tend to be used by other libraries, creating difficult conflicts.

Sometimes upgrading popular libraries like Guava or Jackson can be pretty difficult. Take Jackson; it’s a great serialization library for JSON and XML. The AWS Java SDK uses Jackson, as does Spring Boot too. If your internal shared libraries also use it, and your services use it, it is not a stretch to see that upgrading it will be very difficult and trigger Short Blanket Effects. The advice here is the same as for Big Frameworks, it is fine to use in your services, but avoid them in your internal shared libraries.

[Libraries shipping configurations](contents.xhtml#sc3_148a)

Considering Spring Framework, let’s say you want to use dependency management and Inversion of Control (IoC). If you use Spring in libraries - be careful of the pitfall, Big Frameworks - it’s natural to think about shipping your Spring Beans configurations in libraries. Sounds like a good idea, right? Absolutely not.

Spring Beans are public, and as we explained in previous chapters, public objects are implicitly part of library contracts. Because of this, you need to maintain them alongside all your public APIs, as anyone could couple to them directly. Now suppose lots of libraries do this. You have a complicated situation because services do not own their configuration anymore: remove a Spring Bean, you break a service now.

Libraries can ship configuration if they are static and rarely or never change. If they change frequently, this is a bad idea. The same principle applies to services; however, services should own43 all their configurations. Configurations should be externalized44.

A better approach would be: do not use Spring in your internal shared libraries. Don’t ship spring beans as configs. Instead, use sensible defaults; by having sensible defaults in your code, you reduce library consumers’ usage complexity, and you can still allow the consumers to customize any behavior or parameters.

[Team erosion in shared libraries](contents.xhtml#sc3_149a)

In [Chapter 3](ch03.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Distributed Monoliths, we covered the social phenomenon of team erosion. Team erosion strongly affects internal shared libraries. (Figure 4.5)

For engineers, as we discussed in incentives for library creation, it is very easy to create an internal shared library in most organizations, and end up not maintaining the library. People are opinionated, and oftentimes they want to use specific approaches, libraries, and frameworks, and they might create an internal shared library with the best intentions.

However, libraries might be used only for the projects that a specific person worked on. It’s easy to find underground markets of internal shared libraries in companies since often there is no inventory, no charter, and no business case. Code needs to be maintained because bugs, migrations, and security patches need to be applied. Often, these libraries can get in the way, providing low to zero value and causing more problems than solutions.

It’s very common for internal shared libraries to lack owners. For this reason, internal shared libraries often don’t have decent testing coverage or comprehensive documentation for engineers performing migrations. You always discover (bad) surprises related to these libraries.

Figure 4.5: Team erosion in libraries

Because Team Erosion means internal shared libraries lack owners, it also implies common problems such as lack of testing or low/irregular pass rates, and more importantly absolute chaos when it comes to approving changes or advising on their potential impact: multiple migrations happening at the same time, teams joining migrations without consent and get broken by other teams, bugs, business changes. Again, team erosion is a big amplifier for the pain of internal shared libraries.

[Service drivers](contents.xhtml#sc4_150a)

A driver is software that provides an API to control and manage a low-level interface often associated with hardware45. 

Services can have drivers, also known as clients. Service clients can almost be considered a type of driver, as they manage and control the direct interface of the service for the software invoking it. Service drivers can be another source of problems, and we personally like a lot of them. However, we need to keep in mind that drivers are internal shared libraries, and they can have dependencies and, therefore, all the problems we have reviewed.

Consider the following example of two services, where the Hotel Booking service needs to call the Plane Booking service. Development would be easier if the Plane Booking service provided a driver. Otherwise, the team working on the Hotel Booking service and all other consumers would need to:

  * Create all Java POJOS for the operations they need to call.

  * Map all the REST endpoints alongside the right parameters and POJOS.

  * Perform serialization/deserialization of objects/JSON.

  * Perform any logging if necessary.

  * Apply basic validations for mandatory fields and basic type conversions.




Now imagine that we need to call the Plane Booking service from another 30 services. All the services will need to write the same code. So the rationale is to create a service client or driver (Figure 4.6) or share the contract objects, so people don’t need to define them over and over again.

Figure 4.6: Service Drivers/Clients

A driver/client can be great. Service owners should create drivers. However, drivers are libraries, and libraries need to be lean. A driver should pick dependencies very carefully because it is a source of binary coupling. Otherwise, drivers can suffer from all the problems mentioned in this chapter.

When the service contract changes, of course, the drivers also need to change. Otherwise, consumers might not get all the changes, or worse, be broken. If you use drivers, the side effect could be constant migrations for all consumers to keep them on the latest drivers.

Drivers are interesting because they provide another layer of indirection for service maintainers. It’s possible to implement a backward compatibility mechanism on the driver side. However, we recommend it always be performed on the service implementation side.

Look, for most services, you don’t need a client driver. If you commit to the work involved in calling this service, there’s no real benefit. But if you want to use a client driver, and build it lean, and have clear ownership and commitment to migrations: it can work well.

[Utils](contents.xhtml#sc3_151a)

Utils are libraries that literally do everything (Figure 4.7). Common code for Strings, Numbers, Dates, Error Handling, Validations, Exceptions, Logging, and several parts and functions specific to your company business. Utils also can have dependencies.

The concept of a Utils class is not itself bad and can be a good pattern for code reuse. Unfortunately, in most companies, we rarely see utils with good code and decent modularization. Utils have a huge contract surface area, and nearly all utils classes are part of the contract. Often you see business rules leaking out of the service itself and bleeding into the utils code. While utils exist everywhere, having internal shared libraries with utils can be a huge problem.

The better design is to have modular utility libraries. Still, often what we see in our industry is companies using a single monolith utils library, along with utils per company domain or even utils per service.

Figure 4.7: Utils Internal Shared Libraries

One of the many problems with utils libraries is that, by nature, they are shared across many developers and teams. Lack of ownership, and by nature, they tend to grow in all directions. Often utils libraries grow without oversight and planning, have many 3rd party dependencies to other libraries, and easily infect all your internal libraries and business services.

There are many great libraries on the internet. Do you need to create your own? But yes, it is possible to create a utils library properly - here is some practical advice:

  * Be focused: Make the utils class about one thing. Say it is Strings; stick to Strings, don’t mix other concerns like numbers or File validations.

  * Be small: Do not make big util JARs, otherwise, they tend to be bags of cats and risk having high coupling.

  * Stable contracts: Keep your contracts simple and stable. Make sure you don’t have too many flavors, hundreds of APIs. Ensure the granularity is right and your exposed methods are in fact truly generic.




[Wrappers](contents.xhtml#sc3_152a)

Wrappers are where software engineers decide that the best way to deliver a library or framework is to “wrap it” in their code, delivering new APIs or applying a company-specific new abstraction. Typically, wrappers work on top of external and often open-source libraries. Wrappers happen because engineers and architects feel the need to “isolate” a specific library from their business code. While wrappers can be delivered in more than a few ways, we often see two forms:

  * Extension: Extending some or all APIs from the target library.

  * New abstraction: Overriding the library’s APIs with your own under the guise of protecting your company or “standardizing” it.




[Extension](contents.xhtml#sc4_153a)

Extension is an implementation of the Adapter design pattern, where you are trying to create a new or custom behavior on top of a library’s implementation. While there are some use cases that this might be legitimate, there are clear drawbacks:

  * Overriding some or all of the APIs from the library you want to wrap means effort to deeply understand how the library works for your modification, as well as a battery of custom tests that ensure nothing is broken.

  * Ongoing maintenance of your wrapper is additional work. Every time the target library gets updated, you need to investigate and run tests, ensuring no changes in behavior or breaking API changes affect your wrapper. 

  * If you choose not to support all APIs, you remove features from the libraries other engineers may need. We have seen this repeatedly in the industry, sometimes done accidentally and other times a poor form of gatekeeping. Engineers don’t like it, and either find workarounds or just stop using the wrapper entirely. 




Figure 4.8: (A) Wrappers Extension

Suppose for some reason, your engineers decided to wrap Apache POI. Now, as a consumer you have at least 2 dependencies: the wrapper and the target library, but of course all the external dependencies of the wrapper and the target library itself (Figure 4.8). Your wrapper is custom made, and creating a wrapper for a new library needs to be custom made; this approach does not scale.

Wrappers can be a powerful technique if used with wisdom, evaluating proper tradeoffs; otherwise, it could be an expensive and complex way to add customization. Consider other techniques, such as the Composite Pattern, Java Agents, Network Proxies, Proxy Patterns like JDK Proxy and cglib, ASM, or simple code refactoring.

[New abstractions](contents.xhtml#sc4_154a)

Another way to implement a wrapper is to create a brand new interface/library contract, replacing all methods and delegating to the target library as needed. (Figure 4.9) The wrapper can be used to shield applications entirely from the library code for some purpose, perhaps to:

  * Avoid a bad legacy contract from proliferating, perhaps some legacy systems will die.

  * Abstract a poorly designed or difficult API while a new system is being built.




While there can be some good use cases, we have seen times when this pattern is applied to open source libraries and frameworks where it is not needed at all, such as Spring or Hibernate. It is pointless trying to abstract a common, popular library; the Java Community Process already provides the specifications for these frameworks, giving us portability. In these scenarios, the approach is really a way to limit engineers’ use of features, again a poor form of gatekeeping.

Figure 4.9: (B) Wrappers New Abstractions

If used properly, the wrapper pattern can be useful. However, you need to be careful not to use wrappers as gatekeeping, unnecessarily limiting excellent, powerful Open Source solutions.

[Lack of governance](contents.xhtml#sc3_155a)

What is the cause of all these issues with internal shared libraries? One significant reason is the lack of governance and visibility. Face facts: as your work becomes less visible to product and business, away from the UI, managers can care less and less. Like any technology asset, internal shared libraries need governance, which nowadays is just a less popular name for ownership.

Proper library governance means:

  * Clear ownership: You know who owns the library: an individual, a team, or even an organization.

  * Active maintainers: Internal shared libraries are actively maintained, applying security patches and being upgraded constantly.

  * Documentation: Wiki pages, diagrams, FAQs, and videos explaining common problems and solutions.

  * Rigorous stability: CI builds, test runs with a consistent 100% pass rate, decent coverage, and sufficient test diversity.

  * Observability: Your library must publish metrics, success and error counters, performance statistics, and access counters. Be consumer-obsessed: usage metrics are both useful for your consumers and for you, so you can know about problems before they arise. Metrics can help you understand what features are actively used in your library, plan for future migrations, and clean up/decommission old code.

  * Design integrity: Owners need to make sure the right design is preserved. Engineers and managers should not be able to do whatever they want. PRs should not see an instant LGTM.




Of course, often, what we see is the opposite of governance: pain and teams wrestling with bad internal shared libraries.

[Better options](contents.xhtml#sc2_156a)

As we saw, there are many pitfalls and bad practices related to library design. Now we will cover the considerations and better alternatives for how you can approach internal shared libraries.

[When should we create a library?](contents.xhtml#sc3_157a)

Library creation should be approached with caution. Remember, you will need to maintain it for its complete lifecycle. By nature, libraries are limited to their languages. If we do a library in Java, we cannot easily reuse it in TypeScript or Go. In this way, libraries are more restrictive than services, which can be called and leveraged by any language. A service has the interoperability property that libraries often do not have.

Good reasons to create a library include:

  * You make something truly better: For example, in performance, you make a faster parser for JSON, or you improve React’s rendering. Your library clearly improves on existing options.

  * There is a fundamental flaw in the existing library: The “current” library is slow, inefficient, or has the wrong mental model. Perhaps you need to create a new one because you don’t control the existing library, or they won’t accept your PRs.

  * You have a custom problem no one has fixed: Let’s imagine you create a library to generate charts in Zig, and this library does not exist. A charting library is generic enough and does not exist. It makes sense to create it.

  * Service will not be natural: A service would be too slow or is the completely wrong model, i.e. you cannot abstract the Spring IoC / dependency injection core framework into a remote service, this would add complexity and overheads with no benefits. Some concepts cannot be separated and need to live together.

  * Good reuse: You have code or business logic that needs to be reused, such as in a modular monolith, and the service model is unnatural. For example, take our istiod example; you need logic that accesses a protected local configuration file from multiple modules of the modular monolith, and instead of writing it multiple times in each large module, you create a library. For good reuse, think about actions or methods that need to be broadly used by your organization, and CANNOT be found in open source, for example, parsing a proprietary binary file format; vs bad reuse, like custom rest clients, JSON readers, etc.




[When should we NOT create a library?](contents.xhtml#sc3_158a)

  * You don’t trust the engineers: You think the engineers will make mistakes, and you don’t trust them. Very likely that your problem is a lack of talent destiny and/or architects are being used as gatekeepers rather than true mentors.

  * It’s purely preference-based: You prefer annotations over methods. Some people love annotations; others hate them. Both approaches are fine, but libraries should not exist just because you want to consume functionality in a different way.

  * Hurts the definitions of lean libraries: Regardless of the need, if the library you would produce would be bloated, complex, hard to maintain, or create bad side effects for the consumers.

  * Bad reuse: You are obsessed with reuse and do not want to allow 1 single line of duplication. Not all duplication is bad, but some duplication can be very bad, such as for persistence code to the same table. Of course, in those scenarios, you have bigger problems, such as having a classical or distributed monolith.




Our recommendation is that you don’t consider libraries as the default choice. Services should be the default choice. This advice is especially important for Platform and DevOps teams which tend to create more libraries than services teams.

[Leverage services](contents.xhtml#sc3_159a)

SOA stands for Service Oriented Architecture. In SOA, services are first-class citizens. Sure, you can use internal shared libraries in any style of architecture. But SOA drives home the point of building services instead of libraries. Services provide several key benefits over internal shared libraries, such as:

  * No binary coupling; service dependencies don’t impact their consumers.

  * Much improved flexibility, as you can refactor the service freely without impacting services.

  * Easier migrations or no migrations at all through clear isolation.




Let’s go over each item and understand it in more depth.

[No binary coupling](contents.xhtml#sc4_160a)

Services do not impose binary coupling risks on their consumers if done correctly. Meaning, if you have a service driver, it is a lean library with near zero or zero dependencies and, therefore little to no binary coupling. The lack of binary coupling will pay off in many ways, such as our next two points: flexibility and easier migrations.

[Much improved flexibility](contents.xhtml#sc4_161a)

Without binary coupling, we can focus on the service contract. As long as we do not break that public contract, we can change literally everything under the hood without breaking consumers, for instance:

  * Big controlled migrations: Change major versions of frameworks and libraries inside the service with no impact on consumers or other services.

  * Modernization: Change the whole tech stack, i.e. from Java 8 to Rust.

  * Persistence evolution: Radically change the persistence store, i.e. from Oracle to Cassandra.

  * Rewrite: Completely re-write the implementation. Change all the code as long as we keep the contract valid.

  * Improve granularity: Slice and dice the service into smaller modules or even more services.




The possibilities are endless. Services enable a lot of flexibility, not only for maintainers, but we also make our consumers’ day-to-day lives much easier.

[Easier migrations or no migrations at all](contents.xhtml#sc4_162a)

Having a service instead of an internal shared library, as long as we are not breaking the public contract, whatever internal migration might need to happen will stay within the service. For instance, we can do the following without consumer impact:

  * JDK migration: Change Java from 8 to 17.

  * Major framework modernization: Upgrade big frameworks like Spring, Spring Boot, and Hibernate, even major versions.

  * Database major migrations: Increment versions of your database like Postgres, MySQL, etc.




None of these require code migrations on the consumer side; the possibilities are endless.

Now let’s say we break the contract. If the service contract is STABLE and properly written, we have no dependencies regarding frameworks and libraries in our public service contract. Consumers only need to deal with the business’ changes. So, much easier than dealing with big frameworks and libraries all over your code base!

However, services do not deliver a free lunch. Often better than libraries, but like anything in software architecture, there are trade-offs and drawbacks such as:

  * Critical reliability path: Now the service is in the critical line of fire for its consumers and cannot be down or there is consumer impact.

  * In some cases, performance: Consumers need to call the service, exchange data with the service, and have round-trip network exchanges, which could be worse than libraries in some cases.




Let’s understand each drawback in more detail.

[Critical reliability path](contents.xhtml#sc4_163a)

In Defense of Libraries covers this advantage of internal shared libraries; when you provide one or more internal shared libraries to business services, by nature, these libraries are embedded in the business services themselves. By comparison, services insert themselves more directly into the reliability path of consumers. Any internal shared library should be reliable and well-tested; just because they have fewer effects on the consumer’s reliability path is not an excuse. But, compared with services, in practice, we have seen many teams fail to monitor shared libraries directly - but most monitor services and notice when they’re down!

[In some cases, performance](contents.xhtml#sc4_164a)

Sure, calling a service comes with additional overhead by comparison with an internal shared library. However, architectural techniques such as batch operations, caching, pre-loading, are-processing, async processing, and so many other patterns allow us to overcome these problems most of the time.

Let’s go with an example. Imagine you have a big file you need to upload to a network endpoint, and the file needs to be pre-processed before being sent. Let’s compare both a service and internal shared library approach. In the service use case, we would need to submit the file to the service where it would do the processing, download the results, and afterward upload the processed results. The library implementation instead could take the file, perform the processing internally, and then upload the file.

The library implementation has fewer file moves and if networking is the bottleneck, should be faster. What if the file processing is intensive, and needs a lot of heavy resources - CPU, memory, disk - you don’t have locally? What if we are describing an ML training algorithm that needs hundreds of servers working in concert? Then the service would be faster.

We can always architect things differently. For instance, imagine we reverse the order of the operations; first, we upload the file, and then the processing happens after the upload on the remote network endpoint; that could reduce performance impact. But either way, hopefully you can see our point: the library works much better in some cases, services in others. 

Figure 4.10: Services calling Services instead Services calling Internal Shared Libraries

Services are remote by nature, and internal shared libraries are local and embedded by nature (Figure 4.10). Services are not necessarily product or business services, but could be technical or shared services, providing cross-cutting or shared concerns to the organization, such as:

  * Security: Authentication, Authorization, Entitlements, Encryption

  * Configurations: Code Configuration, Feature Flags, Experiments

  * Shared business capabilities: User Profiles, User Accounts, Global Preferences and many other business specific capabilities that are cross-cutting concerns.




While we think it is better to leverage services, but here are some general tradeoffs:

Services calling Internal Shared Libraries

  * (+) Reliability Path is much easier; library can’t be “down”.

  * (+) You might get some performance gains through an embedded library’s method call.

  * (-) Binary Coupling: Difficult maintenance, hard migrations, impact on consumers all times.

  * (-) Much less flexibility for library owner and library consumers.




Services calling Services

  * (+) No Binary Coupling: Better to maintain and easier to migrate.

  * (+) Much more flexibility: Use and change whatever you want, as long as you don’t break the public contract.

  * (-) Reliability Path needs to be managed; the service can go down.

  * (+/-) Might lose some performance in some cases but gain in others.




It can seem like a lot of work to build and maintain a service. I need to have my own code base and Jenkins deployment pipeline, provide a public contract, have an internal implementation, add proper observability, and test and manage it. Libraries seem to need much less effort, and creating a service for 2-3 classes might be overkill. Well, if it was just 2-3 classes, you should also apply the same logic to the question: are you sure you need a library? Is this really useful across the whole organization? Is the code really meaningful? Maybe, or maybe not; and you should just accept the duplication across services.

[Sidecar pattern](contents.xhtml#sc3_165a)

Sidecars are the middle ground between services and internal shared libraries. A sidecar is meant to be a background, long running process running outside of your software. You could think about sidecars as a special form of service. You might think sidecars are a new pattern, but the core idea behind them is pretty old. In Unix/Linux, we have this concept called a daemon46. Daemons are service processes that run in the background and provide functionality to another process or the operating system itself.

Daemons (Sidecars) each have a unique purpose in Linux. Here are a couple of examples:

  * Printing daemon: Monitors and takes care of printing.

  * Networking daemon: Manages the network stack.

  * Cron daemon: Famous scheduled jobs service, highly used in many software solutions.

  * Common applications: Docker, OpenVPN, and Redis can also run as daemons.




Deamons are an integral part of the Unix/Linux Operating systems. Daemons are actively managed; you can list all your daemons by running this command in Debian Linux: service --status-all, receiving sample output like in Figure 4.11.

Figure 4.11: List of Linux Deamons

Daemons are everywhere in Unix/Linux. It’s not a one off pattern, it’s integral to the operating system. What is good enough for Linux, is certainly good enough for our own software!

[Modern sidecars](contents.xhtml#sc4_166a)

Taking from our example of Daemons, modern sidecars are when you run another application side-by-side with your application. Sidecars don’t require any specific technology or stack. For instance, Netflix has a number of sidecars written in Java, such as:

  * Priam for Cassandra ([https://github.com/Netflix/Priam](https://github.com/Netflix/Priam))

  * Dynomite Manager for Dynomite ([https://github.com/Netflix/dynomite-manager](https://github.com/Netflix/dynomite-manager))

  * Raigad for ElasticSearch ([https://github.com/Netflix/Raigad](https://github.com/Netflix/Raigad))




These examples of Netflix sidecars are Java applications that provide generic capabilities to NoSQL databases such as backups, restores, cluster management and much more. For the Netflix use cases, sidecars are running on EC2 machines in AWS alongside their database software, just like daemons in Linux run in user space on a Linux OS. The principle is the same.

Sidecars can be used beyond database use cases, for instance:

  * Security: Certificates, mTLS, Authentication, Authorization, Entitlements, Encryption

  * Configs: Configuration, Feature Flags, Experiments

  * Observability: Logs, Metrics, Alerts, and Data Routing

  * Several use cases in your business where you might be applying libraries.




Sidecars have several benefits such as:

  * No binary coupling: Like services, since sidecars are different applications, they cannot conflict with your application libraries.

  * Migrations are easier: Since you do not need to touch your application/services.

  * Transparency: Often, consumers don’t need to directly manage their sidecars.




However, like anything in software architecture, there are drawbacks. Let’s understand the cons in regards of modern sidecars:

  * Reliability Path: In some cases, sidecars need to be up and running at all times, otherwise, they might create outages, i.e. PII Encryption. In others, like the Netflix Priam sidecar, if Priam fails after Cassandra has bootstrapped, Cassandra can continue to function normally, albeit without some features.

  * Deployment: Deploying sidecars does require infrastructure changes; you can do it in a generic way, but it is still work. k8s makes deploying sidecars easy, with just a few lines of YAML.

  * Complexity: Sidecars are more complex than internal shared libraries, and can be harder to debug than internal shared libraries or even services.




Netflix sidecars are the state of the art: they are transparent; even if they are down, they do not necessarily produce an outage in the NoSQL database in question (Cassandra, Dynomite, or Elasticsearch). Netflix sidecars also don’t require special clients/drivers. Amazingly, they work as true transparent daemons that some consumers don’t even need to know about! However, not all sidecars are created equally, and some sidecars can have clients/drivers. Here is where you need to pay careful attention. If the sidecar driver is not lean, you will have the same issues as libraries, but with the burden of much higher complexity.

[Sidecars and proxies](contents.xhtml#sc4_167a)

Envoy47 is also an important sidecar and a great enabler of service mesh architectures.

Distributed systems have many challenges, especially at scale. Envoy focuses on solving networking, security, and observability problems in distributed system communication. When you deploy Envoy, all traffic flows through Envoy. Envoy was built at Lyft, in C++, and inspired by learnings from proxy and load-balancer solutions such as Nginx, HAProxy, and hardware load balancers.

Envoy’s key features are:

  * Sidecar Pattern: Out-of-process architecture, which can run with any language or application.

  * Highly performant, small memory footprint.

  * Advanced load balancing: Automatic retries, circuit breakers, global rate limiting, request shadowing, zone local load balancer.

  * HTTP/2 and gRPC protocol support for both incoming and outgoing connections, transparent HTTP 1.1/2 proxying, TLS termination, health checking, and edge support.

  * Rich APIs for dynamic configuration management.

  * Rich observability: Full L7 traffic inspection, native support for distributed tracing, wire-level observability of multiple databases.




Envoy is a well known sidecar, battle-tested at many Silicon Valley companies such as Lyft, Google, Netflix, Airbnb, eBay, Microsoft, Stripe, Pinterest, Twilio, Salesforce, AWS, and many more. You can configure Envoy in a variety of network topologies such as internal load balancers, ingress proxies, or egress proxy on the network edge, and multi-tier topologies for scalability and reliability. 

Figure 4.12: Envoy, Sidecar Pattern for Network/Observability between Services  
Source: [https://www.envoyproxy.io/docs/envoy/latest/intro/life_of_a_request](https://www.envoyproxy.io/docs/envoy/latest/intro/life_of_a_request)

In Figure 4.12, you can see that services don’t call each other directly. For example, Services A wants to send a request to Service B. Service A talks to its local Envoy instance, deployed in the sidecar pattern; Service A’s Envoy talks to Service B’s Envoy, which passes along the request to Service B. Envoy can also be used alongside the Istio service mesh48.

Unlike internal shared libraries, sidecars are great because they do not introduce binary coupling to the application/service. Sidecars allow capabilities that can be direct or transparent; in the envoy case, they are transparent. Envoy is a great solution and an option that you should explore and consider in your solutions.

As you can see, Envoy solves problems using the Sidecar Pattern, whereas other approaches often solve the same problems using libraries. Both Ribbon for Netflix ([https://github.com/Netflix/ribbon](https://github.com/Netflix/ribbon)) using Java, and Finagle ([https://twitter.github.io/finagle/](https://twitter.github.io/finagle/)) for Twitter written in Scala use libraries to achieve similar goals.

Using the Sidecar Pattern, therefore being outside of the applications/services binary path, allows Envoy to be language agnostic and work with a wide variety of languages. This is a big advantage of Sidecars over shared libraries, internal or external.

[Sidecars and Kubernetes](contents.xhtml#sc4_168a)

Kubernetes ([https://kubernetes.io/](https://kubernetes.io/), aka k8s) is an open-source container orchestrator. K8s does deployment automation, scaling, and management of containerized applications. Kubernetes was created by Google using the same principles that allowed Google to run container workloads at Scale for many years. K8s can be run anywhere: mainstream clouds like AWS and GCP, on premises, and even on the edge.

Kubernetes has many features and capabilities like:

  * Automated rollbacks and rollouts: If something goes wrong, Kubernetes can automatically roll back the change.

  * Service discovery and load balancing: Pods are the deployment unit of k8s, each of which can have their own IP and DNS name. K8s can load balance across objects.

  * Self-healing: Restart containers that fail, replace and reschedule containers when the node dies, kill containers that don’t respond, and avoid advertising containers to consumers before they are ready to be called by using readiness probes.

  * Storage Orchestration: Automatic mount of the storage system, many storage solutions supported like local, cloud storage, NFS, Ceph, and Cinder.

  * Secret and Configuration Management: Deploy and update secrets and application configuration without rebuilding the container images.

  * Automatic Bin Packing: Place containers based on resource requirements and other constraints and requirements without sacrificing availability. You can mix critical and best-effort workloads in order to have optimal utilization and save even more resources.

  * Batch Execution: Not only limited to services, but Kubernetes can also manage CI/CD workloads and Batch workloads, replacing containers if they fail.

  * Scaling: Scale vertically, horizontally; scale pods, scale nodes, scale the whole cluster. Use simple commands, a UI, or scale automatically based on standard metrics like CPU or custom metrics.

  * Design for Extensibility: Add features without affecting the application/services.




Kubernetes is well established, in 2016 joining the Cloud Native Computing Foundation (CNCF) [https://www.cncf.io/projects/kubernetes/](https://www.cncf.io/projects/kubernetes/). Kubernetes is used across many companies such as Box, CapitalOne, [Booking.com](https://Booking.com), The New York Times, Nokia, Pinterest, Sling, Spotify, Zalando, OpenAI, ING, Wikimedia, Yahoo Japan, Pearson, IBM, Buffer, Bose, BlackRock, Adidas, Walmart, Airbnb, Yelp, Reddit, Tinder, Intuit, Slack, Zendesk, NuBank, Vodafone, Ericsson, T-Mobile, Comcast, Bloomberg and many more across the globe.

Kubernetes has many benefits, but one of the big ones is that it essentially provides a spec for cloud computing, allowing portability across cloud providers to some degree and having a wide ecosystem of open-source solutions ready to be deployed on it. Using Kubernetes, sidecars can be created and deployed very easily. Specifically, Kubernetes has a declarative YAML file format to describe the cluster’s desired state and applications.

  1. apiVersion: apps/v1
  2. kind: Deployment
  3. metadata:
  4. name: profile-service-deployment
  5. labels:
  6. app: profile-service
  7. spec:
  8. replicas: 1
  9. selector:
  10. matchLabels:
  11. app: profile-service
  12. template:
  13. metadata:
  14. labels:
  15. app: profile-service
  16. spec:
  17. containers:
  18. \- name: profile-service
  19. image: profileservice:2.0.5
  20. ports:
  21. \- containerPort: 8080
  22. \- name: security-sidecar
  23. image: security-sidecar:1.0.1
  24. ports:
  25. \- containerPort: 9090



Code 4.1: Kubernetes Deployment spec in YAML for 2 Containers; one is a Sidecar

Let’s understand the instructions in Code 4.1. In Kubernetes, a Pod is the standard deployment unit; you can deploy multiple containers in a Pod. Pods are deployed on nodes; clusters house multiple nodes. Given the deployment sample we provided, you can see we are deploying a container with the Profile Service, but also, we, on the same Pod, are deploying a sidecar called the security sidecar. Perhaps the security sidecar is handling certificates, mTLS, Encryptions, or any other security concerns.

Kubernetes is really sidecar friendly because, with a couple of lines in a YAML file, we can have sidecars basically for free; we don’t need to make any special changes in our deployment infrastructure thanks to Kubernetes. If we were doing sidecars in EC2, which is possible, we would need to handle all the deployment concerns ourselves.

Kubernetes allows us to add multiple containers in the same Pod. In practice, we could have more than one sidecar alongside our application/service. This configurability is really great because it allows us to create modular sidecars instead of monolithic ones and combine solutions like Envoy with your company’s internal sidecars.

Figure 4.13: Envoy, Sidecar Pattern for Network/Observability between Services

Figure 4.13 describes a Kubernetes cluster architecture with our deployment applied. Kubernetes itself has a control plane, where it does the cluster management alongside the kube-api-server, cluster scheduling that enforces state, controller-manager, etcd, for configurations. The Kubernetes control plane talks to working nodes via the kubelet on each node. Inside the nodes on the data plane, we also have a proxy and the container runtime, which could be docker or another container runtime. Inside the worker nodes (designated by the blue box), we have the Pods; inside the Pods, we have our containers. We can consider the Kubelt a sidecar as well. So Kubernetes is not only built using the sidecar pattern but also allows you to deploy sidecars easily. 

[How can you build a Sidecar? Types of Sidecars](contents.xhtml#sc4_169a)

The beauty of the Sidecar Pattern is that we can build sidecars using any technology. Therefore allowing us to benefit from powerful languages like C++, Rust, V lang or Zig languages.

Sidecars don’t necessarily require system programming languages and can also be built using high-level languages like Java, Scala, Kotlin, or even Python. However, we would argue that since you have binary freedom, and do not necessarily need to use the same language used by your Microservices/Services.

When building your own internal sidecar, you need to consider your use cases. Let’s define two types of sidecars: Transparent, like a proxy or Database Helper, or Client/Server, for instance in our case of PII Encryption (Figure 4.14). Depending on the architecture style of the client/server sidecar, you might need a driver; this is where you need to be careful because, you can lose most of the benefits from the Sidecar Pattern since the driver is just an internal shared library, and therefore has binary coupling. Mind your dependencies! 

Figure 4.14: Types of Sidecars

Transparent Sidecar

Transparent sidecars are the most desired and ideal form of the Sidecar Pattern. We call it transparent, because the service/business code does not make any explicit calls to the sidecar, and there is no binary coupling between the sidecar and the services. This is the case with proxies like Netflix Dynomite Manager.

Client/Server Sidecar

For Client/Server sidecars, the service needs to use the sidecar’s driver. There will be one or more explicit direct calls from the business code within the service to the sidecar driver, such as in our previous examples for PII Encryption.

Suppose we perform all encryption operations on the sidecar. Therefore, the service does not need access to the key material. The application/service needs to call the driver in order to perform encrypt and decrypt operations; we can’t perform it with transparency.

The risk is all about the Sidecar driver; if the driver is lean and has fewer dependencies, we are good. For Client/Server sidecars, consider the following limitations and drawbacks:

  * Drivers might be required: You might need to write drivers for multiple languages.

  * Reliability path: The Sidecar needs to be always up and running; otherwise it will affect the reliability path of the service.

  * Complexity: Some extra complexity, similar to a service approach. You might still be able to perform some optimizations, but it will not be the same as invoking method calls from an internal shared library.




[Sidebar: Service meshes](contents.xhtml#sc4_170a)

Service meshes are another alternative to internal shared libraries. The most famous is Istio. A service mesh architecture pushes the complexity and abstractions of a distributed system to the infrastructure layer, like Kubernetes. The Service Mesh Pattern addresses concerns like security, observability, and networking often handled by internal shared libraries. (Figure 4.15)

Service Meshes also can handle the same concerns that K8s handles, like A/B testing, discoverability, load balancing, canary deployments, rate limiting, access control, encryption, and E2E authentication. Istio uses Envoy. Istio is used by companies like AutoTrader, Airbnb, Splunk, Salesforce, Rappi, T-Mobile, eBay, Atlassian, HSBC, HelloFresh, Gov.UK, Lowe’s, Postman, SAP, Under Armor, Trulia, and many other companies.

Figure 4.15: Istio Service Mesh Architecture

Istio also has a control plane and a data plane. In the data plane, Istio uses Envoy. Like everything in software architecture, we have drawbacks like:

  * No support for async patterns: Pretty much everything is about sync calls, only supporting circuit breakers and retries.

  * Overhead: The more layers we throw on the infrastructure, the more hops, you lose performance and gain complexity. Overtime, Istio is improving its internal performance.




[Simple alternatives](contents.xhtml#sc3_171a)

Simple alternatives are not valid for all possible use cases. These approaches might work in some cases, but are unlikely to be the go-to better option, like services or lean libraries. Even so, these tactical options need to be considered, with your rationale being the same as any architectural, design, and code trade-off. What are you getting, but what are the pain points?

Here are some simple alternatives to consider (Figure 4.16).

Figure 4.16: Simple Alternatives for Internal Shared Libraries

[Leverage existing SDKs and libraries](contents.xhtml#sc4_172a)

You might be surprised at what you have in place already. The Java Development Kit (JDK) has tons of utility functions for everyday needs. Sure, they might not be the coolest or most up-to-date approach - but could it be enough for your use case? Sometimes the solution might be to build a simple and proper utility or wrapper class on top of the existing JDK so you can add the behavior you need.

Similarly for the open-source libraries you already use in your classpath. Why not leverage what you have already? Many times we see teams adding libraries for the sake of preference or curiosity, and which are not necessarily the most important for your use case or give substantial benefits. It’s not uncommon to see Jackson, Gson and json-simple in the same project. Why do we need three different libraries to do JSON parsing?

[Do it yourself](contents.xhtml#sc4_173a)

Sometimes, what you need is not that complex; it could be a simple algorithm that does not change that often, perhaps a few lines of code, or a couple of files. Why not just write it yourself, and skip pulling in yet another library? If you take this approach, always try to be as minimal as possible, especially when building internal shared libraries.

Building it yourself might sound scary and the wrong thing to do. Ditch your skepticism and use your reasoning: analyze the trade-offs and how much you really need from a library. If you are using 80% of the features of a given library, that sounds good. But to include a library full of heavy dependencies when you only need 1 method - is it really worth it? Again, that’s a trade-off analysis you should perform.

[Copy and paste the code](contents.xhtml#sc4_174a)

Another tactical approach could be just copying and pasting the open-source code you need. Sometimes it is pretty isolated and portable, you can easily copy a couple of classes and add them to your solution. Of course, respecting all licenses and credits! If the code is not 100% isolated, you might be able to apply some refactoring and extract the code - even partial reuse might be enough.

[Contribute to open source](contents.xhtml#sc4_175a)

Ultimately, you could step up and help the Open Source community, and build a better library. One is maintained, uses few or no other dependencies, and helps yourself and others to solve a real world problem.

[Proper library design](contents.xhtml#sc2_176a)

Let’s review some of the key ideas to keep in mind when designing a proper library. 

[Duplication vs re-use](contents.xhtml#sc3_177a)

The common wisdom of the crowds is that reuse is always good, and duplication is always bad. We don’t believe this is a universal truth; instead, it sits on a spectrum with tradeoffs. (Figure 4.17) In fact, sometimes duplication is good and reuse is bad! We need to establish what is good duplication and what is bad reuse. 

Figure 4.17: Duplication vs Reuse tradeoff spectrum

[Bad duplication: Business code](contents.xhtml#sc4_178a)

Business code is a no brainer: you should not duplicate business code. Any code that drives the day-to-day business decisions of your company is a good candidate to risk duplication. Ideally you do not have internal shared libraries with business code, only services. The worst aspect of duplication is bugs. You do not want to fix the same business logic bug in 100+ services. The impact of failing to centralize your business rules can be massive, and directly affect a company’s bottom line and reputation.

[Bad duplication: Compliance](contents.xhtml#sc4_179a)

When your company needs to comply with industry regulation or guarantee information security, duplication is dangerous and therefore not desired at all. However not all use cases and scenarios are about compliance.

Leveraging services would be the natural solution. Services would allow us to have the solution in one place and not duplicate the code. Libraries can provide the same benefit, however as we describe in this chapter, libraries have many pitfalls and issues such as binary coupling.

[Good duplication: Migrations](contents.xhtml#sc4_180a)

Duplicated code is isolated. Duplicating code can be used as a tool to reduce the blast radius of an API. Imagine you have 3 different services using the same internal shared library. If you want to decommission the library, you need to migrate the 3 projects to a new solution. But, what if one service will also be decommissioned shortly, in 6 months? Migrating all 3 projects sounds like waste, and it is. Instead, you can reduce this blast radius by copying parts of the library code directly to the service being decommissioned. You decouple by increasing duplication and reducing your overall migration effort.

Again: duplication is evil when we are talking about business code, business rules, or even persistence code for a distributed monolith. The best thing you can do is not have a distributed monolith by not sharing data stores and leveraging services. However, if for some reason you have a distributed monolith, and a shared persistence library, it is still better to use it in this worst case scenario. Better to have one centralized repository rather than multiple code repositories duplicating persistence code.

[Good duplication: Configuration and setup code](contents.xhtml#sc4_181a)

Pressing keys is not the bottleneck; typing is short term. Features might stop at some point, but as long as a piece of software lives, maintenance in the form of migrations could be forever. Why optimize for short term activity instead of optimizing for long term migration?

It is fine to duplicate configuration or boilerplate code, as long as we are not touching business rules or business code. If you need to write 10 lines of code to send an email in Java, the natural thought is to create a library to reduce it to one; if these 10 lines are just boilerplate, is this so important to risk binary coupling? It would be fine to duplicate this code in all your services.

Duplicating the code here implies less reuse, but what we are really doing is:

  * Shifting ownership: As the code lives in the service, and the service team needs to maintain it.

  * Easier migrations: Having few internal shared libraries allows us to make multiple migrations happen in parallel, without a centralized bottleneck.




Let’s be honest, what is the chance you will have a bug in boilerplate code? The good news here is that if we are wrong, you can create a library later; you don’t need to start with a library. But once you create a library and have hundreds or thousands of consumers, you can’t easily put the genie back into the bottle.

[Reuse: The double edged sword](contents.xhtml#sc4_182a)

Engineers are often obsessed with reuse. Reuse is often overvalued and can create more trouble than benefit if it is done wrong. Reuse is not always good. Reuse is especially tricky when we are talking about your company’s internal shared libraries. However, reuse has drawbacks, such as:

  * No control: You might not control the direction and priorities of the team who is providing the software.

  * No decision: You might have little say regarding design decisions and direction.

  * Binary coupling and bloated dependencies: You cannot control the 3rd party dependencies in these libraries, exposing you to binary coupling.

  * Bugs: Your bugs may not be prioritized. Your PRs might not be merged.

  * Coupling: You create coupling by using any library.




When you are reusing code, you can feel good:

  * Saving time: Being more productive, getting more things done.

  * Delivering more with less: Don’t need to worry, someone will solve this problem for me.

  * Speeding up engineering: Now will get 10x more tickets and stories done, right?

  * Business focus: Having time to focus on something else.




However, that might not be true in all cases. If a piece of software is not actively maintained and does not have a good track record, you can easily get in trouble with re-using internal shared libraries. In the context of distributed monoliths, you need to consider binary coupling in the equation.

It’s all about discipline. If you manage to have a small set of internal shared libraries that are actively maintained, consumers (services and libraries) always up-to-date, then your internal libraries are good. Everything depends on the quality of your internal stack and how much technical debt you have, and whether you have a distributed monolith, big or small. 

Figure 4.18: Reuse tradeoffs.

Reuse has tradeoffs (Figure 4.18). Don’t take it for granted. The more you reuse, the more migrations you will have. Often migrations require the service teams to handle them to some degree, and of course to test. Internal shared libraries can be great in the same cases. But the big thing is to always analyze - look at the code and make a conscious call.

The most simple and straightforward way to avoid binary coupling is to avoid having many internal shared libraries. Having few, lean internal libraries allows you to have the flexibility and also move as fast as the business needs.

[Proper dependency management](contents.xhtml#sc3_183a)

The number one rule you need to remember is: libraries used within an isolated construct like a service is one thing, with fewer pitfalls and chances for things to go wrong. However, libraries used by internal shared libraries are where you need to pay much more attention. Here are some best practices that can be applied for libraries and services for dependency management. Similar advice can be applied to services, sidecars or any time you need to add dependencies.

[Cherry pick dependencies](contents.xhtml#sc4_184a)

Earlier in this chapter, we explained the pitfalls in having bloated libraries. The solution to dealing with libraries is simple: avoid using libraries you don’t need. Be specific, cherry pick exactly only what is necessary. 

While it might sound silly, many times people just copy a pom.xml from a working service or library and start from there, unnecessarily including tons of dependencies. So rule #1 - don’t take libraries for granted. And while you are cherry-picking only the libraries you need, make sure you understand what dependencies those libraries will bring to your classpath.

If you are an Apache Maven user, you can run a simple command in your terminal in order to see the full dependency graph of your project; Gradle has this feature as well. Run the command in Code 4.2:

1\. mvn dependency:tree

Code 4.2: Maven command to list all dependencies

This command relies on the maven dependency plugin49, and you will see a result as in Figure 4.19:

Figure 4.19: mvn dependency:tree output on terminal

Another helpful resource to understand dependencies is the mvnrepository website: [https://mvnrepository.com/](https://mvnrepository.com/), which has all this information and much more (Figure 4.20). You can find the XML snippet adding the maven dependency in your pom.xml for any transitive, compile, or runtime dependencies. 

Figure 4.20: mvnrepository entry for Google Guava - [https://mvnrepository.com/](https://mvnrepository.com/)

mvnrepository is a great website for Java engineers, and we use it almost daily. You should spend some time getting familiar with the website, it is useful when you are analyzing open source libraries to include, and if you want to cherry-pick libraries, it can help you explore dependencies before adding them to your project.

Of course, when you have shared internal libraries, they will not show up there. You will need to download the library source code and run mvn dependency:tree or use an IDE plugin. If you are an IntelliJ IDEA user, the maven-helper50 plugin can let you search through your pom.xml files (Figure 4.21). maven-helper is particularly useful during code migrations to figure out what library is bringing what dependency in case you need to do some exclusions.

Figure 4.21: Maven Helper plugin in Action with IntelliJ Idea

Using these 3 tools, you have all the information needed to make assertive decisions. Avoid copying and pasting pom.xml files from other teams and other existing services. While cherry picking dependencies can be a bit time-consuming when you create a new service or library, it pays off in the long run.

Another piece of advice around cherry-picking dependencies is that dependencies are also added during new feature development and even in bug fixes. It’s very important to pay attention to changes in your pom.xml files during code reviews. Add reviewing pom.xml and dependencies review as part of your checklist for code review. 

[Avoid company-wide parent POMs](contents.xhtml#sc4_185a)

Apache Maven has the ability to have hierarchical poms, a powerful feature to avoid duplicating dependency definitions across multiple projects. However, if not used with wisdom, it can lead to binary coupling and other unwanted behaviors. It’s okay to have multiple projects in your services and internal shared libraries. However, you do not want to share a pom.xml either between different services or different libraries.

Suppose the Profile service declared spring-core in its pom.xml. The Reports service is using that same pom.xml from the Profile Service as its parent pom.xml. The Reports service also uses Spring, but now this team wants to use a newer version of Spring. The Reports service can’t change the root pom without affecting the Profile service. Sure, maven allows exclusions, and the local pom.xml always wins in the sense of resolution strategy. Relying on a root pom.xml is not a good practice, and just creates more complexity and coupling, so avoid it.

[Explicitly declare dependencies](contents.xhtml#sc4_186a)

Dependencies have dependencies; but dependencies of dependencies can always change. You should not depend directly on a transitive dependency without explicitly declaring in your pom.xml. It only takes one change from the library provider to break your code, whether internal or external.

In order to be more resilient, only depend on dependencies explicitly declared in your pom.xml. The Maven Helper Plugin we mentioned above also can help you here, because you can easily navigate through the plugin interface, perform searches and see how your dependencies are being resolved. If you have direct imports, your code will break at compile time. If you use reflection, it might only be detected by tests or at the runtime of the application. Never directly rely on transitive dependencies.

[Lean libraries](contents.xhtml#sc3_187a)

The Lean Libraries approach applies to both internal and external libraries. It doesn’t matter whether they’re built in your company, open source or closed source. As problem solvers, when your delivery is an internal shared library, you need to think about the side effects you will bring upon your consumers. The less you influence their classpath, the better. Meaning, you should mind your dependencies and avoid forcing your consumers in specific directions.

Lean Libraries means that you care and design internal shared libraries with ZERO, or very few dependencies which are carefully analyzed and chosen. The task is tough, but it can be done. Lean Libraries have the following properties:

  * Stable contracts: The contract will be minimal and isolated; it will not have hundreds of operations just for sake of preference. Preferences have a cost, and this will impact library maintainability.
