Quick wins/refactoring: Systems, or services that we could improve without much effort because they are isolated or easy to isolate, plus they deliver strong value to the business. The decision is a no-brainer; we should do it.

Rewrite: Our greatest risk and likely the greatest rewards lie in this quadrant: projects that could have a big impact on the company, either bringing direct or indirect revenue, growth, or savings but that will take a lot of effort. We can easily argue this is where the bulk of your development energy should go at all times, towards big swings that can really change the trajectory of your company. Because of the large effort, a rewrite makes natural sense for the systems in this quadrant. Alternatively, if we consider refactoring, we can apply backwards compatibility approaches and great test coverage of your public contract to ensure safe development which can then be followed by a rewrite or larger improvements.

Decentralization is great. Having many empowered teams is how organizations get speed at scale. However, we need to be careful. One important aspect is that you still need to see the whole ecosystem/organization. Organizations with scale, that have tens to hundred of teams, can find it easy to get overwhelmed by the enthusiasm but limited perspectives of their individual teams, say yes to everything and fail to govern the company as a whole.

Mary and Tom Poppendieck, in their 2003 book “Lean Software Development: An Agile Toolkit” explain in detail seven key lean principles:

  1. Eliminate waste

  2. Amplify learning

  3. Decide as late as possible

  4. Deliver as fast as possible

  5. Empower the team

  6. Build Integrity In

  7. See the whole




Principle #7, “See The Whole”, is critical here - you should look at the whole ecosystem, consider the entire business, and avoid local optimizations. Otherwise, all teams will say everything matters, and everything is important. Every technical team has their own priority; Java engineers want to do what is best for them, what makes their life easier, and the same for frontend engineers, DBAs, DevOps engineers, QA engineers… exclusively maximizing your own local results might be great for your team, but it is not really effective for the business.

[Order of events](contents.xhtml#sc3_232a)

Now let’s put it all together in the form of an order of events, things that you should do:

  1. Create a centralized Excel Workbook or Google Docs Sheet, listing the dimensions that are critical for your assessment.

  2. Apply the workbook to 2-3 services yourself, following the principles of code analysis, data analysis and domain mapping laid out in this chapter. Tweak your dimensions as necessary.

     1. Evaluations should be done by looking through the code directly.

     2. Take into account the isolation of databases and other data stores.

  3. Share the sheet with your teams, and explain how to fill it out. Ideally this is a team effort across your organization, not centralized in a few voices. However, if you have software architects it can be a great idea to include them in the entire assessment.

  4. As part of the assessment, perform domain mapping and grouping exercises for services.

  5. Classification and Judgment (Ignore, Decommission, Improve, Rewrite) should not be done in isolation.

     1. It is not one decision, it is one decision per component.

     2. Remember to “See the Whole”, and consider the whole ecosystem.

  6. Combine your assessment results with your technology and business needs to define your modernization strategy.

     1. Ignoring some services might be fine, depending on the results of the assessment.

     2. Consider applying different strategies for different services.

     3. Consider applying backward compatibility solutions and buy, not only build.

     4. For the rewrite use cases, partner with the business, product, and UX in order to come up with new capabilities, and differentiators for your business and users.

     5. Respect the Cone of Uncertainty, and be extra careful with your early estimates.

  7. Collect and share feedback.




[Radical change](contents.xhtml#sc3_233a)

Maybe after all the assessments and outcome analysis, you realize you truly have a hard task. A codebase that is barely understood by your engineers, or with a business fixed in their ways. No-one sees the need to change; according to your leaders, the way it works is the way it always will work. The day to day is about quarterly planning, resources and budgeting, rather than finding a real way forward.

No amount of assessments, domain mapping or modernization strategies is going to solve your problem when you have an ingrained cultural bias against change. All your attempts to innovate or modernize will just lead you to building the same system again; without creativity you will produce the same outcomes. If you expect that performing the same steps with the same approach over and over will produce a different result, you are fooling yourself. Is your proposed 2.0 version really just the 1.0 monolith but with microservices, and nothing else? Change is scary - it’s easier for some people to accept a modernization strategy that looks like the old architecture but slightly improved. But ask yourself, are you fixing any real business problems?

Disruption is the answer. Perhaps your company needs a domain model started from scratch, a new city map where the old borders are blown away; or a UX design that starts with the customer first rather than the business dictating only mandates. True innovation requires you to explode the present to embrace the future.

Fully reviewing the business strategies and technical approaches you can take to disrupt your current business is its own book. But, we have to point out that you might be trapped in a no-win situation with your software. The only answer is creative disruption, being informed by your assessment but breaking the boundaries completely to reach a new design.

Sometimes a radical departure from the past is the only path to the future. True visionary thinking isn’t always easy for people to accept: when you propose starting from scratch and rethinking your business you need to be brave, be bold, be passionate, and be unapologetic. In the end, you might be surprised at the support you get.

[Things to remember](contents.xhtml#sc2_234a)

Congratulations! We reach the end of [Chapter 5](ch05.xhtml), Assessments. You are halfway through the book! Keep going! Here are some important things we learn to remember:

  * Assessment is key. You should not skip it.

  * Your goal with assessment is to make sense of the state of affairs of services and systems.

  * As part of the assessment results, you want to know the specifics per service or software component. Judgment should happen per service as well.

  * Understand the overall modernization journey:

    * Understand your technology and business motivations.

    * Perform your assessment. Never skip this step!

    * Modernization Strategies emerge from a combination of your assessment results with technology and business needs.

    * Agile Execution; do not do a waterfall project.

  * Mind the Cone of Uncertainty.

    * Avoid estimating and committing before the classification work and domain mapping exercise is complete.

    * Later estimates tend to be more accurate as you learn more about the state of affairs and decide what to do for each system/service.

  * Execution is not easy; many projects fail in execution. The more you understand the problem, the better.

  * Perform Build vs Buy Analysis tradeoff analysis before committing to build.

  * Even when you buy, there will be build. You can’t buy all your integrations.

  * Keep customer impact in mind: work backwards and consider user experience first.

  * Rewrite vs Refactor sits on a spectrum; it is not a binary call.

  * When rewriting, be mindful of cost, and try to deliver software that has a business impact.

  * Do not rewrite code just for the sake of it; also consider refactoring and improving things.

  * Consider picking boring technology, and limit the amount of innovation you do at a time until you reach maturity.

  * Backward compatibility is a great way to speed up migrations.

  * Backward compatibility can be used with internal shared libraries, core services, and business services and should always be considered.

  * In order to perform proper backward compatibility, we need to identify all the public contracts and have tests in place. Then we can switch all or any detail of the implementation as long as we preserve the contract integrity.

  * When doing code analysis, consider looking at the dimensions:

    * Who or which teams are the owners?

    * What is the rate of business change?

    * What are the public contracts, and how big is that contract?

    * What are the downstream dependencies?

    * Who are the consumers?

    * Do humans use this component directly, or only machines?

    * What is the solution complexity?

    * Do tests pass consistently and does the component have decent coverage?

  * After the classification work is done, you need to judge each component. The suggested categories are:

    * Rewrite: What components can and should be rewritten?

    * Improve: What components can be saved due to some degree of refactoring?

    * Decommission: What components should be decommissioned?

    * Ignore: What components are good or tolerable as-is?

  * Database Analysis is important; consider evaluating the level of independence of a schema or group of tables by low, medium, or high.

  * Domain Driven Design (DDD) is a powerful tool when designing software architectures.

  * DDD can be applied to both green field development or existing technologies, services, classical monoliths or distributed monoliths.

  * Domain Mapping is a powerful tool to define the right domains for your software architecture.

  * Ubiquitous Language is where engineers and domain experts establish a common vocabulary to avoid mistakes and accelerate development.

  * Grouping services by business domain is a good idea because it makes prioritization decisions clear, and we might see clear opportunities to split/merge services.

  * List all domains you see in a distributed monolith. Later on, you can prioritize splitting one domain at a time.

  * Quarantining a distributed monolith can stop the bleeding but be careful, new code bases and new services can make the problem worse.

  * Look for low-hanging fruits and opportunities for quick wins as part of your assessment work.

  * Quick wins come from looking at the code and identifying usage and consumers of the code; GitHub or similar tools can be used in order to perform usage analysis.

  * Once your assessment is done, consider business impact vs effort as a strategy for prioritization.

  * See The Whole makes sure that while you apply business impact vs effort system by system, you keep your whole organization in mind.

  * Radical Change. Throw it out! Start over! Be bold, challenge the status quo and lead your teams with passion and energy to a better place.

# [CHAPTER 6Principles of Proper Services](contents.xhtml#ch06a)

In Matters of style, swim with the current; in matters of principles, stand like a rock.

— Thomas Jefferson

In this chapter, you will learn the secrets of proper service development. This wisdom will improve every project you ever build going forward. You will learn essential principles that will guide you on your journey, ensure you get things right, and ultimately deliver value to your business.

Principles are everything when it comes to software architecture, and no more so than with service development. Principles are like universal laws; following them will pay off in the long run. Correct principles will help you navigate complex environments and provide a clear path for getting out of the swamp of technical debt you face with bad monoliths.

[Structure](contents.xhtml#sc2_235a)

In this chapter, we will cover the following topics:

  * Service oriented architecture

    * Types of services

    * When to use a service

    * When to avoid services

    * SOA benefits

      * Faster time to market

      * Lower costs and easier maintenance

      * Extensibility and adaptability

      * Independence

      * Summary of SOA benefits

  * Contract first

    * New service

    * Existing component

    * Coding contracts with OpenAPI

    * Backward compatibility

  * SOA and isolation

    * Isolate datastores

    * Isolate libraries

      * Sidebar: flavors versus bridges in libraries and monoliths

    * Isolated public contracts

      * Design reviews

      * Automating contract health

      * Non obvious things

      * Handling errors in contracts

      * Service exposure

      * Things to remember




[Service oriented architecture](contents.xhtml#sc2_236a)

Service Oriented Architecture (SOA) is, like it sounds, a software architecture composed of services: self-contained software units that accomplish a repeatable, defined task without having to share internal implementation details. The concept of SOA was introduced in the late 90s, but unfortunately, SOA started off on its back foot. The industry often associated SOA with big, heavyweight systems and patterns for the integration of disparate software systems. Integration is fine and has value, but SOA is much more than just integration. In order to do SOA, you do not need an Enterprise Service Bus (ESB)61 or Business Process Execution Language (BPEL)62. No specific technology is required: it’s possible to do SOA in any language or stack.

Services provide a variety of business capabilities. As you might imagine, SOA treats services as first-class solutions; in a SOA style of architecture, you orient your solutions completely around services. SOA does not prescribe architecture for other styles of applications or components like frontend applications, internal shared libraries, batch jobs, infrastructure components. However, you definitely can apply service orientation thinking to any kind of component. As long as the component can stand on its own, predictably and repeatedly accomplish its task, and hide its implementation details from its callers, the foundations of SOA are there.

The SOA Manifesto63 was written in the late 2000s, in response to some of the worst projects that tried to do SOA using big vendor technology and failed. The SOA Manifesto is one of the best guides on how to do SOA right; it speaks for itself, and informs many themes throughout this chapter (Figure 6.1):

Figure 6.1: SOA Manifesto

[Types of services](contents.xhtml#sc3_237a)

We could write an entire book on the types and successful implementations of SOA services; here are some useful common patterns when considering the functionality of services.

  * Business service: Reflects a direct business capability, sub-organization or features. The business can easily understand that such software exists, they see these services and appreciate them. The majority of your services should be business services, because they drive business value for your company, to the point that when we just use the word service, it should imply business service. Examples of business services are: sales service, playlist service, account service, payments service, shipping service, ride service.

  * Core service: Core Services implement business functions meant to be reused across an organization or across the whole company. Core services should be business services; however, they deal with broader concepts. Examples of core services are: authentication service, entitlements service, profile service, user preferences service, search service, user alerts service.

  * Wrapper service: A special kind of business service, wrapper services wrap functionality from target services; wrappers are required when the target service in question is not efficient, has a legacy contract, and wrong abstraction. Wrapper services are created because we don’t have the source code or power to change the target service. Examples of scenarios where you would create a wrapper service include: legacy banking functions, translated SOAP APIs to REST, fixing leaky old contracts, solving slow/limited bottlenecks (needing a queue to retry), making a difficult and verbose contract succinct.

  * Platform service: Platform services provide generic technical capability or features. Platform services are not and should not have business code. Platform services have been referred to as technical services. Examples of platform services are: configuration service, image service, encryption service, export service, certificates service, PII anonymization service.




[When to use a service](contents.xhtml#sc3_238a)

In SOA, services should be the default choice, the way to go. You should have many more services than libraries. Here are some criteria that allow you to know when you should build a service instead of an internal shared library or something else:

  * Encapsulating business rules: Related to your core business. For example: calculate interest rate, calculate sales commission, decide the best delivery route for a driver, rent a car, book a flight, process payments.

  * Needs business persistence: Business records should be persisted and queried by services. Internal shared libraries should not access datastores like relational databases.

  * High rate of change: When the frequency of change is high - happening every week or even daily - services are the right choice. Internal shared libraries have many issues, such as breaking isolation and forcing migrations. Services require fewer migrations in comparison with libraries because they stand independent. Services are better equipped to deal with change and have better isolation.




[When to avoid services](contents.xhtml#sc3_239a)

Services are not a silver bullet; they have drawbacks and limitations as well. From our experience, we have some guidance to provide, but keep in mind that nothing is an absolute rule here, each needs more nuanced consideration:

  * Performance: Scenarios where you want the computation close to the data, so move the computation but do not move the data. Internal shared libraries are local and embedded, but services by nature are remote and there is a cost in sending data to a service. Examples would be uploading files to S3.

  * Complexity: Sometimes you have too many services, and every service you add will increase your complexity. Consider having fewer services, or adding the code to an existing service.

  * Reliability path: Every service you add must be up and running. More services require more reliability guarantees. Sometimes it is not worth paying for the reliability path. For instance, to calculate holidays, do you really need a service, with availability and reliability complications, vs a simple library that does math that rarely if ever changes?




[SOA benefits](contents.xhtml#sc3_240a)

SOAs bring many benefits as many, many companies can attest to. Here, we will briefly cover 4 benefits we have seen time and time again:

  * Faster time to market: Reduced lead time is so important to any company, and shareability, discrete components and public contracts allow teams to work quickly and without interference.

  * Lower costs and easier maintenance: With the implementation hidden and isolated, routine improvements can be delivered independently in a centralized, build once, use anywhere service. 

  * Extensibility and adaptability: Each service can extend and adapt to changing market conditions through API and/or implementation changes.

  * Independence: Services can be built and evolve in isolation, pushing forward independently of other services.




These benefits combined make SOA a true business innovation enabler: faster to develop, innately extensible, having easier maintenance, and allowing your teams to stay independent. SOA lets your teams stay focused on the business’ problems.

Let us dive into the details and give you some real examples.

[Faster time to market](contents.xhtml#sc4_241a)

Services are shared building blocks. Back to our common example, let us take a closer look at the payments service for the Acme Widget Company, where in order to drive e-commerce, the payment service supports checks and credit card payments (Figure 6.2).

Figure 6.2: Payments service supporting the E-commerce website

Let us say the business decided to pursue different markets and expand to new business lines by opening up a chain of brick-and-mortar grocery stores. Because the service is independent of the website, you can share the payments service with the grocery business (Figure 6.3). Immediately, the grocery business supports payments capabilities with checks and credit cards. You might need to accommodate cash payments too, but you are leveraging a lot of business capabilities since you do not need to start from scratch.

Because of the payments service, Acme Widget Company took less time to launch its grocery stores, due to the inherent benefits of services:

  * Shareable: Properly designed services expose an API that can be used in different circumstances. No need to code it all again - services can share capabilities to the rest of your company by default. Of course, those access points need to be by design. Reuse by sharing a database table is a bad thing!

  * Real productivity gain: By relying on something you already use, you save development, testing, and bug-fixing time and costs. Your team can have confidence that the implementation works, and hopefully it is even battle-tested.

  * Extensible: Once you add cash payments, it also can be available to your other business.

  * Win-win: In the case of bugs, your fixes are applied in a central place that transparently benefits both businesses. Later down the road, if you improve the performance of the payments service, both businesses will realize the benefits right away. 




Figure 6.3: Payments service shared across 2 business

You decide the best way to increase ACME Widget Company’s profits is to expand into new ways to engage with customers, such as:

  * Mobile app: The default, preferred form of interface for the younger generation, ACME is surely missing out on sales from folks who are looking and cannot find their mobile application to click and buy on the go.

  * Chatbot: Very popular in China or South American countries like Brazil, conversational AI is getting more and more capable, whether the earlier generation of simpler, vanilla, intent-based bots or more complex generative AI LLMs64 such as Claude’s Anthropic65 or OpenAI’s ChatGPT66. Instead of paying for an agent, customers can chat with a bot to find the right widget for their needs.

  * Voice interface: Like Google Assistant or Amazon Alexa, customers can just say, Alexa: buy me a new set of large widgets from the Acme Widget Company!




Before, you had only one interface, a website. Now you have four: website, mobile, chatbot, and voice interface. Each interface has a similar need to perform payments. With your SOA and your reusable payments service, you can leverage all that functionality and launch those new channels much faster. (Figure 6.4) Even in a single business, using service orientation pays off. 

Figure 6.4: Payments service used across four different interfaces

While these are both very different business use cases, they benefit from the same principles of SOA architecture and its shared services. Unlike microservices, which, as the name says, are micro, fine-grained services, SOA does not prescribe granularity; you might have a lot of functionalities in a service, and from the perspective of SOA, that is fine. As long as the functionality is grouped logically, such as in our discussion of Domain Driven Design (DDD) from the last chapter, it is not only fine but desired.

As a counterargument, you might think, it is not so simple. Services do not just save time: sharing a service between two businesses might mean lots of new changes in the Payment service you did not need to make before, such as:

  * Contract changes: You want to add a source field to know if the payment happened via website, mobile, text, or voice.

  * Additional integrations: Text and Voice interfaces might require other integrations with 3rd party services or other tweaks to the Payment service.

  * Data migration: Supporting new channels could mean changing database tables, leading to data migrations that affect all channels.

  * Additional testing: Each channel has its independent use cases, requiring more thorough tests.




Would not all those additional changes increase your time to market in the future? By leveraging backwards compatibility, you only need to change the service, not rewrite it from scratch partially. Such changes will not break the other consumers because you can maintain a stable and well-defined public contract. Even for the new source field there are techniques we can use to apply these changes without breaking the consumers of the payments service, which we will cover in more detail in this very chapter. Maintaining backward compatibility means you can trust that your changes would not affect consumers, allowing you to move faster to support the business.

[Lower costs and easier maintenance](contents.xhtml#sc4_242a)

By applying service-oriented thinking, you can reduce your time and cost maintaining and improving your software: a killer benefit for any SOA implementation. Without SOA, classical monoliths and distributed monoliths have serious maintenance issues, with higher cost and more difficulty due to a series of factors such as:

  * Undesired side effects: Due to high coupling, teams cannot safely work in isolation. One team releases a change that breaks a consumer, and then that consumer team ships a PR in response that breaks 5 other teams in turn, back and forth. No isolation makes simple issues hard to fix.

  * High complexity: Without clear boundaries, it is hard to understand what the code does and how it works; obscurity leads to unnecessary and accidental complexity, both forms of technical debt. The team erosion effect can follow, creating knowledge loss and slowing migrations.

  * Poor testing and feedback: Without clear isolation, systems can lack proper testing to know if you break older functionality (e.g. regression testing). An overall lack of testing coverage removes confidence when releasing software. The result is long feedback cycles, where you find errors in production rather than development.

  * High cost: Engineers are expensive; in most companies, they are your number one cost, more expensive than services, tools, you name it. Bad monoliths make maintenance more painful and more difficult, and so they impact your main technology cost, your people. Combine that with the complexity and dynamic nature of our world, with requirements changing, new legislation, vendors going out of business, and software retiring companies cannot afford painful maintenance and to keep up with the real world.




By this point, you must be thinking, how does SOA help us with all these issues? SOA helps us in many ways because services and service orientation, in general, provide boundaries. Services abstract the implementation from the interface for us; for instance, a service will abstract:

  * Programming language: Callers have no idea what language the service is built in Java, Rust, Typescript, Zig.

  * OS: Abstract the operating system. Does not matter if it is Ubuntu, Amazon Linux 2, or even Microsoft Windows.

  * Infrastructure: Hide the underlying service infrastructure, whether AWS EC2, serverless Lambdas, ECS or EKS, or on-premises - as long as you handle network addressing and keep the public contract.

  * Frameworks and libraries: You could be running Spring, just Apache CXF, or no framework.

  * Datastores: Hide whether your database is relational, NoSQL, or NewSQL.

  * Runtime dependencies: Any downstream dependencies, like other services.

  * Implementation decisions: How the code is organized: By layers, features, or domains; whether your programming style is procedural, object-oriented or functional; your use of design patterns; if you applied clean code.




Whether we say Abstract, or instead Hide, Protect, or Decouple, it would still have the same benefits and meaning. Your properly designed service has a contract - hopefully a stable contract - and a completely separate implementation. In effect, the contract creates a perimeter or boundary, protecting the internals of the service. 

Figure 6.5: Service boundary

The service boundary is like a wall; anything can be on the other side of the wall. As the caller, you cannot observe it, and in fact you are protected from any and all details. (Figure 6.5) You could have a very complex and terrible implementation, but that is fine as long as the contract is stable, clean, and easy to understand. The boundary of the service provides a safe zone to make changes and contain the blast radius at worst to our consumers as long as you do not break your public contracts and maintain backward compatibility.

Proper service boundaries make dealing with obscurity/complexity, poor testing, or long feedback cycles much easier. By decoupling the implementation from the contract, you prevent side effects and high coupling; the consumers do not see the implementation; they only see the public contract.

Consumers call services via remote communication, commonly over HTTP. When your software lives in the same deployment unit as the application and is called locally, you have fewer to no boundaries: if other software components such as internal shared libraries bring 300 libraries, you will have all these libraries in your runtime, and run the risk of binary coupling. Instead, when you have a service, consumers have no exposure to your libraries, whether 10 or 10,000. Even if you mandate a service driver, keep it thin and require few if no libraries.

Boundaries and isolation drive benefits, day after day, lowering costs and giving you an easier time to maintain and improve your software. The natural isolation of SOA provides decoupling and allows the engineering team to:

  * Total freedom of change: Freely change any abstracted concept: programming language, OS, frameworks and libraries, datastores, dependencies, or entire implementations.

  * Improve migrations: Perform any code or data migration without breaking consumers.

  * Experimentation: Experiment with new technology in a controlled and safe way, safely behind the wall of the service boundary.

  * Cost optimization: Cost can be optimized as we leverage and share the service across the business, you do not reimplement code, you have less components to maintain, and you can reduce costs by using less cloud resources.




Now, imagine doing all those things in a monolith, where you have binary coupling and few clear boundaries. Routine activities lead to maintenance headaches; isolation is key, reducing the blast radius and overall risk of changes and lowering the overall, routine risks and costs of your technology stack.

[Extensibility and adaptability](contents.xhtml#sc4_243a)

Sharing business functions and capabilities using shared SOA services is an excellent property of this style of architecture. The benefits do not stop there; there is another excellent and desired property: extensibility.

Today’s world is highly competitive and dynamic; your competitors are both local and global; every year brings change and disruption in many forms, from wars, global pandemics, economic recessions, inflation, and many other kinds of challenges. Companies need to react quickly, and be able to extend and improve existing business capabilities without starting from scratch. For instance, the US Federal Reserve recently launched its FedNow payments backbone, and now the Acme Widget Company’s payments service has to use it to move money faster to satisfy its suppliers.

Let us distinguish adaptability from extensibility as another property that the SOA style or architecture provides us. We are not necessarily talking about adding more but instead tweaking, fine grain tuning what we already have. One example of adaptability could be tweaking the existing payment service to reduce the interest rate from 2% to 1.5% for long term premium customers.

Our key point is that SOA lets you make these changes safely and quickly, extending your services or adapting them to changing market conditions, without rebuilding them or the risk of massive deployments or costly migrations.

[Independence](contents.xhtml#sc4_244a)

Proper services are independently deployed and self-contained. Services intrinsically promote teams working fast, in parallel with other projects, shipping components with less risk of interference and with high business value.

However, independence is not just one perfect, ideal state. There are degrees of independence most teams experience in the real world, because there are other constraints, some good, some bad. Governance is a necessary process in most big organizations, and its purpose is to constrain teams. In a perfect world, these constraints deliver global team alignment, drive best practices, and mandate design reviews which lead to a good and solid software architecture overall. In the real world, they often just represent artificial gates restricting growth, with architects mandating preferences that really have no benefit. These gatekeepers limit what services truly enable: total freedom of change, such as allowing a polyglot language or datastore implementation in your organization and beyond, all of which lets teams drive with speed to push the business forward. Gates and gatekeepers hold teams down.

Introducing gates all over your SDLC process will prevent your organization from scaling; having zero gates turns your company into the wild west, with teams doing whatever they want, however they want. If you want every single variable name in your contracts to be precisely the same, it will slow you down, it will lead you to sharing POJOs, leading to distributed monoliths. Are you really driving value in these choices? You will have hundreds of teams, and thousands of services. Is this the right gate for your company?

Figure 6.6: Independence vs central governance

Organizations need to balance these factors in the right way; companies are trading off standardization, reuse, a common developer experience, and multiple points of coordination against scalability, speed and independence; see Figure 6.6. Good governance covers best practices in your contracts, and promotes lightweight consistency and best practices; bad governance forces itself into the implementation, demanding the reuse of shared libraries and databases (Table 6.1):

Independence |  Central Governance  
---|---  
Good |  Bad |  Good |  Bad  
Code Deployment Implementation Details Technology Choices |  Contracts Business Domains leading to Duplication Snowflake Monitoring |  Contracts Domain Mapping Observability Stack Deployment Tools |  Naming Database Schemas Implementation Details  
  
Table 6.1: Independence vs central governance

Independence really should be a binary, ideal state: you either have it, or you do not. In reality, you will make some compromises. Just do not make the wrong ones and lose the whole point of SOA.

[Summary of SOA benefits](contents.xhtml#sc4_245a)

The overall benefit of SOA is to enable your business to go faster. You cannot afford for every new capability to require engineers to rebuild the whole universe from scratch. SOA services can be easily shared, extended, and adapted, and therefore you have leverage when your business needs change. SOA lets you build software that has so many innate benefits, your business is just better positioned to go faster. (Figure 6.7)

Let us address the SOA naysayers, or teams that reject services or microservices generally. Maybe your monolith lets you reuse all software assets. Why should you chase fads and bother with modern architecture? If you believe that, we would be surprised that you made it this far in our book, but honestly ask yourself: How fast do you deliver to market? How easy are your releases? Is it easy to make any change? Are your engineers happy? Human beings have the ability to adapt to the most difficult environments, that does not mean everything is okay, sane or sustainable.

Or take the statement: All software can enable innovation. Is this true? No, because classical monoliths or distributed monoliths are present in software and are anti-patterns, and deliver the opposite: slowness, friction, and complex change. You should know the answer why by now, anti-patterns, side effects, and amplifiers all founded on a lack of proper principles, specifically:

  * Extreme fragility: Change a table, Enum, POJO, class, or dependency in a pom.xml and you break others, and others break you, while the lack of systemic isolation creates huge problems. Fragility disrupts user experience, increases cost exponentially and eats timelines for breakfast.

  * Insane blast radius: You must worry about dozens or hundreds of classes, and millions of lines of code because the world is too big and lacks boundaries. Any change can break everything, all the time.

  * Hidden problems: You cannot easily see the impact of change or it takes you longer to see bugs, due to the lack of proper testing, lack of shift right testing67, or lack of contract testing.

  * Waterfall projects, sluggish execution: Your technology decisions and choices affect many teams, for instance, reusing bloated libraries leading to binary coupling. Everybody is slowing everybody down.

  * Tech debt, paralysis and bad amplification: Bad monoliths just lead to this end state: your software was not designed to be shared, lacks stable and public contracts, and your attempts to share business capabilities lead to more high coupling between components. Technical debt reigns throughout the stack. Predicting the outcome of change is so difficult that most attempts to improve things in reality just make it worse, amplifying your problems. Everybody is afraid of changing code, refactoring becomes a bad word, and fear of new technology is high.




This is a short list. Without proper principles in place, very bad things happen. And review this list again: every single one of these items slows everything down, no business can move quickly with a tech stack that drags around so much burden. While good principles are important in any software architecture, SOA comes with those principles built into the nature of the stack itself. Building using SOA principles makes it easy to live those benefits without falling into those anti-patterns.

When it comes to the nature of the services you build, you have choices to make. When it comes to size, by focusing on the business and having services oriented by business domain, by nature your services will be a bit bigger than a typical microservice, but will have the tendency to have the right balance between size and functionality. Of course, you are not limited to just business needs - you can have platform or technical services with SOA, like authentication, entitlements, experiments, users/profile, configuration management services, and deployment coordinator services; your imagination is the only limit. 

Figure 6.7: Summary of SOA benefits

SOA is good, it has clear benefits for your organization, but it is not always simple to implement. Let us leave you with a few more key concepts you will hear discussed about or around SOAs, that will benefit you, your designs, your business and your engineers when applied practically:

  * High abstraction and boundaries: Maintain public, stable contracts and clear service scope with defined boundaries. In previous chapters, we have covered the perils of distributed monoliths, and failing to follow these guidelines will put you at risk.

  * Separation of Concerns (SOC): In our last chapter, we covered domain mapping in detail; separating business domains in services lets you separate and group services in the way your business works. Fail to do that correctly, and you have functions that should be sitting together apart, and vice versa, ending up with weird scenarios. Imagine that your sales service orchestrates purchases and has a method to calculate sales tax. When the sales service calls the payments service to make a payment, now the payments service has to call the sales service to get the sales tax calculated. Sales tax should live either separately or in the payments service. Incorrect business domain mapping can lead to silly situations like this.

  * Interoperability: Expose service interfaces using protocols that are widely adopted, ideally open source, which will let teams consume services in various contexts and applications. Avoid writing your own protocols without a remarkably good reason, like custom hardware or a need to deal with other radically different situations than most enterprise companies encounter.

  * Loose coupling: Reducing the information the services know about each other. Do not leak implementation details; that is a great way to pin yourself down to a specific technology and lose your freedom of choice.

  * API contract documentation: We will talk about contracts in a moment, but investing time in a tested, comprehensive OpenAPI68 specification will always benefit consumers. And everyone benefits from a good example.

  * Dynamic registry and discovery: Make your services discoverable by humans with an excellent service registry and inventory; help make services discoverable by service consumers with tools like Netflix Eureka. ([https://github.com/Netflix/eureka](https://github.com/Netflix/eureka))




We will leave you with one final statement: doing SOA with the right principles allows us to scale with engineers and with business needs. You cannot go fast by doing wrong, only when doing right and doing the right thing can you go fast.

[Contract first](contents.xhtml#sc2_246a)

When coding a service, there are two approaches we could use (Figure 6.8). We could start with the implementation, where you work on your business functions and data models, and then figure out the API - this approach is known as implementation first. Beginning with the implementation code is many engineers’ first instinct; how can I figure out what the API needs to be until I design the database model or build a first version?

Alternatively, we could start with the contract, also known as the contract first approach. Start writing the contract code first and create some artifacts representing the contract, WADL for REST, WSDL for SOAP, and OpenAPI for any modern API. Tools can take that OpenAPI yaml file and directly generate contract code based on that yaml file. 

Figure 6.8: Contract first vs. implementation first

Each approach has tradeoffs, and it can seem difficult to choose which approach will be best for a given project. For us, it comes down to a key question: are you building a new service? Or exposing a service on top of an existing component?

[New service](contents.xhtml#sc3_247a)

If you are creating a new service, the answer is clear: you should choose contract first. If you start with the implementation, the contract will reflect the implementation, and this is absolutely wrong. Leaking the implementation details into the contract is one of the easiest ways to stumble into a high coupling scenario, and nullifying your freedom to change, a key SOA benefit.

In proper services, your contract should always be an abstraction of the implementation. Imagine building a service on top of a database with 3 tables and 10 columns on each table, and to you it makes sense to have a contract with 3 operations associated with each table and 10 parameters, with each parameter named after a column. (After all, why not reuse the column name – it is already there?) Such a contract is very wrong; this is not a contract nor a proper service, you are just blindly exposing a database table, a clear anti-pattern we will call remote views. If the contract does not provide abstraction, you just have complexity.

As you go to contract first, it does not make much difference if you want to start by the contract code directly and then generate the metadata (WADL, WSDL, or OpenAPI yaml), whether manually or via runtime by generation via annotations. The same goes the other way around, start with the metadata and generate the contract code. However, this second approach does not always generate the cleanest code and will likely need manual tweaks, but it is all a matter of style and preferences.

[Existing component](contents.xhtml#sc3_248a)

In this scenario, you need to create a service or expose an API on top of an implementation that already exists. Maybe you might have a classical monolith or desktop application that needs to expose a function to an outside caller, or an existing service with a terrible contract that is either too big or too small and needs a rebuild, or any number of other architectures or situations.

Regardless of the nitty-gritty details, you might need to switch between implementation first and contract first in phases to make progress and deliver. You still want to go contract first wherever possible, but you likely need to make compromises, where the first versions of the contract will reflect a lot of the existing data model or whatever form of contract that makes progress. Migrations will be covered in depth in the book’s last two chapters, [Chapter 9](ch09.xhtml), Code Migrations and [Chapter 10](ch10.xhtml), Data Migrations.

Always ask yourself: Who is going to be calling the contract in the existing code? If you do not have consumers for this new contract yet, imagine it is a new UI. Depending on the volume and complexity of the refactoring you are forced to do, sure you might need to make some compromises, but if it is a new UI, you do not have consumers yet so take advantage of that freedom; and even with existing code, you can always reach out and propose a better contract. Do not limit yourself!

[Coding contracts with OpenAPI](contents.xhtml#sc3_249a)

The OpenAPI specification is widely used across the industry for describing service contracts, also called APIs. OpenAPI has good tooling for several languages like Java, NodeJS, Go, Python, PHP, Ruby, and others, visual editors, code-generating tools, the ability to mock data, validations, security, and more.

Figure 6.9: OpenAPI UI

In Figure 6.9, we have the OpenAPI UI shown from a published service metadata file in OpenAPI yaml format. Let us dive into the code to produce such a result. The full code can be checked out here: [https://github.com/diegopacheco/fixing-monoliths-book/tree/main/chapter6/openapi-contract](https://github.com/diegopacheco/fixing-monoliths-book/tree/main/chapter6/openapi-contract), where you can take a look at the OpenAPI yaml file and all the Java code directly.

The ProfileContract contract and implementation example is important. We will refer back to it throughout the rest of this chapter.

All the code is in one project, but the right design would be breaking it into at least two maven modules to separate the contract from the implementation: profile-service-contract and profile-service-impl. Some engineers might generate this split in different forms, like calling the contract an api and the implementation as server.

Note one critical thing about the project- no framework coupling. Do not couple your contract with Spring Boot, Spring, Tomcat, or any framework. Remember, contracts need to be without dependencies. Our sample application is not 100% complete; we are skipping the validations often present in a service class and the persistence in the form of a DAO or repository interface. Our focus of this example is just to show OpenAPI in action.

  1. @RestController
  2. @RequestMapping("/service/profile")
  3. public class ProfileController implements ProfileContract {
  4.   5. private String pattern = "yyyy-MM-dd";
  6. private SimpleDateFormat dateFormat = new SimpleDateFormat(pattern);
  7.   8. @GetMapping("/user/{id}")
  9. @Override
  10. public User getUserById(@PathVariable UUID id) {
  11. // We are just echoing the User back from the UUID
  12. // This is just a sample, regular service would do validations(Service), and call database(DAO).
  13. return new User(id,dateFormat.format(new Date()),"default-"+id+"@company.io");
  14. }
  15.   16. @Override
  17. @GetMapping("/user/batch/{ids}")
  18. @ResponseStatus(HttpStatus.OK)
  19. public List<User> getUserByIds(@PathVariable List<UUID> ids) {
  20. // We are just echoing the List<User> back from the UUIDs
  21. // This is just a sample, regular service would do validations(Service), and call database(DAO).
  22. List<User> result = ids.stream().map( (u)-> {
  23. String normalize = u.toString().replaceAll("-","_");
  24. return new User(u,dateFormat.format(new Date()),"default"+normalize+"@company.io");
  25. }).collect(Collectors.toList());
  26. return result;
  27. }
  28. }



Code 6.1: Rest/JSON exposure via Spring Boot with Spring Doc

Reviewing Code 6.1, we have a simple REST controller where we are implementing the service contract ProfileContract so the contract is not coupled with Spring or Spring Boot. We are mapping two rest operations, one (/user/{id}) where you can pass a user ID (a UUID), and another (/user/batch/{ids}) where you can ask for multiple UUIDs separated by a comma. RFC 2616 does not specify a limit for maximum query string length. However, remember that many HTTP servers limit query length. For instance, Tomcat’s default is 8k, but you can increase it to 65k.

OpenAPI is an excellent standard. You should consider using it in your services, just make sure you do not couple your contracts with the OpenAPI, Spring Doc, or Spring-* libraries.

[Backward compatibility](contents.xhtml#sc3_250a)

As we covered in the last chapter, backward compatibility is a superpower. When you have services contracts breaking all the time, you create a lack of stability and slow down your whole development process; a big problem and productivity killer. In a classical or modular monolith, at least all the code is in one place and you can use your IDE to figure out the refactoring. With distribution, different codebases in different projects, and deployed to different machines, you do not have that simplicity; service contracts must be STABLE and not breaking frequently. Rapid change is fine; constantly breaking consumers is not.

To be backward compatible, we must handle breaking changes differently than we deal with non-breaking changes. So first of all, we need to understand the difference between breaking changes and non-breaking changes. (Figure 6.10)

Figure 6.10: Breaking changes vs non-breaking changes

In a nutshell, breaking changes break your consumers’ API code and cannot be ignored. Non-breaking changes can be ignored by your consumers until they need the functionality or data. In short, you can freely make contract changes in your code if they are non-breaking changes.

Let us take the ProfileContract we reviewed in Code 6:1 and start making some changes! First, let us introduce a breaking change with a new operation, getUserByEmail (Code 6.2).

  1. import java.util.List;
  2. import java.util.UUID;
  3.   4. /**
  5. * Contract Specifications: <br/>
  6. * <b>Ordering</b> : Not Guaranteed. <br/>
  7. * <b>Date Format</b>: yyyy-mm-dd <br/>
  8. * <b>Encryption</b> : Cipher Text <br/>
  9. * <b>Errors </b> : None, user not found returns empty user <br/>
  10. * <b>Idempotency</b>: All operations <br/>
  11. *
  12. */
  13. public interface ProfileContract {
  14. User getUserById(UUID id);
  15. List<User> getUserByIds(List<UUID> ids);
  16.   17. // Non-Breaking Change: Add new Operation
  18. User getUserByEmail(String email);
  19. }



Code 6.2: Contract change with a non-breaking change

getUserByEmail is a new operation and no consumer can call it yet. Consumers can completely ignore it. Consumers continue to call the other two REST operations without interruption, needing no code changes.

Let us say we had a driver for the ProfileContract, it would need to change to introduce the new operation. Consumers can get the new driver version, and they will not require code changes because we are not changing any existing method. We are adding a new method. Therefore, all good. Nothing breaks on the consumers’ side.

Now let us see a code example of a breaking change.

  1. import java.util.List;
  2. import java.util.UUID;
  3.   4. /**
  5. * Contract Specifications: <br/>
  6. * <b>Ordering</b> : Not Guaranteed. <br/>
  7. * <b>Date Format</b>: yyyy-mm-dd <br/>
  8. * <b>Encryption</b> : Cipher Text <br/>
  9. * <b>Errors </b> : None, user not found returns empty user <br/>
  10. * <b>Idempotency</b>: All operations <br/>
  11. *
  12. */
  13. public interface ProfileContract {
  14. User getUserById(UUID id);
  15. List<User> getUserByIds(String[] idArray);
  16. }



Code 6.3: Contract change with a breaking change

Looking at this piece of code (Code 6.3), we can see that the batch operation getUsersById has breaking changes:

  * Breaking change 1: Parameter name has changed type and name from ids to idArray.

  * Breaking change 2: Parameter type has changed from List <UUID> to String[](Array of Strings)




Consumers of getUserByIds cannot ignore this change, because the format, types, and expectations of the contract have changed. Consumers who only use getUserById might think they are safe; they have no code written against getUserByIds. While this might be true in some protocols like JSON over REST/HTTP, it is not true in other protocols like RMI. And if you use a service driver, it will be a mandatory upgrade for all as otherwise there is broken code out there, waiting to be invoked and trigger errors. Even if you never used getUserByIDs and do not care about the change, now there is work.

Sometimes breaking changes are inevitable, and there is no better way; other times, an engineer introduced it, it was not caught and now it is in production. Constantly breaking changes are bad, your development process will suffer and be very slow, but there are still options to try to cope with it:

  * Let it break: Not great, but sometimes just forcing everyone to change is not a big deal. Does not scale.

  * API versioning: Introduce the change in a new version of the public contract exposed by the service; there are several strategies for this approach such as:

    * Version using FQDN69, e.g. profilev1.acme.internal vs profilev2.acme.internal

    * Version using the URL path e.g. /profile/v1/ vs /profile/v2/

    * Version using the package level or object level e.g. ProfileContractV1 vs ProfileContractV2

    * Use version metadata, e.g. HTTP Header, version:v1 or version:v2

  * Indirect backward compatibility: Translate old calls to the new contract in some middle layer: client driver, API Gateway, routing layer like ESB, Envoy, nginx, or AWS ALB.




Versioning is very powerful but has some obvious drawbacks if abused. For instance, how many versions are you maintaining in parallel? More than 3 might be hard. Are you duplicating code to create these versions? You might need to do some object conversion in order to avoid having 3 code bases. 

Figure 6.11: Simple service supporting 3 contracts (3 versions)

Figure 6.11 introduces a service with 3 versions of a contract: V1, V2, and V3. We can do proper backward compatibility by versioning the contracts and introducing a new major version of the public contract every time that we have a breaking change, this way we avoid breaking consumers.

[SOA and isolation](contents.xhtml#sc2_251a)

Perhaps the most relevant contribution from any service/microservice style of architecture, that isolation is everything. As we learned in [Chapter 2](ch02.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Lack of Isolation, isolation needs to be applied to all elements of a service, but there are 3 key areas of any service that you must push to have perfect isolation whenever possible: Public contracts, libraries, and datastores. These areas are your most important battleground or “frontiers” in the fight to protect your service. All three frontiers should be 100% isolated.

Preserving isolation is critical because you want to deliver services with low coupling, high cohesion, and independence from other services. In some areas, our industry did a reasonable job applying the isolation principle:

  * Code bases: Often one per project in a version control system like git/GitHub.

  * Deployment pipelines: CI/CD systems that support independent deployment jobs, such as with TravisCI or Jenkins.

  * Testing suites: Java has multiple very popular test suites, such as JUnit or TestNG.

  * Servers/containers: Running your service or component on its own dedicated instance, with virtualization systems like OpenStack or VMware or cloud solutions like AWS EC2, ECS/EKS.




However, when we look to the three essential frontiers, from our experience, this is where we see industry isolation practices being far from desirable and reflecting anti-patterns in truth:

  * Datastores/databases: Sharing tables, DB users, and schemas across services.

  * Libraries: Binary coupling, hidden configs, non-obvious dependency traps.

  * Public contracts: Badly designed, hidden contracts, leaking implementation details by reflecting table specifics.




Getting the isolation principle right, we must apply it right for each frontier, which means different things. Such complex tasks require constant attention to reinforce principles during the software development life cycle. 

Figure 6.12: Summary isolation principle

Let us deep dive into how we can have proper isolation for the three frontiers, Datastores, libraries, and public contracts. As we learned in [Chapter 2](ch02.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Lack of Isolation, isolation delivers benefits by reducing the coupling between components and the blast radius. Isolation across our three frontiers requires different treatments from engineers and architects (Figure 6.12). We can only have isolation if we pay attention to key software design and architecture decisions, we make during the complete engineering lifecycle; these complex decision points can come throughout the life of a service, from conception through launch and even decommissioning, and making correct decisions requires constant attention to properly support such complex tasks.

To reinforce a key point from this book: software architecture and design matters. Often, they are not being considered carefully, or even completely ignored, especially after launch, which leads to issues. As much as anything in software architecture, awareness of the principles is key. Awareness is great, but we must go beyond awareness to culture, where these principles are an ingrained part of your engineering / development culture. Culture is the key word here, not process; the key principles need to be a living, essential part of how all teams function, not a wiki page that does not reflect reality or a launch checklist that someone just checks off and moves on.

[Isolate datastores](contents.xhtml#sc3_252a)

Remember [Chapter 2](ch02.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Lack of Isolation: datastores should not be shared. It is possible that your service uses purpose-built, dedicated databases and applies polyglot persistence70, using the right tool for the job. However, suppose your service uses a single database such as MySQL or Postgres but there could be plenty of NoSQL datastore like Cassandra, Neo4J, or Riak. You must not share this database access with other services.

Service to service communication must happen via the service interface, never by any other means: accessing the database or manipulating a hidden shared file. To be explicit, properly invoking the service interface communication means:

  * Purposeful exposure: Call what is exposed on the public contract.

  * Remote invocation: You use whatever communication mechanism the service provides like JSON over HTTP/REST, gRPC, GraphQL, or even SOAP/XML for legacy.




Figure 6.13: Isolate databases

As you can see in Figure 6.13, the ACME Widget Company needs its Sales service to call the payments service; the payments service could expose its APIs through a simple REST call returning JSON, or gRPC with Protobuf; it really does not matter. What does matter is that the call only goes through the API; there is no direct database access. Only the payments service has direct access to its datastores, Postgres and Cassandra. Similarly, sales has only one database, MySQL, and it is the only service to talk to that database.

From a DevOps perspective, you can share the terraform script to provision databases and infrastructure components; but ideally, we should deploy each application and database on its own dedicated clusters. Infrastructure isolation by having different clusters is important because it will reduce the blast radius in the case of failure. If one service exhausts its resources, this resource starvation71 will not necessarily take down other services or applications, essential for availability and reliability.

All companies are different, and you might be pressured to reduce costs and share clusters. Wherever possible, do not do this in production! Push back. It is sometimes reasonable to save costs by having smaller setups of these clusters in a non-prod environment, or even shared clusters. Cost savings are always great for your business, but not so much if they encourage engineers to break isolation. Sharing clusters could let a team start developing a project by calling databases across services, breaking isolation, and before anyone notices, the team has written a lot of code, the ship has sailed, and now you have an expensive fix on your hands. Applying the right principles sooner than later will always pay off.

[Isolate libraries](contents.xhtml#sc3_253a)

To recap library isolation from [Chapter 2](ch02.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Lack of Isolation, libraries can break isolation, especially internal shared libraries. Internal shared libraries need to be isolated. Isolation works differently with libraries. A lack of isolation in libraries is one of the most significant issues in our industry that still needs attention. To isolate libraries it is critical that we reduce amplifying effects. We are talking about these bloated libraries, which have a simple function but pull dozens or even hundreds of dependencies. Delivering and using Lean Libraries is an essential step in isolating this frontier. Internal shared libraries need to be maintained and have an active owner.

Figure 6.14: Coupling services via libraries

At first glance, it might be unclear how internal shared libraries create coupling between services. In Figure 6.14, we are back to the ACME Widget Company’s Payments and Sales services; databases have been omitted for space and to focus on the libraries. While these two services have isolated public contracts and isolated datastores, they are indirectly coupled by their internal shared libraries, utils and Spring Bean @Configurations, two common patterns we covered in [Chapter 4](ch04.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Internal Shared Libraries.

To recap, utils libraries often serve as a dumping ground, from simple function utilities like operations for String manipulations, to data validations, number formatting, or common validations for phone number, emails or similar data. Because of the wide variety of concerns it is very easy for business rules to leak into this kind of library, which often is what always happens. Worse than that, utils libraries often bring other libraries for automatic data validation like Hibernate.

Common Spring bean configurations/annotations seem innocent enough to share, but now services do not own their configurations. The price you will pay is trying to figure out between endless layers of delegation to make sense of libraries to fix bugs.

By using these two libraries, the payments and sales services have effectively committed to upgrading these libraries when there is a security patch or bugfix likely forcing all services to migrate at nearly the same time or in a big bang. Binary coupling makes this worse; in our example, data validators brought in Hibernate as a dependency, and the Spring Bean configurations likely Spring. Now upgrades can bring dependency conflicts, and the Payments and Sales teams cannot upgrade their services in isolation any more. Such big migrations are expensive and take a long time, likely leading to long and complex migrations to coordinate across teams, with lots of bugs and challenges for troubleshooting. And of course, Acme Widget company likely has a large services ecosystem, with tens to hundreds of services, so all of these impacts are spread far wider than just two. All of this reduces the key SOA benefit of having Lower Costs and Easier Maintenance, and limiting teams if they want to experiment with new technology.

In both cases, Acme Widget Company engineers created and shared these libraries with the best of intentions: people always see reuse as a good thing, but reuse can be a trap, with its own side effects and consequences. Reuse is not a free lunch. All this results because libraries are often taken for granted, and people do not realize that reuse has a price which is high coupling. By failing to actively manage libraries, the problem can also lurk invisibly, only noticed when it is too late and your library proliferation starts actively interfering with technology migration projects.

What is worse is that all of the libraries in our example are optional! All the side effects and high coupling could be avoided by not creating the libraries; did ACME Widget Company engineers ask themselves are they truly required? Often, libraries are created for the wrong reasons, such as:

  * Bad reuse: Reuse to avoid typing or shallow reuse.

  * Reuse by accident: Premature optimization; engineers think some team might re-use their code, but it never happens. Reuse needs to happen by design not by accident72.

  * Opinionated choices: Engineers might prefer to use hibernate validators instead of using if statements or something similar.

  * Unnecessary abstractions: Sharing complex validations objects rather than straightforward code.




Reuse by accident has a second meaning, which means coupling via reuse happens as an accident, instead of being a conscious choice. Code decisions can lead to reuse by accident73.

Remember the right reasons to create an internal shared library should be:

  * Criticality: It is a complex, risky, and involved problem, i.e. encryption.

  * Performance: Building a service makes the operation slow and is the wrong call, i.e. just moving files to S3.




It is important to think about the notion of essential and non-essential internal shared libraries, and to decide on what matters for your company, you need clear criteria to guide you on when to create a new internal shared library or not. Every company is different, and you should create your own guide, but here are some hints.

  * Avoid creating these non-essential internal shared libraries:

    * Utils

    * Constants and common enums and POJOSs

    * Configuration Beans (@Configuration Spring Beans)

    * Initialization and initializers

    * Internal rest clients

    * Common validations

    * Security setup: TLS, mTLS, certificates

    * Wrappers: around queue systems, reporting frameworks, and other clients

  * Possible candidates for essential internal shared libraries:

    * Observability: centralized logging, instrumentation/metrics, distributed tracing

    * Dynamic properties: feature flags and experiments




We emphasize the word possible because every company has different problems and realities. It could be that possible essential libraries do not need to be developed at all, and you can just use open-source solutions or rely on 3rd party patterns and vendors. However, you still want all libraries to be lean; the properties are the same no matter who implements them.

Once you do an internal shared library for the right reasons, essentially, you need to ensure that the library created is Lean. To recap the lessons from [Chapter 4](ch04.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Internal Shared Libraries:

  * Be really necessary and add value.

  * Have few or no dependencies.

  * Dependencies also need to be cherry-picked and necessary.

  * An internal shared library is not a service; you should make compromises.

  * Code reuse should not be the main reason to create an internal shared library.

  * Opinionated choices and big frameworks need to be avoided.




[Sidebar: flavors versus bridges in libraries and monoliths](contents.xhtml#sc4_254a)

Ownership is key to properly maintaining a shared library or any piece of shared monolith code. Once an internal shared library is being used by dozens or hundreds of services, or deeply within your classical monolith, solid architectural design and leadership is essential, ideally from a single owner. Any code with no owner, especially internal shared libraries, is easily misused because engineers might want to avoid migrations and refactoring and take shortcuts that will cost a lot in the long run. What is tricky sometimes is that the correct and incorrect approaches look the same, bringing us to Flavors vs Bridges.

A flavor is when you have the same algorithm, logic, or solution copied and pasted to different places with few or no changes. Imagine if you have ice cream; you might have flavors like chocolate, vanilla, or strawberry. Flavors are best illustrated with an example.

Figure 6.15: The origins of flavors

In Figure 6.15, we see that we have an internal shared library that can generate predictable IDs, and we can use this id to do persistence in our services. Right now, the internal shared library has only one class called IDGenerator.java. While the IDGenerator.java class generates random sequential ids. The Reports Service team wants to change that code to return a UUID instead of BigInteger for improved isolation, safety and security. But now they have some problems:

  * No owner: No owner exists for this internal shared library, so no authority to review the proposed design change and no one to ask questions to. Like many unmanaged internal shared libraries, there is no documentation and no comments as well.

  * No tests: Such libraries typically have no tests; they rely on consumers to test the code.

  * No consumer inventory: So many teams use this library; you need to know exactly who the consumers are to find out who is introducing bugs or to track down bad actors.

  * Feature factory push: The Reports team is under business pressure to release many features, and only has a little time to think.




Such reality is widespread in our industry; it is a mix of technical complexity and high business pressure. Fear is real for developers, who just want to finish their features quickly. A new class is created called: NewGenerator.java, and the engineer refactors their code and keeps the old class there: IDGenerator.java. See how a new flavor is created? Later another developer might think that UUID is too large, prefers BigInteger to also save some space on the database but wants the IDs incremented differently. So just copy the NewGenerator.java and create a third flavor: AlternativeGenerator.java. Now you have three flavors (Figure 6.16). 

Figure 6.16: Three flavors

Flavors can be manifested in various ways; for this example, you see flavors on the same internal shared library; however, there are other possibilities, like the flavor being present in a monolith, a big service, or even as a dependency of another internal shared library.

Now imagine the following scenario: we found a fundamental flaw with the AlternativeGenerator flavor, and people should not use it anymore. Same context, same result; decommissioning the class does not happen, and the flavor just stays there. Now developers come and go through team erosion, and the current team does not know which one is right and which one is wrong. By accident, some developers end up using the wrong flavor, AlternativeGenerator, and end up with bugs because of that, spending hours troubleshooting; this is how flavors become landmines.

At first glance, having flavors might sound like a good thing because it gives you the ability to reuse existing code with some tweaks. Internal shared libraries make this even worse because they are typically unmanaged and done quickly to satisfy the business without proper software design or architecture. We call library flavors an anti-pattern and a form of technical debt. Flavors can happen even in well managed code or lean libraries if you don’t watch out.

Summarizing the big issues with flavors:

  * Obscurity leading to complexity: Lack of documentation and ownership means no-one can tell right from wrong. What class should I use - who knows?

  * Difficult troubleshooting / rework: Untangling the issues with flavors can be impossible, with different teams creating and leaving them to rot. You cannot reason with the Big Ball of Mud. A lot of time can be wasted troubleshooting basic issues.

  * Landmine effect: The same trap of getting multiple developers over time, people suffering from the same problem over and over: a classic landmine.

  * Poor time to market: All of these issues slow the engineering cycle, affecting the productivity cycle. Many failed attempts and months to deliver.




Figure 6.17: Parallel Bridges in action  
Source: [https://hudsonvalleypost.com/watch-the-tappan-zee-bridge-transform-into-mario-cuomo-bridge/](https://hudsonvalleypost.com/watch-the-tappan-zee-bridge-transform-into-mario-cuomo-bridge/)

Now let us take the same situation but apply a different angle; let us talk about bridges instead of flavors. You build a bridge when you have a river or sea between two cities in order to have a need to build a road between them. But have you ever been taught how engineers build or fix bridges? Imagine the bridge is too small, and you need to have more cars on the bridge. Are you stopping all cars from both cities from using the bridge while you fix the bridge? Imagine that this bridge is connecting an island to a city; stopping all traffic on the bridge is unacceptable. So what engineers do is create a second bridge side by side with the original bridge, also known as parallel bridges (Figure 6.17). Hold on a second, but this sounds like a flavor, right? What is the difference?

Flavors are unfinished bridges but one is technical debt and the other removes it. For bridges, all those unnecessary, copied implementations are replaced, all uses decommissioned, and the code removed. Flavors are anti-patterns because we made the problem worse, and we should have tackled the problem with the right engineering approach. Flavors and bridges are almost the same, the same strategy, but one finishes and the other just rots. But bridges are a good thing, and flavors are an anti-pattern. Let us apply our generator problem in the same context, but let us say we do it slightly differently now. We will use a different approach to address this problem:

  * Inventory: We will build an inventory of the customers and understand their use cases.

  * Testing: We will add tests to discover all the public contracts on the internal shared library.

  * Refactor and cleanup: We will refactor all consumer code and delete dead code/flavors. Deleting dead code is a must! cleaning up is what will make the bridge not become a service.




In short, it is all about approach. Bridges require relentless long term leadership commitment because you might have the good intention to finish the bridge but might be forced to not finish the bridge due to other forces. Therefore, your unfinished bridge turns into a flavor. The forces working against you could be:

  * Team erosion: Engineers coming and going.

  * Change: Regular or black swan event. Change of priorities due to more urgent business matters; change of leadership, and the priority is ignored; or a dramatic change, such as a pandemic, war, or economic slowdown causing the decommission to be forgotten.




Like anything in software architecture, there are trade-offs and scenarios where building bridges via refactoring is also not the right call.

[Isolated public contracts](contents.xhtml#sc3_255a)

Contracts should be public and stable, and stay independent of any language implementation. When companies start being monoglot, only using one language, they get lazy and start sharing objects in their contracts, rather than sticking to conceptual types: reusing POJOs, enums, Data Transfer Objects (DTO), and Value Objects (VO) in their public contracts, shipped in JARs. Gains are marginal, but the coupling is enormous, dangerous, and exposes you to additional anti-patterns:

  * Using libraries: Once you have your contract distributed via a JAR, and code in git, losing control is easy. Anyone can add unnecessary dependencies such as aspects, heavy frameworks, and other internal shared libraries.

  * Migration inertia: Even if you had just one contract-commons.jar module, you would still need to ensure all your hundreds or thousands of services are using it and on the right version. People might not update at all, or by updating, they might get issues if changes lack backward compatibility.

  * Wrong principles: People have the false illusion that by having a common set of POJOs, people will reuse the same objects in their contracts consistently and properly, but that is hard to govern and often not the case.

  * Binary coupling: Ultimately, you are coupling your public contract with other projects and internal shared libraries; that exposes services unnecessarily to more dependencies, and that is bad.




Let’s go back to our ProfileContract example and show how you can best isolate its public contract. 

1\. import java.util.List;

2\. import java.util.UUID;

3\. 

4\. public interface ProfileContract {

5\. User getUserById(UUID id);

6\. List<User> getUserByIds(List<UUID> ids);

7\. }

Code 6.4: Public Contract for Profile Service Interface

As a reminder, your public contract is the one interface where you will define all your service’s capabilities. The contract must have properties like:

  * Clean: No implementation details leak to consumers.

  * Simple: Easy to understand by consumers.

  * Bare: Does not have dozens and hundreds of operations.

  * Lean: Does not have external dependencies besides the local objects defined in the project.

  * No binary coupling: No imports besides the standard Java JDK.




Our ProfileContract (Code 6:4) has all these properties. It is clean; you can’t tell if the service uses a relational database like Postgres, NoSQL database like Cassandra, or a document database like AWS DocumentDB. This is an excellent property because we want the public contract to be about business capabilities, and we want to reflect something other than the underlying persistence storage. It is simple, in that there are operations with descriptive names and arguments, and bare, as there are only two operations. No external dependencies or imports are present beyond the standard Java JDK.
