The public operations return a User object; let us take a closer look (Code 6.5).

  1. import java.util.Objects;
  2. import java.util.UUID;
  3.   4. public class User {
  5.   6. private UUID id;
  7. private String createdAt;
  8. private String email;
  9.   10. public User(){}
  11.   12. public User(UUID id, String createdAt, String email) {
  13. this.id = id;
  14. this.createdAt = createdAt;
  15. this.email = email;
  16. }
  17.   18. public UUID getId() {
  19. return id;
  20. }
  21. public void setId(UUID id) {
  22. this.id = id;
  23. }
  24. public String getCreatedAt() {
  25. return createdAt;
  26. }
  27. public void setCreatedAt(String createdAt) {
  28. this.createdAt = createdAt;
  29. }
  30. public String getEmail() {
  31. return email;
  32. }
  33. public void setEmail(String email) {
  34. this.email = email;
  35. }
  36.   37. @Override
  38. public boolean equals(Object o) {
  39. if (this == o) return true;
  40. if (o == null || getClass() != o.getClass()) return false;
  41. User user = (User) o;
  42. return Objects.equals(id, user.id) && Objects.equals(email, user.email);
  43. }
  44. @Override
  45. public int hashCode() {
  46. return Objects.hash(id, email);
  47. }
  48. @Override
  49. public String toString() {
  50. return "User{" +
  51. "id=" + id +
  52. ", createdAt='" + createdAt + '\'' +
  53. ", email='" + email + '\'' +
  54. '}';
  55. }
  56. }



Code 6.5: Public contract for profile service - User object

As you can see, the User object is a simple POJO; it has: an empty constructor, a default full constructor, getters and setters, equals/hashCode operations and a toString method.

If you pay close attention, you will see some interesting properties:

  * Lean: The User object imports nothing beyond java standard JDK; no open source or internal shared library dependencies.

  * Simple: Basic fields like id, email, and creation date.

  * Interoperable: Date is in String, in favor of intrinsic interoperability across all languages, a key part of the SOA Manifesto.

  * No business rules: The User contract object itself enforces no business rules.




Implementing the contract would be done in a different project and module. While the implementation and contract can all live on the same code base, they must be different projects because to avoid coupling the public contract with the service implementation, which must be avoided at all costs.

[Design reviews](contents.xhtml#sc4_256a)

First of all, you need to be performing design reviews. Code reviews are not enough, because engineers are often not always concerned with the integrity of an SOA contract. Architects can be your allies in reviewing the contract and ensuring it is consistent and correct. Here are some dimensions you want to consider in a design review for public contracts:

  * Review dependencies: Do we have zero dependencies?

  * Documentation: Is the interface clear and easy to understand? Can we tell what the service does by just reading the contract? Can we add some Javadoc for non-obvious things?

  * Service responsibility: Is the service doing too much? Should it be applied in a different service, not because of the number of lines of code in the implementation but because of having too many different business capabilities in the contract?

  * Cross business domain coupling: Does the service span multiple business domains or cross multiple business lines in an awkward way? This can be a big red flag of deeper problems.

  * Avoid delegation: Is the contract the same as in another service? Is this just a delegator?

  * Consistency: Do we have consistency in naming? Are names understandable and descriptive? That is, email is called email, not called field34.

  * Stable Contracts: Is the contract stable, or are we breaking it every sprint?




[Automating contract health](contents.xhtml#sc4_257a)

Besides design review, it is possible to apply some automation to enforce best behavior. Automation alone is not enough; architecture and design requires human judgment and then follow through by teams; what we will teach you here can help to flag actions and point out smells, but cannot fix your problems for you.

Measuring isolation in the public contracts is straightforward; the contract project should have zero dependencies, easy to check in your pom.xml (maven) or build.gradle (gradle). You can have testing dependencies, but there must be zero runtime or compile time dependencies. Simple steps to automate this:

  * Inspect your poms: Simple string matching or grep checks can verify zero dependencies.

  * Parse your artifacts: Parse the dependency file; you can do many more checks but at a minimum count that you have zero dependencies.

  * Use plugins: (Code 6:6) Using the maven-dependency-plugin, install all dependencies in a folder; the folder should be empty. Once you run: mvn clean install, you should check if you have a folder named target/lib. If the folder exists and has JARs, your contract is breaking the dependency rule, if the folder does not exist or is empty great you have no dependencies.




1\. <plugin>

2\. <groupId>org.apache.maven.plugins</groupId>

3\. <artifactId>maven-dependency-plugin</artifactId>

4\. <executions>

5\.  <execution>

6\.  <id>copy-dependencies</id>

7\. <phase>prepare-package</phase>

8\. <goals>

9\. <goal>copy-dependencies</goal>

10\. </goals>

11\.  <configuration>

12\.  <outputDirectory>${project.build.directory}/lib</outputDirectory>

13\. <overWriteReleases>false</overWriteReleases>

14\.  <overWriteSnapshots>false</overWriteSnapshots>

15\.  <overWriteIfNewer>true</overWriteIfNewer>

16\.  <excludeScope>test</excludeScope>

17\.  <includeScope>compile</includeScope>

18\.  <includeScope>runtime</includeScope>

19\.  </configuration>

20\.  </execution>

21\.  </executions>

22\.  </plugin>

Code 6.6: Checking for dependencies

[Non obvious things](contents.xhtml#sc4_258a)

In this chapter, we have taught you the basics of contract design and the importance of isolation. Our very simple public contract is great, and we have avoided some clear and obvious pitfalls. But a lot of other contract concerns are non-obvious, hidden contract behavior that leads to bad outcomes. You might not see them by just looking at the public contract code definition. Let us go back to our ProfileContract example (Code 6:4, 6:5) and look for some hidden risks to consider:

  * Date formats: Is it: mm-dd-yyyy? dd/mm/yyyy? ISO 860174, yyyy-mm-dd? Often people use dates, when they need to specify time and time zone as well.

  * Strings: Strings can hide a lot of hidden contract details. Document any custom format you might have implicit in your strings.

  * Encryption: Does your business consider the email PII? If so, does it need to be protected through encryption? If so, what key or protocol is being used? How will consumers access that key?

  * Ordering: getUserByIds returns a list of users; what is the order of that list? Is it ordered alphabetically by email, or UUID, or in the order requested in the batch request? Ascending or descending?

  * Service Level Objectives (SLOs)75: Do consumers require specific SLOs or non-functional expectations like performance to be met such as:

    * How fast should the service return? Within 10ms-100ms or even faster?

    * What is the impact of downtime? What uptime metrics do your consumers want? Can you afford it?

  * Exceptions and errors: What kind of errors would the service return? How do consumers discover them?

  * Idempotency: Can we safely call these methods over and over?

  * Exposure: Is the service exposing its operations by REST? By SOAP? By gRPC and protocol buffers? Or some other way?




A common way to stumble here is to fall back to dependencies in order to address some of these concerns. To make the encryption method clear we could use an internal encryption library, or to specify the date format using Joda-Time. In this way, we could do a better job of making these contract expectations clear. But then we will break the no dependencies rule and couple our service with Joda-Time or an internal shared library. Not worth it!

We do have better options such as testing and documentation. You can simply write some Javadoc, clarifying some of the behavior (Code 6:7), and you can add proper contract testing and deliver that behavior with consistency.

1\. import java.util.List;

2\. import java.util.UUID;

3\. 

4\. /**

5\.  * Contract Specifications: <br/>

6\.  * <b>Ordering</b> : Not Guaranteed. <br/>

7\.  * <b>Date Format</b>: yyyy-mm-dd <br/>

8\.  * <b>Encryption</b> : Ciphertext <br/>

9\.  * <b>Errors </b> : None, user not found returns empty user <br/>

10\.  * <b>Idempotency</b>: All operations <br/>

11\.  *

12\.  */

13\. public interface ProfileContract {

14\.  User getUserById(UUID id);

15\.  List<User> getUserByIds(List<UUID> ids);

16\. }

Code 6.7: Adding Javadoc to make the contract more obvious

Contract Testing is necessary for us to ensure the properties of the contract stay true at all times, and to provide visibility if engineers break them. Contract testing will be covered in depth in the next chapter, [Chapter 7](ch07.xhtml), Proper Service Testing.

Many engineers argue that documentation, Javadoc, and comments will get stale and obsolete. When you force teams to document obvious things like getters/setters, this is useless work, and it will get stale as they are not motivated to update it. Our non-obvious concerns here are unlikely to change every day. Do not forget that technical debt at scale is not always fixed in one dimension; code can get stale and be dead too, connected to other pieces of code but not really necessary or used.

[Handling errors in contracts](contents.xhtml#sc4_259a)

Error handling is a critical element of your contract. Code 6.1 and 6.2 in effect only cover the happy path; there is no description of what errors are returned or what useful information could be conveyed. Every language and API protocol has its own convention and ways of handling this concern. Some common options include:

  * Return null: You can just return nulls; but this is a terribly bad idea. It is hard to understand what nulls mean in every context - is this an error? Missing data? Partial failure? Best to avoid.

  * Recreate the error on the caller: When using a service driver, you can map the service’s exceptions directly to the caller, translate via the protocol to the client so it occurs in their runtime code. This might not be feasible or realistic in every language, and also is a source of coupling; if you do it badly, and expose implementation exceptions, you are coupling clients directly to your service code.

  * Leverage the network protocol: Leverage implicit concepts in the API or protocol; for instance, your REST GET request can return a 404 if an invalid ID is passed, which is generally understood as not found.

  * Return an empty object: Just return an empty object; in the case of our ProfileContact, we can just return success, but an empty JSON object. It can mean that the object was not found.

  * Return a custom error object: We can explicitly define error objects in our contract, and depending on the protocol, link it to possible return states of our contract. For instance, Spring Boot can map exceptions to custom REST JSON responses, where you can add informative detail to the caller.




Depending on your language and protocol, you may find well established standards to leverage. It is hard to give universal guidance, because not all languages handle errors the same way - not all have exceptions! - and you are ultimately constrained by the communication protocol. But do not disregard defining these unhappy path outcomes explicitly, documenting them, and testing them as part of any contract testing.

[Service exposure](contents.xhtml#sc4_260a)

Exposure is how we choose to deliver our contract to outside callers; the API or network protocol, like JSON over HTTP/REST, gRPC and protocol buffers, or even SOAP for legacy services. Exposure tends to be stable - teams do not break contracts via changing from REST to gRPC too frequently, but subtle changes in behavior can absolutely break customers. If your consumers are expecting a specific payload when you return an HTTP code 500, this must be consistently supported and treated as a possible source of breaking changes. Exposure can imply some behaviors as well; HTTP has verbs with implicit expectations, like GET, which is not expected to change data and is idempotent, and there are well-established protocols around error codes like 404.

Considering exposure, we have a couple of different options. For instance, we could add REST annotations to our contract project, or even a separate project, data-contract and rest contract. We could write a generic layer that handles the service exposure code, ship a service driver, totally abstracting the exposure method and consumers just keep the contract with driver exposed methods and data, with indirect coupling to the transport protocol. Such concerns might sound silly because if the Sales Service needs to call Profile Service, that would always be done via the same communication mechanism; if it is JSON over HTTP/REST, it would always be this way. However, when we analyze a bigger spectrum of consumers:

  * Other internal services, possibly written in different languages, or having very different use cases, like batch or being customer facing.

  * Consumer or employee facing frontend applications: websites, mobile apps, voice interfaces, chatbots.

  * Big Data pipelines or model training.




One networking mechanism might not fit all, and you might need to support multiple means to expose your service. Especially for mature services, there will always be refactorings and improvements. Perhaps you might have some old SOAP services because of some legacy solutions, or 3rd party external integrations with very specific needs. And technology changes; let’s say the majority of your services could be in JSON over HTTP/REST, but if you are starting a new service today, you might want to use gRPC/Protobuf. For all these reasons, your services might need more than one form of exposed network protocol (Figure 6:18):

Figure 6.18: Decoupling contract form exposure

We want to drive our point home through Figure 6.18 and this entire section is that the SOA contract is beyond just a dumb Java object or OpenAPI specification. The SOA contract is data, operations, input objects, output objects, and a set of non-obvious expectations on behavior. Exposure is where we add networking and broader concerns. As seen here, the same contract and service are being used by two different consumers, one consumer being internal and another service consuming REST/JSON. Another consumer is external and has different expectations of performance.

Figure 6.18 shows us the same Profile Service, same contract, exposed differently. There are multiple different ways we can handle multiple exposures in our service, there are options such as:

  * Layer of indirection: Have a standard in-house layer generating exposure for us - maybe based on configs or annotations.

  * API gateway: Simple use of an API Gateway solution like AWS API Gateway can translate services into different protocols.

  * Lightweight ESB: We could use an ESB like Apache Service Mix or Mule ESB from SalesForce.

  * Apache Camel: We could use a lightweight integration solution like Apache Camel.

  * Serverless: We could use AWS Lambda in order to convert formats/protocols.

  * Drivers: We could have two drivers, one with REST and the other with gRPC.

  * Services: We could build another service that would be a wrapper and translate from REST to gRPC, leaving the Profile service with only one form of exposure.




The tradeoffs we have here are: complexity, added overhead, ease of migration, cost, and flexibility. There is no right or wrong, just what makes more sense for your scenario and company.

[Things to remember](contents.xhtml#sc2_261a)

Congratulations on completing [Chapter 6](ch06.xhtml), Principles of Proper Services. We hope you now understand the critical role that services should play in modern architectures to help fix Monoliths.

  * Service Oriented Architecture (SOA) places services at the heart of development.

  * Common service types include Business Services, Core Services, Wrapper Services, and Platform Services.

  * Write services when you need to share business logic, persist business data, or have a high rate of change that is best encapsulated.

  * Avoid services and prefer other solutions when a service would impact performance, unnecessarily increase complexity, or impact the reliability path.

  * SOA is a true business innovation enabler, delivering key benefits such as:

    * Faster Time to Market

    * Lower Costs and Easier Maintenance

    * Extensibility and Adaptability

    * Independence

  * Shared services let you use functionality in different contexts and by different parts of your business. You can leverage existing code instead of starting from scratch.

  * Proper services abstract away everything about their implementation.

  * Independence allows service teams to go fast and scale. To do that, you need to compromise on central governance.

  * Balancing central governance vs. independence is tricky. Tightly govern items like contracts, domain mapping, and your deployment and observability stacks, but let teams drive their own implementation details and technology choices.

  * Advanced SOA concepts to consider include high abstraction, separation of concerns, interoperability, loose coupling, API contract documentation, and dynamic service registry and discovery.

  * Prefer Contract First over Implementation First.

  * Contract Code -> Metadata, vs. Metadata -> Contract Code is a matter of style and preference.

  * Existing solutions sometimes need you to go implementation first. The key factor is how many consumers you have; having a new UI (zero consumers) enables contract first.

  * OpenAPI is a great standard, widely used, and should be considered.

  * When using OpenAPI make sure you don’t couple your contract with OpenAPI, Spring Doc, and Spring-* Libraries.

  * Backward Compatibility is a superpower.

  * Non-Breaking Changes means you do not break consumer code, and therefore, consumers can ignore it.

  * Breaking changes means that you are breaking consumer code, and they need to deal with it.

  * Examples of Breaking Changes:

    * Rename/Delete existing Operation

    * Rename/Delete existing Parameter

    * Rename/Delete existing Field in result Object

    * Remove exposure/format, i.e. REST/JSON

  * Examples of Non-Breaking Changes:

    * Add new Operation

    * Add non-required field on return Object

    * Add non-required parameters with a default value

    * Add a new exposure/format, i.e. gRPC/PB

  * Strategies to deal with breaking changes:

    * Let it break and do not scale

    * API Versioning:

      * FQDN

      * URL

      * Package/Objects

      * Metadata, i.e. HTTP Header

    * Indirect Backwards Compatibility

  * If you apply API versioning, you need to support multiple contract versioning, and there are drawbacks:

    * How many versions will be maintained? There is a cost.

    * How to avoid code duplication? Consider converting code and having 1 implementation instead of multiple codebases.

  * Isolation is the most significant contribution to the SOA/Microservices style of architecture.

  * Isolation needs to be applied in three key frontiers:

    * Datastores

    * Libraries

    * Public Contracts

  * Principles need to be part of your engineering culture rather than your process.

  * Database isolation is key to good services.

  * Services talk to services; they do not access another database besides their own.

  * A service may perform polyglot persistence and use purpose-built databases.

  * Database clusters should not be shared across different services, except potentially in non-production.

  * Avoid building internal shared libraries only for code reuse.

  * Avoid creating internal shared libraries for the wrong reasons:

    * Reuse to avoid typing or shallow reuse.

    * Reuse by accident; engineers think some team might reuse it.

    * Opinionated choices

    * Unnecessary abstractions

  * Focus on essential internal shared libraries:

    * It is a complex, risky, and involved problem, i.e. encryption.

    * Creating a service makes it slow and is the wrong call, i.e. moving files to S3.

  * When building essential internal shared libraries, make sure they are Lean:

    * Are really necessary and add value.

    * Have few or no dependencies.

    * Dependencies also need to be cherry-picked, and necessary.

    * An internal shared library is not a service; you should make compromises.

    * Code reuse should not be the main reason to create an internal shared library.

    * Opinionated choices and big frameworks need to be avoided.

  * Internal Shared Libraries can have technical debt.

  * Flavors lead to all sorts of problems and are anti-patterns.

  * Only build bridges, not flavors by:

    * Add tests to discover all the public contracts.

    * Build an inventory of the customers and understand their use cases.

    * Refactor all consumer code and delete dead code/flavors.

  * Do not implement libraries or try to reuse code in public contracts.

  * Proper contracts are clean, simple, bare, lean, and have no binary coupling.

  * To isolate public contracts, have zero internal or external dependencies, and only use local objects defined in the contract project itself.

  * Perform design reviews to ensure your contracts have integrity, and are consistent and correct.

  * Automating the detection of dependencies in public contracts with maven is possible: maven-dependency-plugin or some simple greps on the pom.xml.

  * Non-obvious concerns in contracts include:

    * Date Formats

    * Strings

    * Encryption

    * Ordering

    * Service Level Objectives (SLOs)

    * Exceptions and Errors

    * Idempotency

    * Exposure

  * Testing and documentation are great ways to communicate non-obvious concerns; never introduce dependencies to solve these problems.

  * Handle Errors in contracts explicitly, as they are a part of your public contract.

    * Avoid returning null

    * Recreate the error on the caller

    * Leverage the network protocol

    * Return an empty object

    * Return a custom error object

  * Exposure is how services deliver contracts to outside callers, using protocols like JSON over REST/HTTP, gRPC/protobuf, or SOAP/XML.

  * Sometimes it is necessary to have multiple exposures of the same contract. You can do this directly, or indirectly using custom indirection layers, API gateways, lightweight ESBs, Apache Camel, Serverless, custom service drivers, or separate delegating services.
