# [CHAPTER 7Proper Service Testing](contents.xhtml#ch07a)

Testing shows the presence, not the absence of bugs.

— Edsger Dijkstra

Modern software solutions require modern testing techniques. Testing plays a critical role in building effective software architectures, no matter the style. Good software is easily testable; the last thing you want to build when exiting your monolith is something that moves just as slow because you didn’t include a diverse testing strategy in your designs.

Testing helps you build confidence when you release changes, allowing you to move faster with stability. If you believe you can achieve that confidence with just End-to-End or integration testing alone, think again; we will teach you advanced techniques such as Chaos Engineering and Testing in Production that will help you balance speed, safety and cost.

Effective testing is one of the hardest things to get right because organizations believe myths and focus on the wrong OKRs when measuring quality. After this chapter, you will be ready to tackle all the challenges of your new software architecture.

[Structure](contents.xhtml#sc2_262a)

In this chapter, we will cover the following topics:

  * Why testing?

    * Correctness

    * Change impact

    * Production ready

  * Traits of software tests

    * Types of Tests

  * Traits of bad tests

    * Inconsistent failure rate

    * Refactoring fragility

    * Data dependency entanglements

      * Inefficient testing cycles

    * Constant test tweaking

    * Test independence

  * Traits of good tests

    * Constant success rate

    * Refactoring resiliency

    * Data dependency isolation

      * Direct inputs

      * Internal state

    * Fast feedback cycles

    * Hands-off tests

    * Self-contained

  * Testing manifesto

    * Testing throughout over at the end

    * Preventing bugs over finding bugs

    * Testing understanding over checking functionality

    * Building the best system over breaking the system

    * Team responsibility for quality over tester responsibility

  * Testing diversity

    * Why testing matters to software architecture

    * Best practices testing services

    * Practical stress testing

      * Gatling for performance testing

    * Test Pyramid

    * Strategies for testing in production

      * Edge router

      * Beta users

      * Live auditing / compare and discard results

      * Replaying traffic

    * Chaos Testing

      * Netflix Simian Army

      * Toxiproxy

      * Resiliency matrix

    * Internal state - advanced deep dive

      * Synthetic data generation

      * Testing interfaces

      * Test data setup

      * Mocking interfaces

      * Things to remember




[Why testing?](contents.xhtml#sc2_263a)

At this very moment, you might be wondering: why testing in a software architecture book? Testing is a critical need for properly architected solutions. Before we cover software architecture, let’s start with the basics of software engineering.

When we are writing software, we are making changes, either adding or removing code, we are taking risks. We can always break things and create an impact for our end users, or even other teams and engineers, upstream and downstream software consumers, by introducing breaking changes. Testing needs to answer basic questions such as (Figure 7.1):

  * Correctness: How do I prove the solution is working?

  * Change impact: Did our change break something? Is there any impact on other teams?

  * Production ready: Do we have confidence to release the solution to production? Is this safe? Did we cover the basics? How are we feeling about it?




Figure 7.1: Why testing matters

[Correctness](contents.xhtml#sc3_264a)

Basic question: no matter what kind of solution architecture you have, whether it is a classical monolith, modular monolith, SOA, microservices, or an anti-pattern like a distributed monolith, as an engineer, when you are making changes, how do you know you are done? When do you declare the code complete? When did you pass your acceptance criteria? When do you reach your team’s definition of done (DOD)? When do you meet all Kanban test queue policies76? Regardless of the software architecture, no matter if your team works with agile methods (Kanban, XP, Scrum, etc.), you need to address this fundamental question: how do you know your software does what it was intended to do?

Testing is a great way to answer this question.

[Change impact](contents.xhtml#sc3_265a)

Even if your solution is correct, you still need confidence that your changes work and will not create any undesirable side effects such as breaking other features that were previously working (regression testing) or breaking upstream / downstream dependencies (contract testing).

[Production ready](contents.xhtml#sc3_266a)

Do you feel confident about releasing your changes to production? Do you trust that the solution will work, both the previous code and your new changes? How do you reach such confidence? Testing is a significant way to help answer this question; trying to answer this perfectly through testing, proving zero risk before hitting production, can be an expensive prospect. Advanced concepts like production auditing, observability, rollbacks, canary testing, and other release / DevOps techniques can be a better way to balance risk vs. effort; however, your basic effort should be around testing.

[Traits of software tests](contents.xhtml#sc2_267a)

Fundamentally, you have to have good tests. You might think you are following the right testing strategies and have the perfect balance in the testing pyramid, but that won’t help you at all if you have bad tests. Same as with software architecture; perfect adherence to SOA won’t help your organization if all the code is terrible.

Our priority is to teach you the traits of good and bad tests. For every test you write, you need to be able to recognize whether the test adds to your understanding of the correctness, impact and production readiness of the code - all tests should give you a strong signal to judge and decide what is good to go. Good tests make that judgment easy; bad tests suffer from pitfalls, anti-patterns, waste, and technical debt in the same way as any piece of code. Bad tests can create a terrible experience for software engineers and your testing organization, making decisions hard, debugging difficult, creating significant risk, and ultimately leading to a fear of change.

Let’s help you avoid all of that!

[Types of Tests](contents.xhtml#sc3_268a)

The full scope of effective software testing is beyond the remit of this chapter.77 We will talk a lot about the role different kinds of tests can play in your testing strategies, so let’s define some of the common types of tests we will describe throughout:

  * Unit tests: Unit tests evaluate the smallest unit of code possible, such as the methods, functions and classes of an individual software component.

  * Integration tests: Spanning multiple components, integration tests evaluate how specific components work together in a broader software system.

  * End to End (E2E) tests: Demonstrate complete application functionality, typically through a fully deployed environment. E2E tests use multiple components, data, and the entire application stack to evaluate specific user flows.

  * Exploratory tests: Free-form testing to discover hidden requirements and unexpected behaviors of a software system, often done by a human. Some exploratory tests can be automated, such as with fuzz testing that passes random possible inputs to a software system to discover breaks.

  * Contract tests: Evaluate that the API supplied for a component or service is maintained between releases. A key part of evaluating that backward compatibility is maintained between certain changes or after a complex refactoring, Contract tests can also serve as a form of documentation.

  * Functional and acceptance tests: Tests that evaluate whether new application functionality works as intended by the business. Acceptance tests are a more formal step in business signoff, and sometimes evaluate non-functional test attributes as well.

  * Usability tests: Check that customers understand and know how to use your system from the presented UI/UX, using the completed system, or better yet, with a functional prototype before you even start development.

  * Smoke tests: Fast tests that prove that the major functionality of a system works. Typically done as part of a production deployment.

  * Stress, performance or load tests: Given that the functionality works, fire off requests to evaluate that the system works under load and directly or indirectly measure overall system performance. Subtypes exist, like stress tests which find the breaking point of a specific deployed configuration of a software system.

  * Chaos tests: Induce failures in a software system, either randomly or targeted, and measure their impact on the system’s ability to continue performing and in a correct or degraded state.

  * Regression tests: A general term for any test that focuses on whether the pre-existing behavior of a software system continues to work. There should not be specific regression tests; most of the test types in this list are run to verify, “Is it still working?”, essentially hunting for regressions.




This list of tests is not comprehensive or meant to be a fully detailed treatment of each type. Some of you might see details missing or want to see sharper lines between specific test subtypes. Leaving these debates aside, this will be sufficient to illustrate the points in our discussion.

[Traits of bad tests](contents.xhtml#sc2_269a)

Bad tests have these traits (Figure 7.2):

  * Inconsistent failure rate: Failing is easy to spot, address, and fix. When you sometimes fail and sometimes pass, it’s a different story entirely and leads to further bad outcomes.

  * Refactoring fragility: Any code refactor breaks a large chunk of your tests every time.

  * Data dependency entanglements: If the data in your environment is not absolutely perfect, the tests immediately break. And anyone can and will change that data.

  * Inefficient testing cycles: Your engineers and testers are waiting too, too long for the test runs they need to prove working code.

  * Constant test tweaking: Your tests require constant hand-holding, tweaking, and adjustments to achieve a successful test run.

  * Test interference: Your tests cannot run independently of each other or your testing environment.




Figure 7.2: What Bad Testing Looks like

Let’s understand each of these traits/anti-patterns in more detail.

[Inconsistent failure rate](contents.xhtml#sc3_270a)

It is not actually wrong for tests to fail. In Test Driven Development (TDD), you write tests to fail! And a failing test can give you confidence that a code change is working. Of course, the tests just need to be fixed.

It is when you have inconsistent test failures; tests that should be passing, but every 4th run, you know, that one test just doesn’t pass. Back to the signal metaphor - what signal does that send your team? An inconsistent one; eventually, the test starts getting ignored, “that test doesn’t work well”; and in extreme cases, the entire test suite behaves that way, and your engineers stop paying attention to your tests completely.

A lack of confidence in your test suite leads to a lot of bad side effects in your teams:

  * Lots of manual tests: If your automated tests can’t be trusted, then you fall back to manual tests. Manual testing requires humans; humans are expensive, and do not scale; humans are imperfect and make mistakes, such as failing to test your profile service for a new user type.

  * Slow and expensive feedback: When test suites fail and you can only trust manual tests, bugs only show up late in the engineering process or even in production. The earlier you spot bugs, the cheaper they can be to fix, such as on an engineer’s local machine; bugs in production cost more money than bugs in non-prod.

  * Fear of releasing changes: Teams that fear releases exhibit dysfunctional behaviors, such as giving very long estimates (sprints and sprints for simple changes!) and having artificial release windows (once every six weeks/quarter). Having a poor track record of bug-free deliveries absolutely slows the lead time to deliver solutions for the business.




[Refactoring fragility](contents.xhtml#sc3_271a)

Engineers are constantly changing code to address business needs or for some technical migration. If every code change leads to breaking tests, your tests are fragile, and this breaks the resiliency property tests should have. Fixing lots of tests on every code change is expensive and wasteful. Often this problem happens due to tests unnecessarily coupling with internal implementation details; while there is a debate in the community as to whether unit tests should test private methods78, you should be testing publicly available methods, letting engineers freely change code as long as they don’t introduce breaking changes. Most importantly, if you need to test private methods, it likely means your software design needs to be improved; that is you have methods that take no parameters and return void. Review your design before writing more tests.

[Data dependency entanglements](contents.xhtml#sc3_272a)

Tests need data as input. Some forms of testing also need services to be in a specific state, ultimately represented as data. Back to the ACME Widget Company’s profile service, let’s say you are writing a test that evaluates whether a function fetches an email correctly from the database. To find the right record for your tests, you queried the database and used id: 23475. You ship this test, and it works, but some other engineers delete this id in their test to cover a different scenario. Now you have tests breaking other tests. All this happened because your testing data dependencies were not isolated. Tests were entangled with one another through data.

[Inefficient testing cycles](contents.xhtml#sc4_273a)

Testing speed is always relative. However, when we are running tests on our local machine, we have expectations of seeing quick feedback, from seconds to a few minutes; when running tests on dedicated servers with superior hardware, you can afford some heavy lifting and tests taking from minutes to a few hours. Different forms of testing have different speed expectations.

Manual testing is one of the most expensive because it requires people. As you add more tests, you either need more time to test or more people. Your timescale goes from hours to days to weeks; and the number of people from 1 to “sky’s the limit”; we have seen 100s of manual testers for brief periods on some projects. Manual testing does not scale well and is expensive: there is a cost to the delay of feedback, and the cost of execution tied to people makes it expensive too.

Manual testing has many problems, and humans can make mistakes. Manual testing is how we take the best out of people and waste it, whereas automation is a better use of time and resources. However, manual testing makes sense when doing exploratory testing, which should be limited but can be remarkably effective in discovering corner cases your software missed.

[Constant test tweaking](contents.xhtml#sc3_274a)

If every sprint, you need to be tweaking the same tests, requiring constant care, you have what we call “hands-on” tests. Hands-on tests are problematic, burn engineering time, prevent your teams from scaling, and overall are not a trait of a stable system. It’s a smell that hides other problems, such as hard-coded ids, infrastructure instabilities, a lack of scalability such as missing auto scaling groups in services, issues with downstream dependencies being down, frequently changing configuration or configuration bugs, and/or a lack of refactoring resiliency, where any change breaks most/all tests.

One could argue that constant test tweaking is just a smell, not really a trait of bad tests. Hands-on tests can be a sign of a very diligent team; you might be suffering from another anti-pattern in this list, but your team is extremely vigilant in fixing tests all the time. Sure, all that constant tweaking ends up with it looking like your pass rates are great, but in fact, there are one or more anti-patterns at work. That’s why we think you should be looking for it as a trait because:

  * Slow feedback: If the issue is on the downstream team, why could they not figure it out there? Why is the affected team the first to notice? Maybe tests are missing in the downstream team, and these constant test tweaks are just a very slow, inefficient way for upstream consumers to essentially “test” it for them. Anti-pattern: slow and expensive feedback.

  * Root causes: If you just keep tweaking the tests, you continue to hide the problems. Root causes need to be addressed, and when root causes are not fixed, maintenance cost grows, test suites are flaky, and teams are inefficient.




[Test independence](contents.xhtml#sc3_275a)

Lack of isolation is the villain in many software architectures; it is no different in testing. Bad tests lack self-containment, that is, they depend on external state or other tests to run successfully, for instance:

  * Tests cannot run independently: Tests need the same inputs and share too much state, preventing individual tests from being run one at a time or efficient test execution strategies. Which is pretty bad.

  * Test ordering is required: Tests should run in any order. Do tests really need a strict order? Maybe you have one massive chain of tests that must be run sequentially. If changing any test, things break, so you can’t use parallelism. Many modern testing harnesses let you run tests in parallel, such as with the Parallel Test Execution Feature introduced in JUnit 5. Not being able to run tests in parallel increases your testing cycle time.

  * Refactoring fragility: You refactor one test, and you break another one or more tests.




A lack of self-containment is a terrible property you don’t want to have in your tests. When tests interfere with each other, you just increase costs and increase lead time.

Now that we understand what bad tests look like, let’s see the traits of good tests.

[Traits of good tests](contents.xhtml#sc2_276a)

Good tests have these traits (Figure 7.3):

  * Constant success rate: Tests pass and fail predictably. Engineers can treat test failures as a strong signal that something is wrong.

  * Refactoring resiliency: Engineers can safely refactor large elements of the tested code, without most of the tests breaking.

  * Isolated data dependencies: Your test suites use direct inputs for their needed data, or directly create the data they need before running. Tests don’t share common data dependencies between them.

  * Fast feedback cycles: Engineers and testers get feedback quickly; if teams need to execute longer/more complex tests, they run later in the engineering process.

  * Hands-off tests: Most tests rarely if ever need to be changed as engineers ship code.

  * Self-contained: Tests are isolated from each other, and don’t depend on other tests or strict ordering to run successfully.




Figure 7.3: What good testing looks like

Let’s understand each of these traits/patterns.

[Constant success rate](contents.xhtml#sc3_277a)

Good tests pass all the time. When good tests fail, it will mean either incorrect code or shifting requirements; the code or the test needs to be adjusted, which is good! A solid pass rate track record is vital for building trust in tests. With a good track record failures are a strong signal for bugs and your engineers will pay attention. Nothing damages trust more than false positives, leading to the side effects previously discussed. Such conditions lead to a decay of quality and poorly written tests, and as a consequence, poorly tested systems.

[Refactoring resiliency](contents.xhtml#sc3_278a)

As an engineer, you want to be able to refactor code without breaking all your tests all the time. Otherwise, tests get in the way. Engineers will differentiate between when a test breaks because of some downstream dependency or a code change introduces a bug, vs. when the test is too sensitive to the implementation. The latter is a problem with the test, not the business code, the infamous “bug in the test” scenario.

It’s essential to preserve this property in good tests to be resilient when code refactoring. Otherwise, engineers will complain when they try to move fast, and try to delete or write fewer tests as they just “get in the way” of maximizing productivity and delivery. Tests should enhance speed; they do it by giving you confidence.

Tests can violate refactoring resiliency in several ways:

  * Complex mocks: When teams try to mimic system behaviors and create complex test mocks, the result is often tightly coupled to the implementation.

  * Test coupling: Testing unnecessary internal implementation details, such as the complete exception message.

  * Wrong assertion mechanism: Checking logging messages, rather than function return types.




Figure 7.4: Test Coupling Tradeoff

When you are designing your tests, you have essentially two choices that impact the coupling of your tests with your implementation and, thus, their refactoring resiliency. Stick to testing the contract only, keeping the internal implementation invisible to your tests; or design the test to directly evaluate the internal implementation, coupling with it directly. Different testing strategies might only be possible by directly coupling with the implementation, like a chaos test disabling the physical infrastructure, database nodes, or similar. Still, even then, there is no coupling with any line of code. But do you want your integration tests to be coupled to your internal implementation?

Any time you couple your tests to the implementation, your tests will not be refactoring resilient. Some types of tests might need more than mocks; you might need to manipulate data and call special APIs in order to test what you need. In many cases, it is still possible to do so without coupling to the internal implementation, but in others you might have little choice, such as when testing bad monoliths. Test coupling sits on a spectrum (Figure 7.4), and you have options you might not have considered:

  * Change the contract: Expose what you need to test on the contract.

  * Package visibility: Java allows you to use package level visibility to test the code.

  * Reflection: It’s always possible to change something in Java by using reflection.

  * Different kind of testing: Sometimes mocks and unit tests are not the answer; shift your testing balance to other forms of testing, such as chaos testing or integration testing, or no test at all in some cases.




We have spoken extensively on the risks of having a leaky implementation in your contracts; the right designs promote isolation, hiding elements and not exposing internal behaviors and APIs. If you need to choose between software testability and the right software design, always pick the right design. Sometimes you will find a way to expose something on the contract and also improve testing without coupling to the internal implementation. That’s a great outcome and a win-win for all. But even if your test suite becomes more complex and challenging, in relative terms, this is not a big deal; the right software design will always yield much greater benefits in the long run.

[Data dependency isolation](contents.xhtml#sc3_279a)

Good tests handle data well, whether on input or within its class implementation as internal state. Handling data well is a key element of good software design, and your tests can be the first to suffer otherwise. Let’s talk about the habits that good tests display when handling data.

[Direct inputs](contents.xhtml#sc4_280a)

Imagine a simple Java calculator class, with basic operations like add, subtract, multiply, and divide. Operations take two parameters each, such parameters typed as int, and all operations return Double. How do you design your tests to pass in this state?

Good tests will keep each parameter isolated for each test. Let’s say you want to test an array of parameters for successful execution; testing 1 and 0 will make sense for sum (1 + 0 = 1), but division will fail (1/0 throws an ArithmeticException). The right approach is to duplicate these parameters on every test method, so each can have its own unique treatment for the function it calls. The wrong approach would be to have those parameters shared in the form of some sort of global state or class property; global changes could break all your tests. Though this is a simple example, we have seen many real world examples of more complex cases that boil down to the same principle, an attempt to share global state that makes tests fragile. Preserve isolation.

[Internal state](contents.xhtml#sc4_281a)

Some code does not return the same results when given the same parameters. While our calculator class always returns the same results given the same parameters, what if we were to use those parameters to query a database? Then the outcome is not solely defined by those parameters; it depends on the state of something outside the code, the database. You can’t replicate this internal state just with the function code, and the internal state is not visible in the contract; and it is this internal state that tests need to replicate.

So how do we properly test internal state? There are multiple safe techniques: like building dedicated testing interfaces which expose operations needed for data setup or otherwise induce the system to the desired state you need; or synthetic data generation, where you generate data on the fly, excellent for PII, privacy, and ease of data setup: Let’s discuss these approaches with one example of data generation and testing interfaces.

Let’s say we need to test the findUserById operation in the Profile Service example. To test the success case, we need to pass a user id that already exists; for an integration test, that user id will need to exist on the database. Bad tests count on the data to already be in the database before they run, like a magic id everyone uses; good tests explicitly set up the data before they run and tear down afterwards. For instance, we could use a testing interface to run code that checks and repairs a magic testing id coded into the Profile Service tests; but what if multiple teams need to run that script at the same time? Better yet, we can run a simple SQL script that inserts a new user in the DB and records the id, returning that id to be used during the test.

By inserting our own data and using the resulting ids during the setup phase of the test, we prevent teams and tests from interfering with each other, and therefore achieving data dependency isolation. All these approaches will be covered with more color later in this very chapter. Stay tuned!

[Fast feedback cycles](contents.xhtml#sc3_282a)

As we said previously, speed is relative, but everyone benefits from an appropriately fast test execution. Speed helps organizations scale; good tests don’t leave engineers waiting 10 minutes for a single test result on their laptop.

If you are struggling to get good tests with a fast feedback cycle, you might need to change your approach; fast feedback is also achieved by testing with the proper technique and strategy. For instance, if all tests are E2E, they will be slow and expensive. You want to have a balance between the forms of testing that are at your disposal, and we will talk more about that shortly when we cover the Test Pyramid in more detail.

Fast feedback can also be achieved with the right tools. For instance, strongly typed languages like Java, C++, Haskell, Scala, Rust, Zig or even TypeScript can save you testing time by enforcing rules and catching errors within the IDE itself, before any actual test ever executes.

[Hands-off tests](contents.xhtml#sc3_283a)

Good tests don’t need constant tweaking every time you change code. In a sense, good tests are like the DevOps concept of immutable infrastructure; they are written once, never altered, and run perfectly regardless of the environment, system configuration, or parameters they accept. Now of course as we add new code, or change behaviors, tests break and need to be fixed - this is good, this is tests doing their job. But good tests don’t need to be changed often in systems that have a consistent, stable trajectory.

[Self-contained](contents.xhtml#sc3_284a)

Each test method in a test class should uniquely evaluate a different test case. Otherwise, you have test duplication, and having too many tests is its own maintenance burden; or you combine too many test cases in one method, piling up too many assertions and making test failures slower to understand and debug. 

Good test cases should have the following properties:

  * Independent: Tests should run individually, and not depend on the output of a previous test, for both inputs and outputs.

  * Support parallelism: Run tests in any order without breaking. 

  * Test isolation: Test cases, test methods, should not break each other and also be refactoring resilient.




In short, test methods should not interfere or otherwise affect one another. Such tests are essentially self-contained, they stand on their own without the support of others. Isolated tests allow for parallel test execution, which is a great way to speed up your feedback cycles.

Let’s go back to our simple calculator example and explore it further with a code sample (Code 7.1).

  1. public interface CalculatorV1 {
  2. int sum(int a, int b);
  3. int sum(int ...a);
  4. int div(int a, int b);
  5. }



Code 7.1: Calculator V1 Interface

Here we have a simple contract that defines 3 operations: 2 operations to sum integers, either taking 2 parameters or any amount (varargs), and another to perform division on 2 integers. In practicality, you might never ever code a service like this, but this is simple enough to illustrate our points. Now let’s take a look at the implementation (Code 7.2).

  1. import java.util.Arrays;
  2. public class SimpleCalculator implements CalculatorV1 {
  3.   4. @Override
  5. public int sum(int a, int b) {
  6. return a+b;
  7. }
  8.   9. @Override
  10. public int sum(int... a) {
  11. return Arrays.stream(a).reduce(0, Integer::sum);
  12. }
  13.   14. @Override
  15. public int div(int a, int b) {
  16. return a/b;
  17. }
  18. }



Code 7.2: Simple Calculator Implementation

Most imports are omitted for the sake of simplicity. Now let’s look at what self-contained tests look like, with test cases coded in JUnit 5 (Code 7.3).

  1. import org.junit.jupiter.api.Assertions;
  2. import org.junit.jupiter.api.Test;
  3. import static org.junit.jupiter.api.Assertions.assertEquals;
  4.   5. public class SimpleCalculatorTest {
  6.   7. @Test
  8. public void testSimpleSum(){
  9. int inputA = 10;
  10. int inputB = 20;
  11. int result = new SimpleCalculator().sum(inputA,inputB);
  12. assertEquals(30,result);
  13. }
  14.   15. @Test
  16. public void testSimpleSumArray(){
  17. int inputA[] = new int[]{1,2,3,4,5,6,7};
  18. int result = new SimpleCalculator().sum(inputA);
  19. assertEquals(28,result);
  20. }
  21.   22. @Test
  23. public void testSimpleDivOk(){
  24. int inputA = 30;
  25. int inputB = 3;
  26. int result = new SimpleCalculator().div(inputA,inputB);
  27. assertEquals(10,result);
  28. }
  29.   30. @Test
  31. public void testSimpleDivByZero(){
  32. int inputA = 30;
  33. int inputB = 0;
  34. Assertions.assertThrows(ArithmeticException.class, () -> {
  35. new SimpleCalculator().div(inputA,inputB);
  36. });
  37. }
  38. }



Code 7.3: Self-Contained Unit Tests for the Simple Calculator

First of all, as you can see, each test case shares nothing. Test cases have isolated input variables, just as we saw in our data dependency isolation example. Refactoring a test case will not affect other test cases. Tests know little to nothing about the implementation; the implementation can change but as long as it supports the same contract, it will all work. Tests are completely independent and can be executed in parallel. Beyond the happy path, scenarios are being tested like the division by zero. Such tests are clean and very easy to understand.

Note that there are very few assertions per test case. Each test method should exercise discrete test cases, and not pile up tests for lots of different corner cases or scenarios in one place; when tests with lots of assertions fail, engineers don’t immediately know what specifically is broken. One method per test scenario is a best practice.

[Testing manifesto](contents.xhtml#sc2_285a)

Figure 7.5: Testing Manifesto  
Source: [https://www.growingagile.co.za/2015/04/the-testing-manifesto/](https://www.growingagile.co.za/2015/04/the-testing-manifesto/)

Before we go further, let’s introduce the testing manifesto, introduced by Growing Agile in 2013.79 The principles of the Testing Manifesto help focus teams on the right mindset, values, and principles to support proper quality and testing practices in agile teams and even to some degree, software design.

[Testing throughout over at the end](contents.xhtml#sc3_286a)

Teams properly practicing agile methods approach testing quite differently. Testing is not seen as a phase or done at the end of development but rather something that happens during every single step of the development process. This very important principle establishes testing as a spectrum, a continuous activity that is not binary and never ends.

[Preventing bugs over finding bugs](contents.xhtml#sc3_287a)

When you separate QA from Engineering, you have created different organizations with different ways to measure value. Historically in our industry, QA organizations are measured by the number of bugs they find; so their goal becomes to find bugs. It is the wrong incentive because it is more expensive to fix bugs, especially later in the development process. Both QA and engineering should be united in preventing bugs from making their way past the IDE, and instead work to make the system more reliable and resilient in production.

[Testing understanding over checking functionality](contents.xhtml#sc3_288a)

Creating an E2E test for every single functionality and matrix of possible variations is very expensive and slow. Focus energy and effort on testing broad scope and functionality; 15 tests are better than 500 if they truly test the depth of the function using multiple techniques. Testing requires diversity; you should be aiming for more than one form of testing and combining these techniques with clear observability and feedback loops. Testing for understanding also means finding intelligent ways to get confidence to deploy and make changes with safety and correctness.

[Building the best system over breaking the system](contents.xhtml#sc3_289a)

What a great value! Everyone’s goal should be to deliver business value, collaborate, and work to improve the overall system, not just prove it doesn’t work and call it done. Testing can deliver insight and suggest improvements to the business. Lean thinking has this principle of built-in quality where quality needs to be part of the process rather than something that happens at the end; tests are a part of this process, not just a “pass or fail” score, but rather provide insights to help improve the design, performance, and understanding of the overall system. 

[Team responsibility for quality over tester responsibility](contents.xhtml#sc4_290a)

As lean thinking wisely teaches us, quality cannot be external. It needs to be built to the team itself. We cannot have a tester solely responsible for the quality of the product; it must be a team responsibility. It is everybody’s job: all functions, engineering, products, everyone should be doing testing. A similar mindset should be applied for security, observability, effective migrations and many other important engineering aspects beyond testing.

[Testing diversity](contents.xhtml#sc2_291a)

Our industry needs to admit that its approach to testing is often broken. Going all in on pre-production E2E testing is not the way to go. Test code should be well written and maintained like any business code in services. Testing only in pre-production environments has lots of limitations; they are not enough to know your system is correct, has low impact and is production ready. No single test coverage number can prove you are correct. It provides a false sense of security. Teams think they win when they see 100%, 90% or even 80% test coverage, but this can just hide a lot of bad tests and worse practices. Coverage tools do not cover all forms of testing; some tools barely cover branch coverage. Teams need to do better, and the answer is more diversification in testing. Diversity has power.

[Why testing matters to software architecture](contents.xhtml#sc3_292a)

Software architecture covers many disciplines, from infrastructure, code and organization design to structure and cost, with all the decisions and tradeoffs associated. At the heart of it is software design, that is, how individual software components are put together and connect with each other.

Good software design has the property of being testable. Testable software does not guarantee good design, but all good design yields testable software. Think back to the bad monolith examples we’ve used throughout this book; a key property of classical and distributed monoliths is the difficulty in testing them, of making changes and clearly knowing all the impact. Bad software architectures can’t be properly tested as an intrinsic trait.

Testing has a unique role in service-style architectures like SOA and microservices: via testing techniques such as contract testing and backward compatibility testing on service contracts. Testing is an important way to prove you have proper services and can help you maintain it. A service contract needs to have testing to maintain value because otherwise you risk the stability of upstream consumers; chances are high that you will get breaking changes.

[Best practices testing services](contents.xhtml#sc3_293a)

In order to properly test services, our code should have lots of unit tests as compared with integration or E2E tests. In fact, unit tests should be the predominant form of testing. Unit tests are great because they provide fast feedback and often have a low cost to develop and maintain if we follow the principles of good tests described in this chapter.

Besides unit tests and integration tests, services heavily benefit from a specific kind of testing, contract tests. Contract tests tell you if your changes will break consumers - this is very important for services. Contract testing also provides a form of documentation on how to call your service. Contract tests come in two forms, local contract tests that heavily use fakes and mocks, and remote contract tests. (Figure 7.6)

Figure 7.6: Local and remote contract tests

Local contract tests: Local tests do not need the code to contact remote resources to function and need to rely on mocks and fakes80; the main goal with them is speed. Engineers can run them all time and know if they break the contract; the more versions of the contract you have to support, the more you want that quick feedback. Local contract tests can even be generated to some degree. If your remote contract tests are fast enough, you might not need this form of test, and feel free to skip them; always great to do more with less.

Remote contract tests: Remote contract tests reach some or all of your dependencies. Be careful here, and avoid calling 100+ services because then you will end up with an expensive integration or E2E test and not something fast and effective. You still want some level of isolation along with some level of remoting, but do not go too crazy.

In order to create contract tests, you do not need any special tool. It can be done with regular testing tools; for instance, in Java, you can use JUnit. For fakes, you can use standard java APIs; for mocks, you can use a framework like Mockito ([https://site.mockito.org/](https://site.mockito.org/)), EasyMock ([https://easymock.org/](https://easymock.org/)), or even PowerMock ([https://github.com/powermock/powermock](https://github.com/powermock/powermock)).

[Practical stress testing](contents.xhtml#sc3_294a)

Stress and load testing are important types of performance testing that can uncover problems that unit tests and integration tests cannot (Figure 7.7). For instance, with stress tests, we can figure out:

  * Race conditions: Does your code have race conditions that only emerge when many attempts are executing at the same time?

  * Leaks: Do we have memory leaks that only emerge under a prolonged period under stress?

  * Capacity: Can we handle our expected load? What if we grow the user base 2x? 5x? 10x? Are we ready or not?

  * Bottlenecks: Under max load, what are the key scalability bottlenecks shown by the system? Do we have a bottleneck on CPU, memory, disk I/O, or on a downstream dependency? 




Figure 7.7: Stress vs Load Testing

Load testing proves the system can handle the expected peak volume, whereas stress testing goes beyond the upper limit of what the service or system can handle. Many companies ignore stress/load testing because they don’t think they need Silicon Valley scale. Beyond scale, stress and load testing can uncover hidden bugs around concurrency and resource utilization. Yes, you can ignore this form of testing and discover all that in the form of production incidents or outages, but there are better ways to handle user experience and due diligence.

[Gatling for performance testing](contents.xhtml#sc4_295a)

Gatling ([https://gatling.io/](https://gatling.io/)) is an open-source tool built in Scala ([https://www.scala-lang.org/](https://www.scala-lang.org/)). You code tests in the Scala DSL, but you can also use other JVM languages like Java or Kotlin ([https://kotlinlang.org](https://kotlinlang.org)). Gatling scales pretty well. Compared with JMeter ([https://jmeter.apache.org/](https://jmeter.apache.org/)), Gatling is more flexible because it is code, not XML. Gatling has support for multiple protocols, such as HTTP, including SSE, TLS, and WebSockets, as well as JMS and MQTT. Gatling has a fantastic report, covering latency with percentiles and failures (Figure 7.8). 

Figure 7.8: Gatling sample report  
Source: [https://gatling.io/docs/gatling/reference/current/stats/reports/](https://gatling.io/docs/gatling/reference/current/stats/reports/)

Code 7.4 shows a sample Gatling application, using the Gatling HTTP DSL:

1\. public class StressTesting extends Simulation {

2\. 

3\.  HttpProtocolBuilder httpProtocol = http

4\.  .baseUrl("http://computer-database.gatling.io")

5\.  .acceptHeader("text/html,application/xhtml+xml,application/xml;q=0.9,*/*;q=0.8")

6\.  .doNotTrackHeader("1")

7\.  .acceptLanguageHeader("en-US,en;q=0.5")

8\.  .acceptEncodingHeader("gzip, deflate")

9\.  .userAgentHeader("Mozilla/5.0 (Windows NT 5.1; rv:31.0) Gecko/20100101 Firefox/31.0");

10\. 

11\.  ScenarioBuilder scn = scenario("BasicSimulation")

12\.  .exec(http("request_1")

13\.  .get("/"))

14\.  .pause(5);

15\. 

16\.  {

17\.  setUp(

18\.  scn.injectOpen(atOnceUsers(1))

19\.  ).protocols(httpProtocol);

20\.  }

21\. }

Code 7.4 \- Gatling for Stress/Load Testing, sample code

We define the protocol we want to use, HTTP in this case, and the REST endpoint we want to call. Line 18 specifies the number of concurrent “users” we want to execute; the example shows 1, but it could be hundreds of thousands as your tests require and hardware can support. Gatling has other execution strategies, such as rampUsers, stressPeakUsers, constantUsersPerSec, and much more.

[Test Pyramid](contents.xhtml#sc3_296a)

Figure 7.9: The Test Pyramid  
Source: <https://martinfowler.com/articles/practical-test-pyramid.html>

You might be familiar with the idea of testing pyramids, introduced by Mike Cohn in his book: Succeeding with Agile, 2009. (Figure 7.9) The idea is that not all testing techniques have the same speed and cost. Some techniques are high-speed and cost almost nothing but aren’t comprehensive, like unit tests, whereas other tests are expensive to build/maintain and also take a long time to run, like UI / E2E tests. A proper testing strategy should rely on many cheap and fast tests vs slow and expensive tests to balance integration, cost and speed.

The testing pyramid is a great contribution to testing and gives us a compass for how to test and mind the cost/execution speed. The pyramid works differently for different software engineering disciplines; for instance, this balance is intuitive for backend software but applies differently to the frontend.

You might ask, “if unit tests are cheap to write and fast to run, why don’t we only use unit tests?” Unit tests have tradeoffs and problems such as:

You need to predict all possible problems: Perhaps the strongest argument. In order to do pre-production testing, you need to be able to “predict” all the problems, and our ability to predict problems has limits. We still should be doing pre-production testing, but we need to combine it with testing in production.

Cost and complexity of mocks: Oftentimes, engineers underestimate the cost and benefit of mocking. You can end up with tests not proving the correctness of your business rules, but instead just testing the mocks. You can even find people building test suites for their mocks! The result is complex mocks that don’t cover every scenario and whose maintenance goes far beyond their dependencies.

Modern software engineering needs a complex spectrum of tests that occur continuously, from the moment an IDE throws an error, to your canary test81 showing a critical failure to a minor subset of your customers. The testing pyramid illustrates that spectrum. Modern testing strategies accordingly deliver a diversity of tests: property testing, mutation testing, stress/load testing, contract testing, and testing in production. Go beyond pre-production testing to balance safe, cheap and effective outcomes!

[Strategies for testing in production](contents.xhtml#sc3_297a)

Testing in production sounds dangerous, right? Production is where all the money, risk and most of all, customers are, isn’t that the last place you want to test your software, right? But the truth is far from it: testing in production is all about finding ways to safely discover issues in production efficiently, and to let you with speed, safety and less overhead. Cindy Sridharan captures the concept behind testing in production and the spectrum between testing in non-production and production in her amazing blog post (Figure 7.10).

Figure 7.10: Cindy’s Testing Spectrum  
Source: [https://copyconstruct.medium.com/testing-in-production-the-safe-way-18ca102d0ef1](https://copyconstruct.medium.com/testing-in-production-the-safe-way-18ca102d0ef1)

We covered the basic questions we need to answer earlier in this chapter: how do we know our code is correct? Do we know the impact of change? Is our code production ready, reliable and the overall system ready to recover from any failure? To answer these questions, you need to test. If you want to test how your system recovers from failure, there is no better way than to create failure in a controlled environment: chaos testing. Now the question in your head must be, why production? Why not test in non-prod?

Unfortunately, in our experience, non-production environments are never up to par with production. There are many ways in which non-production environments will differ from production, such as:

  * Cost optimization: If production has 20 instances in a cluster, in non-production, you might have 3 or 1.

  * Migrations: Some data or code migrations will only happen in prod.

  * Traffic: Real traffic only happens in production.

  * Data: Due to scale, privacy, or regulatory concerns, you cannot have the same data in pre-production as in production.

  * Instability: Pre-production is constantly changing, and so often broken with people making badly conceived errors.




With all those differences, why do we think that only running pre-production tests is sufficient to catch what happens in production? Why not experiment in controlled ways to limit impact? Testing in production does not mean affecting all your users by any means. No sense in testing failure scenarios on 100% of your users, when 1% or less will do fine.

One common technique to limit impact is by deploying software using Red/Black or Blue/Green deployments, where you have 2 clusters in production but only one actively serving most or all traffic; also known as Active/Passive or Live/Dark pool deployments.

For this discussion, we will go with the convention of Live vs Dark; where the Live pool is getting the bulk of the live traffic and Dark pool gets none, or some in the cases we will explore.

Figure 7.11: Live and Non-Live Pools

In Figure 7.11, we depict a typical Live/Dark deployment, and hopefully the idea is self explanatory. We have 2 pools with 3 servers and 3 database nodes in each cluster. Database changes are replicated but otherwise these are 2 isolated clusters. Your users are routed to the live clusters; you can deploy new software on the dark cluster and perform chaos testing there. ZERO live users will be impacted, and you are doing this in production. Let’s explore some of these scenarios further.

[Edge router](contents.xhtml#sc4_298a)

Edge routing refers to the technology stack at the “edge” of your deployment, typically facing customers or very far up your execution stack, and that routes incoming requests to destination servers. Edge routing is a powerful tool to enable complex testing in production scenarios. 

Figure 7.12: Netflix usage of Zuul  
Source: [https://github.com/Netflix/zuul/wiki/How-We-Use-Zuul-At-Netflix](https://github.com/Netflix/zuul/wiki/How-We-Use-Zuul-At-Netflix)

For example, Netflix has an open-source solution called Zuul ([https://github.com/Netflix/zuul](https://github.com/Netflix/zuul)) open-sourced in 2013, which is a dynamic routing solution. Zuul is perhaps the best open source example of a proper edge router. Using Zuul, it is possible to route traffic based on any criteria because it is possible to write code to perform the routing. For instance, previously, we were describing the Live/Dark traffic pool pattern in Figure 7.12; with Zuul, we can write routing code in Java to isolate 1 user or assign 1% of the traffic to a chaos experiment, business-driven A/B testing, or any other form of testing in production. Other API Gateway solutions can allow you to have similar results, such as Nginx ([https://www.nginx.com/](https://www.nginx.com/)), AWS API Gateway ([https://aws.amazon.com/api-gateway/](https://aws.amazon.com/api-gateway/)), Kong API Gateway ([https://konghq.com/products/kong-gateway](https://konghq.com/products/kong-gateway)), and many others.

[Beta users](contents.xhtml#sc4_299a)

Beta users are a simple technique to reduce blast radius and allow safe production tests. Consider your beta users as one of your first canaries. Beta users agree to use experimental software that might have bugs or issues, and in turn they are tagged in a beta user pool and assigned different experiences. Beta programs often give rewards to beta users in exchange for useful feedback, such as discounts and savings. Mobile platforms have explicitly built methods to expose users to custom software through programs like Apple iOS TestFlight, Android Play Store Beta programs. Even if you can’t have external beta users, you can always tag internal users as beta users. The benefit to your organization is that by first rolling out software to your beta users you reduce the blast radius of customers affected by bugs in your code; it is just another way to test in production.

Figure 7.13: Beta users routed via Zuul for Chaos Testing

Figure 7.13 shows how using an API Gateway like Zuul, we can route beta users to different servers and perform different activities in each production pool, such as chaos testing using Toxiproxy, which we will cover later in this chapter.

[Live auditing / compare and discard results](contents.xhtml#sc4_300a)

Having multiple live pools enables other production testing scenarios such as live auditing (see Figure 7.14). Imagine we want to do a data migration, and replace the database implementation of the Profile DB from MySQL to Postgres. Still, we are not 100% confident in the results. Because of the flexibility of Zuul or similar edge routers, we can write code to mirror all live traffic going to our live cluster to our dark cluster, which has a different implementation, a backwards compatible contract, and a completely new database. We are copying/mirroring all live traffic, so real customers only experience interactions with the live cluster; and responses from the dark cluster are inspected for errors and discarded, never returning back to live customers.

Figure 7.14: Testing in Production: Live Auditing

By comparing and discarding results, you can catch errors, check that database integrity is intact and records created as expected, all while leaving customers safely insulated from errors in your dark environment. It can help you gain confidence that both solutions work with little risk. Our solution works best with backward compatibility, but given some effort, it can still be used without backward compatibility.

Going further, we can directly enable real responses to go back to our customers through being implemented within the boundary of a service, without using an edge router, through dual database reads and writes. (Figure 7.15) As requests come in, you write to both databases and wait for both responses with a timeout; if Postgres never responds, return the MySQL result. If the Postgres response has errors, just return the MySQL result. If both return, compare them and inspect for errors. If no errors and they are 100% identical, great, return the Postgres response; else return MySQL. What’s happening here is that we are assuming that MySQL is the source of truth and Postgres is the experiment, only returning Postgres results when there is a complete match.

In both scenarios we discuss here, a clear benefit is that you can generate alerts when the API or data responses don’t match, and customers are insulated in all cases. You have a light and fast rollback - either disable routing or pass a flag to stop writing to Postgres - and a safe way to introduce transformative change.

Figure 7.15: Compare and Discard

Like anything in software architecture, the dual calls/compare and discard pattern has some drawbacks:

  * Downtime: What if Postgres is down for a period of time? The databases will diverge and have different data and will need to be synced.

  * Failure: What if Postgres keeps falling? At some point, some queries might always fail until you sync again. For both downtime and failure, you might have a component to keep both in sync in case of soft or hard failures.

  * Cost: You must run both databases at full production capacity, so such techniques have a cost. It might not be worth it to use in all changes, but if for some critical and delicate changes, the cost will be worth it and it is a good idea, but not all the time. You will have to judge the tradeoffs.




All of our techniques can be applied at multiple levels of the application stack: service contract, service implementation, database, and even downstream dependencies. The biggest benefit is fast and real-time rollbacks; by adding observability and collection metrics, you gain powerful insights into what really happens in production. We will cover more data migration techniques in [Chapter 10](ch10.xhtml), Data Migrations.

[Replaying traffic](contents.xhtml#sc4_301a)

In our previous scenario, we used live traffic mirroring to expose new production code to real stress. But you don’t need to do this live; replaying traffic involves you storing real traffic requests to replay them against your services at a later time. (Figure 7.16)

Replaying traffic is useful for performing load and stress testing because you can replay real requests at much faster speed, but also can be used in chaos testing scenarios. To support these techniques, collect requests in a high-write traffic database like Cassandra or an append-only log solution like Apache Kafka or AWS Kinesis where you would store full requests, with HTTP verbs, full path, body parameters, query parameters, headers and everything. With Kafka or Kinesis, it becomes easy to build a process that reads back and replays all that traffic. But remember this is real production data: are you performing the proper anonymization to ensure you can safely replay production data against non-production or the dark pool?

Figure 7.16: Replay traffic pattern

[Chaos Testing](contents.xhtml#sc3_302a)

Chaos testing or chaos engineering is a form of testing in production, and it sounds scarier than it actually is. When embracing distribution, the opposite of centralization like having a single monolith, such as by hundreds or thousands of microservices running on a public cloud like AWS, failure will happen because there are too many individual moving parts and points of failure. It is not a question of if it will happen but when it will happen. Werner Vogels, AWS’ CTO is famous for the phrase “Everything fails all the time.”; we agree it describes cloud computing and distributed systems very well. (Figure 7.17)

As you come to the realization and accept that your post-monolith architecture will fail, when that failure happens - and it will happen - do you want to be prepared and ready to handle it? Or do you prefer to impact your user experience in production, with all the brand damage and financial loss that goes along with it?

If you want to give your customers a great, resilient user experience, you need to understand your failure modes and actively test for failure. Testing for failure is not dangerous; ignoring failure modes is much more dangerous because failure will happen, and chances are you won’t be ready for it.

In order to test for failure, you do not need to bring your whole website down. Guardrails are necessary to perform chaos engineering in a controlled fashion with zero to minimal impact on real users. Chaos engineering requires discipline and excellent observability in order to recognize and contain the blast radius of failure.

Chaos Testing can and should be applied to many different layers and components in your infrastructure. Our goal is not to prevent failure but to make sure you can recover from failure and contain the blast radius. Systems that recover from failure easily are Anti-Fragile82.

Figure 7.17: Chaos Testing - failure will happen.

Chaos testing is done scientifically. You create a hypothesis of how the system could fail, then you test this hypothesis with experiments, analyze the data, and then determine if the applications and system infrastructure were able to recover from failure without impacting real users. The experiments you perform can be targeted chaos, killing specific services or elements in expected ways, or random chaos, where you impact systems using any number of random techniques.

Figure 7.18: Chaos Testing Applied Science

For example, targeted chaos, let’s consider the sample architecture in Figure 7.18. (1) The Profile service reads and writes to its database, a 3 node MySQL cluster with one node per AZ. (2) The Profile service also publishes events on Kafka, meant for later consumption by big data / analytical streams. (3) The Profile service also calls the Image service in order to fetch the Gravatars83 for the user’s picture.

Now what could go wrong here? What are the failure scenarios? We can start creating some hypotheses, such as:

  * Failure Mode A: What if one of the Profile database nodes is down?

  * Failure Mode B: What if Kafka is down?

  * Failure Mode C: What if the Image service is down?




Once we have our hypotheses, we can write down what our expectations are for each failure mode. For instance, here are some reasonable expectations, assuming we are running on AWS cloud; we don’t expect the service to fail, instead:

  * Failure Mode A: The Profile service will switch to a database node in another AZ and retry.

  * Failure Mode B: The Profile service will write the data to its DB instead of Kafka and retry later, not losing any data.

  * Failure Mode C: The Profile service will return a generic image.




Now we can run some simple integration tests or even load tests that hit the service, and we can check if our expectations match or not.

For random chaos, we can randomly pick components and apply different chaos injections to create failure modes, such as: shutting down a machine, injecting errors, terminating the JVM, introducing latency, introducing TCP packet loss, consuming all memory, filling all local or remote disk, temporarily removing network connectivity, having dependencies returning invalid data types like XML instead of JSON, and many other forms of possible failure. The only limit is your imagination (and your control plane).

Figure 7.19: Chaos Testing big picture

Figure 7.19 shows the whole picture. A set of integration tests are continuously hitting the Profile service or even a dedicated load test that will run for a couple of minutes. Meanwhile, we will generate chaos, either using chaos monkey or another chaos tool. We should see our tests pass and overall deliver as per the expectations we set matching our hypotheses. By running this chaos test against our dark traffic pool, as long as it is isolated, we can test in production and avoid impact on our users.

[Netflix Simian Army](contents.xhtml#sc4_303a)

Figure 7.20: Netflix Simian Army  
Source: [https://www.infoq.com/presentations/netflix-continuous-delivery/](https://www.infoq.com/presentations/netflix-continuous-delivery/)

Netflix is a pioneer in Chaos Engineering. One of the first solutions was Simian Army ([https://github.com/Netflix/SimianArmy](https://github.com/Netflix/SimianArmy)), written in Java and created in 2012. (Figure 7.20) Simian Army defined a number of different components such as:

  * Chaos Monkey: Able to terminate EC2 instances, whether random or targeted.

  * Chaos Gorilla: Able to cut off whole Availability Zones.

  * Chaos Kong: Able to cut off an entire Region.

  * Latency Monkey: Injects latency into network calls.

  * Janitor Monkey: Cleans up unused resources.

  * Security Monkey: Fires security tickets when certificates are about to expire.




Simian Army is an archived project now, but it influenced the whole industry. Chaos Monkey is still maintained and active, now written in Golang ([https://github.com/netflix/chaosmonkey](https://github.com/netflix/chaosmonkey)). Some capabilities were directly incorporated into Netflix Spinnaker: [https://spinnaker.io/](https://spinnaker.io/) and influenced many other solutions such as Gremlin ([https://www.gremlin.com/](https://www.gremlin.com/)) and the AWS Fault Injection Simulator (FIS) service ([https://aws.amazon.com/fis/](https://aws.amazon.com/fis/)).

[Toxiproxy](contents.xhtml#sc4_304a)

Toxiproxy ([https://github.com/Shopify/toxiproxy](https://github.com/Shopify/toxiproxy)) is another chaos engineering tool that lets us create network chaos, also inspired by Netflix’s work. Simulating and seeing how your software responds to subtle networking errors can be a powerful tool to avoid or reproduce rare issues in production. Toxiproxy was created by Shopify in 2014 and provides many ways to create network chaos with several features such as:

  * Timeouts: Hold a socket open but never return.

  * Downtime: Never respond to a request.

  * Latency injection: Inject an arbitrary delay.

  * Bandwidth reduction: Simulate low bandwidth scenarios.

  * Slow close: Delay TCP socket close.

  * Reset peer: Simulates TCP reset.

  * Slicer: Slice TCP data into small bits and optionally introduce delay.

  * Limit data: Close connection when some specific limit of data transferred is reached.




Toxiproxy is a proxy, so it stands between and proxies requests from your solution and a target server or service, which is how it introduces failure injection. Toxiproxy is written in Golang; however, it has clients for several languages such as Java, Go, Haskell, Ruby, Rust, Elixir, NodeJS, PHP, .NET, and Python.

Figure 7.21: Chaos Testing - Network failure injection with Toxiproxy

Figure 7.21 illustrates using Toxiproxy in order to create downtime between our service and a database. In Figure 7.18, we could have used Toxiproxy to create the outages expected in the image service or Kafka as well.

[Resiliency matrix](contents.xhtml#sc4_305a)

Another popular chaos engineering technique is the resiliency matrix84. Take a system and build a matrix with all its downstream dependencies and assess what would be the behavior in failure modes ranging from light (node being down) to extreme (whole cluster down). Then describe fallbacks and possible recovery actions. Let’s map some of our hypotheses from Figure 7.16 to the resiliency matrix in Table 7.1:

Component / Failure Mode |  Node Down |  Cluster Down  
---|---|---  
Profile DB |  Retry, Exponential backoff, Failover to different AZ (green) |  Downtime (red)  
Image service |  Retry, Exponential backoff, Failover to different AZ (green) |  Fallback to Cache default images. (yellow)  
Kafka |  Retry, Exponential backoff, Failover to different AZ (green) |  Write to the ProfileDB. (yellow)  
  
Table 7.1: Resiliency Matrix

Green: System can easily recover from failure.

Yellow: System can still operate but with some features disabled or an experience degradation.

Red: Complete disruption, system cannot operate.

In regard to the Red line, the Profile DB cluster being fully down, we then need to consider options to reduce downtime. For instance:

  * Fallbacks: Should we consider any other possible fallback? Another DB or cached result?

  * DR: Should we have a Disaster Recovery solution in place? Do we have cross-region or offsite backups? What is the restoration procedure? How long does it take?

  * Multi-region: We only have this database across multiple availability zones; should we consider a multi-region solution?




All these options have a cost. Sometimes the best option for your business is to do nothing but have great backups. First, understand your availability and reliability goals and plan accordingly.

[Internal state - advanced deep dive](contents.xhtml#sc3_306a)

Having to recreate a complex internal state that in turn relies on external dependencies is a common problem. Some kinds of tests suffer from this problem more than others; any test where you need to call out to external dependencies will experience more issues, such as remote contract tests, remote integration tests, E2E tests, stress tests, or synthetic testing. Tests that have fewer dependencies or can use fakes or mocks can exercise further control, such as with local integration tests, property tests, or unit tests.

While this problem gets much worse in distributed monoliths where multiple services and applications are reading and writing to the same shared central database. Having teams share the same database massively increases the chance of collision, where one test breaks another test because of shared dependencies on the same test data, such as by using the same id; that’s why again, being able to isolate test data is essential. Let’s look at some advanced techniques to handle this.

[Synthetic data generation](contents.xhtml#sc4_307a)

Synthetic data generation means what it sounds like; generate fake data and use it during your tests. Synthetic data generation avoids trying to use sanitized production data in your non-production data, ensuring anonymization and avoiding any exposure to PII85 or PHI86 risks. Using any library or tool capable of generating fake data and inserting it into your database prior to test setup works well. Examples of this library approach include Ruby’s faker gem and its Java port, Java-Faker87. For more involved cases, synthetic data platforms such as Gretel (https://[gretel.ai](https://gretel.ai/)) can generate entire datasets, a great approach for Big Data teams. (Figure 7.22)

Figure 7.22: Synthetic Data Generation

The synthetic data generation approach has one issue; once the data is generated, you cannot guarantee that the tests will use different ids, as this is up to the engineers writing the tests. You could fix the ids issue by introducing a lookup table or a service in the form of your synthetic generation tool that just routes the ids for the tests, avoiding conflicts and connections (Figure 7.23) or just freshly generate new data before every test run and clean it up afterwards.

Figure 7.23: Synthetic Data Generation with Lookup Service

Another approach would use testing interfaces. Let’s take a closer look.

[Testing interfaces](contents.xhtml#sc4_308a)

The idea behind testing interfaces is that the service exposes particular endpoints to facilitate testing. Let’s say you need to be able to delete some data on the Profile, like URL, to simulate a broken image or any other corner case that is not a supported function, and cannot be induced by just calling the standard public contract. (Figure 7.24)

Figure 7.24: Testing interface

You would expose a separate endpoint with a specific operation: "/profile/test/change/url?ID=12345” The testing interfaces approach has many benefits:

  * Only for testing: You are not exposing the internals of the service, no internal core, no database; just an API like any other method.

  * Better engineering: Service providers can have tests to make sure this interface is always supported and working. Since this lives in your codebase like any other method, your IDE will alert you if you break something, and you can even create tests for it.

  * Reduce exposure: Testing interface would not be exposed to production consumers like other services, external API for customers. Such a mechanism prevents misuses, like any team using the testing interface outside of tests.




Like anything in software architecture, the drawbacks here are:

  * More contracts: Now there are more things for the service team to maintain.

  * Increased service complexity: As a side effect the service gets more complex because you need to handle 2 contracts, the public one and the test contract.

  * Reuse by accident: Limiting exposure is not foolproof, and engineers make mistakes; what if this test endpoint somehow ends up used by an API consumer for a production business use case?




Testing interfaces is a powerful technique, but it is not a silver bullet. You don’t want to end up with services with one valid method and 79 hidden methods to manipulate state. In that case, you might need to rethink your software design or testing strategy. Regardless, it is a powerful tool in your testing toolbox.

[Test data setup](contents.xhtml#sc4_309a)

Simple idea: have scripts that create all test data needed - near the source. Implementing the test data setup approach is as simple as having a folder with database scripts which are run before the tests start and create all required data. (Figure 7.25) We are effectively moving this issue out of the service, into another layer, closer to the data stores themselves. Easy, straightforward to explain and execute and does not require heavy engineering to get it running. The data can also be a synthetic data set you generate solely for testing, working with our other approaches.

Figure 7.25: Test Data Setup

The biggest drawback here is that when the database changes, the scripts will break. Using services instead of direct database inserts is the better approach for refactoring resiliency. However, the problem can be fixed with schema backward and forward compatibility approaches when introducing schema changes on the DB side.

As well, other teams might need specific data to appear in your service for their tests to succeed. Such an approach would require different teams to do PRs to the repository housing your test data setup scripts. Thankfully, they are not touching code that goes to production - this is just code that is executed at test time, such as in a pipeline solution like Jenkins. Much less risk for it to be reused.

[Mocking interfaces](contents.xhtml#sc4_310a)

When you have proper services with stable contracts, clean interfaces, and well understood behaviors, they can be easily mocked which makes your testing problems easier to deal with. Using tools like the open source MockServer ([https://www.mock-server.com/](https://www.mock-server.com/)), you can quickly and easily mock REST calls. The drawback here is that you can’t really mock E2E testing. So MockServer works well for some tests, but not all of them.

Figure 7.26: Overall view of MockServer  
Source: [https://www.mock-server.com/](https://www.mock-server.com/)

MockServer is quite powerful because it allows you to do pattern matchers to capture specific requests and tie them to specific mocks you want, i.e. return a specific JSON for certain ids. Combine that with versioning these JSON files in git, you can create a generic solution that uses a centralized repository and provides an injection mechanism for any service-to-service test scenario.

[Things to remember](contents.xhtml#sc2_311a)

Congratulations you finished [Chapter 7](ch07.xhtml), Proper Service Testing. Testing is very important, some key ideas to remember:

  * Testing Matters.

  * Testing is essential for addressing questions such as:

    * Correctness: How do I know our solution is working?

    * Change Impact: Did we break something? Is there any impact on other teams?

    * Production Ready: Do we have the confidence to release it in production?

  * Tests come in many types and flavors, including: unit tests, integration tests, end to end tests, exploratory tests, contract tests, functional tests, acceptance tests, usability tests, smoke tests, stress, performance or load tests, chaos tests, and regression tests.

  * Knowing the difference between good tests and bad tests will strongly influence the success of your testing strategy.

  * Bad Tests have one of many of these traits/anti-patterns:

    * Inconsistent Failure Rate

    * Refactoring Fragility

    * Data Dependency Entanglements

    * Inefficient Testing Cycles

    * Constant Test Tweaking

    * Test Interference

  * Good Tests have these traits/patterns:

    * Constant Success Rate

    * Refactoring Resiliency

    * Data Dependency Isolation

    * Fast Feedback Cycles

    * Hands-off Tests

    * Self-Contained

  * The Testing Manifesto describes the right principles to follow when testing in your organization:

    * Testing throughout over the end

    * Preventing bugs over finding bugs

    * Testing understanding over functionality

    * Building the best system over breaking the system

    * Team responsibility for quality over tester responsibility

  * Good software design has the property of being testable.

  * Service Oriented Architectures strongly benefit from local or remote contract testing to help preserve backwards compatibility.

  * Load/Stress tests are essential to answer questions like:

    * Do we have race conditions?

    * Do we have memory leaks?

    * Can we handle our expected load? What if we grow the user base 2x? 5x? 10x?

    * Where do we have scalability bottlenecks?

  * The Testing Pyramid establishes the notion of measuring cost vs time to execute for different types of tests. Other advice:

    * Focus on unit tests over UI/E2E tests

    * Pre-production testing has limits, as you cannot predict all possible problems. Consider testing in production

  * Testing In Production

    * Testing in real environments can help you find bugs you could never find in pre-production.

    * Safely test in production using the right guardrails and techniques:

      * Live and Dark Traffic

      * Edge Routing

      * Beta Users

      * Live Auditing / Compare and Discard Results

      * Replaying Traffic

  * Chaos Testing

    * A form of production testing

    * Discovering failure modes is key. Failures will happen, do you prefer to be prepared or damage your brand and user experience?

    * Use the scientific method: hypotheses, experiments, and results.

    * What failure happens, do we understand failure modes? Can we recover?

    * Chaos Monkey and Toxiproxy are useful chaos testing tools.

    * Use a Resiliency Matrix to prepare for chaos experiments.

  * Apply advanced techniques to handle the internal state testing problem:

    * Synthetic Data Generation

    * Testing Interfaces

    * Test Data Setup

    * Mocking Interfaces

# [CHAPTER 8Embracing New Technology](contents.xhtml#ch08a)

Any sufficiently advanced technology is indistinguishable from magic.

— Arthur C. Clarke

Everything is wrong! Companies are afraid of new technology, burned by failed migration attempts, or scared away by other companies’ horror stories. Is your company the same way? Fear is driving their decision-making. We must never let fear lead and control us.

What if we told you everything can be different, and this can all change for the better? The business can have new capabilities, differentiation, and innovation, deliver an amazing experience and solution for their customers. Your technology organization can be excited and reinvigorated, happy to come to the office with the prospect of new projects and opportunities. Everyone can have their place in the sun.

The answer is to embrace new technologies. Do not let fear lead and control your organization. From where you are, it might sound impossible, but there is an amazing universe of opportunities awaiting you. A world where refactoring does not have more gravity and mass than Mars. New technology is like a reinvigorating fire in your organization, motivating people, moving engineers to get more things done, and waking up in the morning pumped to go to work.

[Structure](contents.xhtml#sc2_312a)

In this chapter, we will cover the following topics:

  * Embrace the new, with principles

  * On demand resources - cloud computing

    * Stable contracts

    * Proper isolation

    * One account per service

    * One account per business domain

    * One account for everything

    * Account organization impacts contract granularity

    * Internal shared libraries

    * Serverless

    * How the cloud impacts team organization

  * Pre-built capabilities - cloud services and SaaS

    * Relational and NoSQL datastores

    * AI and analytics

  * Isolated deployment units

    * Containers and Kubernetes

    * Cloud spectrum

  * Real time access to data - streaming

    * Apache Kafka

    * Kafka architecture

    * Kafka and CQRS / ES

    * ksqlDB

  * Efficient engineers

    * Modern languages

    * Language spectrum

    * JVM languages

  * Flexible data models

    * NoSQL databases

    * Going beyond the relational model

  * Lean communications - binary APIs and GraphQL

    * Service-to-Service communication: REST, gRPC, and GraphQL

    * What REST got right

    * Interoperability vs performance

    * GraphQL

    * gRPC
