  * Things to remember




[Embrace the new, with principles](contents.xhtml#sc2_313a)

Your brave new world does not come for free. Using the principles that we have taught you; you can embrace new tech with speed and safety and deliver a win-win for both the business and the technology organization. Trying to use new technologies with the wrong principles is exactly how you create distributed monoliths. So, while embracing the new is exciting, it needs to be done with due diligence, a lot of thinking, careful planning, and a lot of tradeoff analysis.

We have spent much of this book showing you how classical monoliths and distributed monoliths hold you back. Modern architectures need isolation, performance, and to embrace new ideas and new technology. Software architecture is at the heart of the storm; making the right choices and smartly incorporating new technology enables all of this excitement, and new opportunities for your teams and your customers; the wrong architecture will make good people quit and customers suffer.

Our message: when you deliver the right architecture, a modern architecture, and proper services leveraging new technologies in the best way possible, you have gold. Doors will open for you.

Let us go!

[On demand resources - cloud computing](contents.xhtml#sc2_314a)

Cloud computing is much more than just somebody else’s server. Cloud computing is about abstraction. Cloud computing turns hardware like servers, racks, switches, hubs, networking, storage, and data centers into software, accessible and secured via APIs. Cloud computing has the following properties:

  * Automation: APIs, CLIs, and SDKs for literally every capability: storage, computing, networking, security, containers, caches and databases.

  * Elasticity: Quickly scale up and out during burst traffic, and scale down when the resources are no longer needed.

  * Self-service model: Your engineers are fully empowered, with the web console and APIs ready for consumption and usage at any time.

  * Multiple billing models: Advanced billing tools without having to negotiate any complex contract; just enter your credit card. Most cloud providers have multiple models: Pay as you go, like on-demand and serverless, or you can prepay for discounts via reservations, or even pay less if you can compromise on availability through spot instances.

  * Reliable: Achieve high availability through multiple layers of scale and isolation: deploy multiple instances, or across multiple availability zones, regions, and accounts.

  * Global presence: Cloud regions and point of presence (POP) across the globe, with advanced network peering capabilities at network hubs.

  * Managed services: Hands-off operations for databases, caches, messaging, container management frameworks, and other critical components.




Cloud computing is a game changer. Under the hood, how do these technologies achieve such amazing capabilities? If you are reading this book closely so far, you can probably guess one key ingredient: isolation. The underlying networking, control, and data plane powering these systems achieve a degree of safe multi-tenancy rarely if ever seen before the advent of cloud computing vendors.88

Your team can create proper services anywhere, on premises or in the cloud; however, they will be more effective if running in the cloud. Amazon Web Services (AWS) is the lead player in the cloud computing space. As of today, there are more than 200+ services available. AWS’s top services are:

  * EC2: For virtualized servers.

  * EBS: Block storage.

  * S3: Highly scalable and reliable object storage. Even if you use no other cloud service, S3 is so useful that it can play a role in many software architectures.

  * Relational Database Service (RDS): Managed databases service for MySQL, Postgres, Oracle, and SQL Server.

  * Aurora: Cloud database compatible with MySQL and Postgres APIs.

  * Lambda: Serverless Function as a Service (FaaS) engine with multi-language support.

  * ElastiCache: Managed cache service for Redis and Memcached.

  * CloudFront: High speed, low latency content delivery network (CDN).

  * Route53: Managed DNS service.

  * SQS / SNS: Messaging and queueing services with an AWS proprietary API.

  * Kinesis: Streaming data platform, specific to AWS. Kinesis is an Apache Kafka competitor.

  * EMR: Managed map/reduce services, hosting big data projects like Hadoop and Spark.

  * ECS / EKS: Container management and orchestration, with EKS being hosted Kubernetes.

  * EFS: Network file system support, compatible with CIFS and NFS.

  * IAM: Access management systems to control infrastructure security.

  * KMS: Key management, natively integrated into many AWS services, supporting symmetric and asymmetric key security.




Cloud computing and AWS are great, but like anything in software architecture, there are drawbacks:

  * Cost: Nothing in the cloud is cheap, and over the long run it is easy to end up spending more than you intend. Cost optimization is a constant battle, especially at scale. Keep in mind that AWS may also replace in-house staff you would otherwise need to run your data centers; there is no free lunch.

  * Vendor lock-in: While some AWS services mimic standard APIs, many tools are proprietary (SNS/SQS, S3). Instead, you can use open standards and open-source technologies to stay away from proprietary APIs and services, but those will have a cost to support as well.

  * Too many options: Easy to get lost in the sea of services and to understand the nuances between all possible alternatives. Lots of things to learn.




Security vs engineering tension: With all those options, your organization will likely have a knee jerk reaction to lock it all down! InfoSec departments push for security, often implemented by DevOps teams, which tends to limit options, services, and even styles of architecture in order to avoid security mistakes. While DevOps helps security and automation can avoid mistakes, these limits affect the speed of your engineering teams. The side effect is clear: engineering gets slower as every new technology requires gatekeepers and central development. For engineers, it might feel like the cloud is being managed as a data center, with little to no flexibility, stalling innovation.

Throughout this book, we have emphasized the importance of 3 key concepts in modern architectures: isolation, stable contracts, and lean libraries. Let us revisit these key principles of proper service architectures, but now in the context of the cloud. Principles will remain the same, but the considerations will change.

[Stable contracts](contents.xhtml#sc3_315a)

Public, stable contracts remain an absolute must for any scalable software architecture, especially in the cloud. Regardless of the physical organization, the contract presents a hard boundary separating the interface from the implementation: as long as all your public contracts are exposed via an API Gateway or similar method, you can use any AWS service, and it will be hidden from the consumers. Public contracts with private implementations deliver a huge benefit, as now teams can use all the 200+ services in AWS, and it will be completely transparent for consumers.

Remember that public contracts do not mean the accounts or contracts are facing customers directly.

Stable contracts are one way to enforce isolation between software components, but as we saw with distributed monoliths, there are many ways to break isolation: talking to databases directly instead of going through the contracts, sharing managed services, and many of the topics we covered in [Chapter 2](ch02.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Lack of Isolation. The contract needs to be king; stable contracts are our foundation, and infrastructure has to support achieving isolation for proper services and force use of the stable contract.

Your infrastructure can also influence the granularity of your contract, but to fully understand this concept, you need to understand isolation is best implemented in cloud architectures.

Let us explore this further.

[Proper isolation](contents.xhtml#sc3_316a)

Isolation is essential for cloud computing. As we quoted AWS’s CTO, Werner Vogels in [Chapter 7](ch07.xhtml), Proper Service Testing: ”Everything fails all the time.” Under the hood, cloud technologies apply multiple levels of physical and network isolation to deliver multi-tenancy and scale; these systems give you multiple ways to directly isolate technology. Using AWS as an example:

  * Multiple instances and sharded storage: Run your software on multiple EC2 instances or containers; create a database cluster across multiple nodes, and shard data across multiple EBS volumes or even S3, which has its own amazing resiliency safeguards.

  * Multiple availability zones: Every physical region has multiple AZs, which are typically physically separated data centers, with their own dedicated networking, power, HVAC systems. By ensuring your instances are deployed across multiple AZs, if one AZ suffers, your system continues to function.

  * Multiple regions: What if a key networking link is severed, or an entire physical location is compromised? You can run services and store data on different physical regions. Fully multi-region active-active architectures are expensive, but can deliver faster experiences for global customers. Newer NoSQL technologies and AWS Services make it possible to have safe, distributed data stores that cross physical region boundaries.

  * Multiple accounts: Beyond just separating non-production access from production, you can deploy solutions across multiple accounts for safety, security, or organizational isolation. We will discuss more advanced scenarios shortly.

  * Multiple cloud providers/data centers: Why just limit yourself to AWS? Container management solutions abstract underlying technology fundamentals in a hardware and/or cloud provider neutral fashion. Even just keeping your backups on another cloud provider or data center can be an isolation advantage. You might also want to use multiple cloud providers to access technology that is unique to one cloud provider or another.




Most of these cloud solutions achieve isolation for the purpose of availability; having multiple accounts is a way for organizations to achieve isolation from the perspective of technology solutions and teams. It is pretty common for companies to have multiple cloud accounts: at least 2-3 accounts, 1 for production and 2 for non-production; or accounts for different products or business units. But some companies go even further.

Disney streaming (Hulu, Disney, ESPN, and Starz) shared their AWS journey with multiple AWS accounts at AWS re:Invent 2022, sharing some of their experiences with data using AWS in the context of AWS EMR. At the time, Disney was actively managing more than 100+ AWS accounts (Figure 8.1). 

Figure 8.1: Disney streaming services: 100+ AWS accounts  
Source: [https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=y1p0BGsPxvw](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=y1p0BGsPxvw)

The biggest bank in Brazil is called ITAU ([https://www.itau.com.br/](https://www.itau.com.br/)), and they push the boundaries of AWS account management. ITAU shared their amazing transformation during a presentation at AWS re:Invent 2022 in Las Vegas, showing how they manage over 5000 accounts (Figure 8.2).

Figure 8.2: ITAU presentation at re:Invent 2022 - 5k AWS accounts  
Source: [https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=OoakxhLxue8](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=OoakxhLxue8)

Disney and ITAU are showing you the limits of what you can do with AWS account infrastructure. In practice, imagine a spectrum of how many accounts you can choose to have in your cloud infrastructure mapped onto your business architecture (Figure 8.3), where you can have:

  * One account per service

  * One account per business domain

  * One account for everything




Figure 8.3: Spectrum of cloud accounts vs technology

Let us explore these options further.

[One account per service](contents.xhtml#sc3_317a)

Cloud makes it easy to achieve isolation: you have no reason to share databases across services in production, and the best companies are isolating EVERYTHING they can using all the tools at their disposal, especially accounts:

  * Do not share managed service clusters: Make sure every service has its own, isolated and dedicated cluster. Do not share Aurora MySQL clusters, for instance.

  * Failure happens: Invest in chaos engineering to test your infrastructure.

  * Automation pays off: Thanks to the cloud, if you have decent automation, it is much easier to achieve scale on demand and support new, innovative ways to test your software. For instance, you can test in production by spinning up dark traffic clusters in production, using automation to easily create as many as you need.

  * Multiple isolation possibilities: AWS cloud allows you to perform other levels of isolation via accounts.




At the extreme, you can have one service per account.

Figure 8.4: Cloud isolation: one service per account

Figure 8.4 shows a simple example of a proper service, running on AWS. The Profile service code can run on any computing service such as EC2, ECS/EKS for containers, or even in Serverless; and it has a simple Aurora or RDS MySQL database. In the example of one account per service, all of these assets are isolated to a single AWS account, with no virtual networking connections to any other account.

One service account might sound extreme, but the AWS architecture absolutely supports it. The one service per account pattern allows the service team to completely own and operate all elements of their own service, from code to infrastructure to access, a common pattern in Silicon Valley called ”You build it, you run it”. The drawback of this approach is that you will have a lot of accounts: if you have 200 services, you easily have 400 accounts if you split non-production from production. 

Figure 8.5: Proper contracts in at the cloud - using one service per account

One service per account allows the ultimate flexibility for the service team in how they want to implement their service. (Figure 8.5) The contract can be exposed via API Gateway, or shared between accounts using AWS PrivateLink. The AWS account provides a hard boundary around your service with no visibility to outside consumers, where you can introduce any database store you want: RDS, Aurora, Redis, or S3.

With so many accounts and resources, automation is an absolute must; there will be lots of effort to expose services and handle networking, but the benefit is ultimate isolation. Cost also becomes very easy to understand; whatever cost comes from this account can be clearly assigned to a specific team.

What happens if the service is deprecated? The simple approach would be to archive the data to AWS Glacier and just delete the whole account; or perhaps another team needs to take over this account, and now they will have two accounts to manage.

Most organizations have a master or central account to handle security and build/deployment concerns. For this approach to be practical with a one-service per account, you will need to have a cross-account deployment capability.

[One account per business domain](contents.xhtml#sc3_318a)

If this approach seems too extreme, you can reduce the granularity by doing domain mapping. You can use Domain Driven Design (DDD) to group accounts by business domain or by organizational unit, shared under the same VPC and accountable to the same business line. Figure 8.6 shows an example of one account per business domain. The organization has identified an Engagement domain and assigned the Profile Service and Sales Service to it. 

Figure 8.6: Cloud isolation: one account per business domain

Automation is still a must. Sharing by business domain makes it easier for the teams to change ownership without reassigning the account. Cost can still be viewed and rolled up and assigned to the business unit; more refined costs within a domain can still be achieved by using common techniques such as AWS resource tagging.

By having fewer accounts, it is more difficult to achieve full isolation. Teams can affect each other’s resources through mistaken deployment or configuration changes, obviously mitigated through IAM roles but still a greater risk vs the hard boundary of an AWS account. Also, what happens if your organization changes its approach to domain or business organization? Are you moving services between accounts? Keep in mind what you are trading off.

[One account for everything](contents.xhtml#sc3_319a)

On the opposite end of the spectrum of account management is to just put everything into a single account. In our experience, this is the default state and what our industry encourages new cloud users to go with. Companies might have a division between non-production and production, or maybe a few more accounts to separate customer-facing services from offline analytics capabilities, but that is about it.

The risk that teams impact each other is high, and so now organizations want to centralize DevOps, establishing gatekeepers to avoid mistaken impacts, just like in the data center. One advantage is that it is easy for services to change owners since the infrastructure has no firm boundaries. While you can still achieve isolation through security groups, tagging, and IAM roles, it is much easier to make a mistake, cost is more difficult to track, and you are one bad manager away from having services talk to each other’s’ databases directly.

[Account organization impacts contract granularity](contents.xhtml#sc3_320a)

Figure 8.7: Your cloud account design impacts the granularity of your contracts

A very subtle point to understand is how the number of accounts you have can influence the granularity of your contracts. (Figure 8.7) In every case, your services need to be exposed using an API gateway, and from an outsider’s perspective they just see one public contract that the API gateway exposes. So, if you have one service per account you have achieved the smallest granularity possible, and you are exposing one public contract per service (at least).

When you have one account per business domain, all its services share that API gateway. To the outside world, you are presenting a larger contract, with a coarser contract granularity. Even though that account has multiple services, from an outsider’s perspective, you can almost treat it like a single public contract. You have the ultimate isolation, and the whole cloud is your internal implementation. You can change from 5 services to 10 services; you can transform all your services into serverless functions. as long as that API gateway delivers a backwards compatible contract, you can do what you want. But now, you have the full power of the cloud at your fingertips, rather than just a single Java service to execute change. In effect, we are enlarging the concept of a service oriented architecture, where instead of one service you have an entire account to play with for your implementation.

When you get to the other extreme, where everything lives in the same account, this whole concept breaks down - everything sees everything, so it is chaos - but the power lies when you correctly group services together into the same account at the right level of granularity for your business or tech organization.

[Internal shared libraries](contents.xhtml#sc3_321a)

One thing you want to limit is your internal shared libraries. You have access to all these amazing shared cloud services, and near-infinitely scalable hardware: you should be able to count your internal shared libraries on one hand. Five is an arbitrary number, but our point is that this should be as small as possible. The less internal shared libraries, the better.

One thing that is important to keep in mind is that cloud services often have two elements, an API, often REST with JSON/XML, and an SDK, which can be available for many languages like Java, Go, Rust, Python, TypeScript and many other languages. Unfortunately, AWS SDKs in Java use common Java libraries like Guava and Jackson and violate our principle of lean libraries. Think carefully and consider avoiding the big AWS SDKs to make your migrations easier.

If you have one account for everything, internal shared libraries represent risk; that library could be talking to shared databases, shared managed services, and multiple ways that can create a distributed monolith. If you have one account per service or per business domain, you are somewhat shielded; that library can likely only access the services within its account, which limits the potential damage. However, most of these environments have cross-account infrastructure like a platform or DevOps account for deployment or observability. Watch out that this does not lure you back into the distributed monolith, by creating a cross-account, super global library that breaks isolation.

[Serverless](contents.xhtml#sc3_322a)

Serverless computing goes beyond abstracting services; it abstracts hardware and operating systems completely and goes beyond to abstract the language runtime itself using the serverless engine, that is, AWS Lambda. (Figure 8.8) Serverless is easier on engineering teams, where you do not need to patch a serverless function or worry about JDK updates, for instance; serverless gives you the perfect opportunity to experiment with other languages. Serverless is also great if you need to isolate a stack/solution completely from the rest of your cloud or networking plane, for instance if you need to design a PCI compliant stack. 

Figure 8.8: Programming language is abstracted to consumers

You do not need serverless to experiment with new technologies or languages. SOA and microservices styles of architecture can enable you to use different languages: the service boundary protects the implementation behind a unified interface, and you can use simple protocols like HTTP 1/2/3 and REST and JSON. Let us be clear, few organizations can support 100 programming languages, this is hard to maintain, but most medium to large organizations can easily support 3-6 languages. We will cover supporting multiple languages later in this chapter.

Like anything in software architecture, serverless has drawbacks:

  * Engineering limitations: In fact, serverless has servers, but they are abstracted from you; this is both a benefit and a limitation, as you cannot fully leverage the server’s capabilities: you cannot persist data in memory for the long term, you cannot safely store data on the file system between executions. Several of these gaps and much more are reviewed in the paper: Serverless Computing: One Step Forward, Two Steps Back89.

  * External state and risk of breaking isolation: Because you cannot effectively hold local state with serverless architectures, you will need to rely on external services. Using more cloud services will increase cloud consumption and increase the complexity of your reliability path. The result can be more libraries and services, exposing you to more risk of breaking isolation.




[How the cloud impacts team organization](contents.xhtml#sc3_323a)

The big transformation cloud gives you is that it allows you to place software at the heart of your infrastructure design; not the other way around. Gone are the days when you needed a standalone Ops team that had to manage a fleet of servers they parcel out under strict supervision and guidance, or a central team of architects that strictly control how your software architectures are permitted to be built and expand given physical limitations and team structures.

Instead, the infrastructure can morph and change to be exactly what your service requires. The service drives the infrastructure. Team structures need to change as well. You do not need central Ops, you need embedded DevOps, providing help, guidance, and directly helping the team write Pulumi scripts and delivering cloud configuration. The result is a powerful cross functional team that draws on the strengths it needs to deliver business value, and one of those strengths is the mutable power of cloud infrastructure. You need DevOps in that cross functional team, ready to add their strength to bend the cloud to the needs of your service.

Let us be clear; this is not an invitation to chaos. No organization can sustain a ratio of 10 engineers, 20 independent databases and 30 programming languages. The limiting resource here is not infrastructure, but people and the talent density of your organization. Again: embrace the new, with principles, and we stand by the adage, Choose Boring Technology90.

[Pre-built capabilities - cloud services and SaaS](contents.xhtml#sc2_324a)

Scaling software is not easy. It is not just about having the right infrastructure; it is about having the right software architecture and having the right people to build and operate it. Thanks to open source, scaling software today is much easier compared with 20 years ago. We have common, published, open solutions whose characteristics are very well known and understood, and multiple examples of companies operating them to deliver amazing business capabilities.

Given that foundation, this is where cloud and SaaS providers can come in. Instead of having to source and hire teams with the talent and skill to operate software at scale, cloud managed services can deliver a near perfect setup with best practices and cloud native scaling already incorporated. While this is subject to some controversy in cases - see the debates on whether the big cloud providers crush small companies that provide true innovation and just feed off the open - source community without giving back - without a doubt the consumers directly benefit by gaining access to well - tuned services that just work and scale our systems, out of the box.

[Relational and NoSQL datastores](contents.xhtml#sc3_325a)

Why bother figuring out how to run a database at scale, when your cloud can do it for you? Slim down the number of operations experts you need to hire at your company and point those resources at business problems instead; you can rely on your cloud vendor or backing company for support with complex scenarios or to debug.

Let us review a partial, AWS-centric list of cloud managed database services; many more are available from SaaS companies or directly via the AWS marketplace. We have more database ideas for you later in this chapter, too.

  * RDS: Amazon will directly run and operate your database using RDS. RDS supports specific versions of MySQL, Postgres, SQL Server, and Oracle, and will handle many common operational tasks, like instance creation, deployment across multiple AZs patching, scaling, observability, monitoring, and backups without teams having to lift a finger. Using AWS Outposts91, you can even run RDS in your own datacenter!

  * Aurora: Beyond just database hosting, Amazon Aurora delivers a more cloud native implementation of MySQL and Postgres databases, delivering 3-5x improvements in performance given the same cost/instance size, and advanced features like automated read replica creation, global database deployments across regions, and serverless deployments.

  * Spanner: Google Spanner ([https://cloud.google.com/spanner/](https://cloud.google.com/spanner/)) is a completely new type of database from Google, but accessible through a familiar Postgres API. Spanner provides a multi-region, global database with massive horizontal scaling of reads and writes, but that still maintains strong relational properties with Strong-ACID compliance and ordering.92

  * DynamoDB: AWS DynamoDB is a key-value database service; DynamoDB is one of the foundational NoSQL stores; Amazon’s Dynamo paper ([https://www.allthingsdistributed.com/files/amazon-dynamo-sosp2007.pdf](https://www.allthingsdistributed.com/files/amazon-dynamo-sosp2007.pdf)) laid the foundation for many other open source databases, like Cassandra.

  * Cassandra: Cassandra is an open source columnar database, but is fully supported by DataStax, which delivers both the open source and a commercial branch of Cassandra. DataStax also has its own managed Cassandra service, Astra, which can be deployed to multiple clouds: AWS, GCP, and Azure. Amazon also has a Cassandra API compatible managed service, which under the hood, is just an API on top of DynamoDB. While it launched without full API compatibility, Amazon’s Managed Cassandra is quickly catching up.

  * Streaming data: LinkedIn’s Apache Kafka was the pioneer in open - source event sourcing architectures; Confluent ([https://www.confluent.io/](https://www.confluent.io/)) provides a commercial Kafka distribution that runs on all major cloud providers. Coming after Kafka, Amazon has its own Amazon Kinesis streaming data service, but Amazon also hosts its own managed Kafka service.

  * Search: ElasticSearch is one of the key NoSQL search technologies, great for text search, security, logging, and observability scenarios backed by its own company ([https://www.elastic.co/](https://www.elastic.co/)). Elastic Cloud delivers a hosted ElasticSearch environment on AWS, GCP or Azure, with full support and advanced commercial-only features. As we previously covered, Amazon has its own ElasticSearch fork, OpenSearch and provides a managed service as well.




[AI and analytics](contents.xhtml#sc3_326a)

Beyond just managed database services, most cloud companies also deliver advanced capabilities or support entire complex workflows. A few notable examples:

  * ML/AI: Cloud companies use their internal scale to deliver preconfigured, value-added AI services, such as live speech-to-text transcription, text-to-speech, chatbot support, transcription, natural language support, translation, vision, and generative AI.93 Many of the services can be tuned to your own specific use case.

  * Big Data infrastructure: Cloud providers have made significant efforts to fully host the big data lifecycle and facilitate the use of open - source software. AWS has EMR ([https://aws.amazon.com/emr/](https://aws.amazon.com/emr/)) to support Hadoop, Spark, and Hive infrastructure, with support for storage via Apache Parquet ([https://parquet.apache.org/](https://parquet.apache.org/)) or ORC ([https://orc.apache.org/](https://orc.apache.org/)), and modern lakehouse support via Apache Iceberg ([https://iceberg.apache.org/](https://iceberg.apache.org/)) and Hudi ([https://hudi.apache.org/](https://hudi.apache.org/)). GCP has one of the best big data infrastructure platforms via Dataproc ([https://cloud.google.com/dataproc](https://cloud.google.com/dataproc)) and BigTable ([https://cloud.google.com/bigtable](https://cloud.google.com/bigtable)).

  * Data science workflows: AWS’ Sagemaker ([https://aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/](https://aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/)) and GCP’s Vertex AI ([https://cloud.google.com/vertex-ai](https://cloud.google.com/vertex-ai)) provide an end-to-end environment to handle hosted notebooks and collaboration, model training and live inference using pre-canned approaches or by writing your own code and models directly. Both GCP and AWS are more raw, but SaaS alternatives exist; Databricks ([https://www.databricks.com/](https://www.databricks.com/)) delivers one of the premier SaaS alternatives for the Data Science Workflow, with hosted technology on AWS or GCP using open source as well as commercial packages that deliver faster performance than straight open source or even cloud providers for common tasks, along with an amazing support team.




All of these technologies can be used in whole, or in part; from your datacenter today, or as part of a larger modernization initiative. What is stopping you?

[Isolated deployment units](contents.xhtml#sc2_327a)

Isolation is key, isolation is the secret sauce to effective technology; we cannot repeat this enough. Containers are a great way to extend isolation for your services to the lower level of the operating system, but of course they in turn need proper infrastructure and services to be leveraged to their fullest extent. We touched on containers and container management systems such as Kubernetes in [Chapter 4](ch04.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Internal Shared Libraries; let us take a closer look.

[Containers and Kubernetes](contents.xhtml#sc3_328a)

In software, containers let you package your application, dependencies, and operating system requirements in one independent deployment unit. Containers are an old invention, originally designed by humankind to store food, but also used for storage, packing, and transportation. (Figure 8.9) Did you know the standard steel-based shipping container was created in the 1950s, according to Wikipedia ([https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Container](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Container))? 

Figure 8.9: Bottle containers from Kenya  
Source: [https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Container](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Container)

In 2013, the tech industry went through a similar revolution when Docker ([https://www.docker.com/](https://www.docker.com/)) released its open - source container engine. Containers in software are the standard unit that packages application code and dependencies for the application to run quickly and reliably. Just like those big metal shipping containers, but for software. Docker containers themselves are executable packages that can be run in almost any environment. Containers provide some degree of isolation for the application from the environment. Under the hood, Docker leverages LXC, the Linux kernel features such as that allow for process safety and sandboxing (namespaces and control groups).

Containers are not Virtual Machines (VM). (Figure 8.10) Virtual machines are abstractions of the physical hardware; for each VM, you ship the whole Operating System (OS), so VM images are big and they can take a long time to start up. Containers achieve a smaller size by sharing their host’s resources directly, and instead of having to have every single file in the operating system, they use a layered file system approach where the container’s files are merged with a base image. As a result, Docker images are much smaller and can boot much faster, in a matter of a few seconds.94

Figure 8.10: Containers vs VMs  
Source: [https://www.docker.com/resources/what-container/](https://www.docker.com/resources/what-container/)

Docker as a platform has many components and layers. (Figure 8.11) Docker has a CLI where you can issue commands like docker run to run docker containers, docker pull to fetch docker images, and much more. Your docker host will run the docker engine, which will download docker images from a public registry like Docker Hub ([https://hub.docker.com/](https://hub.docker.com/)) or a managed private container registry like Amazon ECR ([https://aws.amazon.com/ecr/](https://aws.amazon.com/ecr/)). 

Figure 8.11: Docker architecture  
Source: <https://docs.docker.com/get-started/overview/>

Containers have multiple benefits, such as:

  * Application isolation: Very important principle, as we continue to emphasize.

  * Flexibility: Engineers can easily specify application dependencies in a single file, without requiring coordination with DevOps teams.

  * Identical behaviors: When you use containers as part of the engineering development process, your engineers are running on their laptops exactly what is running in production. This reduces errors and helps with debugging.

  * Fast start up time: Improved startup time, a great fit for function as a service (FaaS) and short-running processes.

  * Increased security: Do not just have 80 application processes with equal access to the OS; containers isolate processes from the operating systems, and can improve security further by using sandboxes like gVisor ([https://gvisor.dev/](https://gvisor.dev/)).




Containers have cons as well; any time you introduce another abstraction layer, you increase complexity, and potentially impact performance, overhead and throughput. Some examples:

  * IBM has an interesting paper comparing the performance of virtual machines and containers: [https://dominoweb.draco.res.ibm.com/reports/rc25482.pdf](https://dominoweb.draco.res.ibm.com/reports/rc25482.pdf)

  * Uber reported 5-10% overhead by running Cassandra on Containers [http://highscalability.com/blog/2016/9/28/how-uber-manages-a-million-writes-per-second-using-mesos-and.html](http://highscalability.com/blog/2016/9/28/how-uber-manages-a-million-writes-per-second-using-mesos-and.html)

  * Isolated containers compete for CPU, memory, disk, and kernel resources, which means there is some risk of resource contention. The Linux kernel itself is very complex, so kernel collisions, bugs, and inefficiencies can happen, like as described in this interesting article from sysdig: [https://sysdig.com/blog/container-isolation-gone-wrong/](https://sysdig.com/blog/container-isolation-gone-wrong/?ref=hackernoon.com)

  * Another interesting discussion on container performance on StackOverflow: [https://stackoverflow.com/questions/21889053/what-is-the-runtime-performance-cost-of-a-docker-container](https://stackoverflow.com/questions/21889053/what-is-the-runtime-performance-cost-of-a-docker-container)




At the end of the day, nothing is free; introducing more layers of abstractions always has some cost. Regardless, we feel it is still worth pursuing containers, given the isolation, startup time, and flexibility benefits containers bring to the table.

Containers are great because they allow engineers to be more productive due to superior tooling, and engineers can run containers on their machines easily. But containers alone do not scale by themselves; you cannot manually operate hundreds to thousands of containers in production. You need a platform, a cluster, and some way to handle orchestration, which is where container management systems like Kubernetes come in. 

Figure 8.12: k8s cluster  
Source: [https://kubernetes.io/docs/concepts/overview/components/](https://kubernetes.io/docs/concepts/overview/components/)

We covered Kubernetes (k8s) as part of our sidecar conversation back in [Chapter 4](ch04.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Internal Shared Libraries. Review that section for an overview of its obvious benefits, but let us dig a bit into the architecture.

k8s itself was born from more than a decade of operating containers within Google via the Google Borg95 project, mixed with the best of the open - source community. The k8s architecture is composed of two main concepts: the control plane, and the data plane. (Figure 8.12) k8s’ control plane is the brain of k8s, managing worker nodes, pods, and making global decisions about the cluster as a whole, with components such as the API server, etcd, control manager and scheduler. The data plane is where real work happens; it houses worker nodes that host pods/individual container runtimes, a kubelet process to execute control plane instructions, and kube-proxy to handle networking.

Running containers at scale, you need to use a container management system. You can use simple systems, such as AWS’ ECS ([https://aws.amazon.com/ecs/](https://aws.amazon.com/ecs/)), but k8s has many advantages:

  * Community: Huge OSS community, constantly building and innovating on the k8s ecosystem.

  * Less lock-in: Many people consider k8s to be the cloud specification, meaning a standard way to run software on the cloud. k8s allows you to be open and less coupled to public cloud providers.

  * Simplify and reduce DevOps workload: Every company has a shortage of DevOps professionals, and the ratio between engineers and DevOps engineers is not at par. k8s allow engineers to take on more of the DevOps workload directly, and at the same time still follow standards, overall reducing the DevOps team’s backlog.

  * Cost savings: Bin packing allows you to host more applications on the same node without compromising availability. You can do more with fewer resources, this is a big deal especially considering the high costs of public cloud providers.

  * Cloud support: k8s is complicated, but you do not have to do it alone. Most cloud providers have native, hosted k8s solutions handling many common issues, such as AWS’ EKS.




Proper services work perfectly with containers and k8s. APIs are exposed easily and flexibly, and can be exposed between containers with just configuration. k8s does not care about how your software is built, just that the container runs and exposes endpoints as per the configuration; your public contracts will not be coupled with k8s. Language experimentation is much easier since the new language and all of its dependencies can be easily specified inside a container, i.e. satisfy OS setup and dependencies for Rust instead of Java. With all this abstraction, it is straightforward to re-use the same deployment pipeline but have a completely different language.

Like anything in software architecture, there are trade-offs. k8s also has drawbacks:

  * Debugging and troubleshooting: Gets more difficult; there are more layers, networking gets more complex, and lots of things happening in the Linux kernel make understanding errors more difficult. Your team will require training and expertise.

  * Complexity: k8s brings new systems, new technologies, many layers and complexity. None of this comes for free. You will need to migrate to Kubernetes from your legacy stack, change all deployment pipelines, and integrate k8s with your cloud provider. Upgrading k8s versions also requires effort. For many people, abstracting the cloud provider is moving away from delivering business value and just adding unnecessary complexity.




[Cloud spectrum](contents.xhtml#sc3_329a)

Figure 8.13: Cloud spectrum

Cloud computing makes sense but is expensive. Both cloud computing and container architectures solves some problems well, but can create others, draining your pocketbook the entire time. No matter if for security, compliance or for cost savings, sometimes it makes sense to host your solution yourself. Not all workloads need to run in the cloud. David Heinemeier Hansson, co-founder of 37 signals, creator of Ruby on Rails, moved several 37 Signals products like Basecamp and Hey out of the cloud and out of k8s, claiming a potential 1.5M in savings per year96.

Stack Overflow97, is a data center hosted monolith, not in the cloud, has 9 servers and serves 2B+ monthly page views; they even removed their Redis caching layer without degradation. How did Stack Overflow manage to do it? Good engineering, focus on the principles of computer science fundamentals like algorithms and data structures. Sometimes simple solutions are the best solutions.

However not all companies can pull what Stack Overflow did, as we know classical monoliths often are awful and have bigger problems. Cloud and SaaS offers can help companies to scale quickly as you have seen in our previous examples. The fact is, these solutions are not necessarily black and white, and can sit on a spectrum (Figure 8.13), and often do, given that folks are somewhere in their organization’s journey, either across time or technical complexity.

The best answer of where your organization should be, heavily depends on how you got there.

Depending on the age of your company, your infrastructure likely went through one of more of these waves:

  * Wave 1: the mainframe: Massive, complex single machines hosting multiple applications. Little to no isolation, powerful beasts, often the ultimate monolith.98 Mainframes are still out there; AWS even has a dedicated practice for migrating mainframes to the cloud ([https://aws.amazon.com/mainframe/](https://aws.amazon.com/mainframe/)).

  * Wave 2: hardware clusters: The conquest of the intel desktop CPU promoted architectures with large sets of individual servers working in concert. No virtualization; everything running on real hardware instances and using real operating systems.

  * Wave 3: virtualization: It was a real pain to handle physical hardware when you needed to repurpose it; taking an instance down, reformatting/reinstalling everything. What if you have too much hardware, and could run 3 applications on one machine, but they do not play well together on the same OS/lack isolation? Powerful virtualization software meant you could quickly repurpose hardware, redeploy instances with the click of a button.

  * Wave 4: cloud: Virtualization still meant massive racks of datacenter machines you had to manage, power and HVAC to pay for, and individual CPUs, disks and memory that can fail. Why bother with any of this? Many applications worked perfectly well when the fundamentals of any individual machine were abstracted away. Big companies can pool hundreds of thousands of machines together, and gain cost efficiencies with multitenancy - use the machine when I do not, and we both save money.

  * Wave 5: containerization: All these cloud providers have proprietary APIs and do mostly the same thing under the hood. Abstract the cloud itself! Containerization satisfies everything your application needs, and then you can run it anywhere: your laptop, the cloud, your datacenter, even on embedded hardware. Let us get true cloud portability.

  * Wave 6: true hybrid: Now that containerization has delivered a truly portable infrastructure, you can be truly polycloud: use any cloud service or your own datacenter, in the combination and mix that works for your needs and capabilities. Good examples of enabling technologies include the open source Crossplane framework ([https://www.crossplane.io/](https://www.crossplane.io/)) and Google’s Anthos ([https://cloud.google.com/anthos/](https://cloud.google.com/anthos/)).




Though we describe each wave as a progression, actually each class of architecture has its own unique benefits. Just running a monolith on a single big machine is efficient, like we saw with Stack Overflow; bare metal hardware means maximum performance with fewer abstraction layers; virtualization let apps be portable enough for most cases; cloud gives you a huge set of advantages with a shared environment; but cloud providers cost and still tie you down, containers instead deliver you the ultimate portability. True hybrid infrastructure lets you choose any or all of the above as you need to.

Another aspect to consider is the technical complexity and scale of your company. Companies go through different phases of complexity as they evolve, and that can influence the right answer:

  * Early phases/tiny company: Your software is something ruthlessly focused on finding product market fit. You have not discovered every single use case, and in a lot of cases your software would probably run perfectly well on your laptop (for example, classical monolith)! Consider a colocated server or a single purpose hosted hardware vendor like Hertzner ([https://www.hetzner.com/](https://www.hetzner.com/)).

  * Small to medium scale company: Complexity is likely blooming here, and what you need from your software grows, but you are not a massive company. Economies of scale are difficult to achieve. You win with cloud or SaaS solutions, since you get access to an expert designed and operated solution, and can focus your limited personnel on business problems.

  * Large to massive company: You likely own buildings and entire campuses. Complexity has bloomed, but actually, you have the economies of scale where you could afford to dedicate folks to technical solutions. Containerization and k8s on datacenter-hosted servers, combined with customized in-house solutions or specific cloud solutions can hit the sweet spot for a lot of your costs.




Our point here is that the cloud is not the only answer. The correct answer changes over time, and depending on the state of your company any one of these states might have advantages for your unique situation. There is no silver bullet. Costs, company age, maturity, and technical complexity will drive you to one point on this scale. Only you can decide where.

[Real time access to data - streaming](contents.xhtml#sc2_330a)

Modern architectures need comprehensive support for asynchronous distributed systems which in turn need real time access to data. After all, some tasks might run long; some jobs might only occur in a data environment, and have no online component; life is more than just chains of services running synchronously. Designing a modern application means you need to make space for streaming data and asynchronous processing.

Back in [Chapter 2](ch02.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Lack of Isolation, we covered the basics of event sourcing - based architectures, their supporting technology platforms like Apache Kafka, and advanced architectures like command query responsibility segregation (CQRS). Let us take a deeper look to what these can mean to your modern applications.

[Apache Kafka](contents.xhtml#sc3_331a)

Apache Kafka ([https://kafka.apache.org/](https://kafka.apache.org/)) is a distributed event store and streaming data processing platform. At its heart, Kafka is a distributed event log; Kafka as a platform is praised for its resiliency and ability to support low latency data processing applications at massive scale. Kafka was created in LinkedIn by Jay Kreps, Neha Narkhede, and Jun Rao in 2011. Kafka was publicly released in 2011 by LinkedIn, co - authored by Jay Kreps, Neha Narkhede, and Jun Rao, who went on to found confluent ([https://www.confluent.io/about/](https://www.confluent.io/about/)) a company dedicated to building out and supporting Kafka.

Kafka has so many possible technology applications and is heavily used by different organizations across many different industries, such as:

  * Banks, stock exchanges, and insurance companies: Process payments and financial transactions in real - time and without data loss.

  * Logistics and automotive: Kafka easily handles the flood of events as you track/monitor cars, trucks, fleets, and shipment telemetry in real-time.

  * Manufacturing: Continuously capture/monitor sensor data from IoT devices.

  * Travel, retail, E-commerce: Collect an endless stream of customer interactions and UI click events; react to customer events such as orders, hotel bookings, and much more.

  * Health care: Monitor patients in hospital care, predict changes in health conditions, and alert teams to act fast.

  * For all companies that have big data: Capture the massive flood of data from different divisions and organizations, and funnel it into effective ingestion processes. Connect, store, and make data highly available, the foundation for data platforms.




Under the hood, Kafka is all about the concept of a distributed log. What is a log? In this context, we do not mean application logging like with log4j, logback, commons-logging or general observability - though Kafka does handle those use cases just fine! Logs as a concept are the simplest form of storage abstraction, according to Jay Kreps99, Kafka co-creator. A log has the following properties:

  * Append only

  * Totally ordered sequence of records ordered by time

  * Immutable




Figure 8.14: What is a log  
Source: [https://engineering.linkedin.com/distributed-systems/log-what-every-software-engineer-should-know-about-real-time-datas-unifying](https://engineering.linkedin.com/distributed-systems/log-what-every-software-engineer-should-know-about-real-time-datas-unifying)

Figure 8.14 illustrates these properties. The order of records in a log is time based. Entries on the left are older than entries on the right. New entries will only be added on write, the tail of the log, and never changed. Most of the problems in distributed systems really come down to agreeing on what happened, and when; the log solves these problems, and can be a reliable way to keep a remote system in perfect sync: just receive the log and replay it! As such, logs solve two problems in distributed systems, ordering changes and distributing data, a simple data structure that lays the foundation to solve a much more complex and important problem.

[Kafka architecture](contents.xhtml#sc3_332a)

Figure 8.15: High-level view of Kafka architecture

Kafka takes that concept of the log and builts the rest of the supporting conceptual architecture needed to build an effective distributed system (Figure 8.15). A Kafka cluster is organized into Topics, and a Topic can have multiple partitions. Applications that write data to the Kafka cluster are called producers, and applications that read data from Kafka are called consumers. Services can be both producers and consumers of the same or different Topics. The data within a Topic is replicated for fault-tolerance and high availability reasons.

To give you a metaphor: you can imagine a topic like a folder in the filesystem. Events will be files in this folder. Partitions will be sub-folders. Within a Kafka partition, messages are ordered by offset, position of the message in a partition. Producers write at the end of the partition, and consumers can either read from the head or the tail of the partition. 

Figure 8.16: Kafka: Topics, Partitions, Producers, and Consumers

Kafka consumer’s ability to read from the tail or head of the topics is a killer feature (Figure 8.16) because it allows you to do real-time processing by reading from the head, and historical re - processing, read from the tail or from a specific offset in the Topic. Such a feature is super useful for Big Data organizations processing data, but also any case where precise synchronization and event replay can be an advantage, like keeping a precise financial ledger or replaying data within recovery scenarios.

Kafka supports persistent consumer connections across a variety of software languages; start by reading the tail of a Topic, and stay listening. Once a new message arrives, your connection will be activated and the message supplied. Your software can read it and activate, triggering any action needed. You can have a wide number of consumers across different servers, all ready and waiting to react. No need to have one massive orchestrator triggering services; with Kafka’s inherent low latency, Kafka can sit at the heart of a powerful, asynchronous, distributed system.

Beyond these functional characteristics, Apache Kafka has several operational benefits:

  * High throughput: Kafka can deliver messages in under 2ms, even between cluster machines with limited networking.

  * Scalability: Kafka cluster can scale horizontally, supporting thousands of brokers and trillions of messages per day, petabytes of data, and hundreds of thousands of partitions.

  * Durability: Persistent storage with fault tolerance.




Like anything in software architecture, we always have tradeoffs; Apache Kafka’s drawbacks and limitations include:

  * Limited observability: Kafka lacks a full set of observability tools and UIs out of the box.

  * Message tweaking: Kafka was designed to be an append only system. If you need to change any individual message, the system gets much slower.

  * Millisecond latency: Kafka is fast, but only so fast; when you need sub - millisecond latency, such as for high-frequency trading, Kafka is not the right fit. Shared memory is preferred.

  * Unstable networks: Kafka was not built for unstable networks when you have lots of clients reconnecting; MQTT is a much better fit for this scenario. MQTT also has better connectivity when there are tens of thousands of client applications.

  * Kafka is not a file server: The ideal Kafka message size is 1kb; the max message size is 1MB. You are not meant to send large objects, directly handling big files would be slow, and a better fit would be a system like AWS S3. Often JSON is the natural choice for a record, but you can use other text formats.




[Kafka and CQRS / ES](contents.xhtml#sc3_333a)

The CQRS architecture model, where you have one implementation path and storage system for writes/commands, and another for reads/queries, can be powerful. (Figure 8.17) CQRS allows us to isolate reads and writes and therefore has better scalability and isolation, and allows you to use specific technologies that each use case might require; but it brings greater complexity as a result.

Apache Kafka can be used to help implement the CQRS pattern; for instance, your application can dual write to the MySQL database and a Kafka Topic. A Kafka Streams distributed reader can directly respond to that message, take the message from that topic, and write it to the ElasticSearch system used by the query path. Of course, this system is eventually consistent, but still has the ability to use the relational DB for what it is good at, and the NoSQL system for what it can do better, but resulting in a more complex system. A jack of all trades database like Postgres sometimes makes for a better solution with good enough features like text search. 

Figure 8.17: CQRS architecture

Event Sourcing solves another key problem for distributed architectures. Monoliths typically have a simple datastore setup; a central relational database that contains all data, and can answer any query, and all tables are at your disposal; easy-to-do to do joins. With SOA and microservices, you do not have a centralized database anymore, and you absolutely do not want a centralized database for isolation reasons. So how do we join data across services or domains? We need Event Sourcing (ES), streaming, and Kafka to the rescue (Figure 8:18). 

Figure 8.18: ES with Kafka

The profile service would publish an event to Kafka every time a new profile is created. The sales team wants to run some campaigns to sell more products, but they do not have access to the profile database, and they should not have that database access. The sales team could call the profile service, but that would be a sync call and would be inefficient polling, and the profile service also would need to expose this operation. 

Now because the profile service published an event into Kafka, first of all, it is all async, and now sales can do the join on their side. Same could happen to more complex scenarios where you might want to join multiple Kafka topics together in order to process complex real-time joins.

[ksqlDB](contents.xhtml#sc3_334a)

ksqlDB ([https://ksqldb.io/](https://ksqldb.io/)) is a database purpose-built for stream processing applications. Integrated with Apache Kafka, ksqlDB almost allows you to treat Kafka like you would any typical database: you can run real-time queries against Kafka using SQL and create materialized views that support lookup queries against those views. In practice, ksqlDB is an upside-down database: whereas a regular database stores data and lets you run queries, in ksqlDB, you store the queries, which are run against the data as it runs through Kafka Topics. 

Figure 8.19: ksqlDB  
Source: <https://ksqldb.io/>

ksqlDB has some traits of Complex Event Processing (CEP) and solutions like Esper ([https://www.espertech.com/esper/](https://www.espertech.com/esper/))100, but using a completely different implementation by relying on Kafka. The mindset shift here is that inserting data into the relational database and doing the queries later is too slow; the opposite approach worked better since Kafka is pretty fast at ingesting large amounts of data.

Another ksqlDB advantage is that you have fewer moving parts, and a simple language (SQL) that pretty much any analyst or engineer already understands. Queries can be pull or push; pull means you run the query now and get current results, and push means you subscribe to the materialized views and get events as they pop up. All of these traits allow ksqlDB to reduce the barriers to making joins less painful in distributed systems.

[Efficient engineers](contents.xhtml#sc2_335a)

What is efficiency? Is it about getting things done quickly? Or correctly? Quickness and correctness are not opposed; correctness and living by engineering principles do not slow things down, instead, they allow us to move fast with stability.

Engineering is a marathon, not a sprint; runners succeed by putting their foot down in the right place with the right weight and stability, time after time until the end; not going fast and with complete disregard to form, risking injury. Same with engineering: proper design and architecture are not a bottleneck, they ensure you make decisions that benefit the long - term health of your system, and in the long run they are the right way to go. Proper services allow us to explore options to not only make our engineers happier but more effective.

Unfortunately, in the real world, principles get discarded with business urgency, and the most dirty, quick and ultimately full of technical debt option wins. In Brazil such a methodology has a name: Extreme Go Horse101.

A lack of proper software architecture and design often shows up as other problems, such as:

  * Lack of principles: By not applying proper service principles like Isolation, SOA, Lean Libraries, teams create traps for themselves that kill productivity and maintainability over the long run.

  * Lack of talent density: People think architecture slows things down, when in reality people lack expertise. Teams lack talent, decisions turn into a popularity contest, or are entirely driven by fear of change. Poor decisions are made when there is no socialization and, critically, awareness of the better options.

  * Artificial dependencies: Splitting teams into multiple pockets, creating barriers and cross-team dependencies that are not necessary. Such as having separate QA Teams, DBA Teams, DevOps Teams. You can have dedicated teams that focus on a discipline to ensure best practices, but all people, QA, DBAs, Ops should be embedded in a true cross functional team to deliver for the business.

  * Only the process can be slow: We do not have time for architecture or tech exploration! But multi-year SAFe waterfall projects are ok. We absolutely have no time for a retrospective, or maybe just 15 minutes, but nonproductive multi - day planning sessions are okay, plans with hundreds of team dependencies, engineers with 5 different daily status meetings are great. Consider lean thinking; do you know where the waste lives?

  * Bad incentives: Only getting things done is rewarded. No rewards for deleting code, no rewards for decommissioning services. Refactoring often is seen as an offensive word, a risk with no reward.




Instead, we should trust our engineers, give them better tools in order to maximize correctness and improve productivity the right way. Starting off by letting them use modern languages!

[Modern languages](contents.xhtml#sc3_336a)

Programming languages are tools for us to express via code what we need to do and how we want to get it done. It is our way to talk with computers. Many engineers and managers think the bottleneck is in typing code: more lines of code per hour, the better, all about productivity and getting more things done. We believe this is the wrong way to think about programming languages. Languages are about choosing the right tool for the job, allowing us to do more work with less code. Modern languages bring innovation, sure, but they also make maintenance easier and safer.

What drives language success? Should you just choose a language based on its popularity? Consider these factors:

  * Engineering pool: How easy is it to find engineers who know the language?

  * Community support: How well supported is the language? How large is the community? Are there lots of active open - source projects, and libraries? Are multiple companies using the language in production applications?

  * Fit or niche: How much does the language bring to the table? Is it broadly applicable, or only usable for niche applications? What would my organization gain by bringing in this language? Are there design benefits? Performance benefits?




Internal shared libraries and software architecture play a big role here, either encouraging or discouraging your ability to use other languages. For instance, if you have hundreds of libraries written in Java or C#, it is harder to justify porting all this stack to a different language, let us say C++. We have reviewed the ways that a casual approach to internal shared libraries can slow down your organization and even create a distributed monolith; now you see another dimension. We already have enough problems dealing with all these library migrations and infosec requirements with one language, how can we support recreating all these libraries in a new language and doubling our work on support? This is a false choice; have fewer libraries.

In a perfect world, we would be able to abstract languages completely and let that be an implementation detail that every team should be able to pick. But the truth is, language popularity is real, good engineers are hard to find, and they do not want to end up fragmented and wasting your time constantly setting up new IDEs and language configurations. Do not support 100 languages badly, support 3-6 languages really well. Overall, you want to avoid languages that are too bleeding edge and that do not have that community support, but also limit further development in languages that are dying and do not have a big active community innovating for you.

[Language spectrum](contents.xhtml#sc3_337a)

One practice you can do is to classify the languages you use and the languages you are interested in according to your own company activities, language lifecycle, maintenance path, and preferences. For instance: JavaScript is great for frontend engineering, Java is great for backend and long-running processes, for scripting and short-running processes or machine-learning maybe python has a better fit. Try creating a simple table recommending languages for specific tasks like in Table 8.1.

Language |  Recommendation  
---|---  
Java, Kotlin, Scala, Go |  Backend Services, Batch Processing, Long Running Jobs  
JavaScript, TypeScript |  Frontend Applications and BFFs  
Scala, Python, R |  Analytics, Machine Learning, Streaming  
Rust, Zig, C++ |  Low-level solutions, core platform components, sidecars, and security components.  
Python, Zsh, Groovy |  General Scripting, Ops, DevOps small tasks  
  
Table 8.1: Languages and recommendations table

We need to be careful when we talk about languages! Truth is, programming languages are general purpose instruments; you can build a service in Typescript or a UI module in Rust. Engineers can have very strong preferences, and our goal here is not to start a flamewar and upset anyone in the community. Our table is just an example; you should build this table considering your experiences and the reality in your company.

Another practice some companies do is to create a heatmap, or 4-quarter chart mapping out languages across your company’s viewpoints, business activities, language lifecycle and preferences (Figure 8:20).

Figure 8.20: Sample programming language chart

Again, this is just an example; do not email the authors with hate mail if you do not agree with our classification here. But if you have such a chart in your company, it can help your engineers understand whether a language should be used broadly or we should be entirely avoided for any new development. In our Figure 8.20 example, we have one spectrum on whether the language which could be used broadly in multiple use cases or only for niche applications or very specific problems. For instance, a broader, active language is ideal for services; a niche active language could be perfect for certain platform components and services or very specific tasks like security, networking, load balancing, and task management.

Languages are also very important to attract and retain talent; some engineers would never work in some languages. For instance, a Rust engineer might never want to work with Java. Like we said, individuals can have strong preferences! Strong engineers typically like to be working on new technologies; supporting more languages gives you more options for hiring. Diversity is always a good thing, and it is the same with programming languages.

You might be thinking, I do not want to add new languages, I am happy with the languages I have, or I love my monorepo pattern and prefer to have just one language. That is fine! You can achieve a lot of the innovation you need to exit your monolith without introducing a lot of new different, languages; keep in mind that:

  * Engineers can have strong preferences depending on the technology area they work with; and beyond preference, it might be a must have to gain productivity:

    * Frontend engineers would not want to work on Java, and the days of the thick desktop client are far in the past; they want JavaScript/TypeScript.

    * Data analytics professionals rely on hundreds of libraries and frameworks like Spark Streaming for streaming data analysis, or TensorFlow for ML. They need Scala, R and Python, and will not want to write their aggregations and ML models in JavaScript.

    * Many backend engineers will not like to write services in JavaScript; they might prefer Java, Kotlin, Go, C#, or even Rust.

  * Technologies change and language preferences change too; your company might acquire other companies that introduce languages you do not have today. The adaptability principle is key: are you and your technology stacks ready and prepared to react to change?

  * Ask your developers if you are sure they do not want to use new languages. You might be surprised by the answers.




[JVM languages](contents.xhtml#sc3_338a)

Many organizations have Java as the primary language for backend services and analytics. One amazing benefit for the Java ecosystem is the Java Virtual Machine (JVM) and the low-hanging fruit you can consider is to leverage JVM languages, such as Scala, Kotlin, Clojure, Jython, JRuby, Rhino (JS), and Groovy, that will natively run in a Java environment. Adopting these languages can deliver benefits such as:

  * Zero friction to introduce:

    * Reuse your entire internal software ecosystem, all your Java internal shared libraries.

    * Reuse all your infrastructure and runtime servers. If you run Tomcat, for instance, you can continue without change.

    * Reuse a good part of all your tooling, since you can use the same build tools like Maven or Gradle, and very likely, your deployment pipelines will work without modification.

  * Enjoy the ride: Benefit from new syntax, reduced boilerplate code, new libraries, and frameworks run in the JVM, with some limitations but clear benefits.

  * Path to be native: You can start introducing a language that runs on the JVM, and later, switch to the native runtime. For instance, from Rhino to NodeJS, Bun, or Deno.




Yes, you might be losing the latest features, like in the case of Python, JavaScript, and Ruby, and even performance to some degree as there is another layer of abstraction, but depending on your use case, that might be fine to gain all the benefits for the purpose a new language can bring. The JVM can be your go-to solution when introducing Scala, Clojure, Kotlin, or other native JVM languages, but otherwise, consider JVM languages a path for exploration.

[Flexible data models](contents.xhtml#sc2_339a)

A professional chef’s kitchen has many tools; they do not just use one type of pan, and they do not try to zest an orange with a kitchen knife. Software is no different, you need to choose the right tool for the job. Data modeling is a key activity for software design, the right data model makes engineering easy vs having to introduce multiple transformations and workarounds in application code. Your choice of datastore directly influences how you can model your data, and a badly chosen data model can make delivering software and adapting to change really difficult. Flexibility is key. Modern engineers know that most times, you cannot fit all use cases in a relational model, so let us review some additional options.

[NoSQL databases](contents.xhtml#sc3_340a)

Engineers and architects are terrible at naming software; as Phil Karlton explained: “There are only two hard things in Computer Science: cache invalidation and naming things.”102 Engineering names typically do not tell the whole story and can confuse people; NoSQL is no exception. The relational database has been around since the 60s, and they are solid, they scale, they are reliable, and allow us to get things done. Let us be clear, there is nothing wrong with relational databases, they are tools, and they work. There is nothing wrong with SQL, either!

The problem with relational databases is that they are overused in use cases that are not necessarily the best fit for them, like:

  * Caching

  * Queuing

  * Full-Text Searching

  * In-Memory Persistence

  * Real-Time Analytics

  * Streaming Data and Analytics

  * Graph Processing

  * Block Storage

  * Document Storage

  * Time Series Ingestion and Processing

  * Event Bus

  * Machine Learning

  * High-Speed Transactions / Low-Frequency Trading

  * Knowledge Graph




NoSQL is not about replacing relational databases for all its use cases, but instead supporting use cases where it makes sense to take a different approach. So, before we drill down on what NoSQL is about, let us understand what NoSQL is not.

NoSQL is not:

  * About rejecting SQL. A lot of NoSQL solutions have SQL-like or full SQL interfaces.

  * The only way to scale RDBMS solutions; look at Amazon Aurora, Uber’s Serverless ([https://www.uber.com/blog/schemaless-part-one-mysql-datastore/](https://www.uber.com/blog/schemaless-part-one-mysql-datastore/)), or Netflix’ Dynomite ([https://netflixtechblog.com/introducing-dynomite-making-non-distributed-databases-distributed-c7bce3d89404](https://netflixtechblog.com/introducing-dynomite-making-non-distributed-databases-distributed-c7bce3d89404)).

  * About dropping all forms of consistency or durability.




NoSQL solutions, like all software, present trade-offs and options, and not just towards availability. Relational Databases also are highly available, but they tend towards strong consistency rather than availability. A great lens to use when analyzing NoSQL solutions is the CAP Theorem, created by Eric Brewer. (Figure 8.21) Partition tolerance is not really optional in distributed databases because otherwise, they cannot be used as a source of truth. So, partition tolerance (CP) is not really optional, leaving you with the tradeoffs between consistency and availability; which one do you want to favor most?

In relational databases, consistency is often described through the ACID guarantee: Atomicity, Consistency, Isolation and Durability. Mature, strong consistent systems are still available, and highly available systems are still consistent, but NoSQL systems support a looser consistency model than ACID, commonly referred to as BASE:

  * Basically available: NoSQL systems focus on data availability over absolute consistency by replicating data across multiple nodes first.

  * Soft state: Data stores are not necessarily write consistent; replicas might not be consistent with each other at all times.

  * Eventually consistent: Data stores do exhibit consistency but at a later point in time; such as when read back.




Figure 8.21: CAP Theorem for NoSQL

NoSQL allows us to choose the right form of persistence for the use case; it is about design and each system’s unique capabilities. At scale, you need to have shared ownership of your infrastructure: You build it, you run it. Several corporations do not want to add new databases because they perceive a cost of maintaining databases for their operations teams; using databases is not like using a 3rd party library. Such thinking makes sense in a world of classic monolithic applications and old - style centralized operations of on premises applications.

For modern cloud-native applications, this mentality does not make sense and does not scale. As we discussed at length in our section covering pre-built capabilities, we live in a managed-database era. Plenty of products offering fully managed, hands-off database options. The burden is no longer so heavy on operations teams, and making the right choice here should enable organizations to use new database technologies.

Ultimately, we are talking about polyglot persistence here, where engineers are empowered to choose the right database for the job/use case, so long as it is properly supported. Proper services support this goal, allowing us to abstract databases and easily migrate data:

  * Migrate from a SQL database to Cassandra.

  * Add Redis as a cache.

  * Introduce ElasticSearch as a search engine.




In short, proper services let you perform migrations under the hood, on the fly, and to experiment with different technologies with safety and transparency. (Figure 8.22)

Never forget that proper services rely on isolation to deliver this database abstraction, meaning teams must not share that database with other applications. Isolation provides the proper protection that allows teams to change, evolve, migrate, and change their mind about their data stores.

Figure 8.22: Proper services (contracts and isolation) and NoSQL

[Going beyond the relational model](contents.xhtml#sc3_341a)

To help when evaluating NoSQL databases, we can group them by the different ways they model data: key/value store, document store, column oriented, and graph DB (Figure 8.23). 

Figure 8.23: NoSQL types

Key-value store: The simplest form of NoSQL database: you access data via the key, which results in the value. Redis, MemcacheD, ArangoDB, Aerospike, and CouchDB are examples of Key/Value stores. Redis, for instance, allows a series of advanced data structures for the value, for instance: strings, sets, sorted sets, hash, list, stream, PubSub, HyperLogLog, geo, bitmap, and many more. Key/Value stores are a great fit for:

  * Caching

  * Session Management

  * Leaderboards

  * Preferences

  * Shopping Carts

  * User Profiles

  * Product Catalogs




Document: Data is stored in the form of documents, most commonly JSON, but alternatively could be XML or BSON (Binary JSON). MongoDB, RavenDB, RethinkDB, CouchDB, ElasticSearch, and AWS DocumentDB are examples of document databases. This style of NoSQL DB allows you to have a flexible schema, or even letting the set of JSON documents define your schema. Document stores are a great fit for:

  * Blogging

  * Content Management

  * Web Analytics

  * E-Commerce

  * Payment Processing

  * Mainframe offloads

  * Single View or Data Hub




Column oriented: Also known as a wide column or columnar. In a typical relational database, you insert and organize data by row; column-oriented databases organize and store data by columns instead. This design allows us to read data more efficiently in certain use cases. Cassandra, HBase, Google Cloud Datastore, and DynamoDB are examples of this style of NoSQL DB. Column oriented databases are a great fit for:

  * Log Aggregation

  * Content Management

  * Blogging

  * E-commerce

  * Inventory Management

  * Fraud Detection

  * Authentication

  * Personalization

  * IoT, and Edge Computing (Time Series data and fast ingestion)




Graph: Graph databases focus on the relationships between your data; data points are called nodes or vertices, and relationships are called links. Neo4j, JanusGraph, Giraph, OrientDB, TitanDB, and AWS Neptune are examples of NoSQL graph databases. Graph databases are a great fit for:

  * Social Networks

  * Spatial Data

  * Recommendations

  * Knowledge Graphs

  * Identity and Access Management
