  * Life Sciences




[Lean communications - binary APIs and GraphQL](contents.xhtml#sc2_342a)

Communication between machines is always evolving. Such evolution is not only about performance but also about flexibility and creating a better system that is easier to maintain. We will explore options beyond REST.

[Service-to-Service communication: REST, gRPC, and GraphQL](contents.xhtml#sc3_343a)

Since the beginning of networked computing, there have been many protocols and forms of communications for remote APIs. During the early days of SOA, SOAP was a very popular protocol, but it was not the only option for APIs; at the time, Java supported RMI, a popular option alongside EJBs. Figure 8.24 shows a brief timeline of API communication.

Figure 8.24: API timeline

[What REST got right](contents.xhtml#sc3_344a)

The Representational State Transfer (REST) style of APIs are ubiquitous. REST APIs originated to provide a way to deal with APIs over the web / HTTP. While most people associated REST with requesting data via GETs or submitting data via POSTs or PUTs, REST API designs have certain guiding constraints: client-server APIs with strong decoupling, statelessness, cache ability, support for an indirect or layered system, and delivery over a uniform interface, meaning self-descriptive, with clear methods for resource identification and resource manipulation, and using hypermedia as the engine of application state (HATEOAS).

REST APIs have been the de facto standard for services since Roy Fielding coined the term in his 2000 PhD dissertation work Architectural Styles and the Design of Network-based Software Architectures ([https://www.ics.uci.edu/~fielding/pubs/dissertation/fielding_dissertation_2up.pdf](https://www.ics.uci.edu/~fielding/pubs/dissertation/fielding_dissertation_2up.pdf)). While not everyone strictly follows Roy’s original principles, today, REST is the standard: it does not matter if it is a classical monolith, SOA services, microservices, or serverless.

The REST style of architecture has very useful properties such as:

  * Decent network performance: REST encourages small, strongly typed interactions.

  * Simplicity: Via the Separation of Concerns (SOC) principle, implement different functionalities in different components.

  * Modifiability: REST APIs can be updated without introducing breaking changes in your client, unlike the oldest forms of API communication, and this property supports others like:

  * Evolvability: The capacity to evolve solutions over time.

  * Extensibility: The important ability to add more functionality.

  * Customizability: Make custom tweaks easily.

  * Configurability: Change aspects and behaviors via configurations.

  * Reusability: Share functionality via REST interfaces.




REST is very often combined with JSON, but can deliver any defined MIME data type. The benefits of REST are:

  * httP: HTTP is so ubiquitous that REST APIs are interoperable with nearly any programming language.

  * Ecosystem: Huge ecosystem, you can find REST client or server support in everything you can imagine.

  * Cacheable: Thanks to the standards of the web and REST’s support for a layered, cacheable architecture, REST APIs are cacheable via CDNs, proxies, via services and their FQDNs cached by DNS.

  * Debuggability: Considering JSON and REST are a text-based protocol, engineers can make sense of the messages, so they are easier to debug and to understand what is going on.

  * Code on demand: REST APIs can return JavaScript or other scripting languages that can execute on the client side.

  * Battle tested: REST is battle-tested in so many scenarios; the pattern just works very well.




Strongest benefit here is the property of interoperability; REST with JSON makes it easy for different languages, operating systems, and applications to be connected and integrated with reasonable performance. REST is perfect and shines in end user, public-facing API use cases.

However, like everything in software architecture, REST has drawbacks. REST is not a silver bullet and will not work in all use cases:

  * Large data volumes: REST will not handle large volumes of data well, such as with big data use cases.

  * Lack of aggregation support: If you need to call 10 REST services, you will end up with a chatty application, fetching and making multiple roundtrips. Not ideal for mobile apps or anything with poor network connections. You might load data you don’t need by over fetching.

  * Performance: The web is the web; if you need something that works at faster speeds, you need to look beyond REST for options with smaller payloads and less latency.




[Interoperability vs performance](contents.xhtml#sc3_345a)

REST is not the only option for APIs. Before we talk about other forms of communication, we need to keep some other important tradeoffs in mind; the biggest tradeoff between REST and other options like GraphQL and gRPC is interoperability. 

Figure 8.25: Trade-offs: Interoperability vs Performance

Interoperability is important. Earlier in this section, we mentioned EJB and RMI, Java standards. If you pick them for your API standard, you are limiting your options to Java. By picking REST, we can now talk in any language, but interoperability is not the only important factor; performance also matters a lot for some use cases. What are you losing in performance by choosing REST? (Figure 8.25) By the way, we are not saying RMI and EJB are the pinnacle of performance, but work with us for the moment!

What if you could have your cake and eat it too? Use different options for different use cases, even building different API exposure types for your services, as we discussed in [Chapter 6](ch06.xhtml), Principles of Proper Services. Use REST for public facing APIs where you might see a variety of languages from your consumers; for Web UIs, you build the clients, so use a more performant protocol.

[GraphQL](contents.xhtml#sc3_346a)

GraphQL was created by Facebook, open sourced in 2015. GraphQL is a query and manipulation API, allows you to write queries that will cherry-pick only the data points you need. GraphQL is declarative and excels at aggregation requests, letting you fix problems such as:

  * Underfetching: Not loading enough data, requiring additional requests.

  * Overfetching: Loading more data in a single request than you need.

  * Reduce network round trips: Combine calls to multiple services in one, aggregated request; great for mobile applications.

  * Single federated gateway: Create a centralized gateway for reading data from different domains. You need to be careful in your implementation here, otherwise, you will break isolation.




We covered the advantage of classical monoliths with a single relational database, where they can answer any question, the UI might require with a single query to a single database. Distributed service architectures have fragmented data across their domains. Instead, by introducing GraphQL gateway, you can federate and aggregate multiple different services behind one unified gateway. (Figure 8.26) Now, you can get back to having your UI launch one single query.

Figure 8.26: GraphQL

Figure 8.26 also shows how we can mix REST and GraphQL for our two services. The Profile and Sales Services are exposing data via REST and JSON. These APIs can be called directly, but GraphQL creates a federation and aggregation layer on top of the services; so, web UIs and mobile apps can make one call instead of each service over two calls.

GraphQL is great, and makes a lot of sense for mobile and web UIs, but it is not a silver bullet. You still need to write all that federation logic and individual service queries. GraphQL is not optimized like NoSQL or SQL databases are and will not handle volume well; it cannot in any way replace a proper big data stream solutions like Apache Spark, Flink, or Kafka Streams. If you have a high-volume, high performance use case GraphQL is not the right solution for you.

GraphQL has support for many programming languages such as Java, Kotlin, JavaScript, Go, PHP, Python, C#, Rust, Scala, Flutter, R, Groovy, Julia, Clojure, OCaml, Swift, Haskell, C, C++, and many more.

GraphQL has limitations and drawbacks:

  * Break isolation: You can be tempted to treat GraphQL like a proper service, thinking you can go straight to databases; this is a fast track to creating a distributed monolith. When you implement your resolvers, only call services.

  * Now you need to wait: When you were doing multiple REST calls, you could launch some calls in parallel, but now you will need to wait for the whole aggregation to be done. You had better hope your GraphQL service has parallelism. And if you only rely on waiting for one blocking query, you are removing options like asynchronous calls from web clients and desktop.

  * File uploading: Mutation support is limited; you cannot do file uploading.

  * Lack of binary support: GraphQL has no binary JSON support, so it will not be the most efficient transport mechanism.

  * Domain coupling: GraphQL can be powerful, but convenience has a price, and you can couple data in a way that is unwanted. Some services might have PII, or require dangerous privileges to access data. Do not mix these things together.




GraphQL does not kill all REST use cases by any means; they complement each other and can be used together, as they all favor JSON. GitHub also exposes its public-facing API over GraphQL ([https://docs.github.com/en/graphql](https://docs.github.com/en/graphql)). Figure 8.27 shows a query to list the 3 most recent GitHub repositories.

Figure 8.27: Using GraphQL in Github  
Source: [https://docs.github.com/en/graphql/overview/explorer](https://docs.github.com/en/graphql/overview/explorer)

Now, if you are interested in performance let us explore a more interesting option.

[gRPC](contents.xhtml#sc3_347a)

gRPC is a high-performance RPC-like framework, built by Google and open sourced in 2016. gRPC has a client/server architecture; gRPC relies on HTTP/2, with your server talking Google Protocol Buffers with the client stub (Figure 8.28). Google Protocol Buffers are a structured binary message format designed for smaller and compact payloads. gRPC is fast, and great at streaming data, with several streaming options:

  * Unary; no streaming

  * Client-to-server streaming

  * Server-to-client streaming

  * Bidirectional streaming




Figure 8.28: gRPC high-level overview  
Source: [https://grpc.io/docs/what-is-grpc/introduction/](https://grpc.io/docs/what-is-grpc/introduction/)

gRPC is able to generate code and clients in multiple languages, such as Java, Kotlin, Scala, JavaScript, Python, Ruby, Go, Dart, C#, Objective-C, and many more.

Again: gRPC is fast. Even just using Google Protocol Buffers results in speed improvements; LinkedIn migrated its REST APIs from JSON to Protocol Buffers resulting in a 60% latency reduction and an 8% improvement in resource utilization.103

Like all technologies, gRPC has limitations and drawbacks:

  * Limited browser support: Most browsers do not support gRPC, so you will need a web-proxy layer to translate.

  * Harder to debug: gRPC does not deliver data in a human readable format, Google Protocol Buffers is binary. Understanding and troubleshooting problems is harder.

  * No edge caching: No cache on the edge, in comparison with REST.




gRPC would not be the ideal choice for customer facing APIs. However, for internal service-to-service communication situations where you have tight controls on the clients, it is a good choice and a natural successor for REST. (Figure 8.29)

Figure 8.29: gRPC and REST spectrum

One important architecture and design consideration you need to keep in mind when choosing between API communication options like REST, gRPC, GraphQL or other options is that API communication is part of the service contract therefore. If you choose it, you will need to maintain it, and if you change it, you will break consumers.

Let us say your service exposes a contract with REST + JSON, and you have 10 consumers accessing this contract via this API exposure. Your team wants to realize the performance benefits and efficiencies that you would gain by switching to gRPC, like less latency and better user experience. How could you do it without impacting all 10 consumers at once? (Figure 8:30)

Figure 8.30: Migrating from REST to gRPC

[Things to remember](contents.xhtml#sc2_348a)

Congratulations, you have reached the end of [Chapter 8](ch08.xhtml): Embracing New Technology! Let us recap some of your learnings here:

  * Introducing new technologies can reinvigorate your engineers and your business.

  * Do not abandon your principles when embracing new technology.

  * Cloud computing has many highly valuable properties.

    * Automation - APIs, CLIs and SDKs

    * Elasticity, scale up and out or down when needed

    * Self-Service Model empowering engineers

    * Multiple billing models

    * Highly Reliable

    * Global Presence

    * Managed Services and hands-off operations

  * Cloud Computing has drawbacks:

    * Cost

    * Vendor Lock-In with proprietary cloud solutions

    * Too Many Options

    * Security Vs Engineering Tension

  * Cloud computing provides multiple forms of isolation:

    * Multiple Instances and Sharded Storage

    * Multiple Availability Zones

    * Multiple Regions

    * Multiple Accounts

    * Multiple Cloud Providers/Data Centers

  * Never shared managed service clusters across service implementations.

  * Cloud accounts can be configured in multiple ways to support isolation:

    * One account per service

    * One account per business domain

    * One account for everything

  * With accounts, you can abstract the whole cloud behind an API Gateway contract.

  * Serverless can help you explore new languages safely.

  * Cross-functional teams are a great way to get the best from the cloud.

  * Managed services can make life easier for your team.

    * Databases, like RDS, Aurora and Spanner

    * NoSQL Databases, like Cassandra, Kafka and ElasticSearch

    * AI and Analytics, like Big Data and Data Science Workflows

  * Containers / K8s:

    * Containers are a standard unit to package application code and dependencies

    * Containers have great startup times compared with VMs.

    * Containers allow application isolation.

    * Containers provide engineering flexibility and can help to improve security by moving away from OS and leveraging sandboxes like GVisor.

    * Containers have some performance and throughput degradation.

    * Kubernetes has lots of non-obvious benefits, such as:

      * Huge open-source community and ecosystem

      * Less lock-in from public cloud vendors

      * Simplify and reduce the DevOps work queue

      * Cost savings due to the bin-packing solution

    * Containers/K8s allow language experimentation to happen much easier.

  * Apache Kafka:

    * Apache Kafka is a distributed event store and streaming platform.

    * The log is the simplest form of storage abstraction.

    * Distributed logs have the following properties:

      * Append only

      * Totally ordered sequence of records ordered by time

      * Immutable

    * The consumer’s ability to read from the tail or head of the topics is a killer feature because it allows you to do real-time processing, read from the head and also historical re-processing, read from the tail.

    * CQRS allows scale reads/writes and also provides isolation.

    * Services and microservices done right make joins difficult due to the lack of a central. DB, and this is desirable.

    * Kafka allows proper implementation of Event Sourcing and therefore provides a mechanism to perform joins.

  * Modern languages:

    * Consider having fewer internal shared libraries and more languages.

    * Serverless can help you to abstract language runtimes.

    * Even without serverless SOA, Microservices can abstract language via contracts.

    * Consider charting your languages by lifecycle and usage.

    * JVM Languages are a great way to experiment with new languages, using most of what you already have in the sense of internal shared libraries, infrastructure, and tooling.

  * NoSQL:

    * There is nothing wrong with relational databases.

    * Relational databases are overused; some use cases do not fit well.

    * NoSQL is about tradeoffs and options.

    * Proper Services allow transparent database migrations, experimentation, and transparency for the consumers.

    * NoSQL allows us to go by the relational model and introduce another type of database like key/value, document, column-oriented, and graph.

  * REST is a style of software architecture and is great for interoperability.

  * REST has drawbacks:

    * Will not handle large volumes of data

    * Lack of aggregation support

    * Performance

  * GraphQL helps us to handle some problems, such:

    * Underfetching, not loading enough data

    * Overfetching loading data that you do not need

    * Reduce network round trips, great for mobile applications.

    * Single federated gateway for reading data from different domains.

  * GraphQL has limitations and drawbacks:

    * You can break isolation by calling databases directly.

    * Now you need to wait: When you were doing multiple REST calls, you could do some calls in parallel, but now you will need to wait for the whole aggregation to be done.

    * You cannot do file uploading

    * GraphQL has no binary support, so it will not be the most efficient transport mechanism

    * You should not add dangerous services to GraphQL, like those with PII or high sensitivity

  * gRPC is great for service-to-service communications:

    * gRPC relies on HTTP/2 and Google Protocol Buffers

    * gRPC is pretty good at streaming data bidirectionally between client/server

  * gRPC has limitations and drawbacks like:

    * Limited browser support

    * Protocol buffers are binary, harder to debug and troubleshoot

    * No edge caching

# [CHAPTER 9Code Migrations](contents.xhtml#ch09a)

Entropy is the price of Structure.

— Ilya Prigogine

Code migrations will always be required, and there is no escape. However, migrations can be painful or smooth, it is up to us and how we do it. Reducing the blast radius, minimizing the impact of migrations is a must have. Code migration techniques are important because, at the end of the day, you move step by step to a better path out of the classical monolith or even out of the distributed monolith. Several concepts, principles, and practices in this chapter will also be applied to data migrations.

[Structure](contents.xhtml#sc2_349a)

In this chapter, we will cover the following topics:

  * Why do code migrations matter?

    * Inventory, use cases, and POCs

  * Red vs green migrations

  * Elements of proper code, migrations

    * Migration target

    * Difficulty

    * Customer impact - online vs offline

    * Execution - service team vs. platform team

  * Code migration patterns

    * Backward compatibility

    * Lazy migrations

    * Strangler Fig

      * Converting a classical monolith to proper SOA

      * Converting a classical monolith to a modular monolith

      * Converting a distributed monolith to proper SOA

    * Amazon two - way doors

  * Migration roadblocks

    * Roadblock - leftovers

      * Double down on inventories and POCs

      * Distributed migrations and team dependencies

      * Repetition, repetition, repetition

    * Roadblock - friction and entropy

    * Roadblock - high WIP limits, business pressure, compliance, and other traps

  * Post-migration

    * Rollback strategies

  * Things to remember




[Why do code migrations matter?](contents.xhtml#sc2_350a)

Getting out of the monoliths, you need to go through code and data migrations. Code migrations are often very challenging and taken for granted, meaning the preparation, thinking, planning, testing, observability and guardrails are often not done, resulting in a poor and traumatizing experience. Unless you can pay the cost of starting over again and again, you have no choice but to face code migrations. Migrations are one of the topics in software engineering that often is not explored as much as it should be. Large companies do code migrations all the time. Migrations are normal and routine activities in software; for instance, engineers also perform migrations on their machines often by upgrading the OS, developer tools, JDK and SDKs, and browser versions regularly.

Migrations happen for multiple reasons, such as:

  * End of life: The software you are using is dying, at least for version x.y.z.

  * End of the road: Vendor is out of business, and the new vendor does not want to maintain it.

  * Compliance: Infosec, regulations, and external agencies force your company to apply patches and be up-to-date with software.

  * Infrastructure: You are saving cost and gaining agility i.e. from the on-prem to the public cloud.

  * Slicing and dicing: You want to get out the classical monolith or your distributed monolith.

  * Legacy sunsetting: You have a legacy service or solution that does not serve your business anymore, and/or there are better alternatives and options for you, that is you built your load balancer, and now you could switch to an AWS ALB, for instance.

  * New business capabilities: Your business needs new capabilities forcing you to migrate some systems to new systems that allow the business to be more effective or provide a better experience for the users.

  * Consolidations: Your company bought five companies, and it does not make sense to have ten different authentication systems, and you want to standardize on OAuth and Active Directory (AD), for instance.

  * Modernization: Time to rethink your solutions and re-architect them. This will lead to migrations.




As you can see, different reasons all lead to the same road: code migrations. Code migrations are a tool, an instrument; it is how you make change happen. In order to fix monoliths and distributed monoliths, we need to go through code migrations. Modernization, cloud migrations, and digital transformation are very common reasons that will lead you to do code migrations. (Figure 9.1)

Figure 9.1: Several reasons lead us to code migrations

You might be wondering why we are going over and over on this matter. Because many teams and engineering managers push back on migrations and think this is not part of their own duties. In other words, they do not see the value. Migrations are very important. Migrations are a big element in how we will fix the problem with classical and distributed monoliths. Code migrations are a form of refactoring. Can a team never do refactoring? Yes, and unfortunately, it happens, but would the solutions be maintainable? No. Code migrations are hard, do not get us wrong. But they are inevitable. Can you skip them all? No. Often the solution to something you do not do well is to do it more often. So, you will need to get better at doing migrations because it will happen often.

[Inventory, use cases, and POCs](contents.xhtml#sc3_351a)

As we covered in [Chapter 5](ch05.xhtml): Assessments, assessments are critical to any migration, and they start with inventory. Should you start a code migration without understanding what you are doing? No. You need to be able to make sense of the problem, understand the shape and the extent of the problems you will have to deal with, and how many consumers are involved. What are the use cases? It is very tempting to jump into the solution and go fix the problem right now, but you must resist the temptation.

Inventories are necessary to understand the problem space, we will need inventories, analysis of the use cases, and POCs. First of all, start with inventories. You might be wondering, why waste time and build an inventory, if we need to migrate anyways?

Inventories are important because:

  * Scope: Inventories allow you to understand the scope of the migration.

  * Understand the problem: Capture important insights about your consumers, dependencies, and issues.

  * Progress and tracking: Centralized view of progress, otherwise, you are in the dark.

  * Strategy, plan and sanity: An inventory allows you to strategize and plan the migration. It will make everything better.




Inventories will reflect the work you need to do. By analyzing your consumers, you will spot problems and challenges; let us go over an example:

Goal: Migrate from Ehcache to Redis.

Use Cases: LRU caching, DB offloading, hash lookup to kill ifs

Inventory: GitHub repository

Level of Impact: 1 low == transparent, 2 medium == same refactoring, 3 high == lots of refactoring.

Now let us say you discover you have 50 GitHub repositories to migrate, 30 are LRU caching and have a great fit with Redis, 10 are database offloading that also fits well with Redis, and 10 are lookups to kill ifs. Sometimes engineers use data structures to make the code more readable. A common practice is to have a HashMap with keys, and then you do a fast look up there instead of using an if or switch statement. For such cases, you do not really need to migrate to Redis. Because you read the code and build the inventory, and now you understand the use cases, you have options.

Consider the following options:

  * As-is: Could we write a program that converts Ehcache API calls to Redis, Lettuce Java driver API calls? Yes, we could.

  * Reduce scope: Could we kill all hash lookup use cases and just ask people to use regular Java hashmaps? We could migrate to Redis, but for this use case it will be low value.

  * Merging use cases: Could we merge the LRU cache and database offloading use cases? Yes, we could, merging use cases is something to be considered.

  * Transparent migration: Could we hack Ehcache and preserve the APIs, and the new implementation calls Redis under the hood, and therefore the migration is transparent.

  * POC: Maybe migrate one repository to see the kind of issues you will face.




Now this list of options should be turned into POCs so you can check for feasibility and what solution makes more sense using tradeoffs analysis and keeping green migration goals in mind.

Figure 9.2: Inventory and use cases

The example in Figure 9.2 fits well with the distributed monolith anti-pattern, where you will likely have many repositories. Even if you are not dealing with distributed monoliths and you have proper services that are isolated, migrations have a cross-cutting nature. What about a classical monolith where all code base is in one single GitHub repository? Even for other use cases where companies just rely on monorepos? Principles are the same, but your inventory would not have a GitHub repository column but maybe another form to identify the consumers inside the monolith like:

  * List of modules

  * List of classes

  * List of files




You still can have GitHub links or link the code somehow in whatever SCM tool you do use. Now that we have inventory, mapped use cases, and did some code POCs, we talk about migration tradeoffs.

[Red vs green migrations](contents.xhtml#sc2_352a)

Let us share the tale of two migrations:

Red migration: Once upon a time, the code broke on several different fronts; there were compile, runtime, and testing issues; bugs were only spotted in production; it took a long time, cost a lot of money, and the benefit was indirect and difficult for the business to see. Also, difficult to understand the progress of the migration. Rollbacks? Difficult, painful.

Green migration: Considering a different universe, the code does not break all over the place, was done transparently or within a reasonable and controlled blast radius, and changes were minimal; very few bugs were spotted and often very early, few production bugs, benefits for the business were clear, visible and they had crystal clear understanding of the progress. Rollbacks? Manageable.

Which one was better: red or green migration? Green migration, right? The issue with red migrations is that they deliver bad experiences for both the engineering teams but also for the business. Red migrations are sources of disruption and disrupt the business flow. Red migrations tend to be paused, put on hold, have long tails, and get more pushback from all sides.

Sometimes it is not possible to make a migration 100% green, but that is your goal, to make it as green as possible and work in ways that your migrations improve over time and get more efficient, and you deliver them more effectively.

Figure 9.3: Red vs green migrations

As you can see in Figure 9.3, red to green migrations is a spectrum. Now let us learn all the actions we can take to make all migrations as green as possible.

[Elements of proper code, migrations](contents.xhtml#sc2_353a)

Now is the time for us to go into code migration tradeoffs; there are several trade-offs and options here. We group them into 4 different categories: migration target, difficulty, customer impact, and execution.

[Migration target](contents.xhtml#sc3_354a)

The first category is about the target of migration. Here we have 3 sub-elements: type, what, and how. Type and What are very similar. We could even argue they are rather artificial, and both could be merged into one. Let us start with Type. You will migrate multiple targets. You will have several different types of targets (components) such as:

  * Web UI applications

  * Desktop applications

  * Backend for Frontends (BFFs), often NodeJS applications

  * Microservices

  * 3rd party vendor proprietary solutions

  * Classical Monoliths

  * Distributed Monoliths

  * One or All of the above




What do all these elements have in common? Such components can be deployment units, which can all be on one server (big box) or on multiple servers. Oftentimes classical monoliths and distributed monoliths have multiple types of components combined in one single target. Classical monoliths can have multiple servers for scalability and failover but is one big block, whereas distributed monoliths will have more servers and be logically distributed beyond scalability and failover reasons. 

Figure 9.4: Classical monolith vs distributed monolith

A classical monolith has a lot of different types of components combined together in one single deployment unit; in Java it could be JAR, EAR, or WAR. Databases are often another component; for the sake of visualization, they are all bundled together here in this example - Figure 9.4.

Figure 9.5: Classical monolith code migration sample

Figure 9.5 \- Considering this sample, as you can see, our classical monolith is Java, but let us say it is Java 1.6 and you should be using Java 19 or newer at this point. Here the type is a classical monolith, but the what is not one thing but multiple things because we have the following: 2 different frontend applications, one written in JSF and the other written in Apache Wicket, a bunch of Java services, some web services with SOAP others just RPC with RMI, Reports in Java using Jasper, Internal shared libraries and a big shared relational database that has lots of functions and even Java objects running on the database side.

The next element is the Component Unit (the kind or type of component) - which is what migration itself means, and sometimes multiple elements are being changed at the same time, so what could be one unit, a single Java library, or multiple units combined: Java library, Jenkins job and database stored procedure. Component Unit gives you some sense of what the code changes, such as:

  * Configuration

  * Libraries

  * Frameworks

  * JDKs and SDKs

  * Operating System

  * Programming Languages

  * Databases, which will be covered in the next chapter, [Chapter 10](ch10.xhtml), Data Migrations.




While the last sub-element is the how. How are we gonna do it? Are we migrating things as they are with zero to little changes, something cloud providers refer to as Lyft and Shift. Do we need to re-architect and basically rethink everything? Can we do some vendor shifts transparently? For example, could we switch from Oracle JDK 11 to AWS Corretto JDK 11? What changes do we need to do now vs what changes can be made later? Will the frontend be kept in Java with JSF, or will it be migrated to TypeScript and React, for instance?

Figure 9.6: Migration target: type, what, and how

Many possibilities, right? (Figure 9.6) What migrates first? Where do we start? We will answer this question in the chapter. As you learn more options, techniques, and tradeoffs. For now, here is some guidance: Going back to your inventory, you can add some extra columns to classify the type, what kind of work will happen, and how it will be done. You can and should consider different strategies per component. Code migrations can be done in different ways, and you see, there is no right or wrong. Here are some things to keep in mind that can help you to prioritize your target migrations:

  * Criticality: Criticality or business impact, are we having availability issues?

  * Cost: Do we have huge savings that could be achieved?

  * Low hanging fruits: Can the code migrations be easily done?




Cloud computing vendors like AWS classify some of the How strategies as 7Rs.

Figure 9.7: Migration target: type, what, and how  
Source: [https://docs.aws.amazon.com/prescriptive-guidance/latest/large-migration-guide/migration-strategies.html](https://docs.aws.amazon.com/prescriptive-guidance/latest/large-migration-guide/migration-strategies.html)

Figure 9.7, look at some of the how options for cloud migration. Now let us move to the next topic, which is difficulty.

[Difficulty](contents.xhtml#sc3_355a)

Code migrations can be done with little or lots of impact on people or technology. Code changes could be very straightforward in the sense that you might be just changing a version number in your pom.xml, considering Java using Maven, and could be replacing an import from one package to another, like Java EE to Jakarta EE. Code changes can also be brutal and force you to change your business logic. Due to the drop of a feature can introduce breaking behavior. (Figure 9:8)

Figure 9.8: Hands-On vs hands-Off

While the biggest factor that will lead to hands-on vs handoff code migrations is whether you have backward compatibility or not. As seen on the backward compatibility spectrum so it could be 0% or up to 100%. Some or all of your methods or operations could be backward compatibility, easing migration.

Let us say your approach is 0% backward compatible; that does not mean you cannot automate the changes; you could write a program that makes some of the code changes for you, and it would work well for:

  * Code imports

  * Classes and method names

  * Deleting whole classes




Where it would not be easy, are for cases where the order of the parameters change or the business logic needs to be rethought, for those cases, it really would need to be manual. Also, for code automation, you need to consider the cost of automating all possible scenarios and code changes, which sometimes do not pay off.

Let us go back to the Ehcache vs. Redis Code migration scenario.

Figure 9.9: Ehcache to redis code migration - service scenario

(Figure 9.9) The contract in a REST interface, exposed via HTTP 1.1 protocol, and the implementation produces and consumes JSON, the operations supported are two operations: PUT - where you pass in a key and a value which would be stored by the cache server and GET - where you pass the key to perform the lookup.

Consider that on the first implementation, you have a simple cache service. However, the current solution is not working well because you do need more objects to be cached, and every time the service is down, the cache is lost, so there is a cold bootstrap problem creating some latency spikes when the cache is empty; in this example, in the v1 implementation Ehcache is used, and the team decided to move to Redis. So, it would be possible to leverage the cluster and even migrate to AWS Elasticache Redis in the future. You inventory and analyze the code and then discover that there are 100+ consumers of the cache service.

It is possible to make this code migration using a backward compatibility approach since we know the contract. Even with the contract being coupled with an Ehcache interface, it would be possible to implement this interface and preserve the contract, so all REST calls would not break. However, the real implementation (v2) would not be using an embedded Ehcache in Java but Redis instead.

Now, not only is the migration transparent, and we can deliver a green migration by:

  * Testing: Extensive and automated testing of the public contract.

  * Rollbackability: Rollout can be very transparent at the network level. Easy to switch all consumers. No re-deploys are required.

  * Backward compatibility: Avoids difficult refractories due to the backward compatibility approach.




Let us consider another migration scenario, a more challenging one, the cache is an internal shared library and not a service.

Figure 9.10: Ehcache to Redis code migration - library scenario

Figure 9.10 considers a slightly different scenario, 100+ consumers still migrating from Ehcache to Redis, same limitations and issues with Ehcache; the goal is still to do a green migration and leverage backward compatibility. However, in this scenario, we do not have a service, we have an internal shared library.

We would still apply the backward compatibility very similarly. Still, now there is no REST interface, we have a Java JAR, and is the Ehcache interface exposed directly, and there is no JSON; there are more challenges here because:

  * Distribution: 100+ different repositories will need to change the version of the library from v1 to v2. You can and should automate that, but it is something you need to do.

  * Multiple rollouts: Rollout cannot be done as easily as the service rollout; it will require re-deploys.

  * Rollback implications: No escape. There are rollback implications here, rollback will be covered later in this chapter.




We, as the migration team, have more work to do, but we still can achieve a green migration. Classical monoliths and distributed monoliths are much more difficult and challenging; they will be covered later in this chapter. Considering that you will apply as much Backward compatibility as you can, this is how the scale of the challenge is in the sense of complexity and time for different targets of code migrations.

Figure 9.11: Complexity / size vs time / effort for different migration targets

Proper Services are easier to maintain and migrate. (Figure 9.11) Lean internal shared libraries are also easier to maintain. Still, classical internal shared libraries are way more difficult than services for the reasons we saw above; the other two types we will cover soon.

Legacy applications often are much more difficult, and you might not have a path for 100% backward compatibility because you still might need to:

  * Big UI shift: From desktop to web or web to mobile.

  * Huge language shift: Migrate from one language to another language, e.g. from Delphi to Java.

  * Large datastore change: Forced to change database e.g. from Sybase to Postgres.

  * Major framework upgrade: Forced to do major bump and break backward compatibility e.g. from Hibernate 3 to 6.




If you have one item on this list, it is enough to have many challenges. Code migrations never end, even being forced to go through a red migration, it is possible to take actions to make migrations easier and greener over time, such as:

  * Simplification: Simplify your ecosystem, have fewer flavors, leverage standards.

  * Less internal libraries: Have fewer internal shared libraries, a handful of libraries instead of hundreds.

  * Proper services: Following the principles in this book, have proper services and leverage isolation as a way to allow parallel migrations. Leverage flexibility, Isolation, and Proper contracts over performance.

  * Engineering lifecycle: Have a defined life cycle, a path to decommission applications and services.

  * Minimal stack: Have a very minimal stack, and avoid binary coupling as much as possible.

  * Watch you for shared global components: Scrutinize global changes with a big blast radius across all services. Could be in the form of services but especially internal libraries.

  * More incremental migrations: Apply more incremental and constant migrations to reduce deltas and make it easier.




Depending on the state of your legacy applications, you might need to face a couple of red migrations before being able to get greener. Difficulty and team impact is a big aspect of code migrations; now let us go over another important form of impact, not for the internal team but for the customers and your end users.

[Customer impact - online vs offline](contents.xhtml#sc3_356a)

Green migrations have zero impact on the end users, your customers. (Figure 9.12) Proper software architectures using proper services allow us to make changes in a very transparent form. Transparent changes are great because they allow the end users to use your product/solution normally without even no acknowledgment that things are changing under the hood. Such an approach led us to go online. We also have offline, on the other side of the spectrum.

Figure 9.12: Customer impact: offline and online spectrum

Online

As the name says no downtime or no downtime that is noticeable to the customers (end users). Online is really great because it gives you lots of benefits such as:

  * Transparency: Can anyone tell code migrations are going on?

  * No downtime: No financial loss, no explanations. No drama.

  * Maturity: In order to perform several online migrations, the technology organization knows what it is doing and is very likely doing it well.

  * Amazing experience: Build trust in your brand; it is great for customer experience.




Sounds good, right? Well, it is, but there is a price. Online is much more difficult because:

  * More things can go wrong.

  * It might cost more.

  * Testing and rollback might be more expensive.




Offline

Offline might sound like an unreasonable option, but in some use cases it is completely fine. Just because it is offline does not necessarily mean you will have downtime from the customer’s perspective; it depends on how the component works. For instance, when the code migration target type is a customer-facing web UI, if that web UI is not available, you have downtime.

Now let us consider a different type of code migration target, like a batch job. For the batch job that runs in the background in an async fashion, having a couple of seconds or sometimes even minutes could be fine. So here, as you can see, we decoupled offline and downtime; this is not possible for all code migrations and not for all types of components.

Figure 9.13: Offline impact by component

Offline has benefits (Figure 9.13), but like everything in software architecture, there are always tradeoffs:

  * Easier, fewer things can go wrong: Lots of complications are removed from the picture, less things can fail, and everything is simplified. You do not need to worry about race conditions.

  * Costs less: It might cost less, but it will depend on the math: revenue loss while down.

  * Might be the only way: Some legacy applications might be required due to technical limitations.




Depending on the type of your business and services, you will have different levels of criticality; one useful technique that companies use is to have tiers. Tiers allow you to understand what components can tolerate less downtime. Every business is different and might have different levels of Service Level Objectives (SLOs)104 for availability.

For instance, here is one possible example.

Tier |  Availability: Uptime / Downtime |  Component  
---|---|---  
Tier 1 |  Uptime: 99.671%  Downtime: 28.8 hours/year |  MySQL Database  
Tier 2 |  Uptime: 99.741% Downtime: 22 hours/year |  Redis: Products Category Cache  
Tier 3 |  Uptime: 99.982% Downtime: 4 hours/year |  Web UI: Sales  
Tier 4 |  Uptime: 99.995% Downtime: 26.3 minutes/year |  Sales Service, Cassandra Database  
  
Table 9.1: Tiers

(Table 9.1) Having SLO goals and tiers for all your components make planning code migrations easier and make it clear to you what needs to be online and how much offline you can tolerate per component.

[Execution - service team vs. platform team](contents.xhtml#sc3_357a)

In order to perform code migrations, we need execution. Not all migrations are completely automated, especially when we have lots of breaking changes. Sometimes the tradeoffs: cost/time of automating vs time to manually do the migration resulting in red migrations.

When we do not have enough automation, there is a spectrum of execution, which could be basically done by a service team on one side of the spectrum, or a platform team on the other side of the spectrum. (Figure 9.14) Friction happens in this context. We discussed this topic in [Chapter 2](ch02.xhtml), Anti-Patterns: Lack of Isolation.

Figure 9.14: Spectrum of execution

Service teams: Know the business much better in comparison with platform teams and are in the best place to test the solution better in the sense of knowing the business impact. Service team business knowledge is important for classical monoliths and legacy solutions where ownership is not a clear boundary, is shared between multiple teams, and test automation is low or lacks trust. Service teams, however, might not be migration experts and will have pressure from the businesses and product to deliver value. Service teams might push back on migrations as it moves them away from direct value to the business.

Platform teams: On the other hand, they know little about the business or how to test service components properly; however, this can be fixed if the test automation is decent and tests can be trusted. Classical monolith and legacy systems oftentimes do not have decent testing coverage and also require code migrations. So, the benefit of going to this side of the spectrum is that you get technical expertise.

Friction between teams: service vs platform often happens because ownership is unclear and due to complicated relationships, dynamics, and expectations. Such as:

  * Unclear ownership: Service teams expect platform teams to do all migrations because service teams want to focus on the business features and direct value.

  * Lack of due diligence: Service teams also want to freely choose libraries and frameworks that might be unique to the team at the same time; service teams expect some other team to help them to migrate.

  * The basics: Platform teams expect service teams to have ownership of their code base and technical decisions. Expecting the build to pass and tests being stable all the time.

  * Migration engagement: Platform teams also want to move faster and push new technologies and efficiency; however, building something is easier, but migrating all consumers is not so easy. So, platform teams expect service teams to migrate more often and faster.




Hybrid team: It would be creating a temporary team that has both platform and service engineers that combine both strengths. Hybrid teams could be the middle ground to have both worlds where we combine business knowledge and testing know-how with tech expertise and sharp abilities to drive migrations.

A similar idea can be found in the book: Team Topologies: Organizing Business and Technology Teams for Fast Flow by Matthew Skelton and Manuel Pais, published in 2019. Between platform and services teams, called Stream Aligned Teams, there are Complicated Sub System Teams and Enabling Teams, which will be similar to our hybrid team here. (Figure 9:15) 

Figure 9.15: Team topologies

[Code migration patterns](contents.xhtml#sc2_358a)

Now let us understand how we can accomplish code migrations; there are a couple of patterns we will go through. The order of the patterns is rather artificially grouped but aims to be from easier to harder in the sense of effort to implement and complexity. Our pattern classification does not imply better or worse; think in the sense of different tools that you can and should have in your tool belt.

[Backward compatibility](contents.xhtml#sc3_359a)

The backward compatibility pattern is already covered in [Chapter 6](ch06.xhtml), Principles of Proper Services; to recap, we do not break the interfaces, methods, and function calls, therefore minimizing the refactoring required. We will revisit backward compatibility in the context of rollbacks with the Amazon two-way doors approach.

[Lazy migrations](contents.xhtml#sc3_360a)

Lazy migration is a form of transparent migration, basically, we bundle the migration as part of how the software component works. Let us consider some examples. First of all, imagine you have a Profile ID which is a compound string like:

Profile ID V1 = TYPE_PHONE_STATUS

Where:

TYPE: regular, politician, reporter, or celebrity.

PHONE: the phone number

STATUS: Active or Deactivated

Now consider this is stored in MySQL, and it is cached in Redis as well, like in the following Figure 9.16:

Figure 9.16: Lazy migration example on profile service

Now, let us say we need to do the following code changes:

We need to add the country code as part of the phone number. Unfortunately, we did not anticipate running the solution in multiple countries. This will fix the collision we might have, two people with the same number but in different countries. Now what code changes do we need to do? Can we make it backward compatible? Yes, we can! But we will go one step beyond backward compatibility, so first, let us understand the overall code changes.

Consider:

Profile ID V2 = TYPE_COUNTRY_CODE_PHONE_STATUS, which means:

  * Assuming the contract would not change because the ID was mapped as string.

  * The contract tests now will break because we need a new format (adding area code).

  * You need to change the internal POJO that will represent the profile.

  * Persistence changes, either your SQL or JPA mappings.

  * Data migration, which will be covered in [Chapter 10](ch10.xhtml), Data Migrations.




Now the service can basically implement both ID patterns and internally route to the proper code, something like this Figure 9.17.

Figure 9.17: Routing to two different implementations

Now, instead of just changing all the code only to support ID V2 pattern, imagine that you either duplicate the code a little bit or refactor the code to be able to handle both patterns V1 and V2. In addition, we would introduce a Router pattern, which is a class that will analyze the profile ID and figure out if it should route to the implementation V1 or V2. Such routing is an if based on the profile ID string field, which could be done in several different ways like:

  * Check the size - Profile V2 is bigger than V1.

  * Look for _ since V2 has more than V1.

  * Look for the country code being present.

  * All options above.




The tricky thing here is that, because we support both pattern IDs (V1 and V2), consumers, let us say web UI could be reading with pattern V1 pattern and when we store it, we can convert it to V2 pattern internally and skip storing V1 pattern altogether. So, we would read both patterns but only store pattern V2. 

Figure 9.18: Read vs write paths

Data which is HOT (Figure 9.18) means that it is constantly being read (get/select) and write (insert or update) them eventually all users will be using pattern V2, and now you can decommission all code that handles V1. We will have scenarios in which only some IDs will be COLD – let us not worry about that right now; we will revisit this pattern in the context of data migrations in [Chapter 10](ch10.xhtml), Data Migrations.

The important idea to keep in mind here, we can make two very different concepts to live together (ID Pattern V1 and V2) and migrate from the old way to the new way slowly. Rather than just for one ID pattern in the code and being forced to do a data migration up-to-front. So, this is a lazy migration because every time a write operation is being called, we are doing more than the user expects and changing the internal ID pattern. Such an approach is great for consumers as well because it allows them to slowly move to pattern V2, so they have a window where they can work on their changes.

[Strangler Fig](contents.xhtml#sc3_361a)

Strangler Fig is an advanced pattern. It is the key to slicing and dicing classical monoliths and distributed monoliths. The Strangler Fig idea comes from the strangler fig genus of plants, parasitic plants which strangle other older and bigger plants sucking nutrients until the host plant dies. 

Figure 9.19: Ficus Watkinsiana, Australia  
Source: [https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ficus_watkinsiana_on_Syzygium_hemilampra-Iluka.jpg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ficus_watkinsiana_on_Syzygium_hemilampra-Iluka.jpg)

The Strangler Fig idea (Figure 9.19) was ported to software and coined by Chris Stevenson and Andy Pols in an Extreme Programming (XP) conference in 2004 being an Agile Approach to Legacy Systems.

Now let us see the Strangler Fig pattern in practice applied to: classical monolith and distributed monolith anti-pattern.

[Converting a classical monolith to proper SOA](contents.xhtml#sc4_362a)

In a classical monolith, often the application is all in one single code base with one or multiple deployment units. So, you will have something like Figure 9:20:

Figure 9.20: Strangler fig - Classical Monolith step 1

So, on the first step, we have this scenario where multiple consumers are all hitting the classical monolith, and several times the classical monolith is calling himself. Our situation will be very likely to not have a clear boundary and visibility control, everything might be public. Also, clean and well-defined contracts and interfaces might not be present at all, it is a classical monolith. So, you will need to either pick some interface to start or create a new interface or contract; here is some guidance:

  * Subset: You can pick a domain or subset of a domain. i.e. Image, Payments, Profile, Sales, etc.

  * Generic functionality: If you have generic functionality like authentication, authorization, entitlements, or security might also be a good candidate.

  * Contract first: Create a contract and add a new interface and expose that as HTTP.




Figure 9.21: Strangler Fig - classical monolith step 2

(Figure 9.21) Let us say we want to extract the image functionality, this is what it would look like. As you can see here, the contract is very small, and no one is calling the new contract, that is fine; now, we will move all consumers that need to the new contract one at a time. 

Figure 9.22: Strangler Fig - classical monolith step 3

Here is the example (Figure 9.22): we have 5 consumers in your reality; you could have 100s of consumers, so this is a simple approach but can take a long time; once you isolate one consumer, you can move to the next consumer and repeat. 

Figure 9.23: Strangler Fig - classical monolith step 4

One important consideration is just because the whole classical monolith has 5 consumers, it does not mean your new contract will have other consumers. You should avoid migrating fake consumers, only migrate consumers that really need to use the new contract. (Figure 9.23) Once you are there, you can move the code out to a different code-base and standalone service.

Figure 9.24: Strangler Fig - classical monolith step 5

(Figure 9.24) Finally, we reach the end state, and the classical monolith is small; now you can repeat this whole process to the next interface/contract, and if you keep doing that eventually, you will dry out the whole classical monolith, and you can kill it.

[Converting a classical monolith to a modular monolith](contents.xhtml#sc4_363a)

So now let us say we want to keep the monolith, but we want a modern monolith which means it has modularity. Our process is the same but with one big difference, we might not expose the new functionality as a public contract because it might be an internal private module, so you will surely have Java Interfaces representing the contract but not necessarily a REST endpoint, which will depend on the use case (Figure 9:25). 

Figure 9.25: Stranger Fig - modular monolith step 1 to 5

As you can see, there are very similar mechanics, but here the goal is to make the monolith modular. During this process, you will need to make calls on what modules will be public and which ones should be private.

The same principles we learned with proper stable services apply here; the only difference is now we have two types of contracts (public and private); there is what is public to the consumer applications, and there is what is private to the consumer applications but public to the other modules. For instance, the Image functionality on the Profile module could be abstracted by the profile contract and does not necessarily need to be public.

[Converting a distributed monolith to proper SOA](contents.xhtml#sc4_364a)

Applying the Strangler Fig to distributed monolith is different; there are strategies we apply: Gateway and Merging.

Gateway strategy consists of wrapping the individual services with top service, this top service could be done with:

  * A new contract and service that will group and abstract the other services.

  * An API Gateway Solution like AWS API Gateway, Kong API gateway, Nginx, GraphQL and many others technologies.

  * Could also be done with Kubernetes Ingress, Serverless and many other technologies.




The merge strategy is where we basically would merge the service and re-construct the monolith by doing the reverse approach, we would have for example 5 GitHub repositories that would be merged into one single repository. We do not require any special technology here; it is just a code migration. 

Figure 9.26: Strangler Fig - distributed monoliths: gateway vs merging

(Figure 9.26) Let us dive deep into each strategy first, the Gateway approach. Gateway approach allows us to hide the distributed monolith; companies can have hundreds to thousands of microservices. By putting an API Gateway on the top, we are abstracting all the underlying services. Here we will apply the Strangler Fig Pattern too, so the steps would be the same as before, which are:

  * Step one: Add an API Gateway on the top of the distributed monoliths you have.

  * Step two: Identify the first contract that will be moved to the API gateway, if there are no clean, stable contracts or the contracts are not good, you can create new contracts.

  * Step three: Now you need to make the API gateway call the instance service.

  * Step four: Now, you need to refactor all consumers that call the service via API Gateway.

  * Repeat until all calls to the distributed monoliths services are happening via the Gateway.




Once this last step is done, you can start simplifying the underlying services by merging them, decommissioning, re-factoring, re-writing, splitting the database, and so on and so forth. This approach is what Uber did with the Domain Oriented Microservice Architecture (DOMA). (Figure 9.27) [https://www.uber.com/blog/microservice-architecture/](https://www.uber.com/blog/microservice-architecture/) The Uber DOMA post can be summarized as:

  * 2200+ Critical Microservices in Uber.

  * Troubleshooting got complex: investigating 1 issue required to look over 50 services, across 12 different teams.

  * Uber calculated the half-life of a microservice to be 1.5 years, which means that every 1.5 years 50% of our microservices churn which leads to Migration Hell.

  * With Uber DOMA - Onboarding, a new feature time was reduced by 25-50%.




Figure 9.27: Uber DOMA architecture  
Source: [https://www.uber.com/blog/microservice-architecture/](https://www.uber.com/blog/microservice-architecture/)

Airbnb is another silicon valley company that is using the Gateway approach ([https://www.infoq.com/presentations/airbnb-scalability-transition/](https://www.infoq.com/presentations/airbnb-scalability-transition/)), in Airbnb journey however they do not have a single Gateway but layers. Airbnb approach can be summarized as.

  * 4 Big lessons learned: Invest in Common Infra Early, Simplify Service Dependencies, Platform Data Hydration, and Unify Client-Facing API.

  * Server-Driven UI, render the frontend based on the backend service.

  * Tiers of Services, Presentation Services, Data Services.

  * API Gateway.




Now, let us talk about the other approach. Merging might sound strange, but sometimes that is another viable option. Here we literally are going back to the classical monolith and moving all the code base, which is multiple GitHub repositories, to a single GitHub repository. Once all the code is in one place, then you can make the classical monolith a modern monolith create modules, and start fixing things there. One big benefit is that it will be possible to load all code on your IDE and have the benefit of refactoring tools.

An approach that is getting very popular nowadays, going from serverless or microservices to modular monolith. This is what Amazon Prime video did. (Figure 9.28) ([https://www.primevideotech.com/video-streaming/scaling-up-the-prime-video-audio-video-monitoring-service-and-reducing-costs-by-90](https://www.primevideotech.com/video-streaming/scaling-up-the-prime-video-audio-video-monitoring-service-and-reducing-costs-by-90)) Amazon Prime Video had a Serverless solution that was migrated to a monolith style of architecture where it can be summarized as:

  * Amazon Prime Video hit Serverless hard scale limits with 5% expected workload, Lambda / Step Functions.

  * Migration from AWS Step Functions (Serverless) to EC2 and ECS.

  * Prime Video still able to scale workloads with EC2 and ECS.

  * 90% cost reduction as big benefit.




Figure 9.28: Amazon Prime Video architecture - from serverless to monolith  
Source: [https://www.primevideotech.com/video-streaming/scaling-up-the-prime-video-audio-video-monitoring-service-and-reducing-costs-by-90](https://www.primevideotech.com/video-streaming/scaling-up-the-prime-video-audio-video-monitoring-service-and-reducing-costs-by-90)

[Amazon two - way doors](contents.xhtml#sc3_365a)

Amazon has an interesting approach to migrations. Amazon has a leadership principle called avoiding one-way doors, which are decisions you cannot recover from; such a principle is also applied in engineering. Especially in code migrations. AWS has a nice resource called The Amazon Builders’ Library ([https://aws.amazon.com/builders-library/](https://aws.amazon.com/builders-library/)), where they have great articles sharing the lessons learned on how they built AWS. In the article: Ensuring rollback safety during deployments ([https://aws.amazon.com/builders-library/ensuring-rollback-safety-during-deployments](https://aws.amazon.com/builders-library/ensuring-rollback-safety-during-deployments)), Amazon explains that you need to perform migrations with rollback in mind; you need to be able to roll back to the previous changes (Figure 9:29). 

Figure 9.29: Amazon Builders’ Library - Avoid 2-way doors  
Source: <https://aws.amazon.com/builders-library/ensuring-rollback-safety-during-deployments/>

Considering the Amazon article, they suggested that if you want to change a format from XML to JSON, you should not change the code to do it in one shot because this would be a one-way door. You would not be able to easily recover from it. Instead, you would make more phases and more likes, like first reading both formats but writing the old one XML, then at some point reading both but just writing the new one. Eventually, just read/write the final format, JSON.

[Migration roadblocks](contents.xhtml#sc2_366a)

So far in this chapter, we have learned the difference between red and green migrations; we should start with inventories, use cases, and POCs, and we learn the tradeoffs of migrations around migration target, difficulty, customer impact, and execution. In this last sub-section, we learned about code migration patterns such as backward compatibility, lazy migrations, strangler fig, and their application to classical monoliths, modular monoliths, distributed monoliths (Gateway vs Merging), and Amazon’s two-way doors. Now let’s cover some migration roadblocks and give some advice on how to deal with them.

[Roadblock - leftovers](contents.xhtml#sc3_367a)

Red migrations also happen because once you start a migration, you will be facing all forms of roadblocks like:

  * Leftovers - Past Unfinished Migrations

  * Technical Debt

  * Complexity




We have a couple of different ways we can deal with these problems.

[Double down on inventories and POCs](contents.xhtml#sc4_368a)

Another reason to perform code analysis and produce inventories is that it can help to spot all these problems; by doing POCs you can test the waters and reduce the risks of roadblocks during migration. The better your inventory and POCs game is, the smaller the chance you will hit these roadblocks.

[Distributed migrations and team dependencies](contents.xhtml#sc4_369a)

If you decided to run the code migration with multiple distributed teams instead of doing it with a centralized team (Platform vs Services teams), here are a couple of actions you can take to reduce roadblocks:

  * Centralized channel: Have a central slack channel for the migration.

  * Wiki: Make sure you build a shared wiki, teams will likely have the same code issues.

  * Office hours: Deal with exceptions or difficult scenarios with office hours meetings, hybrid teams and videos sharing the lessons learned.




[Repetition, repetition, repetition](contents.xhtml#sc4_370a)

In a distributed team scenario, consider saying the same thing 10x. People are doing multiple projects at the same time; they will drop the ball. The more you repeat, the more they will get it.

[Roadblock - friction and entropy](contents.xhtml#sc3_371a)

Worst kinds of roadblocks for code migrations. Friction happens because there are multiple teams involved, often doing multiple projects at the same time.

One thing worse than friction is entropy, where inertia kills the momentum, you build up on projects. Entropy makes migrations slow down, freeze, then die. Entropy happens because things tend to lose control, and rules for consumption are often not defined, as poor boundaries and lack of the principles we saw over and over in this book. So, the issue with entropy is this, building something better is easier; what is hard is to migrate all consumers to it and delete the old code. Often engineers do not delete code; therefore, the migrations often are partially done; long migrations tend to be canceled also producing leftovers.

Here is how we can deal with these roadblocks:

  * Map dependencies: Make team dependencies clear, and call them out in all meetings, as much as possible.

  * Estimate based on inventories: Make sure you estimate the time for proper migrations.

  * Skillset matters: Consider having the right team with the right skill sets and expertise to run migrations.

  * Backward compatibility: Piggy back on backward compatibility to reduce entropy.

  * Simplification: Simplify your stack and number internal shared libraries to reduce code migration blast radius.

  * Frequent small migrations: Run code migrations more often; smaller deltas tend to be less risky and allow you to move faster.

  * Do not skip decommissioning phase: Consider having a plan and strategy to decommission components, if you just add software and do not delete it, your problem just gets bigger and worse.

  * Product mindset: Make engineering have a product mindset: Understand that speed, bugs, reliability, and availability are part of the customer experience, and engineers are also doing product work.

  * Slack: Make sure teams have slack time, 20% free capacity in all sprints so there is time to accommodate code migrations and roadblocks.

  * Managed services: Leverage managed services and SaaS solutions where you do less and therefore do fewer code migrations.




[Roadblock - high WIP limits, business pressure, compliance, and other traps](contents.xhtml#sc3_372a)

Another common roadblock that makes teams drop the ball is when WIP limits are too high; this could happen because a single team is doing multiple different projects at the same time, but also within a single project the team could be doing lots of multi-task. Often followed by business pressure and or compliance pressure which could come from some regulator, infosec, public and other sources of pressure.

In order to deal with this roadblock, you can do the following:

  * See the whole: Lean principle. Make sure you see all the work your team needs to do.

  * Say no, say later: Consider pushing projects to later time and be honest.

  * Right team sizing: Ask for more people, maybe consider contractors.




Red Migrations suffer much more from this problem than green migrations.

[Post-migration](contents.xhtml#sc2_373a)

Final section of this chapter. We now need to consider what happens after the code migration and when the whole migration is done. Two important elements in code migrations are testing and observability; you need to be able to tell the code migration is not creating problems, and testing is the first way to achieve it. Observability is the second way. We will discuss testing, observability, and everything you need to worry about post-migration for both code and data migrations in greater depth in [Chapter 10](ch10.xhtml), Data Migrations.

Observability has a nice benefit because it can be used after the migration as another opportunity to better understand what your services are doing. Highly recommend instrumenting your services to publish metrics that will give you a clear signal that your migration is going well, such as:

  * Operation, access counters: Total of operations counters.

  * Error observability: Error / exception counters.

  * Latency percentiles: Latency gauges with percentile p50, p75, p90, p95, p99.

  * Centralized logging: Must have. Centralized logging for real exceptions.




Very important to perform verification, meaning if you are migrating code from V1 to V2 or from Arch 1 to Arch 2, how can we tell it is all code? Sometimes this verification can be done with logs, metrics, and deltas from both systems. For instance, if we have system V1 and system V2, can we run our test suite, let’s say it has 5k tests against both systems and get the same results.

[Rollback strategies](contents.xhtml#sc3_374a)

The best rollback strategies are implemented by having backward compatibility and following Amazon’s avoid Two-Way Doors principle. The rollback could be automatic or manual. For manual rollback, consider having a runbook like this:

  * Anomalies: How to detect anomalies? i.e. Dashboard + Alert.

  * Rollback procedures: How to perform rollback? i.e. do a PR reverting the last commit or using the old JAR.

  * Sanity verification: How to ensure everything is, okay? Run the tests again and see no errors in observability.




While this whole process could also be automated. Modern solutions call this: Automated Canary analysis and rollbacks, solutions like Spinnaker and Argo CD, implement this pattern.

[Things to remember](contents.xhtml#sc2_375a)

Congratulations, you have reached the end of [Chapter 9](ch09.xhtml), Code Migrations. A few things to keep in mind:

  * Do not take code migrations for granted.

  * Several different reasons might force you into code migrations:

    * Software going EOL

    * End of the road for a vendor

    * Compliance i.e. Infosec

    * Infrastructure i.e. cloud migrations

    * Modernization: Slice and dice Monoliths

    * Legacy sunsetting

    * New business needs and capabilities

    * Consolidations due to company acquisition

  * There are two kinds of migrations:

    * RED: full of pain points and issues

    * GREEN migrations: smooth migrations due proper due diligence

  * Red migrations:

    * Huge blast radius

    * Lots of breaking changes

    * Slow, late, expensive feedback

    * Late bugs

    * Indirect and hidden business value

    * Poor visibility, difficult to track

    * Painful rollbacks

  * Green migrations:

    * Controlled blast radius

    * Fewer breaking changes

    * Fast, Early, Cheap feedback

    * Early bugs

    * Direct, clear business value

    * Great visibility, easy to track

    * Manageable rollbacks

    * Inventory and POCs are key to have green migrations

  * Inventories are important because:

    * They allow you to understand the scope of the migration

    * They capture important insights about your consumers, dependencies, and issues

    * They provide a centralized view of the progress

    * They allow you to strategize and plan the migration

  * Migration tradeoffs have four categories:

    * Migration target: component type, what it is and how it will be done

    * Team impact

    * Customer impact

    * Execution

    * Proper services allow much easier migrations than distributed monoliths

    * Customer impact is about being online or offline.

    * Execution could be by a platform team, service team, or hybrid

  * Code migration patterns:

    * Backward compatibility

    * Lazy migrations

    * Strangler fig

    * Amazon two way doors

  * Strangler applied to distributed monoliths is different, it has 2 strategies:

    * Gateway: keep as it is, add a top-level gateway.

    * Merging: simplify the problem to evolve to a modular monolith.

  * Leftovers and other roadblocks can be mitigated with proper inventories and POCs.
