# [CHAPTER 10Data Migrations](contents.xhtml#ch10a)

Some men see things the way they are and ask why? I dream of things that never were and ask why not?

— J.F. Kennedy

It has to be obvious; you cannot fix monoliths without fixing their datastores and data models as well. [Chapter 8](ch08.xhtml), Embracing New Technology taught you about choosing the right tool for the job, and one of your primary tools is your database. Code migrations are not going to be enough; you are going to need to move data too. Data migrations are universally needed for slicing and dicing classical monoliths, fixing distributed monoliths, any form of cloud migration, modernization or digital transformation, or upgrading the persistence storage for a proper service.

Yes, Data Migrations are hard as it is. However, it is possible to do them properly and get everything right. In this chapter, we will share common recipes, patterns, and ways to approach data migrations successfully. Your assessment and planning phase is essential to find common patterns and approaches. We will revisit two code migration patterns and their implication in the context of data migrations, lazy migrations and strangler fig. Finally, we will cover post migration aspects such as testing, and monitoring, ensuring we covered everything and then clean up and decommissioning. Let us get started.

[Structure](contents.xhtml#sc2_376a)

In this chapter, we will cover the following topics:

  * Data migration risks

  * Pre-data migrations

    * Inventory and analysis

    * Planning and identifying quick wins

  * Data migration patterns

    * Revisiting - lazy migrations

    * Export and import

    * Database replication

    * Table diff

    * Triggers

    * Change Data Capture

    * Dual writing

      * Cold data

      * Failure in one write operation

      * Transaction delay and complexity

      * Data migration strategies

    * Online vs offline

    * Schema first versus multiple migrations

    * Revising: Strangler Fig

      * Classical monoliths, Strangler Fig, and data migrations

      * Executing migrations

    * Testing

    * Pre-migration sanity testing

    * Performance testing

    * Practical database performance testing with NDBench

    * Sanity test: post-migration structure and data

    * Observability

  * Post-data migrations

    * Ghost hunting

    * Clean up and decommissioning

  * Things to remember




[Data migration risks](contents.xhtml#sc2_377a)

Everything is data. Data is the lifeblood of most modern companies, and so by definition, data migrations are more difficult than code migrations for the high risks associated with them:

  * Data loss, data corruption: If you lose data, you can lose the entire business, suffer brand damage, or find yourself in trouble with regulatory agencies.

  * Huge disruption: If a service is down, you can always bring it back up; if your data is lost through a bad migration, you can disrupt the entire customer experience.

  * Financial loss: Lost data means lost sales or further financial impacts.

  * Difficult rollback: Datastores contain complex representations of state; rolling back a corrupted database is a heck of a lot harder and more complex than falling back to your standby, dark service worker pool.




You need to carefully plan and execute data migrations to be successful. Let us dive into the preparation all teams should do before getting started.

[Pre-data migrations](contents.xhtml#sc2_378a)

Any good migration starts with a proper assessment; a way to make sense of the existing landscape that you can use to create an effective plan for your data migrations. Let us start with analysis and by creating an effective inventory.

[Inventory and analysis](contents.xhtml#sc3_379a)

Building an inventory is critical before starting any data migration; go back to [Chapter 5](ch05.xhtml), Assessments, and review the techniques to assemble a shared inventory sheet reviewed by your engineers and architects. Your inventory sheet can cover additional material if you are focusing on data migrations; you might need to gather information such as:

  * Ownership: Who owns the data? Is there a data governance process at your company? Do you know who the data stewards are?

  * Readers/writers and applications: What applications read and write to the particular migration target? Do you have downstream consumers, like a shared database stream or a big data process?

  * Criticality: How critical is this data? Is this data store the source of truth for the data, or is it just a replica or alternative representation? How much downtime can the organization tolerate?

  * Unit of work: For relational databases especially, what clusters, schemas, tables, columns or even rows are impacted?

  * Cleanup and decommission opportunities: Is all of the data truly necessary and actively used by the business? Any chance of decommissioning instead of migrating?




You can gather this information by leveraging people - asking architects, lead engineers, DBAs, and engineering managers to fill in these sheets and answer these questions - tooling, or even both. (Figure 10.1) Tooling means using automation to extract these answers. You could parse information from your code bases, such as reading out the Hibernate/JPA entities or parsing the direct SQL in your code base. For instance, once you have the queries, you can extract the tables and columns by using a SQL parser like JSQLParser ([https://github.com/JSQLParser/JSqlParser](https://github.com/JSQLParser/JSqlParser)).

Observability can play a role here, using tools to get insights about what is happening operationally; profiling and APM tools like Datadog, New Relic, and Dynatrace, logging stacks like ELK or Graylog, and many more techniques to identify database usage hotspots. 

Figure 10.1: Preparing for data migrations - people vs. tooling approaches

Gathering information to support your data migrations can be done in many different ways; there is no right or wrong balance for leaning on people vs. tooling. Here is some guidance:

  * Frequency: If it is a one-time thing, people might be faster. Good manual work is where you do it once and leverage it multiple times; bad manual work is where you need to repeat it over and over again. It goes without saying that projects should minimize and avoid bad manual work.

  * Size and volume: If you have a massive landscape of teams, services, and datastores, automation via tooling makes more sense. No need to ask 100 people if the automation will answer it for you. For the use cases of fixing monoliths and distributed monoliths, sometimes teams do not have the answers because of team erosion, and automation is a stronger and faster answer.

  * Check your tools: If you already have good SQL logging and observability tools in place or your database supports observability on running queries, tooling will be a good approach. You might be surprised by the amount of data and information that might be already available, either by a CLI tool, API, script or even some admin UI you are not aware of.

  * Do not be afraid of socialization: Really, there is nothing wrong with asking different teams to read the code and figure it out in regards to the data usage. Frankly, your data migration is going to be a challenge; best to get the teams engaged and start to get passionate about the benefits of changing technology stacks.




Good databases often have metadata tables where you can get the queries that are running right now, as you can see in Table 10.1.

Database Engine |  Table / Views  
---|---  
Postgres105 |  pg_stat_activity  
MySQL106 |  INFORMATION_SCHEMA.PROCESSLIST  
Oracle107 |  v$sqlarea, v$session and v$sql  
  
Table 10.1: Relation DB and observability tables

Querying metadata tables is not a silver bullet; a key drawback is that the applications and services need to be in use, either by the end users in production, test suites, or synthetic load. If your applications are quiet, you would not see any queries. And just because the queries show up rarely does not mean they are not important; imagine you have a complex yet important business flow that only runs 1x per month. If you do not query the metadata tables on that specific day, at that specific moment, your database will not spit out the queries, and you will miss it. However, the good news here is if you see the query when you access the metadata tables, it means the application is using it.

Let us review the tooling options of parsing the codebase. Let us say your code base is in a remote version control system like git, using GitHub or GitLab; you can write a crawler to scan all your code base, extract the queries and feed them to JSQLParser to extract tables and columns. Parsing the codebase is not trivial or easy:

  * Difficult scenarios: Parsing might have challenges; code is not always straightforward, that is, the application concatenates dynamic SQL queries.

  * Dead code: Just because you see SQL in the code, does not mean it is used; it could be dead code you could spend months trying to track down.




Hibernate / JPA, SpringData, or frankly any good ORM log SQL queries with a simple configuration change; often a simple Java property like in Table 10.2.

Framework / Solution |  Configuration108  
---|---  
Spring Data JPA |  spring.jpa.show-sql=true spring.jpa.properties.hibernate.format_sql=true  
Hibernate |  log4j.logger.org.hibernate.SQL=debug log4j.logger.org.hibernate.type.descriptor.sql=trace  
  
Table 10.2: Java ORMs configurations to log queries

Logging SQL queries in your applications is subject to the same limitations reading metadata tables, or any database observability tool approach; you need to trigger the code to execute the queries either with real traffic or test suites. As compared with scanning the code, logging queries will work better for dynamic queries, as the ORM will resolve the final dynamic query and log it. (Figure 10.2)

Figure 10.2: Summary of the options to extract database information

Of course, just understanding the source does not tell you the migration target; that requires software architecture, an understanding of your business domain and further tradeoff analysis. You have multiple options:

  * Simple splitting: Slicing and dicing classical monoliths, turning one big database into smaller independent databases.

  * Complex splitting: Apply the Strangler Fig pattern: untangle coupled tables, organize them by business domains, and create dedicated proper services with isolated databases by domain.

  * Merging: Combining the services of a distributed monolith into domain-driven services with proper database granularity.

  * Evolution in place: Schema evolution, changing the data model but keeping the datastore.

  * Datastore migration: Change the underlying persistence store; go from Oracle to AWS Aurora, or apply CQRS and go from SQL server to Postgres, Kafka, and ElasticSearch.




Having all this data in hand in the form of our inventory, we can now proceed to construct a plan, and if you are lucky, we can find some quick wins, also known as low-hanging fruits.

[Planning and identifying quick wins](contents.xhtml#sc3_380a)

Having a good inventory is an essential input for proper data migration planning. With it, you can organize your possible data migrations into groups, and domains that can be organized by criticality, by business unit, or by technology. You might identify POCs that you need to execute to prove that certain migration targets are viable, or performance tests that prove that your RDS Oracle configuration will match or exceed your on-premise physical hardware. Convincing your business on the need to execute data migrations is beyond the scope of this book, but a good plan is going to go a long way to show them that you are well informed and organized.

If you are lucky, your inventory exercise might reveal some quick wins or low-hanging fruits. Maybe there are schemas in a shared database that are independent and do not have many cross-schema dependencies; or within one schema, you have sets of tables that are clearly independent of each other.

To show you how you would realize a quick win in more detail, let us explore a scenario where you have a classical monolith and a big shared central relational database with multiple schemas. In our quick win scenario, you discover 3 isolated schemas, they share the same physical cluster, but they do not have cross-schema communication. All you have to do is create 3 separate database instances, one per schema. (Figure 10.3) Easier said than done, of course; we will cover practical techniques to execute this migration shortly. 

Figure 10.3: Split shared database to 3 instances with 1 schema each

Let us not forget that splitting out 3 separate database clusters implies having proper services, isolated contracts, and isolated instances. You might not be able to do all of this with one single move; you might need to move the database first and then split the service code, or you might be able to do both at the same time. 

Figure 10.4: Create new schemas from isolated sets of tables

The second possibility we discussed is where you have a single schema, but when you analyze table usage, you realize there are 3 sets of independent tables. (Figure 10.4) Go buy a lottery ticket, you just struck the jackpot; you can do the same as mentioned in the previous approach, and go straight to creating more proper services with isolated databases.

Quick wins are not always available; if you do not have them, so be it. But if you have the opportunity, quick wins should take priority in your data migration exercise. Showing your business real, immediate progress is always the best outcome. Your data migration project might take a long time, and waiting until the end to deliver results is always a risky strategy. Even if you deliver low-hanging fruits quickly, you are not out of trouble; eventually, you must do the hard work, which we will cover later in this chapter.

[Data migration patterns](contents.xhtml#sc2_381a)

OK, we are past the easy stuff. Now, let us get into how you can properly execute data migrations.

[Revisiting - lazy migrations](contents.xhtml#sc3_382a)

Lazy migrations were covered in the previous chapter, [Chapter 9](ch09.xhtml), Code Migrations. Let us expand the pattern a little bit more now in the context of data migrations. 

Figure 10.5: Lazy migrations revisited

Figure 10.5 depicts the Profile Service V1 to V2 lazy migration scenario we discussed at length in [Chapter 9](ch09.xhtml), Code Migrations. As a reminder, lazy migrations happen in flight, with the service code migrating data on the application side as the service processes new data. In our example, we are conducting a data migration from profile id V1 to profile id format V2. Our code can read back both V1 and V2, but can only write V2; over time, as users or services access profile data, all the data in MySQL and cached in Redis will be transparently migrated to the new format. The number of data stores used by the service does not matter; here, we are cleanly handling 2 data stores, but it could be any number.

Proper services are the key to lazy migrations, as they provide a firm service boundary separating the interface from the implementation. Your consumers do not know the specifics of how the service works, allowing data migrations and database refactoring generally to happen transparently. Lazy migrations enable multiple scenarios, all controlled by the service:

  * Column refactoring: Transform data, writing it to a different column, combine columns or split columns.

  * Table refactoring: Refactor a table completely into a different table structure.

  * Schema refactoring: Move schemas to a different cluster, as we saw in our quick win examples earlier in this chapter.

  * Datastore migration: Write to different persistence storage; shift from MySQL RDS to AWS Aurora, for example.

  * Data format refactoring: Transform the data format and meaning entirely, as we saw in the profile id v1/v2 example.




Lazy migrations happen while the application is running, bundling data transformation with reads and writes, so it happens slowly. This raises some obvious concerns and drawbacks:

  * Delivery timeline: The data migration would not necessarily finish on a fixed schedule and could take longer to complete than you would like.

  * Cold data: If some ids are never read or written, they are never migrated. You might need to write a separate batch job to migrate the remaining ids; we will revisit cold data later in this chapter when we discuss dual writes.

  * Latency: You are executing more code and accessing more data at runtime, and so you might be adding more latency by doing the feature + migration simultaneously.

  * Errors: What happens when you have errors? Do you abort the migration?




Because of these drawbacks for data migrations, the lazy migration pattern might not be enough. Besides lazy migrations, there are other patterns that can help us to mitigate.

[Export and import](contents.xhtml#sc3_383a)

The export and import pattern is simple to understand, and probably the first pattern you thought about when you read the topic of this chapter: export the database to a file containing SQL commands, and then you import that SQL file into another database. (Figure 10.6) Typically, the dumped file will have SQL using the Data Description Language (DDL) and Data Manipulation Language (DML) syntax.109 In other words, table structure and data. For most databases, these scripts can be automatically generated using a dump tool such as MySQL dump or Oracle Data Pump (Table 10.3), and the dump files themselves are already tuned for speed. Export and import are one of the fastest options to move data, so long as you are not changing the persistence store technology. 

Figure 10.6: Dump and import using SQL, DDL and DML

Exporting and importing has a few obvious drawbacks:

  * Offline: Typically, the generated statements are destructive; they lock tables, and can take a lot of physical resources to execute. Plus, you do not want new records being added or changed while you are importing. For maximum safety, you need to take both databases offline.

  * Locked data persistence technology: SQL is universal, but not all databases work the same way. You cannot just import a MySQL data export into Oracle. If you want to change databases as well, you will need to transform the file.




If you plan to only rely on simple export and import, you are limited in how you can execute your data migration. Export and import are often most effective when combined with other strategies; for instance, imagine exporting data, transforming DML or DDL in the SQL dump to change data or data structure or even persistence store target, then importing the data into a new database, all online; then relying on either a lazy migration strategy, dual writes or even change data capture to catch up and finish. Export and import can be a flexible tool when used intelligently.

Database |  Tool  
---|---  
MySQL110 |  mysqldump  
Postgres111 |  pg_dump  
Oracle112 |  SQL Developer, Oracle Data Pump  
  
Table 10.3: Export and import tools by database

[Database replication](contents.xhtml#sc3_384a)

Most databases support data replication capabilities that are essential to the design of highly available and reliable systems. The simplest versions allow you to create dedicated read replicas, with write transactions only happening on one or more master nodes; advanced relational databases and NoSQL solutions let you run fully databases in a multi-master113 or active-active configuration, where all nodes can handle reads and writes, relying on database replication to keep nodes in sync. Data replication can be used for a variety of benefits and use cases, such as:

  * Scaling out: Read replicas spread out application database read load across multiple machines in order to speed up performance. Multi-master databases accomplish the same thing, but can also handle writes; read replica strategies assume a single master write happens in one node, and reads happen in the replicas.

  * Fault tolerance and disaster recovery: Avoid a single point of failure in your database by replicating data to multiple nodes. If a node fails, you can recover by failing over to a different node. Similarly, replicating data to another site or region can let you recover from a complete site failure. Replication is an important tool for disaster recovery and general resiliency.

  * Analytical workloads: Create dedicated read replicas to handle heavy analytical workloads, while live transaction processing only happens on the master.

  * Backups: Backup services can run on the replicas without disrupting the master and the applications and services reading and writing on the master.

  * Data migrations: As you can imagine, data replication can play a powerful role in enabling data migrations. Teams can use data replication as a mechanism to sync data and then migrate from one environment to another, or even just from one machine to another. Data migrations can replicate data using many different methods, with native database tools, 3rd party software, or even on the application/service side.




Figure 10.7: Data replication from classical monolith to a proper service

Figure 10.7 shows an example where we are migrating from a classical monolith to a proper service, and you have one shared database cluster with many, many tables and schemas. Your team wants to migrate one set of the schemas to a new, isolated database, currently in a master/replica arrangement with Master 1 (M1), Replica 1 (R1), and Replica 2 (R2). We can use database replication in order to copy the data from the old shared cluster to a new isolated cluster. You start off by replicating M1 to a new database cluster with 3 dedicated read replicas, R3, R4 and R5. Once all the replicas on the isolated DB are in sync, you designate one replica to be the new master, then switch the source of truth to the new cluster, cutting off access from the isolated DB to M1, R1 and R2, cleaning up and decommissioning the old data in the old cluster. (Figure 10.11)

Figure 10.8: Switch source of truth from classical monolith to a proper service

While this is illustrated here in 2 simple steps, this is a long and hard task that can take months to years to get done, depending on the coupling and complexity of your systems. We will revisit these steps in more depth when we revisit the strangler fig pattern later in this chapter.

[Table diff](contents.xhtml#sc3_385a)

Another simple technique that complements either export and import or database replication scenarios is to run a table diff; after moving data, you compare all the data in the source table with the data in a destination table, identifying the deltas that are on the source table but are not on the target table. (Figure 10.9).

Table diffs are great to confirm a successful migration or allow you to run some data migrations mostly online, with the delta helping you catch up with a handful of missed records. You can run a table diff using the tools described in the export/import section or by coding your small application. You can also run the table diff periodically before you switch over, but keep in mind that table diffs are resource intensive and would not scale to large tables. 

Figure 10.9: Table diff approach

The table diff approach could be used for two different data migrations use cases:

  * Database refactoring: Migrating schemas, tables, or columns; for instance, where you want to use a new table with a new column, but need to copy data from the original table.

  * Datastore migration: Migrate to a different database entirely, i.e. from MySQL to Postgres. Most simple SQL database table structures are directly comparable, and so a simple table diff might be all you need to sync data if you are transforming a classical monolith to a proper service.




Table diffs have limitations with large tables or datasets because you need to read the source table from time to time. You can reduce some of the impacts of this technique by reading from a read replica instead, but there are better techniques than this one.

[Triggers](contents.xhtml#sc3_386a)

Many modern databases support database triggers: database code that executes in response to predefined database events. Triggers can be used to support data migrations as well. In a simple scenario, a trigger can fire on inserts, updates, and deletes to a source table or group of tables and then record these changes in a log table. Later on, this log table can be used as a source for replicating changes to other tables or even databases. 

Figure 10.10: Trigger approach

Figure 10.10 shows this in more detail. A changelog table is fed by triggers that fire on changes to the source tables. A function then reads the changelog table and sends the table to another table in another database; this is depicted as a database function on the shared DB, but the function could be on the isolated DB instead, or even an independent script or serverless function. Will work well if the new isolated DB has the same database technology as the source database. If the isolated database is in a different persistence technology and the team is changing from Postgres to MySQL for instance, the changelog reader function will need to translate from one database format to another.

The biggest issue with triggers is that they can affect performance by delaying transactions. Also, you will not be able to use the same trigger for all tables in some database technologies and/or might need to combine them with other solutions like stored procedures and functions. What if there is a new table? Would it be captured for the trigger? So, you might need extra scripts and code in order to keep generating triggers for new tables.

[Change Data Capture](contents.xhtml#sc3_387a)

Change Data Capture (CDC) is the ideal solution for data migrations, especially if you need near-real-time data movement. In change data capture, you read the source database’s log of all database changes, and use that log to apply changes to a new target database. Many databases use some form of CDC to implement data replication, as you just read the CDC logs to synchronize the systems, so the two strategies are related. CDC data migrations directly use that data replication log to act, which is useful when complete transformation is required, such as to a completely new datastore. CDC strategies can be done using Apache Kafka to act as a conduit between source and target databases. Depending on the nature of your business and general availability requirements, you might be very intolerant to downtime, and working at scale, your data volume might be very big; in these scenarios, CDC is a great fit. 

Figure 10.11: CDC from classical monolith to proper isolated DB

CDC implementations might seem more complex, but in turn you get great flexibility. Figure 10.11 shows our classical monolith with inserts, updates, and deletes happening to the source tables. As these changes are processed, they are written to the local database transaction logs; those logs are read and sent to a conduit like Apache Kafka. A Kafka consumer application will subscribe to these changes; it could be a simple Java application, a serverless function or even some simple Java code that lives inside a proper service.

The good news is that there are paid and open-source options for CDC. For instance, Oracle has Golden Gate114, and Debezium ([https://debezium.io/](https://debezium.io/)) is a great open-source CDC solution. 

Figure 10.12: CDC - Debezium overall architecture  
Source: <https://debezium.io/documentation/reference/stable/architecture.html>

Debezium has source connectors, which you will use to connect to source databases where you want to extract data, like MySQL, Postgres, Oracle, Cassandra, MongoDB, SQL Server, DB2, Vitess, and many more. (Figure 10.12) Debezium can also send change events to Kafka, and from there, you can use Kafka connectors to send data to a variety of sources like ElasticSearch, Infinispan, and your data warehouse or you can even write your subscriber as part of a proper service.

[Dual writing](contents.xhtml#sc3_388a)

Dual writes are another active way for applications to handle data migrations directly (Figure 10.13). In a dual write scenario, teams write to two different databases at the same time, which might or might not use the same data structure. Proper services are a great way to implement dual writes, as they have isolated databases, and there is no risk of another service writing to one database alone. Dual Writing is also known as the “shotgun” pattern. 

Figure 10.13: Dual write pattern

Dual writes can support a variety of data migration use cases:

  * Slice and dice: Slicing and dicing the classical monolith.

  * Database refactoring: Refactoring your database and schema evolution generally.

  * Datastore migration: Migrating from one database technology to another, i.e. from Oracle to MySQL.

  * Cluster evolution: You are running Apache Cassandra 2.x and want to migrate to Cassandra 3.x, or you want to stay on the same version but upgrade to a new cluster with better hardware.




The main difference between dual writes and database replication is that dual writes are performed by the application side only, the service side. When you ask the service to perform a write operation, you will write to two different tables or databases; the database itself has no knowledge and plays no direct role.

Dual writing has drawbacks as well:

  * Cold data: Like for lazy migrations; dual writes only happen for data the service actively processes. It will not work for cold data, which you are not often updating.

  * Failure: What happens if the write fails in one of the DBs? How do you recover?

  * Latency: Two databases have to acknowledge the write, so this adds some delays and complexity to the overall transaction.




Let us discuss these drawbacks in more detail.

[Cold data](contents.xhtml#sc4_389a)

Data that is hot will be migrated; however, what happens to data points that are never read/written? Well, they will never be migrated. So, the dual write pattern cannot work alone. Thankfully, it has some simple fixes; you can do a table diff to identify the missing data and then trigger your service to process the deltas, live. You can read from the service, then write to the service, or potentially expose a dedicated, migration only endpoint that does the same in an atomic transaction. You can process the deltas quickly during the off hours of your application or slowly, queuing up record ids to be processed over time.

You might ask, why not read all the records in the first place? Just write a script that runs all records forcefully through the dual writes? Because dual writes has a key advantage in that you get to see your application code running live before switching over to it. What if the service code has a bug that only shows up under the high load you see in production? We covered the advantages of testing in production in similar scenarios in [Chapter 7](ch07.xhtml), Proper Service Testing; dual writes lets you kill two birds with one stone, both handling the data migration and testing your code before customers are impacted.

[Failure in one write operation](contents.xhtml#sc4_390a)

Well, for this case, we have two options, either you completely ignore it and let the table diff component take care of that, or you will need to retry. Waiting until a table diff executes creates the issue where the two DBs will be out of sync for a while; eventually, they will be consistent. If you just wait for a retry alone, you are delaying the translation; you also cannot retry forever, so after 3 attempts, what do you do? You can log the errors in another table for some manual work to compensate, or just ignore the failure, let the table diff take care of it, and debug what went wrong with your engineers afterwards.

[Transaction delay and complexity](contents.xhtml#sc4_391a)

No matter what, you are doing more work, and this will add some sort of delay to the main transaction, using more resources on the application side and creating more complexity for the service to handle it. Complexity needs to live somewhere. When you rely on database replication, the complexity is pushed to the DB; in dual writes, the complexity lives in the service. 

Figure 10.14: Dual Write Pattern with Classical Monolith

You might be thinking, But I have a monolith that I am slicing and dicing. I do not want to write any application code that connects to my new, isolated database; that is exactly how you end up sharing databases! So, let us rely on the nature of proper services; have the monolith application write both to this database and the proper service. (Figure 10.14) Migrate all use cases and writers to the new service. The only difference between Figure 10.13 and 10.14 is that the dual write happens by calling a service instead of the monolith directly calling the Isolated DB.

[Data migration strategies](contents.xhtml#sc2_392a)

So far, we learned about the importance of doing an assessment and building a proper inventory to support your data migration, and investigated patterns to approach individual data migrations. Let us go deeper and understand some broader data migration strategies such as online vs offline, schema first, and strangler fig revisited.

[Online vs offline](contents.xhtml#sc3_393a)

Depending on your business, downtime might not be an option. When downtime is an option you can take, take it! Offline data migrations are much easier. Frankly speaking, some databases handle data migrations better than others, so as much as you can, try to make things as simple as possible. For instance, if you can just rely on export and import, go for it. It is in cases where there is high coupling and a lot of entanglement where you need to rely on patterns like CDC and more advanced strategies like strangler fig, which we will revisit shortly. You need to understand your tools and check your assumptions. Review the downtime expectations for your migration solutions, for instance thinking about Oracle replication, you might have a table such as the one in Table 10.4.

Solution |  Downtime Expectations  
---|---  
Oracle Golden Gate |  Zero to seconds  
Oracle Data Guard |  Less than 5 minutes  
Oracle Recovery Manager |  Less than 2 hours  
  
Table 10.4: Oracle115 solutions and downtime expectations example

If your business cannot tolerate downtime or has aggressive reliability requirements, perhaps you should be considering a transition to highly available databases like the ones we reviewed in [Chapter 8](ch08.xhtml), Embracing New Technology such as Cassandra, ScyllaDB116, or AWS Aurora.

Highly available databases often act in a multi-master configuration, like we reviewed in our section on database replication; multi-master databases are perfect for multi-region workloads that need an active-active configuration. If you are not using a highly-available, multi-master database, but your business needs it, you might be able to tolerate some downtime to migrate to a better database.

[Schema first versus multiple migrations](contents.xhtml#sc3_394a)

So, you know you want to transform an existing database and table structure to a new database, with a very different table structure. Do you do the data first, then transform it; or both at once? Each data migration approach has different tradeoffs. Transforming both the data and schema at the same time is the schema first approach; let us define a new schema with a new table design or even move away from the relational model and embrace NoSQL, and go straight there during our data migration. The second approach would be to move to the new database cluster first, using the same schema or as close as possible, and then refactor it later, effectively performing multiple migrations.

Figure 10.15: Spectrum of migration and evolution

Remember there is no right or wrong here, only trade-offs. (Figure 10.15) Schema first is interesting because when you are done, you are completely done; in one fell swoop, you migrate both the data and jump to a new cluster or even new technology. The drawback is greater risk; it is harder since you are doing two things simultaneously, so more things can go wrong.

However, for multiple migrations, we have the benefits of first switching to the new source of truth first and then later focusing on the evolution of the schema. So here means we have more phases, so it is a bit easier, but you will end up doing more than one migration as a drawback. Critically multiple migrations cannot work when migrating to a very different data persistence technology, like from RDBMS to NoSQL. If you are going from MySQL to Cassandra, trying to replicate your MySQL schema in Cassandra is a recipe for failure, whereas that might be fine when going from MySQL to Postgres.

Considering the techniques and patterns we have covered here so far: lazy migrations, export and import, database replication, table diff, database triggers, or dual writes, you can approach any of these data migration patterns via the schema first or multiple migrations approach. It is up to you to decide.

[Revising: Strangler Fig](contents.xhtml#sc3_395a)

We reviewed the Strangler Fig pattern in depth in [Chapter 9](ch09.xhtml), Code Migrations; the strangler fig is an important pattern for advanced code migrations, and it is no different for the data migrations we cover in this chapter. It is the way to go when you need to slice and dice the classical monolith. Regardless of your migration use case, whether you are migrating from classical monoliths or distributed monoliths to proper services, you will be required to migrate data and the strangler fig is a powerful data migration strategy. Let us start by looking at the classical monolith. (Figure 10.16)

[Classical monoliths, Strangler Fig, and data migrations](contents.xhtml#sc4_396a)

Figure 10.16: Classical monolith - step 1

First of all, you need to start with some concept of how you want to split the monolith, which can be done by focusing on specific grouping criteria such as:

  * Business domain: As we covered in [Chapter 5](ch05.xhtml), Assessments, domain mapping is the best way to organize software.

  * Features: Features often cross business domains; this is not ideal, but might be possible.

  * Tables: Your existing data organization likely does not represent your current business structure; far from ideal but better than nothing.




Let us go back to your proper inventory. Let us say you chose to break apart your database by business domain, and you are starting by slicing and dicing the sales domain. You need to identify all readers and writers to the tables in this business domain. You can and should build an inventory like the one in Table 10.5.

Table Name |  Reader |  Writer |  Move to a new service?  
---|---|---|---  
Sales |  SalesDAO.java |  \-- |  YES  
Users |  UserController.java |  \-- |  NO  
Sales |  \-- |  UserAnalytics.java |  NO  
Users |  \-- |  JSP_page_users.jsp  |  NO  
  
Table 10.5: Writes/readers inventory

As with any inventory, your list might end up being very long and we always recommend a shared spreadsheet like Microsoft Excel or Google Sheets. Once you have this information, you can start to design the new service, define the new contract and start implementation. (Figure 10.17)

Figure 10.17: Classical monolith to proper service - step 2

Considering the Strangler Fig, the recommendation is you move the writers first and the readers later. You will need to move each writer one by one. As before, you must choose if you do schema first or multiple migrations; let us assume you go with schema first. So now we must apply the dual write on the classical monolith and move one of the writers to the new proper service. (Figure 10.18)

Figure 10.18: Classical monolith to proper service migration - step 3

Here, we are first conducting a code migration and then a data migration, building the service first then swapping the data, one after the other. You cannot delete the old code yet. So, you repeat this pattern until all writers and readers are switched to the new proper service. You are not done yet; you must delete the dead code and delete the data from the old database as a priority, to avoid confusion and dead code. (Figure 10.19)

Figure 10.19: Classical monolith to proper service migration - step 4

Now, you can proceed to the next domain, feature, or group of tables and repeat this whole process. Strangler Fig can also be applied to distributed monoliths, with the main difference being that instead of having all the code in one GitHub repository, you have the code split across multiple repositories.

[Executing migrations](contents.xhtml#sc2_397a)

Designing and planning your database migration is just the first part; to successfully execute them, you need to know that they are working, and that your secret weapons are testing and observability. Both of them do not stop at the moment of migration, but will benefit your software through its future lifecycle.

[Testing](contents.xhtml#sc3_398a)

Testing is an instrumental tool to execute code and data migrations well. There are different categories and kinds of tests you might want to run, but at a minimum should cover (Figure 10.20):

  * Pre-migration sanity test: Pre-data migration functional testing: is everything good, or do we have issues before we start? It is important to know if your migration is the cause of the problem or if they were already there.

  * Application performance testing: Application performance should be gauged and tested with a tool like Gatling.

  * Database performance testing: DB perspective also needs to be tested, maybe with a tool like NDBench.

  * Post-migration sanity test: You test in the beginning but also in the end. Conduct a post-migration sanity test for structure and data.




Figure 10.20: Database migration testing

[Pre-migration sanity testing](contents.xhtml#sc3_399a)

Before you start any migration, it is a good idea to make sure the application and the source database is in good shape. At a minimum, you want to:

  * Check the build: Make sure all associated code projects have the build passing.

  * Check test pass rate: Ensure the projects’ test suites are all running successfully with a 100% pass rate for all tests. If tests are failing, why? Use the opportunity to clean them up: fix or delete them.

  * Check centralized logs: Make sure neither the applications nor the databases are logging errors, unhandled exceptions, or that there are otherwise surprising log messages. If you do not already have a centralized log solution to capture messages from multiple systems, step back; how else will you find unexpected issues that happen across your applications? Important to know existing issues.

  * Check dashboards: Make sure all your application health dashboards are in good shape, with no alerts being triggered. While checking dashboards to look for what alerts are in place, review the data migration strategy you are taking; will this trigger false alarms? You might need to disable some for the duration of the migration.




Tests should exist before you start a sanity test and already be part of how the applications are maintained and released. However, classical monoliths and distributed monoliths can be challenging and may lack tests.

Adding tests before the migration starts might sound silly, but it is super important and a very good idea. Because if your data migration has errors, you can properly reason with sanity if the error was created by the migration or was already happening before the migration. If you run all tests before starting the migration and all tests pass with no errors, then it is a clue that the error for sure, you suspect it was introduced by the migration.

The reason why we are saying clue and not absolute proof is because the world keeps turning while you are planning and executing your data migration; teams are building and releasing features, and other migrations are happening in parallel (Figure 10.21). Regardless of the state of your bad monolith, are you sure you cannot afford the effort to fix tests and errors before the migration starts? Trust us, we speak from experience: proper testing saves hours and hours of life, panicked troubleshooting and debugging.

Figure 10.21: Reality of migrations

[Performance testing](contents.xhtml#sc3_400a)

You need to understand how your migrations will impact application performance, and performance testing is the best tool. Conduct a baseline performance test before the migration starts, and another one after you finish to validate the migration. For lazy migration or dual write scenarios, it might be a good idea to run a test during the migration itself.

Performance testing is important because perhaps all your functional tests pass, but a missing index or badly engineered function means poor performance under load and possible customer impact. Applications will change their data access patterns through data migrations, especially when changing persistence technologies; you need to spot the anti-patterns before they go live. You might be introducing a new database; do you know how it will perform under load? Perhaps running hot on CPU is expected for this new technology, but that is a red flag with your previous stack.

In practice, you will conduct two forms of performance testing:

  * Black box testing: Create load on your application, and check for latency and response time. Take a broad view, do not just look at your application components but at the whole graph of dependencies. We covered Gatling as an effective way to conduct performance testing in [Chapter 7](ch07.xhtml), Proper Service Testing. Use gatling to conduct a load test, simulate expecting production traffic, so you can measure how the service behaves and check that performance meets or beats that expectation after the migrations.

  * White box testing: Take a look at it from a database perspective; check that your destination hardware is correct for your needs, verify query performance, indexing, execution plans and many internal details and parameters.




Let us take a closer look at a specialized tool to help with white box / database performance testing: NDBench.

[Practical database performance testing with NDBench](contents.xhtml#sc3_401a)

We now need to conduct effective performance testing from a database point of view, which needs a database tool that can measure the query performance, execution plan, and time to execute queries against various datastores. Using such a tool is even more important when you change from one database engine to another, like from Oracle to MySQL. Netflix has an interesting tool called NDBench ([https://github.com/Netflix/ndbench](https://github.com/Netflix/ndbench)) where you can perform NoSQL white box testing on the database side for: Cassandra, Redis, Elasticsearch, Elassandra, Amazon DynamoDB, JanusGraph, Netflix Dynomite.

Netflix used NDBench to help with its migration from Cassandra 2.0 to 2.1 ([https://github.com/Netflix/ndbench/wiki/NDBench-at-Netflix](https://github.com/Netflix/ndbench/wiki/NDBench-at-Netflix)). Figure 10.22 shows the NDBench Runner tool, where you can select your cluster, connect and configure the database driver, choose a test strategy, and launch; instance statistics lets you understand the impact of your tests on overall database performance. 

Figure 10.22: Netflix NDBench UI  
Source: [https://github.com/Netflix/ndbench/wiki/NDBench-UI](https://github.com/Netflix/ndbench/wiki/NDBench-UI)

[Sanity test: Post-migration structure and data](contents.xhtml#sc3_402a)

Great; you have completed the initial phase of data migration, and all the lights on your dashboards are green. Now you need to verify the sanity and consistency of your new database structure and data. Let us say you are migrating a relational database like a self-hosted Postgres cluster to RDS Postgres. You will want to check:

  * Tables sanity: All tables were migrated: 100 tables in Postgres == 100 tables in RDS Postgres. All columns, rows, indexes, and configurations are present as expected.

  * Data sanity: All data points were migrated; if you have 80.124 million rows in Table A, you have 80.124 rows in Table B.




The sanity test is one of the last tests we run in a data migration. To perform these tests, you need to be able to count and compare the number of tables and columns on both sides, and export your schema to compare SQL DDL instructions. Comparing the data at scale is very tough; another powerful technique is to spot check rows using checksums via hashing; if they match, the data is the same, and if not, it might warrant investigation.

[Observability](contents.xhtml#sc3_403a)

Vital for code and data migrations. Observability is how you make sense of and understand what your systems, services, and databases are doing and the overall state of your data. We covered testing previously, testing is a form of observability, but observability is much more.

Figure 10.23: Observability

The heart of observability is understanding what is going on with our software at runtime. You might already have tools in your environment that help you answer this question. Centralized logging is pretty popular nowadays, but logging alone is not the most effective way to understand what your system is doing or to troubleshoot errors. Dashboards and alerts are important for reliability and production operations, but they are just as critical for successful code and data migrations. Generally, teams need to instrument their code and publish customer metrics like:

  * Success counters: Count all successful operations.

  * Error counters: Count all operations with errors - error observability.

  * Latency percentiles: Measure performance with percentiles for latency: metrics for P50, P70, P75, P90, P95, P99.




Your tests are part of the observability of the system; tests should publish metrics too. Publishing metrics to an Application Performance Monitoring (APM)117 system like Prometheus ([https://prometheus.io/](https://prometheus.io/)) is a great way to go because then you can correlate events in its central time series database with database changes, logs, and other system output. Tracing individual process invocations through your stack is also very important; distributed tracing118 is important because one call could bridge many, many services, and you need to see the flow of calls across all our services. Distributed tracing / APM can also be leveraged for all migrations by identifying:

  * Overall performance: Key metrics like CPU, memory, disk I/O, and queue size.

  * Top used queries: Most frequent run queries on the system.

  * Slow queries: Top slow queries.

  * Execution time: Transaction, query, or process execution time.




During any migration, you must watch for performance bottlenecks and possible disruptions to the user experience. Observability for database performance and query or transaction monitoring is important to validate that the migration produces acceptable results.

Table 10.6 shows some observability tools you should consider using.

Observability tool |  Utility  
---|---  
[https://docs.datadoghq.com/database_monitoring/query_metrics/](https://docs.datadoghq.com/database_monitoring/query_metrics/) |  Datadog - APM for Queries for MySQL, Postgres, and SQL Server.  
[https://newrelic.com/lp/mysql](https://newrelic.com/lp/mysql) |  New Relic - APM for Queries for MySQL, +600 integrations.  
[https://www.solarwinds.com/database-performance-monitoring-software](https://www.solarwinds.com/database-performance-monitoring-software) |  Solar Winds, APM, and performance monitoring for Oracle.  
[https://grafana.com/](https://grafana.com/) |  Open-Source Monitoring Alternative  
  
Table 10.6: Observability solutions to consider

We have only begun to touch on how observability can help you; anything you can measure, you can observe. Observability can and should be used in many, many different contexts to see active use:

  * Hardware metrics: All application components (UI, services, websites, mobile apps, etc) need to publish basic metrics like CPU load, memory, disk usage, disk I/O, network I/O.

  * Tables: What tables are being accessed; read counters, write counters.

  * Columns: What columns are used for reading and writing.

  * Classes and methods: What classes in your code use more memory or are called more often?

  * Features: Instrument your mobile app with analytics such as Mixpanel ([https://mixpanel.com](https://mixpanel.com)/), and report out on whether specific features are ever used.

  * UI applications: How many requests does your website serve per day? Per hour? Per minute? What pages are the most popular in your app, and what is their render time? Classify and present this information by product, by business domain, or by organization.

  * Configurations: Is all software configuration actually used by your app? What configurations are used the most?

  * Jenkins jobs: What is the last time the job ran? What is the average failure/success rate of your jobs? How fast or slow is the job?

  * Tests: How many tests are running compared with how many are defined in your codebase? How many passes? How many fails? What is the pass rate? Are they flaky? Classify and present this information by product, by business domain, or by organization.

  * Services: How often is the service called? Who calls it, and what operations or data they request?

  * Serverless functions: How many functions are being actively called vs how many are defined? How often do they succeed or fail? Can you make sense of them; are they properly tagged to identify use cases?

  * Batch jobs: How many batch jobs are running vs. how many are defined? How often do they run? What is the execution time? How often do they fail or run successfully? Identify the dependency graph of your batch jobs if it exists.

  * Load balancers and API gateways: How many endpoints are defined vs how many are actively used? How many transactions are processed per hour? Per minute? Per second? Classify and present this information by product, by business domain, or by organization.

  * DNS names: How many DNS names do you have in production? How often are new DNS names being added or have their resolution changed? How many CNAMEs do you have vs A entries? Are your DNS servers overloaded? Overall, it helps to have a proper DNS structure to analyze observability data; a decent and predictable pattern like: $app_name.$business_domain.$cloud_az.$cloud_region.

  * Security groups: How many security group rules do your services have on average? Which services or assets are the outliers? Find services that permit too many callers and might be breaking isolation.

  * Clusters: Where are your servers deployed: EMR, EC2, ECS or EKS? Who owns the clusters? How much do they cost?

  * Servers: How many servers do you have? Are they properly tagged to identify use cases? Who owns them? What is the utilization/idle ratio of their resources? If under or overutilized, rightsize instances automatically to a better type.




Observability is highly valuable for both code and data migrations because classical monoliths and distributed monoliths tend to have dead code. Observability makes it easy to discover and delete dead code, making not only the migration easier but also saving costs by cleaning up unused resources, reducing complexity, reducing the scope of future migrations and just making everything better.

Everything should be observable.

[Post-data migrations](contents.xhtml#sc2_404a)

Once you are done, you have to clean up and truly decommission the old code. But you are not done until EVERY consumer is cleaned up - are you sure you caught everything? Some of the practices we will describe here are not just for post-migration; you will benefit from ghost hunting at pre-migration and during migration as well.

[Ghost hunting](contents.xhtml#sc3_405a)

Our migrations are performing well, and now it is time to decommission the old solutions and databases. Not so fast - we need to make sure we perform some auditing tasks to make sure we have not missed any consumers or applications. Verification is important because as you shut down servers, you might create outages or disrupt the user and developer experience.

Our nickname for this process is ghost hunting. Ghost hunting means running an analysis combined with your software inventory to confirm that all the consumers captured in our initial inventory are all the complete universe of real consumers. Here are a couple of possibilities that can easily happen:

  * Missing consumers: You miss some consumers that are not written in your usual stack. That is your service is written in Java, but this consumer was a C# application running on Windows or a Visual Basic macro someone in accounting heavily depends on.

  * Snowflake deployments: Some applications that were manually deployed, skipping your CI/CD pipelines. Do not forget that laptop shoved under a desk propping up the whole company.

  * Different teams and organizations: An application from another organization you are not familiar with, and you do not have access to the source code in your common repository.

  * New component: After you finish your inventory and start your migration process, a new service or application pops up, created without your knowledge.




And many more possibilities.

Figure 10.24: Ghost hunting spectrum

How can we detect ghosts? Using the same resources, people and tooling you used to conduct your inventory in the first place. (Figure 10.24) Let us say you are migrating from Postgres to MySQL, you can hunt ghosts by:

  * Ask around: Go ask your architects, DBAs, and tech leads; they might be aware of things you do not know. Maybe App X and Service Z will be decommissioned next month, and you do not need to take care of them as consumers, or you will learn of a new application being rushed to production by a busy team or even some hidden observability tool you did not know that existed that will expose a whole world of consumers you missed.

  * Reading the code: Search across repositories and read the source code; search for the Postgres JDBC driver JAR, for instance. Whatever you do, always read the code.

  * Know your tools: Maybe you are missing key metrics that teams do not know about and can be used to discover hidden consumers. Decent solutions always have admin UIs, APIs, and other metrics or ways to understand what is happening. Read the official documentation and research your tools before concluding you know the limit of what they can tell you.

  * Networking: Check your network via switch or AWS ELB / ALB access logs. List all IPs hitting the Postgres database you want to decommission and trace back the applications from there. Do not forget most application services like Tomcat also have access logs.

  * Observability: Go check your observability / APM tools for a list of all applications or database consumers to your Postgres instance in production.




Usually, ghost hunting should be done multiple times over the course of your project; regardless of whether this is a code migration, data migration, or any kind of migration. Migration projects can take a long time, and things change. If you can automate your ghost hunting techniques, great; otherwise, do it manually. You should be constantly consulting and using ghost hunting to improve your inventory to double check you have all the apps, even after you finish creating it for the first time! Ghost hunting is a great way to socialize your migration; you can give a heads-up to managers on what is happening and use these touch points to proactively call out teams that might need to perform actions as part of the migration.

Practicing ghost hunting early on in the migration process is valuable because imagine you are planning to release a solution using Java, and then you discover you have a consumer written in ASP.NET; well, your solution might not work for these consumers, and you might need to take a completely different approach! Now imagine an even scarier scenario: you find a snowflake consumer, a proprietary solution written in C, and you do not have the source code, only binaries. You might need to build a special solution. Better discover this sooner than later because late breaking surprises kill migrations.

[Clean up and decommissioning](contents.xhtml#sc3_406a)

Finally, after the ghost hunting it is time to decommission the old system, archive the old code, shut down the servers, and declare victory. Here are some tips for decommissioning systems:

  * Communication: Notify your teams well in advance of the decommissioning data; at least 2-4 weeks ahead of time.

  * Chaos: Shut down the services before you decommission them; if you did your job right, there should be zero complaints.

  * Archive code: Do not delete the code completely; archive the repository in git, GitHub or GitLab for instance.

  * Full data backup: Do not just shut down the servers and throw away the data; fully backup the old data and archive it in S3 / Glacier119.

  * Delete consumer code: Do not leave behind dead code; decommission consumer code by deleting code and cleaning up dependencies.




Cleaning up code and data is probably one of the most underperformed activities in our industry. People do not delete code, and this is wrong. Dead code leads to confusion, unnecessary migrations, and complicates troubleshooting. Dead code and dead data costs you money in people and infrastructure. Migrations are not done when you fire the last PR and deploy the last server; you are not done until you clean up and delete code and data. So do not hesitate.

[Things to remember](contents.xhtml#sc2_407a)

Congratulations, you have reached the end of [Chapter 10](ch10.xhtml), Data Migrations. Here are a few things to keep in mind:

  * Data migrations are challenging because of their high risks:

    * Data loss, data corruption

    * Customer experience disruption, possible brand issues

    * Financial loss

    * Rollback is harder and more complex

  * Assessments, inventories, and proper analysis are critical to data migrations.

  * An inventory in the form of a sheet should answer the questions:

    * Who owns the data?

    * What applications read and write to the particular migration target we have?

    * How much criticality does the data have? How much downtime can we tolerate?

    * What schema, tables, and columns are we talking about?

    * Can we decommission any data?

  * There are two approaches to gathering the data for the inventory:

    * People, by interviews and asking questions.

    * Tooling, via scanning and parsing the code base, using an observability APM tool or ORM observability.

    * An inventory is a great input for planning.

  * You might be lucky and find quick wins like:

    * Multiple schemas that can be split out to multiple instances.

    * Isolated sets of tables that can split out to independent schemas.

  * Lazy Migrations allow the application to handle database refactoring use cases such as:

    * Column refactoring

    * Table refactoring

    * Schema refactoring

    * Datastore migrations

    * Data format refactoring

  * However, lazy migrations have drawbacks like:

    * Delayed delivery timelines

    * Handling cold data

    * Adding latency by introducing new code

    * How do you handle errors

  * Export and import are a simple and effective data migration pattern, except:

    * Need to be performed offline

    * Harder to change data persistence technologies

  * Database replication can be used for several use cases:

    * Scaling out

    * Fault tolerance and disaster recovery

    * Analytical workloads

    * Backups

    * Data migrations

    * Database replication can be used to sync two different databases.

    * Table diffs and triggers are two different ways to support data migrations.

    * Change Data Capture (CDC) is the preferred way to handle complex data migrations.

    * Debezium is a good open-source option for CDC.

    * In dual writing, you write to both your source and target databases from your application.

  * Dual writes has drawbacks like:

    * Handling cold data

    * Dealing with failure

    * Increased latency

    * Offline migrations are easier, but your business might not accept the downtime.

    * Schema first migrations move data to a new schema all at once.

    * The multiple migration strategy moves the data first, then transforms it to the new schema.

    * Strangler Fig is the ideal pattern to slice and dice classical monoliths and distributed monoliths.

    * Proper testing is essential for both code and data migrations.

  * Proper migration testing consists of:

    * Pre-migration sanity testing

    * Application performance testing

    * Database performance testing

    * Post-migration sanity testing

  * Pre-migration sanity tests include:

    * Check that the build is passing.

    * Check that all your tests have a 100% pass rate.

    * Check for errors in your logging systems.

    * Check that all your dashboards are clean and no fake alerts are being triggered.

  * Migration performance testing comes in two flavors:

    * Black box testing, which checks performance from an application perspective

    * White box testing, which checks performance from a database perspective

    * NDBench is a good database performance testing tool.

    * Sanity checks your database structure and data post-migration.

    * Are all tables, columns, indexes and configurations migrated correctly?

    * Are all data points successfully migrated?

    * Observability is how you make sense of and understand your entire system.

    * Application Performance Monitoring (APM) systems are a great way to capture and report on observability metrics.

    * Count usage of all assets and publish usage metrics for key items, like hardware metrics, tables, columns, classes and methods, features, UI applications, configuration, Jenkins jobs, tests, services, serverless functions, batch jobs, load balancers and API gateways, DNS names, security groups, clusters, and servers.

    * Post-data migration activities like ghost hunting, cleanup, and decommissioning are essential to wrap up any migration.

  * Ghost hunting is a form of auditing that can catch missing consumers, using techniques like:

    * Ask around

    * Read the code

    * Know your tools

    * Networking

    * Observability

    * Ghost hunting should be performed at multiple points during your migration process.

  * Tips to be followed before decommissioning:

    * Communication

    * Chaos

    * Archive code

    * Full data backup

    * Delete consumer code

  * Maintaining dead code is wrong, and costs organizations both money and time.

  * Do not hesitate to delete unused code and data.
