# [CHAPTER 11Epilogue](contents.xhtml#ch11a)

In the end, there is no end.

— Robert Lowell

You reached the end of the book! However, your journey does not end here. Here we want to reinforce some ideas, and principles and again give more direction. Let’s go!

[Structure](contents.xhtml#sc2_408a)

In this chapter, we will cover the following topics:

  * Encore!

  * Principles matter the most

  * Education

  * City planning

  * Vision

  * Onward




[Encore!](contents.xhtml#sc2_409a)

Software engineering is a complex field. You might not realize it, but we do the impossible every day.

An effective software engineer practices many professions, not just one; they are:

  * A superhero drawing on skills, abilities, and hidden strengths they never knew they needed when they signed up for Computer Science 101;

  * A firefighter, running to the fire to handle production incidents;

  * A private investigator, the ultimate sleuth, hunting through logs and lines of code to find the root of the problem;

  * A doctor, quickly diagnosing the health of a system, identifying design gaps and both long and short-term fixes;

  * A scientist, creating and testing hypotheses and only paying attention to real data;

  * An archaeologist, investigating dusty old codebases, making sense of arcane architectures, software, and code;

  * A teacher and a student, learning both business and technology to solve problems, mentoring engineers in the right practices and principles to follow;

  * A lawyer, litigating the right solutions and designs like there is no tomorrow; convincing architects, managers, and the business to pursue the best outcomes;

  * A craftsman, building beautiful and durable software, fast, efficient, secure, isolated solutions that will stand the test of time;

  * A runner, sometimes sprinting to reduce the impact in production, or finishing a year-long marathon to ship a painful migration;

  * An artist, producing beautiful, elegant experiences that delight their customers;

  * A chef, working hard as a team to cook delicious dishes and providing leadership the whole way.




On top of it all, technology constantly changes, and complexity constantly grows. We live in a world of old solutions that were once great but are now aged and new solutions hastily built by a team to add the latest and greatest tech stack to their resume. We have complexity beyond measure, and fighting complexity is a never-ending battle. There is no end to the problems, and you might ask yourself: if we are in a never-ending game, why bother? After all, why care about classical and distributed monoliths if we could all be on the beach having mojitos, right? Much easier to just look the other way.

We are here to tell you: that you can play better, you can fight, and you can win! Do not look the other way! No matter what you are going through, do not give up, and do not surrender; the only way is forward, constantly improving, making things better for you and your team even if no one sees it. Remember: the right thing is the right thing even if no one else is doing it, and the wrong thing is the wrong thing, even if everybody is doing it. Do things better because it is the right thing to do.

You are not alone. You can read, study, learn, and do it better if you just believe. Because we must, we can, and we will! Passion is contagious. If you can influence 2 people, and they influence 2 more, you will soon be surrounded by amazing people, and you will change the world. Have patience but do not settle. Forward is the only way!

[Principles matter the most](contents.xhtml#sc2_410a)

Figure 11.1: Principles by Thomas Jefferson  
Source: [https://www.azquotes.com/quote/145670](https://www.azquotes.com/quote/145670)

Principles are not optional. If there is only one thing you take away from this book, it should be that principles matter. Always leverage and double down on your principles. Isolation, stable contracts, and lean libraries must be the bread and butter of your thinking every day. Technology changes all the time, but principles are solid and lasting.

Embracing any new technology means change. Change is hard; change comes with risk. When you embrace new technology, you can and will make mistakes. But by doing nothing, by not upgrading, there is no escape from your current problems. Pushing forward with new technology and proper software designs is the only answer; however, you can, and you must follow the principles.

  * Isolation is everything: Isolate everything, starting with the business domains, design stable constraints, never share datastores, be careful, and mind internal shared libraries.

  * Proper services: SOA is the way to go! Leverage services everywhere you can. Do more services than every other style of component, and do fewer libraries.

  * Fight complexity: You need to be fighting complexity all the time, non-stop. You cannot deliver the best user experience quickly and constantly add business value if you are in the middle of toxic waste land. Managers might not help you fight complexity, and education can help open their eyes. Everybody needs to be complexity aware.

  * Architecture is all about tradeoffs: Do not take anything for granted; everything has pros and cons, strengths and drawbacks. If you think your favorite new technology is perfect, think again; if you cannot see the drawbacks, you do not know enough. Look for the problems, consider how things can go wrong, and identify the corner cases.




Do not take any technology decision for granted. Attention to detail is a must. Have inventories, have observability, and make sure you think about decisions. When you want to design a service, run a tradeoff analysis and ask whether it should be a library; when you design a library, run a tradeoff analysis and ask yourself whether it should be a service. Tradeoff analysis is a constant activity because technology is constantly changing. Yesterday’s correct decisions are today’s incorrect ones. Tradeoff analysis should be constant, it is how you should be operating and, ultimately an essential part of proper software architecture. Push back and do not go with the flow without thinking about the consequences.

[Education](contents.xhtml#sc2_411a)

Figure 11.2: University of Bologna, Italy: World’s oldest continually operating university  
Source: [https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Learning)

The first step in any software engineering journey is to acknowledge your ignorance; however much you know, you do not know enough. Blindly pursuing past patterns can drive you to dead ends. That applies to you, and that applies to your entire team. Education is everything; you cannot transform your company if people do not learn what a better answer looks like. You cannot fix your bad monoliths if teams make the same mistakes over and over again. Learning needs to be prioritized.

Doing technical training is not enough. Online training often does not teach you principles. You need to understand and discuss principles, techniques, and how to isolate things constantly.

Retrospectives are a must to build a culture of learning and introspection. Retrospectives must be long and deep to discover root causes and promote great behaviors.

Modernization is a war too big to be fought alone. Stop thinking your engineers do not get it, and they get it. Believe in them, teach them the principles, and expose them to new engineering techniques. Teach and give people freedom. Teams that know and apply principles would not go wrong; exercising tight control on everything will not make better software. Having proper services is about building effective teams that will go above and beyond to do the right thing.

[City planning](contents.xhtml#sc2_412a)

Figure 11.3: Aerial view of Barcelona, Spain  
Source: [https://www.dreamstime.com/stock-photo-aerial-view-typical-buildings-eixample-district-barcelo-residential-barcelona-spain-image65880510](https://www.dreamstime.com/stock-photo-aerial-view-typical-buildings-eixample-district-barcelo-residential-barcelona-spain-image65880510)

We get it; sometimes you need to deal with production issues and fight fires, but you cannot live in crisis mode 24/7 and design effective software architectures. Software architecture is about preemptive planning, thinking two or three steps ahead, anticipating success, but also planning for failure.

Instead of micromanaging chaos, you need to think like a city designer; you need to do city zoning. Do you want to have all industries near the public schools? Where do you want people to live, shop and work? A city designer lays out their plan, and zoning blocks for work, for play, and for everything in between, and lets their builders build. Just like in software, architects articulate their principles, design the overall system, and then give space for developers to experiment and grow; proper teams will understand, step up, and fill in the gaps with the needed solutions. The city designer did not worry whether there would be a coffee shop on that specific corner; he wanted people to live their lives and be happy. Likewise, effective software balances rules and freedom. The architect’s design lives in the overall system, not every detail.

A city is an infinite game120: there is life and death, creation and destruction happening every day. Change is constant; cities are living beings, continuously in a cycle of rising and falling, buildings being demolished, and houses being built at the same time, all the time. Just like companies, they will grow and shrink over time, companies acquired and divisions divested. Change is inevitable; mistakes are inevitable. You will never be able to rewrite all software at once. You will always have a mix of good services, bad services, monoliths, and distributed monoliths.

Figure 11.4: Decision building blocks for modernization

Can you make everybody move to a different city while you rebuild the whole city in 50 years? No, it is not possible. Instead, part of the city is being built, part of the city is being torn down, and part of it is being constantly improved with renovations. The same is true for software. You need to set a long-term goal; you cannot fix it all at once, but you can and should improve over time. Lean thinking is about long-term thinking, you should be thinking long-term.

Remember the building blocks for software modernization: decommission, improve, rewrite, or ignore. (Figure 11.4) You cannot just pick one. You will always have to triage services that you will rewrite and ones you cannot afford to yet, but that does not mean you stop there. Do not just sit there and wait for the rewrite, and do not rewrite for the sake of rewriting! You need to improve services all the time; perhaps one of the most important things you can do. Fixing monoliths is not binary, it is a complex spectrum and a living system that should be in a continuous state of change. You do not decide to tear down and rebuild the entire city at once; you can tear down some, rebuild others, and renovate the parts that need it, zone by zone, neighborhood by neighborhood.

Your business is an infinite game, software improvement is continuous, the cat-and-mouse game of security is forever, attackers will never stop attacking your company, maintenance never ends, and the business constantly comes with new requirements. You will always need to improve your software. People will always produce tech debt. Your company will acquire other companies or will get acquired, resulting in issues, bad technology, and someone breaking isolation.

Projects are the wrong approach121. Projects are a finite game; projects assume there is an end, and they look to balance time, cost, and resources. You know the story by now: you try to finish your project within the (incorrect, unrealistic) deadline, quality is destroyed, you compromise on your principles, you break isolation, and often you do not get a better product, but for sure you get more technical debt.

Modernization projects fail because they are projects. By definition, modernization projects will fail because modernization is an infinite game; you cannot finish modernization in 1, 2 or 5 years. In truth, the work never ends except if your company goes bankrupt and closes. Software should be a continuum, and modernization should be continuous. Fixing monoliths should be a philosophy of life, not a project.

[Vision](contents.xhtml#sc2_413a)

Why do you want to fix monoliths? Yes, they have terrible problems. But the problems they create are not just about software; they also impact people. The monolith shapes your organization, makes it one that demands unnecessary processes, and creates a culture that fears change. The monolith is bigger than just technology, and it makes your teams think in broken ways. Bad software grinds on, taking and taking, draining the energy and motivation from the people who must be paid to work on it. The companies that allow classical and distributed monoliths are not good companies to work for!

So, when you bought this book, without realizing it, you were embarking on a journey that was not just about refactoring code; it was about changing your company’s culture. You bought a book about software; it turned out to be about philosophy, about changing mindsets, and ultimately about changing lives.

After all, if you follow our principles, your software will no longer force the wrong thing on your engineers. Instead, you will have proper services and empowered teams, happy customers, and success. When you do the right things, you will be giving your people opportunities to learn, to grow, to work on exciting projects, to be delighted in their work, and to delight your customers in turn. You will transform your company into a better company, one worth working for. You spend so much of your waking life at your company; should not you love it?

You have all seen the Apple’s Think Different commercial: Here is to the crazy ones.122 Changing your company’s culture starts with one person that truly believes in a better way of doing things, a leader with a vision of a brighter future.

That person needs to be you.

[Onward](contents.xhtml#sc2_414a)

Share! educate others, and help to spread the word. You cannot change anything alone, do your part by convincing others. When you do not share, you do not learn; sharing is not only caring but is learning.

Creating output is necessary for learning. How do you learn math? By doing math! Call it homework, exercises or just plain practice. What is the point of just reading something and doing nothing with it? Tell others, write blog posts, and apply the thinking in this book to your day-by-day work.

We do not want you to just read this book, we want you to effectively learn it. You cannot learn without practice. You do not need a business project to practice; while the best way to learn anything is to apply it in your day-to-day work, you can explore many of the ideas of this book through your POCs. Our recipe for learning should not be applied to this book but, in fact, to any book, to anything you aim to learn. Theory requires practice, and practice needs theory.

Go change the world! Be a punk rocker, do not accept the status quo, introduce new, amazing things into the world. Do not be afraid; believe in yourself, you can do this; we can do this. By finishing this book, you are taking the first step!

Thank you for reading this long book. We appreciate your time.

“Vamos que vamos”

Diego Pacheco

Sam Sgro

# Preface

Why did we write this book?

Software architecture is an amazing discipline, with many styles and forms coming in and out of fashion over time. Some tend towards centralization, like Blackboard and Monolith; others focus on distribution, like Event-Driven, Service Oriented Architecture (SOA, which is dear and warm to our heart), Microservices, Peer-2-Peer, REST, or Remote Procedure Call (RPC). Some styles are good for decisioning systems, like Rule-Based; others are great for concurrency and parallelism, like Share-Nothing and Actor, or focus on layering, like Client-Server, N Tier, or Component-Based. Some styles are good for data and long-running background tasks like SEDA and Streaming.

Architecture is cool, exciting, motivating, and warms our hearts, but there are traps too: bad practices, dark anti-patterns, and monoliths that live in the heart of that darkness.

How often did you hear complaints from the business or another engineer that a system is terrible, hard to maintain, and holding us back? How often have you heard from the business that the tech organization is slow and doesn't deliver? How often have you heard from engineers that they are drowning in technical debt and not as productive as they could be?

The answer is architecture; bad architecture created these problems, and good architecture will fix them. It's possible to have better systems, systems that can be maintained, sane, scalable, following proper design and architecture principles, and deliver what the business wants and what the engineers want. You can have your cake and eat it too. The work is not impossible, but we will not lie: this is difficult, complex, a never-ending battle that requires Homeric amounts of discipline and attention to detail. You can’t do such a task without care and passion: for yourself, your coworkers, your company, and the work that you do.

We wrote this book to share our passion and perspective about the common problems all companies face across all industries. Problems include technical debt, lack of correct principles, distributed monoliths, internal shared libraries, code and data migrations, and other essential concerns. Such problems are not new; they have been around for a long time and will likely be around as long as humans or AIs are writing software.

Our book will not give you easy answers or a magic formula for success; we are here to make you think, perform tradeoff analysis, and make the best-informed decision possible. If you want a magic wand to fix your problems, this book is not for you.

We will be very visual in this book, so expect a lot of diagrams to help convey our points. Our book is Java-centric but not code-heavy. You will see some pseudocode examples but do not expect complete applications built end to end. This is not a tutorial book.

Our goal with this book is to make sure you can fully understand the problems around monoliths and how you can approach them correctly and effectively. We will help you stop the bleeding, make sense of your reality, and have a path forward to better days and better systems using solid architectural principles and a bit of creativity.

We will be very technical in this book and will connect many different subjects, but don't worry. We will explain things in depth and with lots of practical scenarios and examples. The topics we will cover are wide-ranging, and sometimes we will review the same points from different angles to uncover different perspectives. We hope you like it, and thank you in advance for your readership.

While reading this book, you can expect:

  * Examples: Practical examples from our experience in technology.

  * Tradeoff Analyses: Architecture is all about tradeoffs, so expect many comparisons of pros and cons.

  * Figures: Many diagrams and pictures to illustrate scenarios, tradeoffs, and options.

  * Multiple Options: We will provide multiple options and the best analysis to make you consider the entire problem space.

  * Repetition: We will repeat some principles over and over, analyzing them in different contexts to find new understanding.

  * Summary and Learning: Every chapter will have a summary of things to remember from each chapter. This is a long book, and you might need to read it multiple times; make notes in whatever way works for you, and look back on what you find interesting or disagree with. (The authors like productive disagreements!) Remember that when you just passively read (input) you don’t learn as much as when you produce (output). You can write a blog post, run a lightning talk or presentation to your engineers or company, or talk to a friend. It is important to produce output, and we believe that’s the best way to learn anything, not only this book.




However, this book will not have:

  * Easy Answers: There is no magic formula to fix your monoliths, just options we will help you navigate and digest to find your own answers. No quick fixes to your complex problems, just reality.

  * Tutorials: You won't find step-by-step instructions on how to build applications. This is not a tutorial book.
